IN  THE

Supreme  Court  of the Gnited  States
OcToBER  TERM,  1975.

No.

METROPOLITAN  HOUSING  DEVELOPMENT
CORPORATION,  ETAL.,

vs.

Respondents,

THE  VILLAGE  OF  ARLINGTON  HEIGHTS,  &rT  AL.,

'

Petitioners,

NORTHWEST  OPPORTUNITY  CENTER  AnpD
ELUTERIA  D.  MALDONADO,

Intervening  Respondents.

PETITION  FOR  WRIT  OF  CERTIORARI  TO  THE
UNITED  STATES  COURT  OF  APPEALS
FOR  THE  SEVENTH  CIRCUIT.

The  Village  of Arlington  Heights,  an  Illinois  municipal  corpo-
ration,  petitions  for  writ  of  certiorari  to  review  the  decision  of
the  United  States  Court  of  Appeals  for  the  Seventh  Circuit,
decided  June  10,  1975.

OPINIONS  BELOW.

QUESTIONS  PRESENTED  FOR  REVIEW.

The  Seventh  Circuit’s  decision  reversing  the  District  Court  is
reported  in 517  F.  2d  409  and  is reprinted  in full  as  Appendix
A  to  this  petition.  The  District  Court’s  decision  is  reported  at
373  F.  Supp.  208  and  is  reprinted  in  full  as  Appendix  B  to
this  petition.

JURISDICTION.

The  judgment  of the Court  of Appeals  for the  Seventh  Circuit
was  entered  on  June  10,  1975.  The  order  en  banc  denying  the
petition  for rehearing  was  entered  on  August  13,  1975,  with  the
majority  of  the  active  members  of  the  Court  having  voted  to
deny  a hearing  en  banc  and  three  judges  voting  that  the  petition
be granted.  A copy  of the  order  of August  13,  1975  is reprinted
as  Appendix C to  this  petition.  The  jurisdiction  of this  Court  is
invoked  pursuant  to the  provisions  of 28  U.  S.  C.  § 1254.  This
petition  for  writ  of certiorari  has  been  filed  in  accordance  with
28  U.  S.  C.  § 2101.

1.  Does  the  failure  to  grant  a  rezoning  request  for  multiple-
family  housing  for  low-  and  moderate-income  families  in  the
midst  of  a  single-family  area  violate  the  Fourteenth  Amend-
ment,  even  though  the  Village  was  admittedly  maintaining  the
integrity  of its  zoning  plan  and  protecting  neighboring  property
values?

2.  Does  an  alleged  discriminatory  housing  pattern  in  the
Chicago  metropolitan  area  impose  upon  a suburban  municipality
an  affirmative  duty  to  ignore  its  admittedly  proper  zoning  ordi-
nance  in  order  to  permit  the  construction  of  multi-family  low-
and  moderate-income  housing?

3.  Is  housing  a  fundamental  constitutional  right  which  re-

quires  the  application  of  the  “compelling  interest”  test?

4.  Did  the  plaintiffs  overcome  the presumption  of  validity
attaching  to  the  zoning  ordinance  of  the  Village  of Arlington
Heights?

5.  Did  the  plaintiffs  have  sufficient  standing  to  litigate  the

merits  of  the  zoning  ordinance?

CONSTITUTIONAL  PROVISION  AND
STATUTES  INVOLVED.

This  case  involves  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  of the  Consti-
tution  of  the  United  States;  42  U.  S. C.  A.  §§ 1981-1982,  the
Civil  Rights  Act  of  1866;  42  U.  S.  C.  A.  § 1983,  the  Civil
Rights  Act  of  1871;  42  U.S.  C.  A.  §§ 3601,  et  seq.,  the  Fair
Housing  Act  of  1968.  The  relevant  provisions  are  set  forth  in
Appendix  D to  this  petition.

rer se
per

STATEMENT  OF  THE  CASE.

(References  Are  to  Appendices)

The  plaintiff,  Metropolitan  Housing  Development  Corpora-
tion,  is a  not-for-profit  corporation  organized  for  the  purpose  of
developing  low-  and  moderate-income  housing  in  the  Chicago
metropolitan  area.  It  has  a  99-year  lease  and  sale  agreement
with  the  owners  of  the  subject  property  which  is  a  vacant  15-
acre  tract  in the  southeast  corner  of an  80-acre  parcel  owned  by
the  Clerics  of  St.  Viator  who  maintain  a  high  school  and
novitiate  on  a  portion  of  the  larger  tract  in  the  Defendant
Village  of  Arlington  Heights.  The  contract  and  lease  were
subject  to  rezoning  for  multi-family  purposes  and  the  securing
of  a  Section  236  financing  commitment.

The  property  is zoned  for  R-3  One-Family  Dwelling  District
under  the  Zoning  Ordinance  of  the  defendant  Village,  and  has
been  zoned  for  single-family  use  since  the  first  Village  Zoning
Ordinance.  Under  the  Comprehensive  Plan  of  1959,  the  prop-
erty  was  designated  for  single-family  use.

Under  the  R-3  classification  the  property  can  be  developed
for  single-family  detached  dwellings  at  a density  of  8,750  square
feet  of  lot  area  per  dwelling.  Approximately  50  single-family
units  can  be  placed  upon  the  subject  property.

The  purchase  price  of  the  subject  property  is $300,000.  The
plaintiffs  propose  to  use  the  land  for  190  townhouses  which
were  to  be  financed  pursuant  to  Section  236  of  the  National
Housing  and  Urban  Development  Act  of  1968.

All  the  land  surrounding  the  subject  property  is  zoned  and
A map  showing  the  exist-
developed  for  R-3  single-family  use.
ing land  use  and  zoning  and  photographs  of the  development  of
the  surrounding  area  which  were  received  in evidence  appear  as
Appendices  E and F to  this  petition.

5

The  plaintiff  applied  for a zoning  change  to  the  R-5  multiple-
family  classification.  After  public  hearings,  the  Plan  Commis-
sion  recommended  against  the  rezoning,  and  the  Board  of Trus-
tees  of the  Village  voted  six  to  one  to  deny  the  request.

Suit  was  then  instituted  by  the  plaintiff,  corporation,  and
certain  individual  plaintiffs  seeking to represent  moderate  income
minority  persons  who  claimed  to  seek  housing  in  Arlington
Heights.  A Mexican-American  resident  of Arlington  Heights  and
a local  community  organization  intervened.  Federal  jurisdiction
was  invoked  by the  plaintiffs’  claim  of  a  violation  of  the  Four-
teenth  Amendment,  Sections  1981,  1982  and  1983  of  the  Civil
Rights  Act,  and  the  Fair  Housing  Act  of  1968,  42  U.  S.  C.
§§  1981,  1982  and  1983,  and  §§ 3601,  et  seq.

The  plaintiffs’  evidence  showed  that  14 other  sites  in Arling-
ton  Heights  were  examined;  9  of  them  were  rejected  because
they  were  too  far  removed  from  public  facilities  or  were  sur-
rounded  by  single-family  homes;  6  other  sites  were  rejected
because  they  were  too  expensive.  The  corporate  plaintiff  had
previously  developed  a  212-unit  low-  and  moderate-income
multiple-family  project  in  an  unincorporated  area  three-fourths
of  a  mile  outside  the  corporate  limits  of  the  Village.

The  1970  population  of the  Village of Arlington  Heights  was
64,800.  Based  on  1970  census  data,  the  plaintiffs’  evidence
showed  that  3,244  Blacks  could  afford  to  live  in  Arlington
Heights.  The  plaintiffs  had  not  made  a  survey  of  housing  pref-
erences  of  Blacks  employed  in  Arlington  Heights  and  had  not
made  an  investigation  of  the  number  of  Blacks  or  Spanish-
speaking  persons  in  Arlington  Heights  in  1971  at  the  time  of
the  local  hearing.

In August of 1973,  there  were  14,444  owner-occupied,  single-
family  homes  and  6,085  multiple-family  units  in  Arlington
Heights.  At  the  time  of  the  MHDC  zoning  hearing,  there  was
land  zoned  but  not  developed  for  a  total  of  9,073  multiple-
family  dwelling  units.  The  vacancy  rate  for  apartments  in

6

7

Arlington  Heights  according  to  the  1970  census  was  11.5%.
At the  time  of the  MHDC  hearing  before  the  Plan  Commission,
at  least  seven  parcels  which  were  zoned  for  R-5  multiple  pur-
poses  were  for  sale.

The  defendants’  evidence  established  the  single-family  charac-
ter  of the  area  in question,  and  the  consistent  Village  policy  of
using  the  R-5  classification  as  a  buffer  between  single-family
uses  and  commercial  or  industrial  uses.  The  subject  property
would  not  constitute  such  a  buffer.

The  defendants’  expert  testimony  indicated  that  the  proposed
development  would  have  an  adverse  effect  on  adjacent  property
owners  and  would  have  a  detrimental  effect  on  the  value  of
single-family  homes  in the  area.  The  defendants  also  introduced
evidence  showing  that  the  subject  property  could  be  economi-
cally  developed  for  single-family  homes  at  a  price  range  com-
parable  or  higher  than  the  existing  single-family  homes  in  the
area.

The  trial  court  held  that  the  evidence  established  that  the
Village  Trustees  were  motivated  by a legitimate  desire  to  protect
property  values  and  the  integrity  of the  Village’s  zoning  plan.  It
further  found  that  low-income  workers  do  not  have  a  constitu-
tional  right to low-rental  housing  either  where  they work  or  else-
where.  The  trial  court  found  that  there  was  no  evidence  proving
discrimination  against  racial  minorities  as  distinguished  from
underprivileged  persons  generally,  and  that  the  decision  was  not
an  arbitrary  or  capricious  act  in  violation  of  the  Fourteenth
Amendment.  (B5,  B7)

The  Court  of  Appeals  in  reviewing  evidence  of  other  re-
quested  zoning  changes  in  the  Village  found  that  the  evidence
did  not  necessitate  a  finding  that  the  Village  was  administering
its  buffer  zone  policy  in  a  discriminatory  manner.  (A5)  It
also  concluded  that  the  District  Court’s  finding  that  the  defend-
ants  were  concerned  with  the  integrity  of  the  Village’s  zoning
plan  was  not  clearly  erroneous.  (A5)  The  Court  of  Ap-

peals  recognized  that  merely  because  racial  minorities  consti-
tuted  a  higher  percentage  of  the  low-  and  moderate-income
category,  this  disparity  did  not  necessarily  mean  that  the  zoning
change  refusal  had  the  type  of racially  discriminatory  effect  that
required  the  invocation  of  the  compelling  interest  test.  (A6)
The  Court  of  Appeals  however  noted  that  historically  the
Chicago  metropolitan  area  has  been  segregated  and  that  in  1970
there  were  Only  27  Black  residents  in  Arlington  Heights,  and
that  in the  four-township  northwest  Cook  County  area  of which
Arlington  Heights  is  a  part  there  was  a  population  increase
from  1960  to  1970  of  219,000  people,  but  only  170  of  these
were  Black.  The  Court  held  that  under  the  facts  of the  case,  the
Village’s  rejection  of  the  Lincoln  Green  Project  had  racially
discriminatory  effects.  It  applied  the  compelling  public  interest
test  and  concluded  that  the  planning  rationale  behind  the  buffer
zone  policy  and  the  fact  that  neighboring  property  might  be
diminished  in value  could  not  meet  the  compelling  interest  test.
It  concluded  that  since  no  compelling  justification  for  the
decision  existed,  the  refusal  to  rezone  was  a  violation  of  the
Equal  Protection  Clause  of  the  Fourteenth  Amendment.

Chief  Judge  Fairchild  dissented,  noting  that  at  the  time  of
the  Village’s  decision  to  deny the  variance,  there  were  at least  9
undeveloped  tracts  of land  in  excess  of  15  acres  zoned  for  R-5
multiple-family  use,  and  that  the  record  did  not  contain  a  suffi-
cient  showing  that  it was  not  reasonably  possible  to  construct
the  proposed  project  on  one  of  these  sites.

REASONS  RELIED  ON FOR  ALLOWANCE  OF THE  WRIT.

The  writ  should  be  granted  in  this  case  because  the  decision
of  the  Court  of  Appeals  in  reversing  the  trial  court  is  clearly
in conflict  with  the  applicable  decisions  of this  Court  and  estab-
lishes  a  novel  view  of  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  which
threatens  the  validity  of  zoning  ordinances  not  only  in  the
Chicago  metropolitan  area,  but  throughout  the  nation.  The
explicit  conclusion  of  the  majority  of  the  Court  of  Appeals
is  that  a  refusal  to  rezone  property,  which  is  clearly  suitable
for  single-family  purposes  in  the  midst  of  a  single-family  area,
constitutes  a  violation  of  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  simply
because  there  is a  possibility  that  a  larger  percentage  of  Black
residents  may  occupy  the  proposed  multiple-family  development
than  if  the  area  were  developed  for  single-family  purposes.
The  majority’s  opinion  holds  for  the  first  time  that  zoning
principles  must  give way  to racial  considerations,  while  admitting
that  the  zoning  itself  is  proper  by  all  conventional  zoning
standards,  and  that  the  trial  court’s  finding  that  the  defendants
were  concerned  with  the  integrity  of  the  Village  Zoning
Ordinance  was  not  erroneous.

Moreover,  the  opinion  applies  the  “compelling  interest”  test
to  a zoning  controversy,  notwithstanding  the  fact  that  this  Court
has  specifically  refused  to  apply  that  test  in comparable  factual
situations.

The  opinion  now  imposes  for  the  first  time  a  duty  on  a
municipality  to  disregard  its  Comprehensive  Plan  and  its
Zoning  Ordinance,  which  were  admittedly  not  discriminatory  in
any  manner,  and  to  take  affirmative  steps  to  zone  for  racial
integration  because  of  segregation  in  the  metropolitan  area
generally.  The  opinion  is  one  of  great  consequence  which
demands  the  scrutiny  of  this  Court.

I,

THE  DECISION  IS  IN  CONFLICT  WITH  THIS  COURT’S  RE-
PEATED  HOLDINGS  THAT  HOUSING  AND  ZONING  ARE
NOT  SUBJECT  TO  THE  “COMPELLING  INTEREST”  TEST.
IT  STRIKES  DOWN  AN  ADMITTEDLY  VALID  LEGISLA-
TIVE  ACT  SIMPLY  BECAUSE  THE  POSSIBLE  IMPACT
FALLS  UPON  A  LOWER  INCOME  GROUP  WHICH  COULD
HAVE  A  HIGHER  PERCENTAGE  OF  BLACKS  THAN
WHITES.

The  Court  of Appeals’  opinion  in this  case  breaks  new  ground.
The  majority  of  the  Court  in  reversing  the  trial  court  conceded
that  the  findings  of  the  trial  court  with  respect  to  the  lack  of
discriminatory  intent  or  application  of  the  Arlington  Heights
Zoning  Ordinance  were  correct.  Nevertheless,  the  Court  applied
the  “compelling  interest”  test  to  invalidate  a  local  zoning  ordi-
nance  simply  because  the  economics  of the  marketplace  dictated
that  a  lower  income  group  might  otherwise  be  barred  from  the
use  of  the  subject  property  and  that  lower  income  group  might
contain  a  higher  percentage  of  Blacks  than  would  be  the  case
for  a  use  permitted  under  the  ordinance.  It should  be  noted  that
the  importance  of this  case  is such  that  three  of the  eight Circuit
Judges  voted  in favor  of granting  a  rehearing.

The  need  for  this  Court  to  settle  the  question  raised  by the
Court  of  Appeals’  opinion  is obvious.  If the  opinion  is allowed
to  stand,  no  zoning  ordinance  in  the  United  States  will  be
sustained  in  the  face  of  an  argument  that  a  higher  percentage
of  a  minority  group  or  poor  people  generally  could  occupy  an
area  if the  zoning  was  downgraded.

It  should  be  noted  in  the  first  instance  that  the  trial  judge
in  this  case  made  specific  findings  in  his  opinion  which  were
not  disputed  by the  Court  of Appeals.  The  trial  judge  found  that
the  plaintiffs  failed  to  carry  their  burden  of  proving  discrimina-
tion  by  the  defendants  as  to  racial  minorities  as  distinguished
from  the  underprivileged  generally.  There  is  no  doubt  that  as

eee

10

11

the  trial  judge  found,  housing  for  low  earners  is  scarcer  in
Arlington  Heights  than  in some  other  communities.  But,  as  the
trial  court  pointed  out,  this  affects  the  entire  low-income  group,
not  merely  Blacks.  It  is clear  that  the  Fourteenth  Amendment
and  the  Civil  Rights  Act  prohibit  discrimination  against  Blacks
and  minorities,  but  they  do  not  afford  rights  to  poor  people
as  such.

The  record  is also  clear  that  Blacks  and  other  minorities  do
live  in  Arlington  Heights  and  that  at  the  time  of  the  original
decision  by the  Village  Board,  there  was  an  11%  vacancy  rate
for rental  units  in the  Village.  The  individual  intervenor-plaintifi,
Eluteria  D.  Maldonado,  is  in  fact  a  Mexican  American  who
already  resides  in  the  Village.  The  Court  of  Appeals  notes
in  passing  that  Blacks  could  occupy  5%  of  the  housing  in
Arlington  Heights  based  upon  the  cost  of  presently  available
housing  of  all  types.

The  Court  however  ignored  the  testimony  of  the  plaintiffs’
own  witness,  deVise,  that  2,500  Blacks  whose  income  would
qualify  for Lincoln  Green,  the  proposed  project,  were  also  quali-
fied  for  other  housing  in the  Village  of Arlington  Heights  which
was  in  existence  in  1970.  deVise  testified  that  3,244  Blacks
could  afford  to live  in Arlington  Heights  based  upon  the  housing
supply  in existence  in  1970.  Moreover,  in  his  computations,  he
considered  only people  moving  from  house  to  house  or  apartment
to  apartment,  not  the  possibility  of Blacks  moving  from  existing
apartments  in other  communities  to  houses  in Arlington  Heights
or  Blacks  moving  from  houses  in  other  communities  to  apart-
ments  in Arlington  Heights.  We  submit  that  this  evidence  clearly
indicates  that  it has  not  been  the  zoning  pattern  of  the  Village
which  has  kept  Blacks  from  moving  into  the  community.

We  suggest  that  the  finding  of  the  trial  judge  that  there  was
insufficient  evidence  to  show  discrimination  by  the  defendants
against  racial  minorities  is dispositive  of the  whole  controversy.
The  Court  of Appeals  in its  opinion  specifically  found  that  the
Village  zoning  policy  was  not  administered  in  a  discriminatory

manner  and  supported  the  District  Court’s  finding  that  the
Village  was  concerned  with  the  integrity  of  the  zoning  plan.
The  trial  court  stated  the  basic  issue  was  whether  low-income
workers  have  a constitutional  right  to  low-rental  housing,  either
where  they  work  or  elsewhere,  and  concluded  that  there  is  no
such  rule  of  law.  The  trial  court’s  conclusion  in  this  respect  is
of  course  well  supported  by  the  decisions  of  this  Court  and
other  Federal  Courts  throughout  the  country.  Village  of  Belle
Terre  V.  Boraas,  416  U.  S.  1, 39  L.  Ed.  2d  797;  San  Antonio
Independent  School  District  v.  Rodriguez,  411  U.S.  1, 36 L. Ed.
2d  16;  Lindsey  v.  Normet,  405  U.  S.  56,  31  L.  Ed.  2d  36;
James  v.  Valtierra,  402  U.  S.  137,  28  L.  Ed.  2d  678;  Palmer
Vv.  Thompson,  403  U.  S.  217,  29  L.  Ed.  2d  438;  Acevedo  v.
Nassau  County,  500  F.  2d  1078;  Mahaley  v.  Cuyahoga  Metro-
politan  Housing,  500  F.  2d  1087;  Citizens  Committee  for Fara-
day  Wood  Vv.  Lindsay,  362  F.  Supp.  651;  Lawrence  v.  Oakes,
361  F. Supp.  432.

The  Court  of  Appeals  in  reversing  the  trial  court  does  not
deal  with  that  proposition,  nor  does  it cite  any  authority  which
would  give  special  privileges  with  respect  to  housing  and  zoning
to  low-income  persons,  regardless  of  race.  It  is  implicit  in  its
opinion  however  that  low-income  persons  apparently  do  have
such  special  privileges  if there  is a  possibility  that  there  will  be  a
higher  percentage  of racial  minorities  in their  number.

The  trial  court  found,  and  the  Court  of  Appeals  recognized,
that  the  Village’s  Comprehensive  Plan  had  been  generally
systematically  followed.  The  trial  court  found  that  the  evidence
indicated  a  serious  damaging  of the  value  of surrounding  single-
family  homes,  and  the  trial  court  concluded  that  the  weight  of
the  evidence  proves  that  the  defendants  were  motivated  by  a
legitimate  desire  to  protect  property  values  and  the  integrity  of
the  Village’s  zoning  plan.  The  trial  court  concluded  that  this
was  not  an  arbitrary  or  capricious  act  in  derogation  of  the
plaintiffs’  Fourteenth  Amendment  rights.  These  findings  were
supported  by  the  evidence  and  should  not  be  disturbed.

Ee  ee  a

TE

12

Northcross  v.  Board  of Education  of Memphis,  397  U. S. 232,  25
L. Ed.  2d 246;  United  States  v.  79.95  Acres  of Land,  459  F.  2d
185,  187; Davis  v.  Cities  Service  Oil Co.,  420  F.  2d  1278,  1279.
The  Court  of  Appeals  nevertheless  substituted  its  judgment
for  that  of the  trial  court  with  respect  to  the  facts.  As  this  Court
pointed  out  in the Northcross  case,  where,  as  here,  the  findings  of
the  District  Court  were  supported  by substantial  evidence,  the
Court  of Appeals  erred  in substituting  its own  findings.

In  reversing  the  findings  of  the  trial  court,  the  Court  of
Appeals  offered  no  legal  support  for  the  proposition  that  a
zoning  ordinance,  which  is otherwise  legitimate  and  well  within
the  legislative  discretion  of the  municipal  body,  is invalid  because
the  result  might  be  the  exclusion  of  low-income  persons,  who
in turn  might  be  Black.  The  logic  of this  position  would  dictate
that  there  can  no  longer  be  any  single-family  zoning  district  in
any  municipality  if  a developer  seeks  to  build  housing  which  he
can  show  might  be  occupied  by  a  greater  percentage  of  a
minority  group  than  presently  existed  in  that  municipality.
There  is  no  basis  in  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  or  in  any
decision  of  this  Court  or  any  other  court  cited  to  support  such
a  conclusion.

The  Court  of  Appeals  has  failed  to  recognize  the  basic
decisions  of  this  Court  in  dealing  with  the  questions  at  issue
here.  It has  chosen  to  impose  the  “compelling  interest”  test  to  a
legislative  decision,  which  is  clearly  well  within  the  range  of
legislative  discretion.  In  its  opinion  the  Court  of Appeals  totally
ignores  the  basic  holdings  of  this  Court  in  a  series  of  cases  in
which  it has  refused  to  classify  housing  as  a  fundamental  right
and  has  refused  to  apply  the  “compelling  interest”  test.

Most  recently  in the case  of  Village  of Belle  Terre  v.  Boraas,
416  U.  S.  1, 39  L. Ed.  2d  797,  the  Court  pointed  out  in  a  zon-
ing  case  that  limiting  occupancy  under  a  zoning  ordinance
involved  no  fundamental  right  guaranteed  by  the  Constitution.
The  Court  went  on  to  say:

13

“We  deal  with  economic  and  social  legislation  where  legis-
latures  have  historically  drawn  lines  which  we  respect
against  the  charge  of  violation  of  the  Equal  Protection
Clause  if  the  law  be  ‘reasonable,  not  arbitrary’  (quoting
Royster  Guano  Co.  v.  Virginia,  253  U.S.  412,  415,  64
L.Ed.  989,  40  S.Ct.  560)  and  bears  ‘a rational  relationship
: a ae  State  objective.’  Reed  v.  Reed,  404  U.S.

Here,  the  protection  of surrounding  properties  from  depreciation
and  the  preservation  of the  zoning  ordinance  are  clearly  permis-
sible  objectives.

In  Lindsey  v.  Normet,  405  U.  S.  56,  31  L.  Ed.  2d  36,  the
Court  held  that  social  importance  was  not  the  critical  deter-
minate  for  subjecting  legislation  to  strict  scrutiny.  The  Court
recognized  the  importance  of decent,  safe  and  sanitary  housing,
but  it  went  on  to  point  out  that  there  was  no  constitutional
guarantee  of  access  to  dwellings  of  a  particular  quality  or  any
recognition  of  the  right  of  a  tenant  to  occupy  any  particular
dwellings.  The  Court  concluded  that,.absent  constitutional  man-
date, the  assurance  of  adequate  housing  is a legislative  not  a
judicial  function.  405  U.  S.  56,  74.

This  Court  in James  v.  Valtierra,  402  U.  S.  137,  28  L. Ed.  2d
678,  held  that  a  legal  requirement  may  have  a  greater  impact
upon the  poor  or  other  miiority,  but  that  does  not  render  the
requirement  invalid  under  the  Fourteenth  Amendment.

To  the  same  effect  was  this  Court’s  decision  in  San  Antonio
Independence  School  District  v.  Rodriguez,  411  U.  S.  1,  36
L.  Ed.  2d  16,  where  the  Court  specifically  held  that  there  was
no  fundamental  right to housing  guaranteed  by the  Constitution.
It cited  Lindsey  in pointing  out  that  there  was  no  constitutional
mandate  to  assure  adequate  housing.  The  Court  at  411  U.  S.  1
43,  said:

“As  in the  case  of housing,  the  central  importance  of wel-
fare  benefits  to  the  poor  was  not  an  adequate  foundation
for  requiring  the  State  to  justify  its  law  by showing  some

14

compelling  state  interest.  See  also,  Jefferson  v.  Hackney,
406  U.S.  535,  32  L.Ed.2d  285,  92  S.Ct.  1724  (1972);
Richardsen  v.  Belcher,  404  U.S.  78,  30  L.Ed.2d  231,  92
S.Ct.  254  (1971).”

In the  Rodriguez  case,  the  Court  moreover  rejected  the  concept
that  housing  involved  a  suspect  class.

These  decisions  are  well  summarized  in the  case  of Lawrence
Vv.  Oakes,  361  F. Supp.  432, decided  by a three  judge panel  in the
District  Court  of  Vermont.  The  Court  there  pointed  out  that,
under  the  decisions  of  the  United  States  Supreme  Court,  the
compelling  interest  test  is not  to  be  applied  in  cases  similar  to
that  at  bar.  At  page  440,  the  Court  said:

“At  the outset,  we  reject  plaintiii’s  argument  that  we  should
adopt  the  compelling  interest  test,  a  test  that  has  been
invoked  by the  Supreme  Court  only  in  cases  in  which  the
challenged  provision  impinged  on  a  ‘fundamental  right,’  or
in which  the  class  involved  was  a  suspect  class.”

It is  clear,  therefore,  that  under  the  decisions  of  this  Court,
the  right  to  housing  although  socially  of great  significance,  does
not  involve  a fundamental  constitutional  right,  and  the  Court  of
Appeals’  application  of the  “compelling  interest”  test  is inappro-
priate  and  erroneous.

That  Court’s  further  reliance  upon  its  own  decision  in  the
case  of Clark  v.  Universal  Builders,  Inc.,  401  F.  2d  324,  is also
misplaced.  The  Clark  case  was  decided  on  the  question  of  the
propriety  of  granting  a  directed  verdict  for  the  defendants  at
close  of plaintiffs  case.  Moreover,  that  case  involved  not  munici-
pal action  pursuant  to  an  undoubted  grant  of police  power,  but
involved  actions  by private  parties  taking  advantage  of  a  dual
market  in real  estate  with  respect  to price.  The  Court  held  that,
when  allegations  were  made  which  set  up  a  prima  facie  case  of
two  prices  dependent  upon  race  for  housing  in the  same  market,
there  was  a  violation  of  the  Civil  Rights  Act.  No  such  factual
situation  exists  here.  There  is no  evidence  to  indicate  that  blacks

15

do  not  have  equal  access  to  housing  in Arlington  Heights  at  the
same  price  as  do  whites.

The  fact  that  more  blacks  than  whites  might  be  affected  by
refusing  to  rezone  does  not  render  the  zoning  violative  of  the
Fourteenth  Amendment.  In  Palmer  v.  Thompson,  403  U.  S.
217,  29  L.  Ed.  2d  438,  this  Court  held  that  the  closing  of  all
public  swimming  pools  by a municipal  authority  was  not  uncon-
stitutional,  notwithstanding  the  fact  that  Negroes  would  be
affected  to  a  greater  extent  than  whites.  The  Court  concluded
with  respect  to  the  record  that  “it  shows  no  state  action  affect-
ing blacks  differently  from  whites.”  Here,  of course,  the  fact  that
the  plaintiffs  argued  that  the  Village’s  action  would  have a dis-
proportionate  effect  on  blacks  does  not  render  a  legitimate  zon-
ing decision  a  violation  of  the  Fourteenth  Amendment.

In  the  recent  case  of  Milliken  v.  Bradley,  418  U.  S.  717
(1974),  41  L.  Ed.  2d  1069,  this  Court  in  a school  segregation
case  held  that  racial  imbalance  in itself  is not  to  be equated  with
a constitutional  violation  calling  for  a  remedy.  It indicated  that
the  fact  that  Detroit  schools  were  segregated  did  not  require  an
inter-district  plan  outside  the  corporate  limits,  absent  any  show-
ing that  the  suburban  school  district  had  in  fact  discriminated.
Thus,  the  fact  that  Arlington  Heights  is located  in  the  Chicago
metropolitan  area,  which  the Court  of Appeals  described  as  seg-
tegated,  does  not  impose  an  affirmative  duty  upon  the  Village  of
Arlington  Heights  to  change  its  otherwise  valid  Zoning  Ordi-
nance,  absent  a showing  of discrimination  by Arlington  Heights
in the  administration  of  an  ordinance  clearly  valid  on  its  face.
Milliken  v.  Bradley,  418  U.  S.  717,  41  L.  Ed.  2d  1069,  1091.
The  fact  that  enforcement  of  r  unicipal  ordinances  might
have  a greater  impact  on  the  poor  of course  does  not  render  the
ordinance  invalid  under  the  Equal  Protection  Clause.  James  v.
Valtierra,  402  U.  S.  137,  28  L.  Ed.  2d  678;  English  v.  Town
of Huntington,  448  F.  2d  319,  324;  Ybarra  v.  City  of Town  of
Los  Altos,  503  F. 2d 250  (1974).

16

The  decision  of the Court  of Appeals  here  is inconsistent  with
the  holdings  of  several  other  circuits.  In  Ybarra  the  Court  of
Appeals  for  the  Ninth  Circuit  refused  to  apply  the  “compelling
interest”  test  in  a  case  challenging  a  large  lot  zoning  ordinance
on  the  grounds  it excluded  Mexican  Americans.  The  trial  court
had  found  that  the  ordinance  prevented  poor  people  from  living
in Los  Altos  and  that  if Mexican  Americans  did  not  live  there,
it was  because  of  poverty.  The  Court  at  page  253  said:  “We
agree  that  discrimination  against  the  poor  does  not  become  dis-
crimination  against  an  ethnic  minority  merely  because  there  is a
statistical  correlation  between  poverty  and  ethnic  background.”
The  Court  based  its  decision  on  this  Court’s  opinion  in
Rodriguez.

In  the  very  recent  case  of  Construction  Industry  Association
of Sonoma  County  v.  City  of Petaluma,  No.  74-2100,  decided
August  13,  1975  by the  Court  of Appeals  for the  Ninth  Circuit,
that  Court  reversed  the  trial  court  and  rejected  the  concept  that
an  allegedly  exclusionary  zoning  ordinance  was  a violation  of
the  Fourteenth  Amendment.  At page  11  of the  slip opinion,  the
Court  said:

In  attacking  the  validity  of  the  Plan,  appellees  rely
heavily  on  the  district  court’s  finding  that  the  express  pur-
pose  and-the  actual  effect  of the  Plan  is to  exclude  substan-
tial  numbers  of people  who  would  otherwise  elect  to  move
to  the  City.  375  F.  Supp.  at  581.  The  existence  of  an
exclusionary  purpose  and  effect  reflects,  however,  only  one
side  of the  zoning  regulation.  Practically  all  zoning  restric-
tions  have  as  a  purpose  and  effect  the  exclusion  of  some
activity  or  type  of  structure  or  a  certain  density  of  inhabi-
tants.  And  in reviewing  the  reasonableness  of a zoning  ordi-
nance,  our  inquiry  does  not  terminate  with  a  finding  that
it is for  an  exclusionary  purpose.’?  We  must  determine  fur-
ther  whether  the  exclusion  bears  any  rational  relationship
to  a  legitimate  state  interest.  If it does  not,  then  the  zoning
regulation  is  invalid.  If,  on  the  other  hand,  a  legitimate
state  interest  is furthered  by the  zoning  regulation,  we  must
defer  to  the  legislative  act.  Being  neither  a  super  legisla-

17

ture  nor  a zoning  board  of  appeal,  a  federal  court  is with-
out  authority  to  weigh  and  reappraise  the  factors  con-
sidered  or  ignored  by  the  legislative  body  in  passing  the
challenged  zoning  regulation.

In  the  case  of  Acevedo  v.  Nassau  County,  500  F.  2d  1078
(1974),  the  Second  Circuit  was  considering  the  liability  of
Nassau  County,  New  York,  for  abandoning  plans  for  construct-
ing low-income  family  housing.  At page  1080,  the  Court  noted:

“Appellees  have  no  constitutional  or  statutory  duty  to
provide  low  income  housing.  There  is  no  ‘constitutional
guarantee  of  access  to  dwellings  of  a  particular  quality.’
Lindsay  v.  Normet,  405  U.S.  56,  74  (1972).”

The  Court  went  on  to  say  at  page  1082:

“The  Fair  Housing  Act  does  not  impose  any  duty  upon  a
governmental  body  to  construct  or  to  ‘plan  for,  approve
and  promote’  any  housing.”

In  the  case  of  Mahaley  v.  Cuyahoga  Metropolitan  Housing
Authority,  500  F.  2d  1087  (1974),  the  Sixth  Circuit  Court  of
Appeals  held  that  the  failure  of Cleveland  suburbs  to  enter  into
a  cooperation  agreement  for  the  construction  of  federally
assisted  housing  did  not  violate  federal  civil  rights  laws  or  the
United  States  Constitution.  The  Court  relied  heavily  on  Palmer
v.  Thompson,  403  U.  S.  217.  At  page  1093,  it said:

In the  present  case  the  failure  of the  suburbs  to  provide
low-rent  heusing  affected  alike  both  low-income  blacks  and
whites.  There  may  have  been  a greater  impact  on  the blacks,
but  that,  under  Palmer,  is  not  sufficient  to  establish  a
constitutional  violation.

In  Lindsey  v.  Nomet,  405  U.S.  56  (1972),  the  Court
held  that  no  one  has  a constitutional  right  to  adequate
housing.  Since  a person  has  no  right to public  housing  in his
own  city,  it follows  that  he  has  no  such  right  in  a  muni-
cipality  in which  he  does  not  reside.

See  also  Ranjel  v.  City  of Lansing,  417  F.2d  321  (6th
Cir.  1969),  cert.  denied,  397  U.S.  980,  rehearing  denied,
397  U.S.  1059  (1970),  on  neutral  action  of  referendum.

18

19

The  Court  also  cited  Citizens  Comm.  for  Faraday  Wood  Vv.
Lindsay,  362  F.  Supp.  651  (S.  D.  N.  Y.  1973)  and  its reliance
on  Palmer  and  Valtierra.

In the  case  of  Warth  v.  Seldin,  ............  i  eee  45  L.  Ed.  2d
343,  decided  June  25,  1975,  this  Court  denied  standing  of
certain  petitioners  to  attack  a  zoning  ordinance  which  they
contended  was  exclusionary.  The  Court  however  recognized  that
it could  be  economics  rather  than  discriminatory  zoning  which
to  housing  in  the  municipality  in  question.
denied  access
The  Court  noted  at  page  359:

“Indeed,  petitioners’  descriptions  of  their  individual  finan-
cial  situations  and  housing  needs  suggest  precisely  the
contrary—that  their  inability  to  reside  in  Penfield  is  the
consequences  of the  economics  of the  area  housing  market,
rather  than  of respondents’  assertedly  illegal  acts.

It  must  be  concluded  that,  as  this  Court  has  repeatedly
pointed  out,  access  to  housing  does  not  involve  a  fundamental
constitutional  right  which  requires  the  application  of  the  “com-
pelling  interest”  test  in  determining  the  validity  of  a  zoning
ordinance  which  would  allegedly  limit  access  to  housing  by
minority  groups  because  statistically  they  have  a  greater  per-
centage  than  the  majority  group  in  the  low-income  class.  The
Court  of  Appeals’  conclusion  in  this  regard  is  manifestly
erroneous  in  light  of  the  repeated  holdings  of  this  Court
in Belle  Terre,  Rodriguez,  Lindsey,  Palmer,  James,  and  Warth,
and  Court  of Appeals’  decisions  in English,  Ybarra,  and  City  of
Petaluma.  The  Court’s  opinion  thus  is  a  patent  departure  from
the  decisions  of this  Court  of other  Courts  of Appeal  throughout
the  country  and  should  not  be allowed  to stand.

I.
THE  PLAINTIFFS  FAILED  TO  OVERCOME  THE  PRESUMP-
TION  OF VALIDITY  ATTACHING  TO THE  ZONING  ORDI-
NANCE.  THE  LEGISLATIVE  JUDGMENT  WAS  CLEARLY
REASONABLE  AND  THE  COURT  OF  APPEALS  ERRED
IN  SUBSTITUTING  ITS  JUDGMENT  FOR  THAT  OF  THE
MUNICIPAL  LEGISLATIVE  BODY.
The  Court  of  Appeals  conceded  in  its  opinion  that  the  trial
court  was  not  erroneous  in concluding  that  the  defendants  were
concerned  with  the  integrity  of the  Village’s  zoning  plan.  It also
concedes  that  decisions  that  affect  those  in  the  lower  income
bracket  more  than  others  are  not  thereby  rendered  racially
discriminatory.  The  Court  recognizes  that  this  Court  is James
v.  Valtierra,  402  U.  S.  137,  has  held  that  racial  disparity
alone  does  not  amount  to  racial  discrimination.  The  Court
however,  as  we  have  indicated  above,  chooses  wrongfully  to
apply the  “compelling  interest”  test  to declare  an  otherwise  valid
zoning restriction  to be repugnant  to the Fourteenth  Amendment.
Nowhere  in  its  opinion  does  the  Court  of  Appeals  suggest
that  the  Village  exercised  its  zoning  powers  with  respect  to  the
land  in  question  in  a  discriminatory  manner.  Its  opinion  totally
ignores  the  basic  rules  that  this  Court  has  laid  down  in  the
consideration  of  zoning  questions.  The  Court  nowhere  cites
or  attempts  to  distinguish  the  controlling  cases  of  Village  of
Belle  Terre  v.  Boraas,  416  U.  S.  1,  39  L.  Ed.  2d  797,  and
Euclid  v. Ambler  Realty  Co.,  272 U. S. 365,  71 L. Ed.  303,  cited
below  by the  defendants.  These  two  cases  are  clearly  dispositive
of  the  zoning  questions  presented.

This  case  should  be nothing  more  than  a garden-variety  zoning
controversy.  Although  the  plaintiffs  have  introduced  the  issue
of  racial  discrimination  and  the  Court  of  Appeals  decided  the
issue  on  Fourteenth  Amendment  grounds,  it is obvious  that  the
only real  question  is the reasonableness  of the  zoning  restriction,
which  both  on  its  face  and  in  fact  is not  aimed  at  any  racial

20

21

minority.  The-record  is absolutely  clear  that,  based  upon  sound
zoning  principles,  the  retention  of  a  single-family  classification
on  the  subject  property,  which  is  surrounded  by  single-family
homes  and a school  development,  is a legitimate  and  valid  exer-
cise  of  the  legislative  discretion  vested  in  the  local  authorities.
The  law  is  well  established  that  a  zoning  ordinance  is
presumptively  valid,  and  the  party  assailing  its  validity  must
show  by  clear  and  convincing  evidence  that  it  is  invalid.
This  fundamental  rule  was  adopted  by  this  Court  in  the  land-
mark  decision  of Euclid  v.  Ambler  Realty  Co.,  272  U.  S.  365,
71  L.  Ed.  303.  In  that  case  this  Court  specifically  held  that,
if the  validity  of the  legislative  classification  for  zoning  purposes
is fairly  debatable,  the  legislative  judgment  must  be  controlling.
At  page  388,  the  Court  said:

“If  the  validity  of  the  legislative  classification  for  zoning
purposes  be  fairly  debatable,  the  legislative  judment  must
be  allowed  to  control.  Radice  v.  New  York,  264  U.S.  292,
294,  68  L.ed.  690,  694,  44  Sup.Ct.Rep.  325.”

In the  more  recent  decision  of Village  of Belle  Terre  v.  Boraas,
416  U.  S.  1,  39  L.  Ed.  2d  797,  this  Court  summarized  its
broad  holding  in  the  Euclid  case.  At  page  5  the  Court  said:
The  main  thrust  of the  case  in the  mind  of the  Court  was
in the  exclusion  of industries  and  apartments  and  as  respects
that  it  commented  on  the  desire  to  keep  residential  areas
free  of  “disturbing  noises”;  “increased  traffic”;  and hazard
of  “moving  and  parked  automobiles”;  the  “depriving  chil-
dren  of  the  privilege  of  quiet  and  open  spaces  for  play,
enjoyed  by those  in more  favored  localities.”  Id.,  at  394, 71
L  Ed  303,  54  ALR  1016.  The  ordinance  was  sanctioned
because  the  validity  of  the  legislative  classification  was
“fairly  debatable”  and  therefore  could  not  be  said  to  be
wholly  arbitrary.  Id.,  at  388,  71  L Ed  303,  54  ALR  1016.

The  Court  in  Belle  Terre  at  416  U.  S.  1,  at  page  5, referred
to  Berman  Vv.  Parker,  348  U.  S.  26,  99 L.  Ld.  27,  in  noting  the
broad  scope  of the  police  power,  indicating:  “We  refused  to  limit
the  concept  of  public  welfare  that  may  be  enhanced  by zoning
regulations.”

In  Belle  Terre  the  Court  was  concerned  with  an  attack  in-
volving  equal  protection  rights  under  the  Fourteenth  Amend-
ment.  As here,  the District  Court  had  upheld  the  constitutionality
of  the  ordinance,  but  the  Court  of  Appeals  had  reversed.  This
Court  specifically  rejected  the  claim  that  fundamental  consti-
tutional  rights  were  involved  in  a  zoning  ordinance  which
restricted  occupancy  of  single-family  dwellings.  As  here,  it was
charged  that  the  ordinance  bars  people  who  were  uncongenia!
to  the  present  residents  and  that  it expressed  the  social  prefer-
ences  of  the  residents  for  more  congenial  groups.  It  was  urged
that  the  ordinance  was  contrary  to  an  open,  egalitarian  and
integrated  society.  416  U.  S.  1, 7. This  Court  at  pages  7 and  8
of  its  opinion  used  language  which  is  particularly  appropriate
and  diespositive  to  the  case  at  bar.  It  said:

It  involves  no  procedural  disparity  inflicted  on  some  but
not  on  others  such  as  was  presented  by Griffin  v  Illinois,
351  US  12,  100  L Ed  891,  76 S Ct  585,  55  ALR2d  1055.
It  involves  no  “fundamental”  right  guaranteed  by  the
Constitution,  such  as  voting,  Harper  v  Virginia  Board,  383
US  663,  16  L  Ed  2d  169,  86  S  Ct  1079;  the  right  of
association,  NAACP  v  Alabama,  357  US  449,  2 L Ed  2d
1488,  78  S  Ct  1163;  the  right  of  access  to  the  courts,
NAACP v Button,  371  US  415,  9 L Ed  2d  405,  83  S Ct
328;  or  any  rights  of  privacy,  cf.  Griswold  v  Connecticut,
381  US  479,  14  L Ed  2d  510,  85  S Ct  1678;  Eisenstadt
Vv  Baird,  405  US  438,  453-454,  31  L Ed  2d  349,  92  SCt
1029.  We  deal  with  economic  and  social  legislation  where
legislatures  have  historically  drawn  lines  which  we  respect
against  the  charge  of  violation  of  the  Equal  Protection
Clause  if the  law  be  “  ‘reasonable,  not  arbitrary’  ”  (quoting
Royster  Guano  Co.  v Virginia,  253  US  412,  415,  64  L Ed
989,  40  S Ct  560)  and  bears  “a  rational  relationship  to  a
[permissible]  state  objective.”  Reed  v  Reed,  404  US  71,
76, 30 L Ed 2d 225,  92 S Ct 251.  (Emphasis  added. )
Echoing  Euclid’s  determination  that  single-family  areas  may

be  constitutionally  protected,  the  Court  at  page  9 noted:

A  quiet  place  where  yards  are  wide,  people  few,  and
motor  vehicles  restricted  are  legitimate  guidelines  in  a

22

23

land-use  project  addressed  to  family  needs.  This  goal  is
a  permissible  one  within  Berman  v  Parker,  supra.  The
police  power  is not  confined  to  elimination  of filth,  stench,
and  unhealthy  places.  It  is  ample  to  lay  out  zones  where
family  values,  youth  values,  and  the  blessings  of  quiet
seclusion  and  clean  air  make  the  area  a  sanctuary  for
people.

The  Illinois  Supreme  Court  has  of course  universally  adhered
to  the  proposition  that  the  zoning  ordinance  is  presumptively
valid  and  the  party  assailing  its  validity  must  show  by clear  and
convincing  evidence  that  it is  invalid.  If  there  is  any  room  for
a  reasonable  difference  of  opinion,  the  court  will  not  substitute
its  judgment  for  that  of  the  legislative  body.  Exchange  National
Bank  v.  County  of  Cook,  25  Ill.  2d  434,  439;  Trendel  v.
County  of Cook,  27  Ill.  2d  155,  161;  Bennett  v.  Chicago,  24
Ill.  2d  270,  273;  Sutter  v.  Village  of  Mundelein,  27  Ill.  2d
589,  593.

The  Supreme  Court  of  Illinois  in  the  recent  case  of  LaSalle
National  Bank  v.  City  of  Evanston,  57  Ill.  2d  415,  sustained
the  City  of Evanston’s  Zoning  Ordinance  which,  like  the  present
ordinance,  restricted  residential  density  as  applied  to  the  subject
property.  The  Court  at  page  428  of its  opinion  said:

“The  law  governing  the  disposition  of  contentions  chal-
lenging  zoning  classifications  has  been  set  forth  in numerous
decisions.  As  stated  in Exchange  National  Bank  of Chicago
v.  County  of Cook,  25  Ill.2d  434,  439-40,  ‘It is always  pre-
sumed,  in an  attack  upon  an  ordinance,  that  the  enactment
is valid,  and  the  burden  of proving  its  invalidity  falls  upon
the  one  who  attacks  the  ordinance.  (Jacobson  v.  City  of
Evanston,  10  Ill.2d  61.)  Before  a  court  will  intervene  it
must  be  established  by clear  and  convincing  evidence  that
the  ordinance,  as  applied  to  plaintiffs,  is  arbitrary  and  un-
reasonable  and  has  no  substantial  relation  to  the  public
health,  safety,  or  welfare.  These  rules  are  based  upon  a
recognition  that  zoning  is  primarily  a  legislative  function,
subject  to  court  review  only for  the  purpose  of determining
whether  the  power,  as  exercised,  involves  an  undue  invasion
of  private  constitutional  rights  without  a  reasonable  justi-

fication  in relation  to public  welfare.  (People  ex  rel.  Joseph
Lumber  Co.  v.  City  of  Chicago,  402  Ill.  321;  Morgan  v.
City  of Chicago,  370  Ill.  347.)  Where  it appears,  from  all
the  facts,  that  room  exists  for  a  difference  of  opinion  con-
cerning  the  reasonableness  of a classification,  the  legislative
judgment  must  be  conclusive.  (Krom  v.  City  of Elmhurst,
8 Ill.2d  104.)”

The  Illinois  Supreme  Court  incidentally  in  the  LaSalle  National
Bank  case  cited  Belle  Terre  as  authority.

No  serious  attempt  was  made  by the  plaintiffs  to  attack  the
reasonableness  of  the  Zoning  Ordinance  on  traditional  zoning
grounds,  The  Court  of Appeals  clearly  did  not  attempt  to  make
its  determination  in  terms  of  normal  zoning  criteria.  It  in  fact
conceded  that  the  ordinance  would  be  valid  if the  “compelling
interest”  test  was  not  to  be  applied.  We  have  demonstrated
above  that  the  “compelling  interest”  test  is  not  applicable
because  access  to  housing,  even  for  low-income  people,  is not  «
fundamental  constitutional  right.  It  is  clear  that  the  plaintiffs
failed  to  overcome  the  presumption  of validity  attaching  to  the
Zoning  Ordinance  of  the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights.  The
legislative  judgment  of  the  Village  officials  must  clearly  prevail
under  the  holdings  of  this  Court  in  Euclid  and  in  Belle  Terre
and  the  holdings  of the  Illinois  Supreme  Court.

We  respectfully  suggest  that  the  Zoning  Ordinance  in  ques-
tion  was  a  reasonable  exercise  of  the  unquestioned  legislative
authority  vested  in the Village  of Arlington  Heights.  The  specious
claim  that  the  ordinance  was  racially  discriminatory,  was  clearly
not  supported  by the  evidence  and  contrary  to  the  factual  find-
ings  of  the  trial  court  who  heard  all  the  witnesses  and  carefully
examined  all  the  evidence.  We  suggest  that  the  factual  situation
is not  really  in doubt.  Absent  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  claim,
there  can  be  no  question  but  that  the  zoning  restriction  here
challenged  would  stand.

We  suggest  that  there  is no  basis  in  the  Fourteenth  Amend-
ment  or  the  decisions  of  this  Court  which  would  require  the

a

24

valid  legislative  decisions  of  the  local  municipal  body  to  be  set
aside  merely  to  permit  occupancy  by a  low-income  group  that
might  in  fact  have  a  higher  percentage  of  minority  members
than  the  percentage  already  residing  in the  community.  To  rule
otherwise  would  destroy  the  basis  of zoning  law  in this  country
and  elevate  racial  considerations  beyond  the  legitimate  needs  of
the  police  power.

Hil.

THE  PLAINTIFFS  LACK  STANDING  TO  BRING
THIS  ACTION.
The  Village  filed  a  motion  to  strike  the  complaint  and  dis-
miss  the  cause,  alleging  in part  that  the  plaintiffs  had  no  standing
to  bring  this  action  in  the  first  instance.  A  predecessor  judge
denied  the  motion,  and  the  trial  court  heard  the  matter  on  its
merits.  (B3)  The  trial  court  found  however  that  this  was
not  properly  a  class  action.

Two  Black  plaintiffs  who  were  non-residents  of  Arlington
Heights  testified.  One  resided  in  another  Chicago  suburb  and
the  testimony  failed  to  establish  that  he  and  his  family  were
eligible  by  reason  of  income  to  reside  in  the  Lincoln-Green
project.  The  other,  as  the  trial  court  had  noted,  chose  to  move
to  the  south  side  of  Chicago  in  order  to  buy  a  two-flat  apart-
ment  building.  The  trial  court  concluded  that  the  individual
plaintiffs  did  not  represent  a  definable  and  manageable  class.
One  intervenor,  Eluteria  Maldonado,  was  a  Mexican  American
who  resided  in Arlington  Heights  and  therefore  clearly  could  not
have  been  denied  the  right  to  live  in  the  Village  because  of  the
Village’s  zoning  pattern.  The  trial  court  noted,  with  respect  to
the  Metropolitan  Housing  and  Development  Corporation,  “the
plaintiff  corporation  acquires  no  different  status  than  any  other
corporation  merely  because  it has  been  created  for  the  purpose
of developing  housing  for  low-income  tenants.”

The  Court  of  Appeals  did  not  deal  in  any  manner  with  the
question  of  standing.  It is clear  however  that  this  Court’s  recent

25

opinion  in  the  case  of  Warth  v.  Seldin,  ...........  a
eee ,  45
L.  Ed.  2d  343,  is  dispositive  of  the  question  and  clearly  indi-
cates  that  none  of the  plaintiffs  here  had  standing  to  contest  the
Village’s  action.  The  Court  of Appeals’  decision  was  based  solely
on  the  Fourteenth  Amendment.  It made  no  decision  with  respect
to  the  alleged  violations  of  the  Civil  Rights  Act  or  the  Fair
Housing  Act.  The  trial  court  had  found  that  no  violations  of
either  statute  or  of the  Fourteenth  Amendment  had  taken  place.

It should  be  noted  that  the  not-for-profit  intervenor  corpora-
tion  (Northwest  Opportunity  Center)  has  no  standing  under  the
doctrine  of  Warth  v.  Seldin.  The  non-resident  Black  plaintiffs
were  not  members  of a class  and  clearly  have  no  standing  based
upon  their  own  factual  situation.  Maldonado  was  clearly  not  the
victim  of denial  of equal  protection  because  she  was  residing  in
the  Village,  and  thus  had  not  been  “zoned  out”;  Metropolitan
Housing,  the other  corporate  plaintiff,  had  an  interest  which  was
contingent  upon  rezoning  and  upon  the  ability  to  secure  236
financing.  The  trial  court  specifically  found  that  this  latter  cor-
poration  lacked  the  present  capacity  to  carry  out  its  proposed
project.  (B6,  B7)  This  finding  of  fact  was  in  no  way  disputed
by the Court  of Appeals  which  chose  not  to  deal  with  this  aspect
of  the  situation.  The  actual  owner  of  the  property  who  might
have  had  standing  was  not a party  to  the  case.

The  law  is  clear  in  Illinois  that  a  contract  purchaser  whose
contract  is conditioned  on  rezoning,  as  is here  the  case,  has  no
standing  to  challenge  the  validity  of  a  zoning  ordinance.  Clark
Oil  &  Refining  Corp.  v.  Evanston,  23  Ill.  2d  48.  This  rule  of
law  is even  more  compelling  when,  as  here,  the  plaintiffs’  rights
were  contingent  not  only  on  zoning  but  on  securing  financing
under  a  program  which  no  longer  exists.  Thus,  the  whole  pro-
posal  may  well  be  moot.

With  respect  to  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  question,  it
should  be noted  that  the  Court  of Appeals  specifically  refused  to
set  aside  the  trial  court’s  finding  that  the  Village  had  not  dis-

26

criminated  in the  application  of its  zoning  ordinance  in denying
the  rezoning  requested  for  the  subject  property.  The  Court
specifically  noted  that  the  evidence  does  not  require  the  conclu-
sion  that,  had  the  proposed  development  been  purely  commer-
cial,  the  requested  change  would  have  been  granted.  (A4)  Thus,
the  equal  protection  argument  of the Fourteenth  Amendment  was
specifically  rejected  not  only by the  trial  court,  but  the  Court  of
Appeals  as  well.

We  suggest  moreover  that,  as  noted  by  the  trial  court,  the
corporate  plaintiff  acquires  no  different  status  than  any  other
corporation  merely  because  it has  been  created  for  the  purpose
of developing  housing  for  low-income  tenants.

The  application  of the  Fourteenth  Amendment  to  a  corpora-
tion  in  order  to  prevent  racial  discrimination  is  improper
under  the  present  factual  situation.  The  corporation  has  no
racial  identity.  It  is  conceded  that  it  was  treated  exactly  the
same  as  any  other  property  owner  in  similar  circumstances
would  have  been  treated.  This  Court  has  frequently  held  that
the  application  of  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  to  corporations
under  such  circumstances  is  highly  suspect.  See,  for  example,
the  dissenting  opinion  of Mr.  Justice  Douglas  in  Wheeling  Steel
Corp.  v.  Glander,  337  U.  S.  562,  576;  Western  Turf  Assn.  v.
Hyman  Greenberg,  204  U.  S.  359,  51  L.  Ed.  520;  and  Selover
Bates  &  Co.  v.  Walsh,  226  U.  S.  112,  57  L.  Ed.  146.

We  believe  that  this  Court  should  reverse  the  Court  of
Appeals  on  the  merits.  We  suggest  however  that  under  the
doctnne  of  Warth  v.  Seldin,  these  plaintiffs  were  not  entitled  to
be in the  Federal  Court  in the  first  instance  and  that  our  motion
to  dismiss,  which  was  acted  upon  by  the  District  Court  on
October  3,  1972,  would  undoubtedly  have  been  granted  had
this  Court’s  decision  in  the  Warth  case  then  been  available  to
the  trial  judge.

27

CONCLUSION.

This  Court  should  grant  the  Petition  for  Writ  of  Certiorari
because  the  opinion  of  the  Court  of  Appeals  is clearly  contrary
to  the  Court’s  repeated  holdings  that  the  fact  that  legislation
Otherwise  valid  may  have  a  disproportionate  impact  on  the
poor,  including  racial  minorities,  does  not  thereby  render  such
legislation  discriminatory  governmental  action.  The  zoning  re-
striction  challenged  in  the  instant  case  would  inevitably  screen
Out  some  persons  who  could  not  afford  housing  in  Arlington
Heights,  but  that  fact  alone  does  not  render  the  zoning  restric-
tion  invalid  or  unconstitutional  under  the  Fourteenth  Amend-
ment.  It  affects  Blacks  and  Whites  as  well.  The  Court  of
Appeals  erroneously  has  applied  the  “compelling  interest”  test
in  an  area  where  this  Court  has  held  that  such  a test  is  im-
proper.  Moreover,  the  plaintiffs  lacked  standing  under  Warth
v.  Seldin.

We  believe  that  the  Court  has  already  answered  the  question
raised  here  by  its  opinions  in  Palmer,  Rodriguez  and  Belle
Terre,  but  the  Court  of  Appeals  has  ignored  those  answers  in
its  opinion  here.  If  this  Court  concurs  that  these  cases  are  in
fact  controlling,  it  should  grant  the  writ  because  the  Court  of
Appeals  for  the  Seventh  Circuit  has  in fact  rendered  a  decision
On  an  extremely  important  and  far-reaching  question  in  conflict
with  the  decisions  of this  Court  and  other  Courts  of  Appeal.  If
the  Court  believes  that  the  question  raised  here  has  not  in  fact
already  been  answered,  it  is  a  vitally  important  question  of
federal  law  that  should  be  settled  by this  Court.

The  holding  of  the  Court  of  Appeals  ignores  the  clear  and
continuing  rule  laid  down  by  this  Court  in  Euclid  and  Belle
Terre  and  universally  applied—the  basic  and  unyielding  prin-
ciple  that  under  the  Equal  Protection  Clause,  legislative  solu-

28

tions  must  be  respected  if  they  in  some  way  advance a legiti-
mate  state  interest.  That  respect  for  the  legislative  judgment  is
paramount  to  the  administration  of  justice  in  a  democratic
society.

Respectfully  submitted,

JAcK  M.  SIEGEL,

39  South  LaSalle  Street,
Chicago,  Illinois  60603,
312-263-2968,

Attorney  for  Petitioners,
Village  of  Arlington
Heights,  et  all.
