FINAL  PRETRIAL  ORDER.

L.

STIPULATION  OF  UNCONTESTED  FACTS.

1.

Plaintiff  Metropolitan  Housing  Development  Corpora-
tion  (“MHDC”),  407  South  Dearborn  Street,  Chicago,  Illinois
60605  is  a  not-for-profit  corporation  organized  under  the  laws
of  the  State  of  Illinois  in  1968  for  the  purpose  of  developing
low-and  moderate-income  housing  in  the  Chicago  metropolitan
area.

2.

Intervening  plaintiff  Northwest  Opportunity  Center,  Inc.
(“NOC”),  3411  N.  Kirchoff  Road,  Rolling  Meadows,  Illinois
60008  is  a  not-for-profit  corporation  organized  under  the  laws
of the  State  of  Illinois  in  1971.

3.  Defendant  Village  of  Arlington  Heights  is  a  municipal
corporation  organized  under  the  laws  of  the  State  of  Illinois.

4.

Defendants  Ralph  Clarbour,  Alice  Harms,  Frank  Pala-
tier,  James  Ryan,  Theodore  Salinsky,  and  Dwight  Walton  were,
at  the  time  of action  on  MHDC’s  request  for  rezoning,  members
of  the  Board  of  Trustees  of  defendant  Village  of  Arlington
Heights.

5.  Defendant  John  Woods  was  the  Mayor  of  Defendant

Village  of  Arlington  Heights.

6.  Defendant  L.  A.  Hanson  is and  was  the  Village  Manager

of  defendant  Village  of  Arlington  Heights.

7.

Defendant  Martin  Munsen  is  and  was  the  Director  of
Building  and  Zoning  of Defendant  Village  of Arlington  Heights.

8.  The  Clerics  of  St.  Viator  is  a  Catholic  religious  order
formed  in  1890  and  holds  fee  title  to  80  acres  of  property
including  15  acres  where  MHDC  proposes  to  build  a  236-
development  in  Arlington  Heights,  Illinois.  The  80-acre  parcel
is bounded  on  the  west  by Dryden  Lane,  on  the  north  by Oak-
ton  Street,  on  the  east  by  the  west  property  line  of  homes
fronting  on  Drury  Lane  and  on  the  south  by Euclid  Avenue.

9.  On  November  7,  1970,  the  Clerics  of  St.  Viator  entered
into  a  ninety-nine  year  lease  and  accompanying  agreement  to
sell  to  MHDC  the  vacant  parcel  of  fifteen  acres  (643,000
square  feet)  in the  southeast  corner  of the  80  acres  described  as
follows:

That  part  of  the  East  half  of  the  Southeast  quarter  of  the
Northeast  quarter  of  Section  29,  Township  42  North,
Range  11,  East  of  the  Third  Principal  Meridian,  bounded
and  described  as  follows:  Beginning  at  a  point  on  the  East
line  of  said  Southeast  quarter  of  the  Northeast  quarter,
40  feet  North  of  the  Southeast  corner  thereof,  thence
West  on  North  line  of  South  40.0  feet  of  said  East  half
of  Southeast  quarter  of  Northeast  quarter,  a  distance  of
607.99  feet  to  the  East  line  of  the  West  50.0  feet  of  said
East  half  of  the  Southeast  quarter  of  the  Northeast  quar-

66

67

ter;  thence  North  on  said  East  line  of West  50.0  feet  of the
East  half  of the  Southeast  quarter  of the  Northeast  quarter,
a  distance  of  424.0  feet;  thence  east  at  right  angles  to  last
described  course,  a  distance  of  140  feet;  thence  North-
easterly  on  a  line  forming  an  angle  of  59  degrees  30  min-
utes  00  seconds  to  the  left  from  the  prolongation  of  the
last  described  course,  a  distance  of  190.0  feet;  thence
Northwesterly  at  right  angles  to  the  last  described  course,
a  distance  of  274.41  feet  to  said  East  line  of  West  50.0
feet  of  East  half  of  the  Southeast  quarter  of  the  Northeast
quarter;  thence  North  on  said  East  line  of  West  50.0  feet
of East  half  of the  Southeast  quarter  of the  Northeast  quar-
ter,  a distance  of 424.44  feet  to  the  South  line  of the  North
131.03  feet  of  said  East  half  of  the  Southeast  quarter  of
the  Northeast  quarter;  thence  East  on  said  South  line  of
North  131.03  feet  of  the  East  half  of  the  Southeast  quar-
ter  of  the  Northeast  quarter,  a  distance  of  609.18  feet  to
the  East  line  of  said  Southeast  quarter  of  the  Northeast
quarter;  thence  South  on  said  East  line  of  the  Southeast
quarter  of  the  Northeast  quarter,  a  distance  of  1151.21
feet,  more  or  less,  to  the  place  of  beginning,  all  in  Cook
County,  Illinois.

10.

The  subject  15  acres  is bounded  on  the  north  by prop-
erty  owned  by the  Clerics  of St.  Viator  containing  a high  school,
recreation  fields,  and  a  nursery.  It is  bounded  on  the  west  by
property  owned  by the  Clerics  of  St.  Viator  containing  a  three-
story  novitiate  with  communal  living  facilities  and  a Montessori
school.  It  is  bounded  on  the  east  by  the  backyards  of  single-
It  is  bounded  on  the
family  residences  facing  Drury  Lane.
south  by Euclid  Avenue.

11.

The  subject  property  is zoned  R-3  in Arlington  Heights,
a  single-family  detached  home  residential  district  with  a  mini-
mum  lot  size  of  8,750  square  feet.

12.  MHDC  proposed  to  build  on  the  subject  property  a
planned  unit  development  in  the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights
with  190  single  family  attached  homes  (the  “proposed  develop-
ment”)  under  the  R-5  multifamily  zoning  classification  of  the

Arlington  Heights  Zoning  Ordinance  or  12.7  units  to  the  acre
as  follows:

a.  The  development  would  be built  for low-  and  moderate-
income  families  and  individuals  with  subsidies  as  pro-
vided  under  Section  236  of  the  National  Housing  Act
of  1968.

.  The  housing  would  be  clusters  of  two  stories  or  less,

with  separate  entrances  and  private  patios.

.  The  unit  breakdown  would  be:

i.  100 one-bedroom  units,  mostly  for elderly;

li.  48 two-bedroom  units;

iii.  30 three-bedroom  units;

iv.  12 four-bedroom  units.

.  The  buildings  would  cover  less  than  12%  of  the  prop-

erty.

.  More  than  61%  of  the  property  would  be  open  space.

.  Mature  trees  along  Euclid  to  be  saved  and  planting
screening  would  be  provided  along  the  backs  of  the
single-family  residences  on  Drury  Lane.

of  the  development  and  in  every  instance  in  which  changes
were  recommended  by the  staff,  including  changes  in fire  engine
access,  garbage  disposal  and  drainage,  such  changes  were  in-
corporated  in  the  MHDC  plans.

15.

|Public  hearings  were  held  before  the  Plan  Commission
of  Arlington  Heights  on  March  3,  1971,  April  21,  1971,  and
June  16;  1971.

68

16.

The  MHDC  Board  of  Directors,  to  meet  objections  at

public  hearings:

a.  passed  a  resolution  on  May  13,  1971  to  retain  per-

manent  control  of  the  development;

b.  secured  offers  to  manage  the  property  from  leading

real  estate  managers;

c.  passed  a  resolution  agreeing  to  create  a  citizens  ad-
visory  board  to  be  selected  by the  Mayor  of  Arlington
Heights  to  participate  in  planning  and  management  of
the  development.

17.

The  Chairman  of  the  Plan  Commission  read  into  the
record  reports  of  the  Fire  Chief,  the  Building  Commissioner,  the
Director  of Public  Works,  and  the  Acting  Director  of  Engineer-
ing.

18.

Nevertheless,  on  June  16,  1971,  the  Plan  Commission
voted  7  to  2  to  recommend  to  the  Village  Board  of  Trustees
that  it  reject  MHDC’s  rezoning  petition.

19.  On  September  28,  1971,  the  Village  Board  of  Trustees
heard  presentations  before  voting  6  to  1  to  deny  MHDC’s  re-
zoning  petition.

20.

The  Village  Board  of  Arlington  Heights  approved  the
report  of  the  Joint  Study  Committee  on  Low  and  Moderate  In-
come  Housing  which  recognized  a  need  for  and  recommended
development  of  150  to  250  units  of  moderate-income  housing
and  50  units  of  low-income  housing  in  Arlington  Heights.

21.

Arlington  Heights  had  a  population  in  1970  of  64,884,
more  than  twice  its  1960  population  of  27,878  and  more  than
seven  times  its  1950  population  of  8,768.  The  black  population
of Arlington  Heights  was  | in  1950,  4 in  1960,  and  27  in  1970.

22.

The  Village  staff  planner,  Mr.  Kesler,  was  not  asked
for  nor  did  he  give  any  written  or  oral  opinion  to  the  Plan  Com-
mission  or  the  Village  Board  of  Arlington  Heights.

List  OF  CONTESTED  ISSUES  OF  FACT  AND  LAw.

A.

Contested  Issues  of Fact.

Black  persons  who  presently  work  in  Arlington  Heights
cannot  find  decent  housing  in Arlington  Heights  at  a  price
they  can  afford.

Black  persons  who  presently  work  in  Arlington  Heights
qualify  for  and  seek  the  type  of  housing  proposed  to  be
built  by  MHDC.

Plaintiffs  Isaac  Greenwood,  Arthur  Guthrie,  and  Willie
Ransom  cannot  find  decent  housing  at  a  price  they  can
afford  in  Arlington  Heights.

Such  Plaintiffs  lived  in  overcrowded  sub-standard  housing
outside  Arlington  Heights.

Isaac  Greenwood  lived  in  a  segregated  area  with  buildings
having  code  violations.  There  is  no  public  transportation
between  his  home  and  his  job  in  Arlington  Heights  and
he,  therefore,  drives  long  distances  daily  at  a  cost  of
time  and  money.

The  Greenwoods  have  been  looking  for,  but  have  been
unable  to  find,  decent  housing  at  a  price  they  can  afford  in
the  vicinity  of  Mr.  Greenwood’s  job.  The  Greenwoods
seek,  and  are  eligible  for,  housing  of  the  type  Plaintiff
MHDC  seeks  to build  in Arlington  Heights.

Arthur  Guthrie  lived  in  a  segregated  area  with  buildings
having  building  code  violations.

There  is  no  public  transportation  between  his  home  and
his  job  in  Arlington  Heights  and  he  therefore  drives  the
long  distance  daily,  a  time  consuming  trip  involving  exces-
sive  expense.

70

71

10.

11.

2.

a3.

14.

tS.

The  Guthries  have  been  looking  for,  but  have  been  unable
to  find,  decent  housing  at  a  price  they  could  afford  in  the
vicinity  of  Mr.  Guthrie’s  job.  The  Guthries  seek,  and  are
eligible  for,  housing  of  the  type  Plaintiff  MHDC  seeks  to
build  in  Arlington  Heights.
Willie  Ransom  lived  in  a  segregated  area  with  buildings
having  building  code  violations.
There  is  no  public  transportation  between  his  home  and
his  job  in  Arlington  Heights  and  he  therefore  drives  the
long  distance  daily,  a  time-consuming  trip  involving  ex-
cessive  expense.
Plaintiff  Ransom  has  sought,  but  has  been  unable  to
locate,  adequate  housing  for  himself  and  his  sons  in  the
vicinity  of  his  job.  Plaintiff  Ransom  seeks,  and  would
qualify  for,  the  housing  which  Plaintiff  MHDC  seeks  to
build  in  Arlington  Heights.

Euclid  Avenue  is  a  heavily  traveled  road.
MHDC  has  expended  in  excess  of  $35,000  developing
its  proposal  and  preparing  for  hearings  before  the  Arling-
ton  Heights  Plan  Commission  and  the  Arlington  Heights
Trustees.
The  failure  of  the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights  to  rezone
the  subject  property  from  an  R3  single-family  classifica-
tion  to  an  R5  multiple-family  classification  is  racially
discriminatory  in  that  it has  the  effect  of  preventing  low
and  moderate  income  minority  group  members,  who  make
up  a  substantial  portion  of  the  low  and  moderate  income
population  of  the  Chicago  area,  from  moving  from  areas
of minority  concentration  to  a  white  area,  thereby  denying
them  the  opportunity  to  live  in  a  decent  integrated  en-
vironment  and  perpetuating  residential  segregation  in  the
Chicago  area.

16.

Arlington  Heights  is  one  part  of  the  rapidly  expanding
white  suburbs  in  the  Chicago  area  which  is witnessing  the

17.

18.

19.

20.

development  of  most  of  the  new  employment  facilities  in
the  Chicago  area.

The  Centex  Industrial  Park  was  developed  in  the  vicinity
of  Arlington  Heights  and  contributes  substantial  amounts
in taxes  to  High  School  District  No.  214  serving  Arlington
Heights.  A  number  of  industries  have  also  built  plants
within  the  Village  limits  of Arlington  Heights,  among  them
Honeywell,  Inc.  These  plants  employ  large  blue  collar
forces  including  substantial  numbers  of  minority  group
employees.

Minority  group  employees  have  been  unable  to  find  decent
housing  in  Arlington  Heights,  and  must,  therefore,  com-
mute  long  distances  to  their  jobs  in  Arlington  Heights.
These  workers  subsidize  residents  of the  Village  by coming
in  in  the  morning  to  man  tax-producing  properties  of  the
Village  but  must  leave  at  night  because  the  Village  does
not  permit  housing  for  them.

Because  of  the  difficulty,  time,  and  expense  of  commuting
from  ghetto  areas  to  Arlington  Heights,  many  minority
group  members  are  effectively  discourage  from  seeking
employment  in  Arlington  Heights  and  the  Centex  Indus-
trial  Park.

At  the  same  time  white  suburbs  have  become  increasingly
prosperous  with  the  rapid  increase  in  their  tax  bases  as
industry  has  moved  to  the  suburbs,  minority  group  mem-
bers  who  work  or  desire  to  work  in  Arlington  Heights
have  been  confined  to  areas  where  the  loss  of  an  adequate
tax  base  has  meant  deterioration  of schools  and  municipal
services.

on

Arlington  Heights’  exclusion  of  multi-family  dwellings
extends  only  to  low  and  moderate  income  housing  since
the  Village  has  often  rezoned  land  for  multi-family  units
designed  for  the  relatively  affluent.

72

ae.

y 4

y

26.

30.

x)

The  proposed  rezoning  is  consistent  with  the  Village's
zoning  policy  and  practice,  is  a  reasonable  use  of  the
subject  property,  and  meets  a critical  regional  and  com-
munity  need.  There  are  no  compelling  zoning  and  planning
reasons  for  denying  the  rezoning  application.
Property  situated  similarly  to  the  subject  property  has
been  rezoned  upon  the  landswner’s  request  to  RS.  In
numerous  cases  the  rezoned  property  was  adjacent  to
single-family  and  institutional  uses.
The  proposed  development  is  consistent  with  Arlington
Heights’  zoning  policy  and  practice  as  it  concerns  use  of
multi-family  districts  as  transitional  use  districts  or  buffers
between  single-family  districts  and  more  intense  uses.
The  proposed  development  would  not  adversely  affect
public  facilities  available  to  service  the  subject  property.
Tax  revenues  attributable  to  the  proposed  development
would  exceed  municipal  and  school  expenditures  attri-
butable  thereto.

The  proposed  development  will  not  adversely  affect  the
character  of  the  surrounding  neighborhood  and  will  not
diminish  property  values.

Plaintiff  Isaac  Greenwood  is  a  black  citizen  of  the  United
States  residing  in Chicago,  Illinois  and  employed  by Honey-
well,  Inc.  in  Arlington  Heights,  Illinois.

Plaintiff  Arthur  Guthrie  is  a  black  citizen  of  the  United
States  residing  in Chicago,  Illinois  and  employed  by Honey-
well,  Inc.  in  Arlington  Heights,  Illinois.

Plaintiff  Willie  Ransom  is  a  black  citizen  of  the  United
States  who  resides  in  Evanston,  Illinois  and  is  employed
by  Honeywell,  Inc.  in  Arlington  Heights,  Illinois.

Intervening  plaintiff  Eluteria  D.  Maldonado  is a citizen
of  the  United  States  residing  in  and  working  in  Arlington
Heights,  Illinois.

The  Northwest  Opportunity  Center  was  formed  for  the
purpose  of  representing  the  interests  of  low  and  moderate-
income  residents  of  the  northwest  Cook  County  suburbs
in  Chicago,  Illinois.

Isaac  Greenwood,  at  the  time  of  filing  of  the  action,
lived  with  his  wife  and  three  children  in  an  apartment  on
the  south  side  of  Chicago.

Arthur  Guthrie,  at  the  time  of  filing  of  the  action,  lived

Willie  Ransom  is  divorced  and  at  the  time  of  filing  of

R-3  allows  a  density  of  five  units  to  an  acre  and  with
requirements  of  the  ordinance  approximately  60  single-
family  units  could  be  built  on  the  property.

af.

The  proposed  development:

a.  would  meet  all  requirements  of  Section  236  of  the

National  Housing  Act  of  1968;

b.  is  planned  for  moderate  income  families;

will  have  an  affirmative  marketing  program  as  required
by  the  Department  of  Housing  and  Urban  Develop-
ment  and  be rented  on  an  equal  opportunity  basis  which
will  resuli  in  a  racially  integrated  development.

39.

The  proposed  development  meets  all  requirements  of  R-5
zoning.

All  but  one  of the  reports  of  the  Fire  Chief,  Building  Com-
missioner,  Director  of  Public  Works  and  Acting  Director
of  Engineering  (that  on  water  run-off)  contained  no  men-
tion  of any  problem.  The  report  on  water  run-off  suggested
certain  changes  which  MHDC  then  incorporated  in  its
plans  for  the  proposed  development.

74

75

40.  There  is a  need  for  decent  housing  for  those  who  work  or
desire  to  work  in  Arlington  Heights  but  cannot  afford  the
housing  presently  available  in  the  Village.

41.  The  Village  of  Arlington  Heights  has  zoned  its  land  pre-
dominantly  for  single-family  houses  which,  absent  sub-
sidies,  low  and  moderate  income  persons  cannot  afford.
Although  federal  subsidies  are  available  under  the  National
Housing  Act  of  1968  for  the  construction  of  single-family
houses,  the  cost  of  land  in  Arlington  Heights  and  the  cost
of construction  in the Chicago  metropolitan  area  prevent  the
development  of  such  low  and  moderate  income  housing  in
Arlington  Heights.

42.

In  addition  to  the  need  for  low  and  moderate  income  hous-
ing  for  nonresident  workers  and  potential  workers  in  Ar-
lington  Heights,  there  is  a  need,  recognized  by  Arlington
Heights  itself,  for  low  and  moderate  income  housing  for
elderly  residents  of Arlington  Heights  who  can  neither  afford
to  maintain  and  pay  property  taxes  on  single-family  homes
nor  afford  the  apartments  presently  available  in  Arling-
ton  Heights.

43.  When  the  decision  was  made  by the  Village  Board  of  the
Village  of  Arlington  Heights  on  September  28,  1971,  the
Board  members  had  no  information  before  them  other  than
that  presented  at  the  public  hearings  by  MHDC  and  that
adduced  by  the  testimony  at  public  hearings  from  wit-
nesses  from  the  audience  and  Village  staff.

B.

(i)

Contested  Issues  of Law.

Certain  contested  issues  of  law  have  already  been  decided
by orders  of  court  on  the  3rd  of October,  1972  and  on  the  2nd
of January,  1973  in favor  of plaintiffs.  Copies  of such  orders  are

|

attached  hereto  and  hereby  made  a  part  hereof  as  Exhibits
J and  K.  The  following  remain  as  contested  issues  of  law  which
are  unresolved.

1.  The  refusal  of the  Village  to  rezone  the  subject  property
violates  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  of  the  United  States  Con-
stitution,  the  Civil  Rights  Act  of  1866  (42  U.  S.  C.  § 1981,
1982),  the  Civil  Rights  Act  of  1871  (42  U.  S.  C.  § 1983),
the  Fair  Housing  Act  of  1968  (42  U.  S.  C.  §§ 3601  ef  seq.)
and  Article  I, Section  2 of  the  Illinois  Constitution  and  24  III.
Rev.  Stat.  § 11-13  et seq.

2.  The  action  of  the  Village  denying  the  use  of  property

is  unreasonable,  arbitrary  and  capricious.

3.

Defendant  Village  and  Defendant  Trustees,  in refusing  to
rezone  the  subject  property  from  R-3  to  R-5,  have  discriminated
against  the  individual  plaintiffs  by perpetuating  racial  segregation
in  housing  and  in  so  doing  have  denied  the  individual  plaintiffs
integrated  housing  opportunities  and  an  opportunity  to  share  in
the environmental  benefits  of land-use  planning,  while  extending
those  opportunities  to  wealthier  whites  who  desire  to  live  in
Arlington  Heights.  Defendant  Village  and  Defendant  Trustees
have  violated  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  of  the  United  States
Constitution  and  the  Civil  Rights  Act  of  1866  (42  U.  S.  C.
§§  1981,  1982),  the  Civil  Rights  Act  of  1871  (42  U.  S.  C.
§  1982),  the  Fair  Housing  Act  of  1968  (42  U.  S.C.  § 3601
et  seq.)  and  Article  I, Section  2 of  the  Illinois  Constitution.  In
addition,  such  action,  which  results  in  the  exclusion  of  racial
minorities  from  the  Village  is not  in  the  general  welfare,  thereby
violating  the  Illinois  Zoning  Enabling  Act,  24  Ill.  Rev.  Stat.
§ 11-13  et seq.,  and  is beyond  the  police  power  of the  state,  there-
by  violating  the  due  process  clauses  of  the  Fourteenth  Amend-
ment  of  the  United  States  Constitution  and  of  Article  I, Section
2 of  the  Illinois  Constitution.

4.

Defendant  Village  and  Defendant  Trustees,  by unreason-
ably,  arbitrarily,  and  capriciously  denying  Plaintiff  MHDC  its

76

77

right to use  the property  in which  it has  an  interest  in a reasonable
manner  have  violated  the  due  process  clause  of  the  Fourteenth
Amendment  of  the  United  States  Constitution  and  Article  I,
Section  2 of the Illinois  Constitution.

B.

(ii)

Defendants’  Contested  Issues  of  Law.

1.

2.

Is  a  substantial  Federal  question  raised?
Is the  single  family  residential  zoning  as  a  matter  of  law

unreasonable?

3.

Are  the  allegations  of  Complaint  sufficient  to  overcome

the  presumption  of  validity  of  the  zoning  ordinance?

4.

Is  the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights  required  to  use  a
different  standard  for  low  and  moderate  income  housing  than
other  types  of  land  usage?

Exhibit  B.

Norman  Drummond,  AIP

Norman  Drummond,  AIP

Partner

In  1966  Norman  Drummond  was  appointed  Director  of  the
Lake  County  (Illinois)  Regional  Planning  Commission.  His
public  agency  planning  career  began  in the  offices  of the  Lorain
County  (Ohio)  Regional  Planning  Commission.  Between  1957
and  1969  he  gained  broad  experience  in  city  and  regional
planning,  neighborhood  redevelopment,  site  planning,  and  ad-
ministration.  In  addition  to  being  Director  of  the  Lake  County
agency  from  1966  to  1969  he  was  Chairman  of the  Community
Shelter  Plan  Technical  Advisory  Committee,  Secretary  of  the
Lake  County  Housing  Study,  and  a member  of the  Lake  County
Transportation  Study  Policy  Committee.

Prior  to  forming  Associated  Planners,  Norman  Drummond
operated  his  own  planning  consulting  firm.  He  was  planning
consultant  to  the  Illinois  Zoning  Laws  Study  Commission  and
helped  draft  a  new  zoning  enabling  law  for  Illinois.

As  an  undergraduate  Norman  Drummond  studied  both  archi-
tecture  and  sociology  at  The  Ohio  State  University.  In  1961
he  was  appointed  J.  C.  Nichols  Fellow  of  the  Urban  Land
Institute  at  Ohio  State  where  he  received  his  Master  of  City
Planning  degree  in  1963.

Norman  Drummond  is  a  member  of  the  American  Institute
of Planners,  the  American  Society  of  Planning  Officials,  and  the

ARCHITECT'S  EXPERIENCE,  BACKGROUND,
AND  ACCOMPLISHMENTS

The  architect,  has  twenty  years  housing  experience  with  the
last  eight  years  almost  exclusively  devoted  to  moderate  and  low
income  housing,  utilizing  federal  sponsored  programs  of  221(d)
(3),  and  236.  He  has  completed  the  second  phase  of  a  highly
successful  moderate  income  project  utilizing  the  Planned  Unit
Development  concept  in  Rockford,  Illinois.  His  office  had  re-
cently  completed  the  building  of  a  large  housing  complex  in
Chicago  at  18th  and  Kostner  and  another  at  37th  & Lake  Park.
These  are  the  first  developments  in  this  region  using  the  236
program.

The  office  has  two  moderate  income  projects,  awarded  by the
DUR,  in  the  Lincoln  Park  Project  I.  One  project  entails  five
city  blocks  of  mixed  moderate,  middle  and  low  income  in  a
planned  development.

The  architect  has  a home  in this  area  and  has  involved  himself
in  community  action  for  some  years.  He  was  Chairman  of  the
Planning  Committee  of  Lincoln  Park  Conservation  Association.
This  office  also  was  responsible  for  a  number  of  buildings,  new
and  remodeled,  adjacent  to,  or  within  this  area  of  devel-
opment.

The  office  is  presently  engaged  in  three  large  federal  or  state
sponsored  programs—two  being  within  the  City of Chicago  and

100

one  being  a  large  planned  unit  development  in  the  Metropolitan
Area.

He  has  been  witness  in  many  zoning  hearings  on  planned
developments  for  projects  in Chicago  and  outlying  areas.  Includ-
ing  Addison,  Schaumburg,  Streamwood,  Waukegan  and  Joliet
in  Illinois;  in  Milwaukee  and  Madison  Wisconsin;  in  Detroit
Michigan;  in Denver  Colorado,  and  Stockholm  Sweden  as  zoning
expert  and  architect  of  the  projects.

180  North  LaSalle  Street,  Chicago,  Illinois,  in  1973.  The  firm,

Illinois  in  1969.  The  Charter  permits  Real  Estate  Appraising  and

request  and  only  with  the  explicit  permission  of  parties  or
clientele  involved.

In  the
Supreme  Court  of the United  States
Octover  ‘Term,  1975.

No.  75-616

VILLAGE  OF  ARLINGTON  HEIGHTS,  ET  AL.,

Petitioners,

VS.

METROPOLITAN  HOUSING  DEVELOPMENT
CORPORATION,  ET  AL.,

Respondents,

NORTHWEST  OPPORTUNITY  CENTER  and
ELUTERIA  D.  MALDONADO,

Intervening  Respondents.

On  Petition  For  Writ  Of  Certiorari  To  The  United  States
Court  Of  Appeals  For  The  Seventh  Circuit

BRIEF  FOR  THE  RESPONDENTS  IN  OPPOSITION

2

3

QUESTIONS  PRESENTED  FOR  REVIEW

1.  Do  municipal  defendants  violate  the  Fair  Housing
Act  of  1968  (42  U.S.C.  §$3601  et  seq.)  and  the  Civil  Rights
Act  of  1866  (42  U.S.C.  $1982)  by  perpetuating  racial  seg-
regation  through  refusing,  without  a  compelling  public  in-
terest,  to  rezone  property  so  as  to  permit  an  integrated
housing  development  which  would  appreciably  dimin-
ish  segregation  in the  municipality,  especially  when  histori-
cally  the  area  is  racially  segregated  through  the  operation
of dual  housing  markets  and  the  municipal  defendants  have
accommodated  the  housing  needs  of  the  municipality’s
white  residents  but  have  not  in  the  past  sponsored  or  par-
ticipated  in  any  subsidized  housing  development?

2.  In  an  area  segregated  racially  through  the  operation
of  dual  housing  markets,  do  municipal  defendants  who
have  accommodated  the  housing  needs  of  the  municipali-
ty’s  white  residents  but  have  not  in  the  past  sponsored  or
participated  in  any  subsidized  housing  developments  vio-
late  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  and  the  Civil  Rights  Act
of  1871  (42  U.S.C.  $1983)  by perpetuating  racial  segrega-
tion  through  refusing,  without  a  compelling  public  interest,
to  rezone  property  so  as  to  permit  an  integrated  housing
development  which  would  appreciably  diminish  segregation
in  the  municipality?

CONSTITUTIONAL  PROVISION  AND
STATUTES  INVOLVED

In  addition  to  the  statutes  and  constitutional  provision
set  forth  in  Appendix  D to  the  Petition,  this  case  involves:

Interference,  coercion,  or  intimida-
42  U.S.C.  §3617.
tion;  enforcement  by civil  action.  It  shall  be  unlawful

to  coerce,  intimidate,  threaten,  or  interfere  with  any
persons  in  the  exercise  or  enjoyment  of,  or  on  account
of his  having  exercised  or  enjoyed,  or  on  account  of his
having  aided  or  encouraged  any  other  person  in  the
exercise  or  enjoyment  of,  any  right  granted  or  pro-
tected  by  §$3603,  3604,  3605,  or  3606  of  this  title.  This
section  may  be  enforced  by appropriate  civil  action.

STATEMENT

On  September  28,  1971,  the  Trustees  of  the  Village  of
Arlington  Heights  refused  to  rezone  a  fifteen-acre  parcel
of  land,  bounded  on  two  sides  by  single-family  homes,*
upon  which  respondent  MHDC  proposed  to  build  Lincoln
Green,  a  low-  and  moderate-income  housing  development
for  which  MHDC  had  spent  $38,000  planning  and  designing.
Lincoln  Green,  unlike  the  rest  of  the  Vil-
(Tr.  276)
lage,  undoubtedly  would  have  been  an  integrated  com-
munity  (Pet.  App.  A8)**  which  might  well  have  increased
the  minority  population  of  the  Village  by  over  1000  per-
cent  (Pet.  App.  A8),  and  the  question  of  racial  integration

*  Petitioners’  statement  that  the  subject  property  is  surrounded
by  land  “developed  for  R-3  single-family  use”  is  mistaken.  While
it  abuts  single-family  homes  on  one  side  and  on  a  second  faces
single-family  homes  across  a  major  arterial  street  (Tr.  233-34,  391-
92,  788-89),  on  two  sides  it  abuts  undeveloped  St.  Viator  property.
(Pet.  App.  A2)

**  Thus  petitioners’  assertion  that  the  Court  of  Appeals  ruled  that
there  was  merely  a  “possibility”  that  Lincoln  Green  “might”  con-
tain  a  higher  percentage  of  blacks  than  an  R-3  single  family  subdi-
vision  built  on  the  site  (Pet.  8  and  9  respectively)  is  totally  falla-
cious.  Respondents  had  proved  at  trial  that  40%  of  those  eligible  for
Lincoln  Green  would  be  black  (Pet.  App.  A6),  that  MHDC  mar-
kets  its  housing  so  as  to  attract  minorities  (Tr.  45,  229)  and  that
it  had  successfully  done  so  elsewhere.

(Tr.  251)

+

5

was  clearly  before  the  Trustees  when  they  denied  MHDC’s
application  for  rezoning.“

Lincoln  Green  will  not  be  a  highrise  development,  but
merely  a  cluster  of  two-story  townhouses  no  higher  than
the  surrounding  single  family  homes.  As  noted  by two  dis-
senting  members  of  the  Arlington  Heights  Plan  Commis-
sion,  in  terms  of  density,  architecture  and  most  other
characteristics,  such  townhouses  are  more  similar  to  R-3
dwellings  than  R-5  buildings.
(Pet.  App.  A10)  Under
the  plan  more  than  sixty  percent  of  the  property  was  to  be
maintained  as  open  space.  (Pet.  App.  A2)  The  buildings
would  cover  16%  cf  the  site  while  the  abutting  single-fam-
ily  homes  cover  24%  of  the  lots  upon  which  they  are  lo-
cated.  (Tr.  377-78  and  Ps.  Ex.  11,  p.  7).  There  would  be
better  screening  and  traffic  control  within  the  development
than  could  be  provided  in  a  single-family  subdivision  (Tr.
380  and  381).  There  would  be  no  adverse  impact  upon
municipal  facilities  and  services  (Ps.  Ex.  14  and  15).  The
development  would  make  a  net  contribution  in  terms  of
revenue  to  both  the  Village  and  the  school  districts  within

(Ps.  Ex.  48-1).

Another  letter  complained:

“T’m  a  bit  tired  of  hearing  and  reading  about  the  Low  Income
Housing  in  Arlington  Heights  for  the  benefit  of  our  colored
and  Spanish-American  friends  in  Chicago.  One  wonders  who
is  running  our  village,  our  Mr.  Walsh  or  Mayor  Daley.”
(Ps.  Ex.  48-2).

which  it lies.  (Pet.  App.  As-A3;  Tr.  243-47  and  Ps.  Ex.  26,
p. 5).

After  the  trustees  denied  the  application  for  rezoning,
this  suit  was  instituted.  Jurisdiction  over  the  Village  was
based  upon  28  U.S.C.  $1331  (the  jurisdictional  basis  of the
Fourteenth  Amendment  claim),  28  U.S.C.  §1343  (for  the
$1982  claim)  and  42  U.S.C.  $3612  (for  the  §§ 3604  and  3617
claim).  Jurisdiction  over  the  individual  defendants,  all  offi-
cials  of  the  Village,  was  based  upon  $1331  (for  the  Four-
teenth  Amendment  claim),  $1343  (for  the  §§ 1982  and  1983
claims,  and  $3612  (for  the  $§ 3604  and  3617  claims).

After  a  trial  on  the  merits,  the  district  court,  concluding
that  there  was  no  racial  discrimination  involved  or  other
violation  of  constitutional  or  statutory  rights,  denied  the
plaintiffs  any  relief.*  The  Seventh  Circuit,  per  Swygert,
J.,  reversed.**

At  the  trial,  evidence  of  prior  zoning  in  Arlington
Heights  showed  that  petitioners  rezoned  60  parcels  of land

*  Petitioners’  argument  that  the  trial  judge’s  “findings”  of  no  dis-
crimination  were  reversed  by the  Court  of  Appeals  without  a  deter-
mination  that  they  were  clearly  erroneous  (see,  e.g.,  Pet.  6,  9 and
10)  ignores  the  simple  fact  that  the  “findings”  to  which  petitioners
refer  are  the  trial  judge’s  ultimate  conclusions  of  law  which  were
the  subject  of  the  appeal.  The  Court  of  Appeals  accepted  the  few
findings  of  the  trial  court  and  fleshed  out  the  record  when  the  trial
court  was  silent  on  a  necessary  question  of  fact.

**  Fairchild,  Ch.  J.,  dissented  on  a  question  of  fact  but  agreed
that,  as  a  matter  of  law,  “if  plaintiffs  establish  that  the  Village’s
enforcement  of  its  zoning  scheme  makes  construction  of  housing  for
low  and  moderate-income  individuals  not  reasonably  possible,  the
refusal  perpetuates  racial  segregation  and,  absent  compelling  gov-
ernmental  justification,  the  plaintiffs  would  be  entitled  to  the  relief
they  seek.”

(Pet.  App.  11A)

6

7

in Arlington  Heights  to  R-5  for  large  multi-family  projects
(e.g.,  Ds.  Ex.  10  and  11),  53  of  which  were  adjacent  on  at
least  one  side  to  single-family  homes  (Ps.  Ex.  12  and  52).
As  a  result,  there  were  some  5,239  market-rent  apartments
(Tr.  692)  By  1973  there
in  Arlington  Heights  in  1970.
were  6,085  and  zoning  for  9,073  more.  (Pet.  5)  However,
the  minimum  monthly  rents  were  so  high  that  low-  and
moderate-income  persons  could  not  afford  these  apart-
(Tr.  77-79;  479-80;  489-90).  Indeed,  the  Village
ments.
President,  a  Village  study,  and  the  principal  spokesman
opposing  Lincoln  Green  all  publicly  acknowledged  the
need  for  low-  and  moderate-income  housing  in  the  com-
munity  (Final  Pre-trial  Order,  Stipulation  of  Uncontested
Facts,  #20  and  Ps.  Ex.  43,  pp.  3-4).  The  trial  court  found
that  such  housing  was  scarce  (Pet.  App.  B4),  and  the
Court  of Appeals  noted  the  great  demand  for  it.  (Pet.  App.
A2)

While  the  Village  accommodated  the  housing  needs  of
the  affluent  whites  who  could  afford  the  new  apartments,
it  rejected  the  only  two  applications  ever  before  it  for  re-
zonings  to  accommodate  low-  and  moderate-income  hous-
ing.  (See  Ds.  Ex.  10,  #21  and  Tr.  853).  It  has  no  subsi-
dized  housing  (Pet.  App.  A2),  and  the  supply  of  low-cost
housing  in  Arlington  Heights  is  actually  declining.
(Tr.
483-84;  Ps.  Ex.  18,  p.  19).  MHDC  was  unable  to  find  an
economically  feasible  and  suitable  alternate  site  for  Lin-
coln  Green.  (Pet.  App.  A8)

After  taking  judicial  notice  of  the  high  degree  of  racial
residential  segregation  in  Chicago  and  its  environs  (Pet.
App.  A7),  the  Court  of  Appeals  went  on  to  set  forth  the
detailed  facts  of  residential  segregation  in  Arlington
Heights  and  its  immediate  area.  See  Pet.  7.  According  to
statistics  of  plaintiffs’  expert,  demographer  and  urbanolo-

gist  Pierre  de  Vise,  Arlington  Heights  is the  most  residen-
tially  segregated  community  in  the  Chicago  metropolitan
area  among  municipalities  with  more  than  fifty  thousand
residents.”  The  percentage  of  Blacks  in  the  four  county
area  of  which  Arlington  Heights  is  a  part  actually  de-
creased  from  1960  to  1970  while  the  percentage  of the  popu-
lation  in  the  entire  Chicago  metropolitan  area  that  was
black  increased  from  fourteen  percent  to  eighteen  percent.
(Pet.  App.  A7-A8)

As  the  Court  of  Appeals  observed,  the  impact  of  this
statistic  can  be  fully  appreciated  only  in  the  context  of the
shift  in  employment  opportunities  during  the  same  period.
While  the  City  of  Chicago  lost  230,000  jobs,  the  number  of
jobs  in  the  four-township  Arlington  Heights  area  rose
from  100,000  to  200,000.  Blacks,  however,  have  not  been
able  to  take  full  advantage  of  these  job  opportunities.  In
1970  only  137  of the  15,000  people  who  worked  in Arlington
Heights  were  black.  Part  of  the  explanation  for  this  is
that  many  black  workers  have  been  unable  to  find  housing
they  can  afford  in  Arlington  Heights.  (Pet.  App.  A7-A8)

*  Petitioners  mistakenly  characterize  Prof.  de  Vise’s  testimony
when  they  claim  that  he  testified  that  3,244  blacks  could  afford  to
live  in  Arlington  Heights.
(Pet.  10)  That  figure  is  the  number
of  blacks  who  would  hypothetically  live  in  Arlington  Heights  in  a
color-blind  market,  assuming  random  dispersal  based  upon  income
and  cost  of  housing.

ARGUMENT

‘The  petitioners  fail  to  identify  the  rule  of the  case  which
conflicts  in  any  way  with  any  of  the  cases  they  cite.  Their
rhetorical  generalizations  (Pet.  App.  8)  have  no  relation-
ship  to  the  decision  below,  which  judged  the  impact,  as-
sessed  in  terms  of  historical  context  and  ultimate  effect
(Pet.  App.  A7),  of  petitioners’  refusal  to  rezone  one  par-
cel  of  land  upon  which  a  private  developer  sought  to  build
integrated  low  and  moderate-income  housing.  That  his-
torical  context  included  two  elements.
First  is  ‘‘a  high
degree  of  racial  residential  segregation’’  (Pet.  App.  A7),
quoting  Clark  v.  Universal  Builders,  501  F.2d  324,  334-5
(7th  Cir.)  cert.  denied,  419  U.S.  1070  (1974),  which,  the
Seventh  Circuit  noted  in  Clark,  501  F.2d  at  334,  resulted
in  dual  housing  markets  in  the  Chicago  metropolitan  area.
Second  is  the  Village’s  exploitation  of  that  problem  in  al-
lowing  itself  to  become  almost  100%  white  (Pet.  App.  AQ),
by  permitting  the  development  of  housing  (including  a
great  number  of  apartments)  which  was  being  marketed  to
and  occupied  by whites  almost  exclusively,  by virtue  of  the
dual  housing  markets,  and  by failing  to  sponsor  or  partici-
pate  in  any  subsidized  development  (Pet.  App.  A8).  In
this  context,  the  impact  of  petitioners’  obstruction  of  one
small  private  effort  toward  eliminating  the  pervasive  prob-
lem  of  segregated  housing,  the  court  concluded,  had  a  dis-
criminatory  effect  and  could  not  be  justified  by  a  compel-
ling  public  interest.

(Pet.  App.  A9-A10)

This  ruling  does  not  conflict  with  any  decision  of  this
Court  or  of  the  Courts  of  Appeals  and  does  not  raise  an
important  question  of  federal  law.

t  9

I,

There  Is  No  Conflict  of  Decision.

1.  There  simply  are  no  Supreme  Court  cases  which
deal  with  a  municipality’s  duty  not  to  interfere  with  pri-
vate  desegregation  efforts.  The  cases  petitioners  cite  are
not  in  conflict  with  the  decision  of  the  Seventh  Circuit.

The  petitioners  argue  that  there  is  no  ‘‘right  to  hous-
ing,’’  citing  Lindsey  v.  Normet,  405  U.S.  56  (1972)  and
San  Antonio  Independent  School  District  v.  Rodriguez,  411
U.S.  1  (1973).  (Pet.  13-14)  Since  neither  respondents  nor
the  court  below  has  ever  relied  upon  this  theory  of  the
case,  these  cases  are  irrelevant  to  the  issue  at  bar.  Similar-
ly  irrelevant  is  petitioners’  argument  that  an  ordinance
which  has  a  greater  impact  upon  the  poor  than  others  is
not,  by  virtue  of  that  fact,  unlawful.
(Pet.  15)  Respon-
dents  have  never  so  argued,  and  the  court  below  never  so
ruled.

Petitioners  also  argue  that  the  decision  conflicts  with
James  v.  Valtierra,  402  U.S.  187  (1971),  and  Palmer  v.
Thompson,  403  U.S.  217  (1971),  which  purportedly  stand
for  the  proposition  that  a  classification  which  dispropor-
tionately  impacts  minorities  is  not  thereby  rendered  un-
lawful.
(Pet.  18  and  15)  While  respondents  believe  that
these  cases  stand  for  no  such  proposition,*  the  court  below

— &« ~s

*  Valtierra  dealt  first  with  voter  rather  than  governmental  action,
giving  great  deference  to  California’s  long  tradition  of  referenda,
and  with  a  claim  of  economic  rather  than  race  discrimination.  The
Court  ruled  that  the  record  simply  did  not  support  a  case  of  racial
discrimination  (402  U.S.  at  141),  and  the  dissent  addressed  itself
In  Palmer,  the  chal-
solely  to  the  issue  of  economic  discrimination.
lenged  action  was  the  closing  by  Jackson,  Mississippi  of  the  four

(footnote  continued )

10

11

specifically  refused  to  rule  for  respondents  on  the  basis  of
the  law’s  disproportionate  impact.  (Pet.  App.  A6-A7)  The
decision  is thus  consistent  with  petitioners’  reading  of  Val-
tierra  and  Palmer.

Lastly,  petitioners  argue  that  the  decision  is  inconsis-
tent  with  Village  of Belle  Terre  v.  Boraas,  416  U.S.  1 (1974)
and  Milliken  v.  Bradley,  418  U.S.  717  (1974).  (Pet.  12-13
and  15  respectively)  But  Belle  Terre  has  no  relevance,
not  dealing  at  all  with  racial  issues.  Milliken  is also  irrele-
vant  since  the  Court  of  Appeals  has  not  ruled  that  racial
imbalance  alone  is  a  constitutional  violation.  As  noted
above,  the  court  ruled  that  the  petitioners,  absent  a  com-
pelling  public  interest,  have  a  duty  not  to  interfere  with
private  desegregation  efforts,  which  they  have  actively
prevented,  when  the  context  is one  of  racial  discrimination
and  dual  housing  markets.

2.  Petitioners  also  argue  that  respondents  do  not  have
standing  and  that  the  Court  of  Appeals  decision  conflicts
sub  silentio  with  Warth  vy.  Seldin,  45  L.Ed.2d  343  (1975).
(Pet.  24-26)  Petitioners  did  not  raise  the  standing  issue
on  appeal.  It  was  not  a  question  presented  to  the  Court
of  Appeals  for  review,  and  this  Court  will  not  ordinarily
consider  issues  neither  raised  before  nor  considered  in
the  Court  of  Appeals.  See,  e.g.,  Adickes  v.  S.  H.  Kress  and
Co.,  398  U.S.  144,  147,  n.2  (1970)  and  cases  cited.

(footnote  continued )

white  and  one  black  public  swimming  pools,  with  one  for  whites  and
one  for  blacks  being  subsequently  reopened  by private  persons.  (403
U.S.  at  218  and  252  respectively).  There  obviously  was  no  dis-
proportionate  impact  upon  blacks,  and  the  Court  never  stated  there
was.

In  any  event,  respondents  have  standing.  The  individual
respondents  have  looked  for  and  been  unable  to  find  hous-
ing they  can  afford  in Arlington  Heights  (see,  e.g.,  Tr.  220-
2)  and  would  be  eligible  for  Lincoln  Green.  (See,  e.g.,  Tr
43-44;  Ps,  Ex.  24  [| Ex.  B];  and  Tr.  207-208).  They  there-
fore  have  the  particularized  personal  interest,  in  a  particu-
lar  project,  sufficient  to  grant  them  standing.  See  Warth  at
358-60  and  n.  17-18  and  cases  cited  therein.

3.  Petitioners’  assertion  that  the  decision  below  con-
flicts  with  those  of other  circuits  also  lacks  merit.  The  cases
that  do  deal  with  the  issue  at  bar  are  all  supportive  of  the
decision  below.

Petitioners  cite  English  v.  Town  of Huntington,  448  F.
2d  319  (2d  Cir.  19)  (Pet.  15  and  18)  for  the  proposition
that  enforcement  of  an  ordinance  having  a  greater  impact
upon  the  poor  does  not  render  the  ordinance  invalid  under
the  Equal  Protection  Clause.  Since  this  case  does  not  deal
with  economic  discrimination,  English  is  irrelevant.

(1975)

Construction  Industry  Association  of Sonoma  County  v.
City  of Petaluma,  ........  i
(Pet.  16-17),  is
similarly  irrelevant.  The  ordinance  in  question  was,  from
the  point  of  view  of  racial  minorities,  ‘‘inclusionary,’’
since,  by  giving  some  priority  to  developments  including
low-  and  moderate-income  housing,  it  ‘‘offers  new  oppor-
tunities,  previously  unavailable,  to  minorities  and  low-  and
moderate-income  persons.’’  (........  woe  OF  ........ ,  n.16).

Ybarra  v.  City  of the  Town  of Los  Altos  Hills,  503  F.2d
250  (9th  Cir.  1974),  (Pet.  16)  and  Mahaley  v.  Cuyahoga
Metropolitan  Housing  Authority,  500  F.  2d  1087  (6th  Cir.
1974),  (Pet.  17),  which  petitioners  cite  for  the  proposition
that  discrimination  on  the  basis  of  poverty  does  not  be-
come  discrimination  on  the  basis  of  race  because  of  a  sta-

12

13

tistical  correlation  between  race  and  poverty,  are  readily
distinguishable,  since  the  Seventh  Circuit  explicitly  accept-
ed  this  proposition  and  went  on  to  rule  for  respondents  on
the  desegregation  ground.*  Significantly,  unlike  the  case
at  bar,  the  Mahaley  court  emphasized  that  ‘‘[t]here  was
not  even  a  scintilla  of  evidence’’  that  the  defendant  munici-
palities  had  ever  been  asked  to  approve  any  subsidized
housing  projects.  (500  F.2d  at  1094)

However,  when  they  are  asked  to  do  so  and  where  there
is  residential  segregation,  the  circuits  have  unanimously
ruled  that  municipalities  may  not  obstruct  private  desegre-
gation  efforts.  U.S.  v.  City  of Black  Jack,  508  F.2d  1179,
1184  (8th  Cir.),  cert.  denied,  95  S.Ct.  2656  (1975)  (Under
Title  VIII,  ‘‘[t]he  discretion  of  local  zoning  officials  ..  .
must  be  curbed  where  ‘the  clear  result  of  such  discretion  is
the  segregation  of  low-income  Blacks  from  all  white  neigh-
borhoods.’  ’’)  (citations  omitted) ; United  Farmworkers  of
Florida  Housing  Project  v.  City  of Delray  Beach,  493  F.
2d  799,  811  (5th  Cir.  1974)  (ander  the  Fourteenth  Amend-
ment,  ‘‘.  .
.  in  the  realm  of  federally  assisted  housing,
local  authorities  have  an  obligation  to  further  the  na-
tional  housing  policy  of balanced  and  dispersed  public  hous-
ing  [including  low-  and  moderate-income  housing]  and  to
refrain  from  frustrating  efforts  to  carry  out  that  policy.’’)
(citations  omitted);  Kennedy  Park  Homes  Assn.  Ince.  v.

*It  should  be  noted  that  respondents  would  prevail  under  the
law  of  the  case  in  Ybarra,  since  the  court  ruled  that  a  claim  of  dis-
crimination  on  the  basis  of  poverty  would  be  sustained  if  plaintiffs
had  been  able  to  show  that  the  ordinance  prevented  them  from  living
in  the  municipality  and  that  there  was  inadequate  low-cost  housing
near  their  jobs.  (503  F.2d  at  254)  Respondents  made  both  showings
in  the  case  at  bar.

City  of Lackawanna,  436  F.2d  108  (2d  Cir.  1970),  cert.  de-
nied,  401  U.S.  1010  (1971).*

The  Second  Circuit  in  Acevedo  v.  Nassau  County,  500  F.
2d  1078  (1974),  a  case  petitioners  argued  is  inconsistent
with  the  decision  below  (Pet.  17),  did  not  challenge  cases,
including  Delray  Beach  and  Lackawanna,  which  enjoined
‘¢..  the  governmental  obstruction  of private  projects  bene-
ficial  to  minority  groups  or  to  integration’’  (500  F.2d  at
1081).  It simply  held  that  it would  not  impose  ‘‘an  affirma-
tive  duty  to  construct  housing.’’  (Jd.)

II.

The  Decision  Does  Not  Present  an  Important  Question
of  Federal  Law.

This  case  merely  involves  an  effort  to  prevent  one  mu-
nicipality  from  unlawfully  interfering  with  the  develop-
ment  of  one  housing  project  on  one  small  site  in  a  large
urbanized  area.  Contrary  to  petitioners’  assertions,  it does

*As  the  Second  Circuit  stated  in  Citizens  Committee  for  Faraday
Wood  v.  Lindsay,  507  F.2d  1065,  1069  (2d  Cir.  1974),  cert.  denied,
421  U.S.  948  (1975),

.
.  the  City  of  Lackawanna  was  clearly  segregated  ..
Against  this  background  we  held  that  the  city  could  not,  ab-
sent  a  compelling  interest,  thwart  the  efforts  of  a  private  or-
ganization  to  build  housing  for  low-income  families  in  the  white
area  of  the  city  by,  among  other  things,  refusing  to  accede  to
a  reasonable  rezoning  request.  Other  courts  have  reached  simi-
lar  results.

(Citations  omitted).

The  Lindsay  court  also  stated  that,  whereas  “in  the  cited  cases  a  city
acted  to  thwart  a  private  developer’s  attempt  to  construct  housing
for  low-income  persons”  and  in  Lackawanna  the  plaintiffs  “merely
ask[ed]  us  to  order  the  city  to  remove  barriers  to  private  devel-
opment,”  the  Lindsay  plaintiffs  were  asking  it  to  order  the  city  to
take  affirmative  steps  to  insure  that  the  project  in  dispute  would
be  built.

(Jd.)

14

not  present  issues  of  the  affirmative  duty  of  municipalities
to  construct  housing;  or  to  plan  for,  approve,  and  promote
housing  (Pet.  17);  or  ‘‘to  take  affirmative  steps  to  zone
for  racial  integration’’
(Pet.  8).  Any  injunction  issued
upon  remand  will  be  prohibitory;  no  mandatory  action  by
the  petitioners  is  required.  Questions  of  law  which  involve
the  affirmative  responsibilities  of municipal  officials  to  plan
for  or  construct  low-  and  moderate-income  housing  can  be
resolved  when  a  proper  case  raises  the  issue  before  this
Court.  Warth,  supra,  certainly  insures  that  such  an  issue
will  be  adjudicated  in  the  most  intense  adversarial  context
possible  and  that  petitioners  fear  of  impending  doom  is
groundless.

While  the  decision  below  may  have  a  prophylactic  effect
elsewhere,  exclusionary  zoning  litigation  involving  but  one
project  is always  limited  in  its  application  by a  plethora  of
planning,  housing,  and  environmental  facts  dealing  with
the  parcel  and  project  in issue.  Given  this,  pronouncements
of  general  principles  are  almost  impossible  and  certainly
unwise,  at  least  at this  point  in time  when  doctrinal  develop-
ment  is still  in its  infancy.  Thus,  the  Seventh  Circuit  wisely
refrained  from  pronouncing  a  broad  rule  of  law  and  limit-
ed  the  decision  to  ‘‘the  facts  of  this  case.’?  (Pet.  App.  A9-
A10)

Petitioners  would  insist  nevertheless  that  the  precedent
created  invalidates  ‘‘zoning  ordinances  ...  around  the
country.’’  (Pet.  8)  But

‘  ‘...  [petitioners’|  prophecy  of  high  universal  applica-
tion  of  a  constitutional  precept  so  peculiarly  depen-
dent  for  its  invocation  upon  appropriate  facts  fails  to
take
‘Differences
in  circumstances
[which]  beget  differences  in  law,’  Whitney  v.  State
Tax  Commission,  389  U.S.  530.’’  Burton  v.  Wilmington
Parking  Authority,  365  U.S.  715,  726  (1961)

account

into

15

ITI.

The  Decision  is  Correct

1.  Under  the  Equal  Protection  Clause,  a  municipality
cannot  obstruct  private  desegration  efforts,  Delray  Beach,
supra,  especially  if  within  the  context  of  segregation  and
private  discrimination  in  the  housing  market,  Lackawanna,
supra.

While  there  are  no  cases  of  this  Court  directly  on  point,
a  number  of  cases  are  highly  supportive  of  the  analysis
undertaken  by  the  Courts  of  Appeal.  It  is  axiomatic  that
municipalities  cannot  directly  foster  racial  segregation.
Buchanan  v.  Warley,  245  U.S.  60  (1917)  ; Harmon  v.  Tyler,
273  U.S.  668  (1926);  City  of Richmond  v.  Deans,  281  U.S.
704  (1930).  Since  the  measure  of  a  state’s  action  is  its
effect,  Wright  v.  City  of Emporia,  407  U.S.  451,  462  (1972),
any  action,  which,  absent  a  compelling  public  interest,  has
the  effect  of  fostering  such  segregation  must  fail.
It  is,
after  all,  ‘‘the  reality  [of]  the  law’s  impact’’  which  mat-
ters.  Hunter  v.  Erickson,  393  U.S.  385,  391  (1969).  That
reality  achieves  particular  significance  when  the  historical
context  is  one  of  private  housing  discrimination  and  when
the  ultimate  effect  of  the  state’s  action  is  to  permit  that
discrimination  to  continue.  Rettman  v.  Mulkey,  387  US.
369,  373  (1967).

Once  a  conclusion  of  racial  discrimination  is  drawn,  the
classification  must  be  justified  by  a  compelling  public  in-
terest.  As  the  Court  of  Appeals  determined  (Pet.  App.
A10-A11),  there  is no  such  compelling  public  interest  here.*

*  Petitioners’  repeated  assertion  that  the  zoning  ordinance  is  “ad-
“‘in

mittedly”  valid  by  all  traditional  zoning  criteria  and  that  it  is

(footnote  continued )

16

17

(Brief  for  Plaintiffs-Appellants

2.  Petitioners’  action  also  violated  the  Fair  Housing
Act  of  1968  (Title  VIII  of  the  Civil  Rights  Act  of  1968,  42
U.S.C.  $3601  et  seg.)  and  the  Civil  Rights  Act  of  1866  (42
U.S.C.  $1982).  Respondents  also  urged  these  grounds  be-
low.
and  Intervening
Plaintiffs-Appellants,  pp.  31-41)  Petitioners  have  violated
42  U.S.C.  $3604(a),  by  denying  housing  on  the  basis  of
race,  and  42  U.S.C.  §3617,  by  interfering  with  the  right
of  equal  housing  opportunity.  U.S.  v.  City  of Black  Jack,
508  F.  2d  at  1181.  They  aiso  have  violated  42  U.S.C.  $3604
(a),  by  otherwise  making  housing  unavailable  to  minori-
ties.

It  is  elemental  that  statutes  are  to  be  construed  so  as
to  effect  the  intent  of  the  lawmakers.  District  of Columbia
v.  Carter,  409  U.S.  418,  420  (1973).  Additionally,  ‘‘[i]t  is
well-established  that  civil  rights  statutes  should  be  read
expansively  in  order  to  fulfill  their  purposes.  See  Griffin
v.  Breckenridge,  403  U.S.  88,  97  (1971);  Daniel  v.  Paul
[395  U.S.  298  (1969)  ],’’  Mayers  v.  Ridley,  465  F.2d  630,
635  (D.C.  Cir.  1972)  (per  curiam)  (J.  Skelly  Wright,  J.,
eoncurring)  (Title  VIII  case).  Section  1982  must  be  simi-
larly  construed.  ‘‘A  narrow  construction  of  the  language  of
$1982  would  be  quite  inconsistent  with  the  broad  and
sweeping  nature  of  the  protection  meant  to  be  afforded
by  $1  of  the  Civil  Rights  Act  of  1866  [$1982].’’  Sullivan  v.
Inttle  Hunting  Park,  396  U.S.  229,  237  (1969).

(footnote  continued )

fact  conceded  that  the  ordinance  would  be  valid  if  the  ‘compelling
interest’  test  was  not  applied”  (Pet.  23)  is  an  astonishing  misstate-
ment.  Respondents  argued  at  length  in  the  Court  of  Appeals  that
they  ‘‘are  entitled  to  judgment  even  if  the  reasonableness  standard
of  traditional  zoning  cases  were  appropriate.”
(Reply  Brief  for
Plaintiffs-Appellants  and  Intervening  Plaintiffs-Appellants,  p.  27;
see  discussion  at  27-31).

Both  the  legislative  history  and  judicial  construction  of
Title  VIII  make  it clear  that  it was  designed  to  make  pos-
sible  the  eradication  of  the  social  evil  of  residential  segre-
gation  and  not  simply  to  prohibit  private  discrimination.

The  legislative  history  of  Title  VIII  consists  mainly  of
the  debates  on  the  floor  of  each  house.*  In  introducing  the
bill  initially,  Sen.  Mondale  indicted  segregated  housing
patterns  and  the  problems  they  have  caused—segregated
schools,  the  unavailability  of  suburban  employment  oppor-
tunities  and  the  alienation  of  white  and  black  Americans
due  to  ‘‘lack  of  experience  in  actually  living  next’’  to  each
114  Cong.  Rec.  2274-76.**  He  went  on  to  quote
other.
a  1967  report  by  the  U.S.  Commission  on  Civil  Rights
the  dimensions  of  the  problems  that  the
to  indicate
amendment  was  intended  to  solve.
That  report  noted
that,  in  part,  the  inability  of  blacks  to  secure  hous-
ing  in  suburbs  and  other  white  areas
‘‘stems  from
the  refusal  of  suburbs  and  other  communities  to  ac-
cept  low-income  housing’’  and  that  ‘‘an  important  factor
contributing  to  the  exclusion  of  the  Negro  from  such
areas’’  is  the  ‘‘policies  and  practices  of  agencies  of  gov-
ernment  at  all  levels.’’  114  Cong.  Ree.  2277.

The  Act  has  consistently  been  interpreted  along  these
lines.  As  this  Court  has  said  in  construing  Title  VIII,

*  See  U.S.  v.  Hunter,  459  F.  2d  205,  210,  n.4  (4th  Cir.  1972)

**  See  also,
Cong.  Rec.  3421
114  Cong.  Rec.  at  2279-80,  9559,  9591,  and  9590.
Brooke,  a  co-sponsor  of  the  measure,  declared:

e.g.,  further  discussion  by  Sen.  Mondale  at  114
and  similar  statements  by  other  sponsors  at
As  Sen.

...  [W]e  can  and  should  make  it  possible  for  those  who  can
to  move  to  where  better  schools  and  services,  decent  homes  and
jobs  are  most  plentiful.  That  is  the  simple  purpose  of  this  bill.

114  Cong.  Rec.  2280.

CONCLUSION

For  the  foregoing  reasons,  respondents  respectfully  sug-

gest  that  the  petition  be  denied.

Respectfully  submitted,

F.  Wiiuis  Caruso
Wuuiam  J.  McNatiy
GeraLp  J.  MULLER
Carnot  M.  PETERSEN
Rosert  G.  ScHWEMM

Attorneys  for  Respondents
and  Intervening  Respondents

Nov.  20,  1975.

18

quoting  Sen.  Mondale,  ‘‘the  reach  of  the  proposed  law  was
to  replace  the  ghettos  ‘by  truly  integrated  and  balanced
living  patterns,’  ’’  Trafficante  v.  Metropolitan  Life  Insur-
ance  Co.,  409  U.S.  205,  211  (1972).

Petitioners’  actions  are  in  conflict  with  MHDC’s  one
small  effort  to  bridge  the  ghetto  wall  and  create  an  inte-
grated  and  balanced  living  pattern.

Equity  in  addition  to  law  supports  the  decision  below.
It  is  eminently  fair.  It  gives  to  some  blacks  trapped  in  the
inner-city  the  opportunity  to  use  a  Congressional  program
to  live  in  a  decent  integrated  environment  where  employ-
ment  and  educational  opportunities  are  plentiful.

And  what  is  lost  in  the  process?  The  proposed  develop-
ment,  well-designed  housing,  of no  greater  height,  and  with
more  open  space  than  the  abutting  single-family  homes,  will
harmonize  with  the  neighborhood,  will  have  no  adverse  im-
pact  upon  municipal  facilities  and  services  and,  significant-
ly,  unlike  tax-exempt  public  housing,*  would  make  a  net
contribution  to  the  revenues  to  both  the  Village  and  the
school  districts  within  which  it  lies.  Lincoln  Green  is
clearly  in  the  public  interest.  Respondents  only  claim  of
loss  is a  private  interest—a  speculative  diminution  in  prop-
erty  values  of  some  Village  residents.  What  is  gained  for
those  Americans  long  victimized  by  racial  discrimination
in  all  its  forms  trivializes  the  Village’s  desperate  efforts
to  protect  its  insiders  who  refuse  to  share  their  public
world.

*  Compare  Valtierra,  402  U.S.  at  143.

OF  APPEALS  FOR  THE  SEVENTH  CIRCUIT.

PETITIONERS’  BRIEF.

OPINIONS  BELOW.

The  opinion  of  the  United  States  Court  of  Appeals  for  the
Seventh  Circuit  is  reported  in  517  F.  2d  409  and  appears  as
Appendix  A  to  the  Petition  for  Writ  of  Certiorari  filed  herein.
The  opinion  of the  United  States  District  Court  for  the  Northern
District  of  Illinois  is  reported  at  373  F.  Supp.  208  and  is
reprinted  as  Appendix  B  to  the  Petition  for  Writ  of  Certiorari
filed  herein.

JURISDICTION.

The  judgment  of  the  Court  of  Appeals  for  the  Seventh  Cir-
cuit  was  entered  on  June  10,  1975;  the  order  en  banc  denying
the  Petition  for  Rehearing  was  entered  on  August  13,  1975;
the  Petition  for  Writ  of  Certiorari  was  docketed  October  23,
1975;  the  Petition  for  Writ  of  Certiorari  was  granted  on  De-
cember  15,  1975.  The  Petition  for  Writ  of  Certiorari  was  filed
in  accordance  with  28  U.  S.  C.  § 2101.  The  jurisdiction  of  this
Court  is  invoked  pursuant  to  the  provisions  of  28  U.  S.  C.
§ 1254.

CONSTITUTIONAL  PROVISIONS  AND
STATUTES  INVOLVED.

This  case  involves  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  of  the  Con-
stitution  of  the  United  States;  42  U.  S.  C.  A.  §§  1981-1982,
the  Civil  Rights  Act  of  1866;  42  U.  S.C.  A.  § 1983,  the  Civil
Rights  Act  of  1871;  42  U.  S.  C.  A.  §§  3601,  et  seq.,  the  Fair
Housing  Act  of  1968.  The  relevant  provisions  are  set  forth  in
an  Appendix  to  this  brief.

QUESTIONS  PRESENTED  FOR  REVIEW.

1.  Does  the  failure  to  grant  a  rezoning  request  for  multi-
ple-family  housing  for  low-  and  moderate-income  families  in  the
midst  of  a  single-family  area  violate  the  Fourteenth  Amend-
ment,  even  though  the  Village  was  admittedly  maintaining  the
integrity  of  its  zoning  plan  and  protecting  neighboring  property
values?

2.

Does  an  alleged  discriminatory  housing  pattern  in  the
Chicago  metropolitan  area  impose  upon  a  suburban  municipal-
ity  an  affirmative  duty  to  ignore  its  admittedly  proper  zoning
ordinance  in  order  to  permit  the  construction  of  multi-family
low-  and  moderate-income  housing?

3

3.

Is  housing  a  fundamental  constitutional  right  which  re-

quires  the  application  of  the  “compelling  interest”  test?

4.

Did  the  respondents  overcome  the  presumption  of validity
attaching  to  the  zoning  ordinance  of  the  Village  of  Arlington
Heights?

5.

Did  the  respondents  have  sufficient  standing  to  litigate

the  merits  of  the  zoning  ordinance?

STATEMENT  OF  THE  CASE.

The  respondent,  Metropolitan  Housing  Development  Corpora-
tion,  is a  not-for-profit  corporation  organized  for  the  purpose  of
developing  low  and  moderate  income  housing  in  the  Chicago
metropolitan  area.  It  has  a  99  year  lease  and  sale  agreement
with  the  owners  of  the  subject  property,  which  is  a  vacant  15
acre  tract  on  the  southeast  corner  of  an  80  acre  parcel  owned
by  the  Clerics  of  St.  Viator  who  maintain  a  boys  high  school
and  a  novitiate  on  the  larger  tract  in  the  Village  of  Arlington
Heights.  The  petitioners  are  the  Village  and  the  Village  Trus-
tees.
The  contract  and  lease  under  which  the  respondent
claims  an  interest  in  the  property  is  subject  to  rezoning  for
multiple-family  purposes  and  the  securing  of  a  Section  236
financing  commitment.  The  owners  of  the  property  are  not
parties  to  the  lawsuit.

The  subject  property  is zoned  for  R-3  One-Family  Dwelling
District  under  the  Zoning  Ordinance  of  the  petitioners,  Village,
and  has  been  zoned  for  single-family  use  since  the  first  Village
Zoning  Ordinance.  Under  the  Village  Comprehensive  Plan
adopted  in  1959,  the  property  was  designated  for  single-family
use.  Under  the  R-3  classification,  property  can  be  developed
for  single-family  detached  dwellings  at  a density  of 8,750  square
feet  of  lot  area  per  dwelling  unit.  Approximately  50  single-
family  units  can  be  placed  upon  the  subject  property.  The  pur-
chase  price  of  the  subject  property  is  $300,000.

4

5

The  respondents  propose  to  develop  the  land  for  190  town-
houses  which  were  to  be  financed  pursuant  to  Section  236  of
the  National  Housing  and  Urban  Development  Act  of  1968.
All  of  the  land  surrounding  the  subject  property  is  zoned  and
developed  for  R-3  single-family  use.
A map  showing  the  existing
land  use  and  zoning  and  photographs  of the  development  of  the
subject  property  and  the  surrounding  area,  which  were  received
in  evidence,  appear  in  the  Appendix  Exhibit  Volume  (Def.  Ex.
1  R.  693,  809,  Def.  Ex.  2 R.  700,  809,  Def.  Ex.  14,  R.  704,
809).

Respondent  applied  for  a  zoning  change  to  the  R-5  multiple-
family  classification.  After  public  hearings  the  Plan  Commission
recommended  against  the  proposed  rezoning  and  the  Board  of
Trustees  of  the  Village  voted  6  to  1  to  deny  the  requested
rezoning.  Suit  was  then  instituted  by  the  MHDC,  corporation,
and  certain  individual  plaintiffs  seeking  to  represent  moderate
income  minority  persons  who  claimed  to  seek  housing  in Arling-
ton  Heights.  A Mexican-American  resident  of Arlington  Heights
and  a  local  community  organization,  the  Northwest  Opportunity
Center,  intervened.

At  the  time  of the  decision  of  the  Village  Board  in  September
of  1971,  the  Village  had  5,239  existing  multiple-family  units.  In
addition  to  that,  it had  already  zoned  vacant  areas  for  multiple-
family  use  which  would  provide  an  additional  9,073  multiple-
family  units.  Since  1962  the  Village  had  considered  34  addi-
tional  requests  for  R-5  rezoning  that  were  not  granted  (R.  709).
The  Village  had  adopted  a  policy  of  utilizing  R-5  multiple-
family  zoning  districts  as  buffers  between  single-family  zones  and
commercial,  industrial  and  other  high-intensity  areas  (R.  770).

After  the  denial  by the  Village  of the  Lincoln  Green  applica-
tion,  the  Village  Manager  cooperated  with  respondents’  Director
of  Real  Estate  in  searching  out  other  sites.  Fourteen  alternate
sites  in the  Village  were  investigated;  nine  of them  were  rejected
because  they  were  too  far  removed  from  public  facilities  or  were

surrounded  by single-family  areas;  six  other  sites  were  rejected
because  the  respondent  MHDC  thought  them  too  expensive.

The  corporate  respondent  had  previously  developed  a 212  unit
low  and  moderate  income  multiple-family  project,  Rand  Grove
Village,  in  an  unincorporated  area  three-fourths  of  a  mile  out-
side  the  corporate  limits  of  the  Village.  This  site  was  one  mile
away  from  the  Honeywell  factory  which  is  the  principal  em-
ployer  of  minority  persons  in  the  Village.  The  Lincoln  Green
site  sought  here  was  six  miles  removed  from  the  Honeywell  site.
The  nearest  shopping  center  and  school  to  Rand  Grove  is
located  two  miles  away,  notwithstanding  the  fact  that  some
sites  in  Arlington  Heights  were  rejected  because  they  were  a
mile  or  a  mile  and  a  quarter  away  from  shopping  facilities.

The  1970  population  of  the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights
was  64,800.  Based  on  the  1970  census  data,  the  respondents’

evidence  showed  that  3,244  blacks  could  afford  to  live  in  the
Village  (R.  588,  593).  The  respondents’  demographer  testified
that  378  black  households  could  buy  housing  in  Arlington
Heights,  and  473  black  households  could  rent  housing  in
Arlington  Heights  based  on  economic  data  revealed  by  the
census.  He  further  testified  that  2,500  blacks,  who  based  upon
the  census  data  would  be  eligible  for  Lincoln  Green  housing,
were  also  eligible  for  other  housing  in  the  Village  (R.  595).

The  respondents  had  made  no  survey  of  housing  preferences
of blacks  employed  in Arlington  Heights,  nor  had  they  made  an
investigation  of  the  number  of  blacks  or  Spanish  speaking  per-
sons  in  Arlington  Heights.  Their  evidence  showed  that  Arlins-
ton  Heights  had  the  least  industry  of  the  surrounding  com-
munities  (R.  629),  and  their  expert  characterized  Arlington
Heights  as  a  dormitory  suburb  (R.  631).

The  director  of the  intervenor  Northwest  Opportunity  Center,
testified  that  there  were  188  families  in  a  category  of  low  or
poverty  income  living  in  the  Village,  and  they  lived  in  a
variety  of  housing  (R.  481).  The  Village  had  financially  sup-

6

7

ported  the  intervenor  in  1971-72  and  1972-73.  Some  of  the
families  they  served  had  incomes  too  low  to  qualify  for  Lincoln
Green.  The  organization  served  1,500  families  in  the  seven
township  area.

In August  of  1973,  there  were  14,444  owner-occupied  single-
family  homes  and  6,085  multiple-family  units  in  Arlington
Heights.  At  the  time  of  the  MHDC  zoning  hearing,  there  was
land  zoned  but  not  developed  for  a  total  of  9,073  multiple-
family  dwelling  units.  The  vacancy  rate  for  apartments  in
Arlington  Heights  according  to  the  1970  census  was  11.5%
(R.  798).  At  the  time  of  the  hearing  before  the  Plan  Com-
mission,  at  least  seven  parcels  which  were  zoned  for  R-5
multiple-family  purposes,  were  for  sale  (R.  822).

The  petitioner’s  evidence  established  the  single-family  char-
acter  of  the  area  in  question  and  the  consistent  Village  policy
of  utilizing  the  R-5  classification  as  a  buffer  between  single-
family  use  and  commercial  or  industrial  uses  The  subject  prop-
erty  would  not  constitute  such  a  buffer.

The  petitioner’s  expert  testimony  indicated  that  the  proposed
development  would  have  an  adverse  effect  on  adjacent  prop-
erty  owners  (R.  788,  793),  and  would  have  a  detrimental
effect  on  the  value  of single-family  homes  in  the  area  (R.  931).

The  petitioners  also  introduced  evidence  showing  that  the
subject  property  could  be  economically  developed  for  single-
family  homes  at  a  price  range  comparable  or  higher  than  the
existing  single-family  homes  in  the  area  (R.  929).

The  respondent’s  own  witnesses  conceded  that  the  subject
property  was  suitable  for  single-family  development  on  the  un-
subsidized  market  (R.  74),  and  that  the  property  was  worth  at
least  $375,000  under  the  single-family  classification  (R.  471).

The  trial  court  found  that  the  respondents  failed  to  carry  their
burden  of  proving  discrimination  by  petitioners  against  racial
minorities,  373  F.  Supp.  208,  210.  The  Court  pointed  out  that
the  weight  of  the  evidence  proves  that  the  petitioners  were

motivated  by a  legitimate  desire  to  protect  property  values  and
the  integrity  of the  Village’s  zoning  plan,  373  F.  Supp.  208,  211.
It  further  found  that  low  income  workers  do  not  have  a  con-
stitutional  right  to  low  rental  housing  either  where  they  work  or
elsewhere.  The  Court  concluded  that  the  zoning  decision  was
not  an  arbitrary  or  capricious  act  in violation  of  the  Fourteenth
Amendment.

The  Court  of  Appeals  reversed  the  trial  court,  but  in  doing
so  generally  sustained  the  findings  of  the  trial  judge.  The  Dis-
trict  Court  had  found  that  the  claim  of  a  denial  of  equal  pro-
tection  had  not  been  proven  and  the  Court  of  Appeals  declined
to  hold  that  that  finding  was  clearly  erroneous,  517  F.  2d  409,
412.  The  Court  of  Appeals  also  held  that  the  District  Court’s
finding  that  the  petitioners  were  concerned  with  the  integrity
of  the  Village’s  zoning  plan  was  not  erroneous,  517  F.  2d  409,
412.

In  reviewing  evidence  of  other  requested  zoning  changes  in
the  Village,  the  Court  of  Appeals  found  that  the  evidence  did
not  necessitate  a  finding  that  the  Village  was  administering  its
buffer  zone  policy  in  a  discriminatory  manner.  The  Court  of
Appeals  further  recognized  that  merely  because  racial  minorities
constituted  a  higher  percentage  of the  low  and  moderate  income
category,  this  disparity  did  not  necessarily  mean  that  the  zoning
change  refusal  had  the  type  of racially  discriminatory  effect  that
required  the  invocation  of  the  compelling  interest  test,  recog-
nizing  the  impact  of  this  Court’s  decision  in James  v.  Valtierra,
402  U.  S.  137,  28  L.  ed.  2d  678  (1971).

The  Court  of  Appeals  also  noted  that,  based  on  economic
factors,  blacks  would  occupy  five  per  cent  of  the  housing  in
Arlington  Heights,  517  F.  2d  409,  414,  and  conceded  that  the
Village  had  not  created  the  segregated  housing  pattern  which

the  Court  said  existed  in the  entire  Chicago  area,  517  F.  2d  409,
414.

Notwithstanding  these  findings  which  were  clearly  indicated
by the  record,  the  Court  of  Appeals  held  that,  under  the  facts

8

>

of  the  case,  the  Village’s  rejection  of  the  Lincoln  Green  project
had  racially  discriminatory  effects.
It  applied  the  compelling
interest  test  and  concluded  that  the  planning  rationale  behind
the  buffer  zone  policy  and  the  fact  that  neighboring  property
might  be  diminished  in  value  could  not  meet  the  compelling
interest  test.  It concluded  that  since  no  compelling  justification
for  the  decision  existed,  the  refusal  to  rezone  was  a  violation  of
the  equal  protection  clause  of  the  Fourteenth  Amendment.

Chief  Judge  Fairchild  dissented,  noting  at  the  time  of  the
Village’s  decision,  there  were  at  least  nine  undeveloped  tracts
of  land  in  excess  of  15  acres  zoned  for  R-5  multiple-family  use,
and  that  the  record  did  not  contain  a sufficient  showing  that  it
was  not  reasonably  possible  to  construct  the  proposed  project
on  one  of  these  sites,  517  F.  2d  409,  416.

SUMMARY  OF  ARGUMENT.

I,

The  zoning  action  complained  of  herein  was  undertaken  by a
legitimate  desire  to  protect  the  integrity  of  the  Village’s  zoning
plan.  It was  predicated  solely  and  completely  upon  sound  zoning
principles.  The  area  in  question  is  in  the  midst  of  a  well  estab-
lished  and  uniform  single-family  area.  The  Village  had  more
than  5,000  existing  multiple-family  units  at  the  time  of  the  zon-
ing  application  and  had  zoned  other  areas  for  an  additional
9,000  multiple-family  units.

There  were  a  substantial  number  of  low  income  families
residing  in  Arlington  Heights  and  the  census  figures  indicated
that  substantial  numbers  of  blacks  could  afford  to  move  to
Arlington  Heights.  The  zoning  pattern  of  the  Village  was  not
conceived  and  did  not  operate  to  exclude  black  residents  or  low
income  families.  The  Village  has  consistently  applied  its  policy
of  utilizing  R-5  Multiple-Family  Districts  for  buffer  zone  pur-
poses.  The  mere  fact  that  some  blacks  or  Spanish  speaking

persons,  like  whites,  could  not  afford  to  live  in  single-family
homes  on  the  subject  property  does  not  require  the  Village  to
abandon  its  long  established  and  rational  zoning  plan.

The  trial  court  and  the  Court  of  Appeals  found  that  the
Village  zoning  policy  was  not  administered  in  a  discriminatory
manner  and  that  the  Village  was  concerned  with  the  integrity  of
its  zoning  plan.  These  findings  were  supported  by  the  evidence
and  should  not  be  disturbed.

The  fact  that  the  Zoning  Ordinance  might  have  a  greater
impact  upon  poor  people  or  a minority  group  does  not  invalidate
it. The  fact  that  the  Ordinance  might  exclude  poor  persons  from
residing  on  the  subject  property  does  not  violate  the  Constitu-
tion,  the  Fair  Housing  Law,  or  the  Civil  Rights  Acts.  There  is
no  fundamental  constitutional  right  to  housing,  and  the  Court  of
Appeals  erred  in  applying  the  compelling  interest  test.

II.

A  zoning  ordinance  is presumed  valid  and  the  parties  attack-
ing  it  must  show  by  clear  and  convincing  evidence  that  it  is
invalid.  A  court  will  not  substitute  its  judgment  for  that  of  the
legislative  body  and  federal  courts  do  not  sit  as  super  zoning
boards.

The  ordinance  in  question  is reasonable  and  is not  on  its  face
or  in fact  aimed  at  any  racial  or  economic  minority.  The  reten-
tion  of  a  single-family  classification  on  the  subject  property  is  a
legitimate  and  valid  exercise  of  the  legislative  discretion  vested
in  the  Village  Board.  There  is substantial  evidence  in  the  record
to  indicate  that  the  proposed  development  would  have  a  detri-
mental  effect  upon  nearby  properties.  The  zoning  is  consistent
with  the  established  land  uses  in  the  area  and  the  subject  prop-
erty  is  suitable  for  the  purposes  zoned.

10

Iii.

The  respondents  lack  standing  to  bring  this  action.  It is not  a
proper  class  action.  The  individual  respondents  have  voluntarily
resided  in other  communities  and  have  not  shown  themselves  to
be  in  a  class  which  was  discriminated  against  by  the  Village.
The  individual  intervenor  already  resides  in  Arlington  Heights.
The  corporate  respondent  is  a  mere  contract  purchaser  whose
interest  is dependent  upon  rezoning  and  therefore  is rfot  entitled
to  challenge  the  validity  of  the  ordinance,  particularly  in  the
absence  of the  property  owner  as a party  plaintiff;  as  a  corpora-
tion,  it has  not  been  subject  to  racial  discrimination.

The  judgment  of the  Court  of Appeals  for  the  Seventh  Circuit

should  be  reversed  and  the  decision  of  the  trial  court  affirmed.

1]

ARGUMENT.

I.

THE  FAILURE  TO  GRANT  THE  REZONING  REQUEST  OF
THE  RESPONDENT  DID  NOT  VIOLATE  THE  CIVIL
RIGHTS  ACTS,  THE  FAIR  HOUSING  LAW,  OR  THE
FOURTEENTH  AMENDMENT.

This  is  an  action  challenging  the  Zoning  Ordinance  of  the
Village  of  Arlington  Heights  as  it  applies  to  a  15  acre  vacant
tract  of land  zoned  for R-3  single-family  use.  The  tract  is located
in the  heart  of  a  single-family  area  which  is also  zoned  for  R-3
single-family  use  and  is totally  developed  for single-family  homes,
parks,  schools  and  churches,  except  for  additional  vacant  land
held  by the  owners  of the  subject  property  in conjunction  with  a
boys  high  school  and a clerical  novitiate.

The  Village  of  Arlington  Heights,  as  both  the  trial  court  and
the  Court  of Appeals  recognized,  refused  to  rezone  the  property
to  R-5  multiple-family  purposes  because  the  petitioners  were
motivated  by a legitimate  desire  to  protect  the  integrity  of  the
Village’s  zoning  plan.  The  action  of  the  Village,  as  the  trial
court  found,  was  predicated  solely  and  completely  upon  sound
zoning  principles  pursuant  to  the  police  power  granted  to  the
Village.  The  Court  of  Appeals  refused  to  ascribe  any  racial
motivation  to  the  Village’s  action.

The  respondents  attempted  to  transform  a  simple  zoning  case
into  a  civil  rights  action.  They  have  attempted  to  raise  issues  of
