eral  government  provides  non-profit  corporations  like
MHDC  with  subsidies  in  the  form  of  reduced  finance
charges  for  the  construction  of  rental  units.*  The  savings
are  passed  on  to  the  apartment  residents  in  the  form  of  re-
duced  rents,  which  are  approximately  two-thirds  of  the
rents  charged  in comparable  commercial  properties.  To  in-
sure  that  the  program  benefits  low  and  moderate  income
persons,  prospective  tenants  must  meet  certain  eligibility
requirements  based  on  their  income  and  family  size  that
are  established  specifically  by HUD  (Tr.  43-44;  Ps.  Ex.  24).
For  example,  the  established  income  limitation  at  the  time
for  a  one-person  family  was  $7,250  per  year;  for  three-and-
four-person  families,  $10,350;  for  five-and-six  person  fami-
lies,  $11,950;  and  for  families  of  seven  or  more,  $13,450
(td).

The  Lincoln  Green  Proposal:  Location,  Architecture,  and
Land  Planning.

The  15  acre  site  chosen  for  Lincoln  Green  is bounded  on
the  north  and  west  by undeveloped  St.  Viator  property,  on
the  east  by  single  family  homes,  and  on  the  south  by  En-
clid  Avenue,  across  which  are  single-family  homes  (517
F.2d  409,  411).**  It  is  well  located  with  respect  to  the
central  business  district  of  Arlington  Heights  and  is
readily  accessible  to  schools,  shopping  and  recreation  fa-
cilities,
and  employment  opportunities
(Tr.  360-63,  394;  Ps.  Ex.  11,  pp.  4-5  and  Ps.  Ex.  53).

transportation,

*In  1974  Congress  passed  the  Housing  and  Community  De-
velopment  Act  which  provides  subsidies  for  rents  under  Section  8
Pub.  L.  No.  93-383;  88  Stat.  633;  42  U.S.C.  $§5301
of  the  act.
ef  seq.

**  Thus,  while  the  entire  Viatorian  property,  including  the  three
story  novitiate  communal  living  facility  and  high  school,  has  always
been  zoned  “R-3”,  much  of  it  is  vacant  and  petitioners’  assertion
that  all  the  land  surrounding  the  subject  property  is  zoned  and  de-
veloped  for  R-3  single-family  use  (Pet  Br.  4)  is  wrong.

After  the  contracts  for  the  Viatorian  land  had  been
executed,  MHDC  assembled  a  group  of  experts,  including
an  architect,  engineer,  planners,  and  other  housing  con-
sultants,  to  design  a  development  for  that  site  (Tr.  235).
Many  of  those  involved  were  chosen  because  they  had
special  expertise  in  designing  and  planning  high  quality
housing  for  low  and  moderate  income  persons  (Tr.  235-36,
342-46).

The  architectural  plans  for  Lincoln  Green  (Ps.  Ex.  7)
called  for  190  units  in  clusters  of  single-family  attached
townhouses  of  two  stories  or  less  with  separate  entrances
and  private  patios,  designed  to  harmonize  with  the  de-
sirable  features  of  the  landscape  and  the  single-family
homes  to  the  east  (Tr.  236-39;  Ps.  Ex.  11,  p.  5;  App.  I 66-
67).*  The  development  was  to  be  screened  or  ‘‘buffered,’’
from  the  adjacent  homes  by  trees  and  plants  (App.  I 66-
67),  and  more  than  60°¢  of  the  property  was  to  be  main-
tained  as  open  space,  with  the  Lincoln  Green  townhouses
accounting  for  a  relatively  smaller  Jand  coverage  than  the
single-family  houses  to  the  east  (/d.;  Ps.  Ex.  11,  p.  7;  Tr.
377-78).  Although  Arlington  Heights’  multi-family  zon-
ing  classification  (‘‘R-5’’)  would  permit  17  units  to  the
acre  (Tr.  878),  the  190  townhouses  planned  for  Lincoln
Green  amounted  to  less  than  13  units  to  the  acre  (App.  I
66-67).

Physically,  the  Lincoln  Green  units  were  ‘‘in  many  re-

Although  similar  to  neighboring  houses  in  all  material,
physical  respects,  the  Lincoln  Green  proposal  could  not
be  built  unless  it was  zoned  for  multi-family  purposes.  As
two  members  of  the  Arlington  Heights  Plan  Commission
noted:

In  the  Village  Code,  there  is  no  separate  classifica-
tion  for  townhouse  development.
Therefore,  it  of
necessity  accepts  the  classification  of  R-5.  The  pro-
posal  in  no  way  approaches  the  density,  architecture,
or  other  characteristics  of  R-5.  Professional  planners
designate  townhouse  construction  of  this  nature  akin
to  R-3  rather  than  R-5.  The  Lincoln  Green  proposal
suffers  from  the  lack  of  a  separate  classification  in
our  ordinance  (Ps.  Ex.  438,  p.  4).

Among  the  experts  assembled  by  MHDC  to  work  on  the
Lincoln  Green  proposal  was  Norman  Drummond,  a  pro-
fessional  land  planning  consultant  with  special  experience
in  developing  subsidized  housing  (App.  I  66-67;  Tr.  240,
343-46).  Mr.  Drummond  prepared  a  series  of  planning
and  zoning  reports  on  the  proposed  development  (Ps.  Ex.
11,  12,  31,  32,  33  and  34),  the  principal  conclusions  of

*  The  map  of  the  area  introduced  as  an  exhibit  shows  graphically

coverage.  There  would  in  fact  be  much  more  open  space  in  Lincoln

which  were  that  the  15  acre  parcel  was  best  suited  for
R-5  zoning  and  that  Lincoln  Green  was  needed  in  Arling-
ton  Heights  and  was  compatible  with  the  single-family
homes  to  the  east  and  the  overall  zoning  and  apartment
policies  of  Arlington  Heights.

To  determine  what  impact  Lincoln  Green  would  have
on  the  revenues  and  expenditures  of  Arlington  Heights
and  its  schools,  a  tax  impact  study  was  prepared  by
MHDC  (Tr.  243-47).  As  church  land,  of  course,  the  Via-
torian  property  had  not  previously  been  subject  to  taxa-
tion  at  all.  The  conclusions  of  the  study  were  that  the
proposed  development  would  make  a  net  centribution  to
the  Village  and  to  the  Arlington  Heights  schools  (517
F.2d  409,  410,  411,  Ps.  Ex.  26,  p.  5, Ps.  Ex.  17  at  3  and
4).*

MHDC  also  contracted  with  Barton-Ashman  Associates,
Inc.,  a  consulting  firm  specializing  in  traffic  and  urban
planning,  to  do  a  study  of  the  traffic  and  related  problems
that  might  be  generated  by Lincoln  Green  (Tr.  239).  That
study  (Ps.  Ex.  13)  was  complete’)  in  March  1971  and  con-
cluded  that  ‘‘the  proposed  development  is  expected  to
have  a  relatively  minor  impact  on  traffic  in  the  vicinity  of
the  site.’’  (id.,  p.  iv).  There  would  be  better  traffic  con-
trol  within  the  development  than  could  be  provided  in  a
single-family  subdivision  (Tr.  380-81).

*  Per-pupil  revenues  generated  by  Lincoln  Green  would  be  great-
er  than  if  the  site  were  developed  for  single-family  use,  and  school
officials  testified  that  the  MHDC  proposal  would  cause  no  serious
difficulty  in  terms  of  school  capacity  (Ps.  Ex.  17  at  5-6).  In  addi-
tion,  reports  by  Arlington  Heights  officials  showed  the  adequacy  of
water  and  sewer  facilities  and  fire  protection  (Pl.  Ex.  15),  and
there  would  be  no  significant  increase  in  the  use  of  neighborhood
parks  and  playgrounds  because  of  the  play  areas  and  substantial
amounts  of  open  space  retained  on  the  site.

MHDC  had  consulted  with  the  staff  of  the  Village  for

The  Need  for  Low  and  Moderate  Income  Housing
and the  Availability  of  Market  Housing

In  addition  to  being  designed  and  planned  in  the  most
professional  manner,  the  190  units  to  be  provided  by  Lin-
coln  Green  would  begin  to  respond  to  a  desperate  and
growing  need  for  low  and  moderate  income  housing,  espe-
cially  for  non-white  persons,  in  Arlington  Heights  and  the
surrounding  northwest  Cook  County  area.
Arlington
Heights  has  recognized  this  need.**  Indeed,  MHDC’s  de-
cision  to  develop  subsidized  housing  on  the  Viatorian

*  For  example,  when  some  concern  was  expressed  regarding  sub-
sequent  control  and  management  of  the  development,  the  Board  of
Directors  of  MHDC  secured  offers  to  manage  Lincoln  Green  from
leading  real  estate  firms  and  passed  resolutions  confirming  that
MHDC  would  retain  permanent  control  of  the  property  and  that  a
citizens’  advisory  board  selected  by  the  Mayor  of  Arlington  Heights
would  participate  in  its  planning  and  management  (App.  I  68;
Ps.  Ex.  20  and  35;  see  also  Ps.  Ex.  21,  22,  and  27).

**  Petitioners  have  recommended  development  of  150  to  250  units
of  moderate  income  housing  and  50  units  of  low  income  housing  in
Arlington  Heights  (App.  I  68).  At  the  public  hearings,  even  the
principal  spokesmen  opposing  Lincoln  Green  conceded  that  there
was  a  need  in  Arlington  Heights  for  such  a  development  (Ps.  Ex.
43,  pp.  3-4).

property  was  based  on  studies  confirming  the  fact  that
the  tremendous  recent  growth  in  population  and  employ-
ment  in  the  northwest  suburbs  of  Chicago  had  created  a
strong  demand  there  for  moderate  priced  and  lower
priced  housing  (Tr.  308).*  As  the  Court  of  Appeals  noted,
Lincoln  Green  ‘‘would  have  been  the  only  subsidized  hous-
ing  in  Arlington  Heights  despite  a  great  demand  for  such
housing  in  that  area’’  (517  F.2d  409,  411).

Petitioners  have,  however,  rezoned  60  parcels  of  land
in  Arlington  Heights  for  large  commercial  multi-family
projects  (e.g.,  Ds.  Ex.  10  and  11),  53  of  which  are  adja-
cent  on  at  least  one  side  to  single-family  homes  (Ps.  Ex.
12  and  52).  As  a  result,  there  were  some  5,239  market-
rent  apartments  in  Arlington  Heights  in  1970  (Tr.  692);
zoning  existed  for  thousands  more  (Pet.  Br.  4, 6).**  How-

Residential  Segregation  in  the  Arlington  Heights  Area

As  the  Court  of  Appeals  noted,  there  is  a  high  degree
of  racial  segregation  in  the  Chicago  metropolitan  area.
Specifically,  the  massive  growth  in  population  in  Arling-
ton  Heights  in  the  past  two  decades*  has,  with  rare  ex-
ceptions,  been  limited  to  whites.  Of  Arlington  Heights
1970  population  of 64,884,  27,  or  less  than  0.1  percent,  were
black  (App.  I  68;  Tr.  549-50,  597).  Arlington  Heights  is
the  most  residentially  segregated  community  and  has  the
most  racially  exclusionary  housing  stock  in  the  Chicago
metropolitan  area  among  the  municipalities  with  more
than  50,000  residents  (Ps.  Ex.  50,  Tr.  575).**

*  Arlington  Heights’  population  in  1950  was  8,768  (App.  I  68)
and  in  1960  was  27,878  (App.  I 68).  At  the  trial  in  January  1974
the  Village  Planner  estimated  the  Village’s  population  to  be  72,000
(Tr.  692).

total

(Footnote  continued)

A  similar  pattern  of  growing  residential  segregation
has  developed  in  the  four-township  northwest  Cook  Coun-
ty area  of  which  Arlington  Heights  is  a  part.  From  1960
to  1970,  the  population  of  this  area  increased  by  219,000
people,  only  170  of  whom  were  black;  the  percentage  of
blacks  in  the  area  actually  fell  (Tr.  533-4,  606).*

The  virtually  all  white  growth  patterns  of  Arlington
Heights  and  its  surrounding  area  contrast  dramatically
with  the  figures  for  the  overall  Chicago  metropolitan  area,
in  which  the  number  and  percentage  of  blacks  rose  sub-
stantially  during  the  1960’s  (Tr.  554-55).
While  their
numbers  grew,  however,  almost  all  minority  persons  in
the  Chicago  area  were  still  confined  to  segregated  Chicago
neighborhoods  in  1970  (Tr.  545-46).

As  the  Court  of  Appeals  observed,  the  devastating  im-
pact  on  minorities  reflected  by these  statistics  can  be  fully
appreciated  only  in  the  context  of  the  shift  in  employment

(Footnote  continued)

sure  the  importance  of  type  and  cost  of  housing  in  explaining  the
incurable  segregation  of  blacks  in  the  metropolitan  area.”
(Tr.
607,  608)  What  it does  is show  that  if there  had  been  no  racial  dis-
crimination  in  Arlington  Heights  and  if the  housing  choice  of  blacks
were  based  on  cost  alone,  5%  of  the  existing  housing  stock  of  the
Village  would  be  occupied  by blacks  (Tr.  583),  a  figure  lower  than
any  of  the  other  cities  in  the  SMSA,  but  nevertheless  an  increase  of
over  100  times  the  present  number  of  black  residents  in  the  Vil-
lage  (Ps.  Ex.

*  Most  of  these  black  workers  (108),  like  Respondent  Arthur

opportunities  during  the  same  period.  While  the  City  of
Chicago  lost  230,000  jobs,  the  number  of  jobs  in  the  four-
township  Arlington  Heights  area  rose  from  100,000  to
200,000  (517  F.2d  409,  417  fn.  2).

As  job  opportunities  have  moved  out  of  Chicago,  the
difficulties  of  blacks  in  finding  and  holding  these  jobs  have
increased  (See  Ps.  Ex.  44  and  46;  Tr.  504).  In  1970,  only
137  of  the  13,000  people  who  worked  in  Arlington  Heights
were  black,  reflecting  the  fact  that  black  workers  cannot
find  affordable  housing  there.*

The  relocation  of  jobs  to  Arlington  Heights  and  the
severe  problems  created  because  of  the  segregated  hous-
ing  market  for  both  minority  workers  and  firms  seeking
to  hire  them  have  been  documented  in  a  number  of  re-
ports,  including  a  study  issued  by  the  Cook  County  Office
of  Economic  Opportunity,  at  the  time  of  the  first  hearings
on  Lincoln  Green  (Ps.  Ex.  44).  As  the  Court  of  Appeals
noted,  this  study  confirmed  that  almost  all  of  the  black
workers  in  Arlington  Heights  resided  in  Chicago  and  that
the  main  problem  Arlington  Heights’  employers  faced
in  hiring  minorities  was  the  lack  of  adequate  housing
within  a  reasonable  distance  of  their  plants  (517  F.2d  416,
417).*

*  Among  the  major  conclusions  reached  by  this  OEO  study  on

Arlington  Heights  were  (Ps.  Ex.  44,  pp.  12-14):

each  day  traveling  to  and  from  work...  .
There  is  no  public  transportation  linking  suburban
communities  and,  therefore,  minorities  that  live  in  the

The  Effect  on  Minorities  of  the  Rejection  of  the  MHDC
Development

The  effect  of  petitioners’  refusal  to  permit  construction
of Lincoln  Green  will  be  to  maintain  the  virtually  all-white
housing  patterns  of  Arlington  Heights  and  deprive  non-
white  citizens  who  are  precluded  from  living  in  the  project
of  housing  and  employment  opportunities  (see,  e.g.,  Tr.
292-96,  480-80,  500-03,  504-06,  591-93;  Ps.  Ex.  44,  45  and
50).  The  record  established  that  blacks,  who  make  up  18%
of  the  population  of  the  Chicago  metropolitan  area,  con-
stitute  40%  of  those  eligible  for  Lincoln  Green  (Tr.  554-
55,  592-93),  and  that  MHDC’s  affirmative  marketing  ef-
forts  will  attract  substantial  numbers  of  blacks  to  Lincoln

(Footnote  continued)

minorities  working  in  Arlington  Heights  have  many
problems  and  this  situation  would  discourage  other
minorities  from  seeking  employment  in  the  area.
These  factors  must  also  contribute  to  the  high  turn-
over  rate  which  many  employers  reported.  .

.

.

9.

Based  on  the  information  gathered  concerning  incomes,  time
and  costs  of  minorities,  we  believe  there  is  a  definite  need
for  low  and  moderate  income  housing  in  the  Arlington
Heights  area...  .

Considering  their  incomes,  many  of  the  minorities  working  in  Ar-
lington  Heights  would  be  eligible  for  low  or  moderate  income  hous-
ing.  The  housing  problem  is  one  of  the  greatest  obstacles  confront-
ing  disadvantaged  people  in  their  efforts  to  improve  their  standard
of  living  and  live  within  a  reasonable  distance  of  their  job.  Another
example  of  the  difficulties  of  pairing  low-  and  modest-income  blacks
with  jobs  in  the  Arlington  Heights  area  is  provided  by the  Minority
Information  Referral  Center,  through  which  30  companies  with  facili-
ties  in  the  northwest  suburbs  attempt  to  make  job  openings  available
to  minority  workers  living  in  Chicago  (Tr.  497,  500-02).  In  a  re-
cent  six  month  period,  some  1,176  jobs  were  referred  by businesses
to  this  referral  center,  but,  as  its  director  testified,  “(t)he  problem
is  that  industry  cannot  find  adequate  numbers  of  persons  for  their
jobs  because  they  have  no  way  of  getting  out  there  or  for  housing.”
(Tr.  504,  510).

16

17

Green,  as  they  have  to  other  MHDC  developments  else-
where  (Tr.  45,  229,  251).  As  the  Court  of  Appeals  con-
eluded,  ‘‘if  Lincoln  Green  were  built,  it,  unlike  the  rest
of  the  Village,  would  be  an  integrated  community’’  and
its  construction  might  well  result  in  increasing  Arlington
Heights’  minority  population  by over  1,000%  (517  F.2d  409,
417).  In  addition,  it  would  act  as  a  signal  to  other  blacks
who  could  afford  housing  in  Arlington  Heights  that  the
Village  was  no  longer  closed  to  them,  thus  increasing  inte-
gration  in the  area  even  more  (Tr.  500-01,  583).

The  Village  Hearings  and  the  Community  Response

MHDC  supported  its  proposal  for  Lincoln  Green  before
the  Plan  Commission  by  having  iis  design  team  present
evidence  on  architecture,  planning,  and  tax  and  traffic  im-
pact  (See  Ps.  Ex.  17  and  42;  Ps.  Ex.  1-7  and  11-40),  all  of
which  were  before  the  Plan  Commission  and  the  Trustees
when  they  rejected  Lincoln  Green  (Tr.  158-61,  175-5,  193).
Not  once  at  all  of  the  Plan  Commission  hearings  nor  at
the  meeting  of  the  Board  of  Trustees  nor  throughout  the
nine  months  from  the  time  the  MHDC  petition  was  filed
until  it  was  rejected  by  the  Trustees  was  there  ever  a
single  word  of  professional  advice  or  testimony  given  in
opposition  to  Lincoln  Green.
Indeed,  neither  the  Plan
Commission  nor  the  Trustees  ever  received  or  even  asked
for  the  opinion  or  advice  of  Mr.  Kesler,  the  Village’s
planner,  with  respect  to  the  proposed  development  (App.
I 68;  Tr.  114-115).

Both  the  Commission  and  the  Trustees  were,  however,
besieged  with  advice  on  the  ‘‘social  issue’’  that  much  of
the  Arlington  Heights  community  felt  Lincoln  Green
raised.  There  was  no  question  but  that  the  tenants  of  the
proposed  development  would  include  a  substantial  num-
ber  of  blacks  and  other  minorities  and  that  its  construc-

tion  would  materially  increase  integration  in  Arlington
Heights.

The  Trustees  were  well  aware  that  Lincoln  Green  was
proposed  as  an  integrated  development  when  they  re-
jected  it  (Tr.  175-185;  Ps.  Ex.  25).  There  were  racially-
explicit  letters  to  the  local  newspaper  (e.g.,  Ps.  Ex.  48-1,
48-2,  48-3,  48-7,  and  48-9).  Thousands  of  white  home-
owners  filed  petitions  in  opposition  to  Lincoln  Green  and
an  unprecedented  number  of  homeowners  and  ‘‘civic’’
groups  representing  hundreds  of  residents  from  through-
out  the  Village  spoke  against  the  development  (Ps.  Ex.
17,  42,  37,  and  38  [sample  of  petition  containing  3,300  sig-
natures|).  The  local  supporters  of  the  proposal  were  also
a  unique  combination  of  religious  and  church  groups,
members  of  human  relations  committees,  the  League  of
Women  Voters,  and  individual  citizens  (Ps.  Ex.  17,  36).
The  public  hearings  were  characterized  by  emotional,
often  racial,  reactions  on  the  part  of  the  overflow  crowds
(Tr.  311-13,  316,  396-97;  Ps.  Ex.  48,  16  residents  ‘‘terri-
fied’’  by  crowd).*

One  letter  from  a  local  resident  appearing  in  the  Arling-
ton  Heights  Herald  a  month  before  the  first  Plan  Com-
mission  hearing  began:

‘‘Concerning  your  editorial,  ‘Housing:  An  Ignored
Issue’:  It  isn’t  ignored,  it’s  unwanted.  We  do  resist
low-income  housing  because  it  is  a  ploy  to  export

discrimination  was  found.  See  also  Kennedy  Park  Homes  v.  City

After  a  number  of  people  had  spoken  against  Lincoln
Green  at  the  initial  Plan  Commission  hearing,  the  follow-
ing  exchange  concluded  the  remarks  from  the  floor,  as
reflected  in  the  official  minutes  of  the  meeting:

Rosemary  Splitt,  320  N.  Carlisle,  Arlington  Heights,
was  placed  under  oath.  She  stated  that  she  heard  a
young  girl  seated  behind  her,  this  young  girl  being
from  another  country,  state  that  this  was  unbeliev-
able,  she  didn’t  think  this  could  happen  in  America.

Mr.  Zviagzne,  208  E.  Oakton,  Arlington  Heights,
was  placed  under  oath.  He  stated  that  they  have  the
right  to  choose  their  friends,  when  at  a  picnic  they
have  the  right  to  choose  who  they  wanted  to  sit  with,
he  believes  in  humanity,  in  all  races,  but  doesn’t  be-
lieve  he  should  be  obliged  on  a  moment’s  notice  to
change,  for  somebody  to  come  from  Chicago  and  tell
him  what  he  should  do,  what  is  right  for  him.
He
stated  he  forgave  the  young  lady,  she’s  too  young.
When  you  live  longer  you  change  your  opinion.

He  explained  that  in  Brazil  there  is a  lot  of mixing
between  colors  and  he  hoped  that  someday  we  would
live  together  like  that,  too,  but  not  by being  provoked
—no  shotgun  wedding  has  lasted  very  long,  and  that
is what  he  felt  was  happening.  After  more  discussion
in  this  regard,  he  concluded  that  the  people  in  ques-
tion  don’t  help  you,  us  or  anybody,  they  just  make
things  worse.
Therefore,  he  hoped  the  committee
would  represent  the  people  who  live  here,  who  plan
to  live,  work  and  die  here  (Ps.  Ex.  17,  pp.  11-12  (3/
13/71)).

So  emotion-charged  were  the  Plan  Commission  hear-
ings  and  so  vociferous  were  the  crowds  in  opposition  to
those  who  supported  Lincoln  Green  (Tr.  312-13)  that  the
two  Commission  members  who  voted  in  favor  of  it  felt
compelled  to  begin  their  official  statement  with  the  assur-
ance  that  they  had  voted  for  the  development  not  on  the
‘‘social  issue,’’  but  because  it  represented  good  zoning
(Ps.  Ex.  43,  p.  1).  After  the  Trustees  voted  down  the
MHD0C’s  application,  Village  President  Walsh  stated  that
while  there  was  a  need  for  low  and  moderate  income
housing  in  Arlington  Heights,  he  felt  ‘‘the  objections  of
the  residents  is  (sic)  a  mandate  to  reject  this  proposal’’
(Ps.  Ex.  42,  p.  6).

The  Proceedings  Below

This  suit  was  then  instituted  by  MHDC  and  the  indi-
vidual  respondents  against  the  Village  of  Arlington
Heights  and  the  members  of  its  Board  of  Trustees.
(Northwest  Opportunity  Center,  another  not-for-profit
corporation,  and  Eluteria  Maldonado  were  allowed  to
intervene  as  respondents.)  The  complaint  alleged  that  the
refusal  to  rezone  made  housing  unavailable  because  of
race,  perpetuated  segregation,  and  denied  MHDC  the
right  to  use  its  property  in  a  reasonable  manner  in  viola-

20

21

tion  of  the  Fourteenth  Amendment,  the  Fair  Housing  Act
of  1968  (42  U.S.C.  $§$3601  et  seq.),  the  Civil  Rights  Act  of
1866  (42  U.S.C.A.  §1982),  and  the  Civil  Rights  Act  of  1871
(42  U.S.C.  §$1983).*  The  principal  relief  requested  a  de-
claratory  judgment  invalidating  the  Arlington  Heights
zoning  ordinance  as  applied  to  the  subject  property  and
an  injunction  restraining  petitioners  from  preventing  or
interfering  with  the  development  of  the  housing  proposed
by  Metropolitan  Housing  (517  F.2d  412).

The  record  at  trial  included  substantial  evidence  regard-
ing  the  racial  and  employment  impact  of  Lincoln  Green,
as  well  as  most  of  the  evidence  on  architecture,  demand,
taxes,  traffic,  and  other  zoning  and  planning  considera-
tions  that  were  before  the  Trustees  when  they  rejected  the
development.  In  addition,  the  evidence  showed  that  peti-
tioners  had  previously  rezoned  60  parcels  of  land  in  Ar-
lington  Heights  for  large,  multi-family  projects  (Ds.  Ex.
10  and  11),  most  of  which  were  adjacent  to  single-family
homes  (Ps.  Ex.  12  and  52).  The  result,  as  petitioners  con-
cede,  is that  by 1973  there  were  over  6,000  high-rent  apart-
ments  in  Arlington  Heights  with  proper  zoning  available
for  some  9,000  more,  while  not  a  single  unit  of  subsidized
housing  has  been  permitted  by petitioners.  Thus,  the  zon-
ing  policies  of  Arlington  Heights  have  turned  the  1950
village  of  8,768  persons  into  the  most  racially  exclusion-
ary  and  residentially  segregated  community  in  the  Chi-

*  Jurisdiction  over  the  Village  was  based  upon  28  U.S.C.  §1343
(for  the  §1982  claim),  and  42  U.S.C.  $3612  (for  the  §§ 3604  and
3617  claim).  Jurisdiction  over  the  individual  defendants,  all  officials
of  the  Village,  and  the  Village  was  based  upon  §1331  (for  the  Four-
teenth  Amendment  claim),  $1343  (for  the  §§ 1982  and  1983  claims),
and  §3612  (for  the  §§ 3604  and  3617  claims).

cago  metropolitan  area  among  municipalities  with  more
than  50,000  residents.*

The  District  Court,  concluding  that  there  was  no  racial
discrimination  nor  violation  of  constitutional  or  statutory
rights  involved,  denied  any  relief.  The  Court  of  Appeals
reversed,  noting  that  the  MHDC’s  development  presented
the  only  hope  of  easing  the  segregated  housing  patterns
of  Arlington  Heights  and  holding  that  ‘‘under  the  facts
of  this  case,  Arlington  Heights’  rejection  of  the  Lincoln
Green  proposal  has  racially  discriminating  effects’’  and

*  Despite  petitioners’  recognition  “in  theory”  of  the  tremendous
need  for  low  and  moderate  income  housing  in  Arlington  Heights
(supra,  p.  9),  their  rejection  of  Lincoln  Green  was,  in  effect,  a  re-
fusal  to  permit  any  integrated,  subsidized  housing  in  the  Village,
since  no  economically  feasible  and  suitable  alternative  site  was  avail-
able  (517  F.2d  409,  414).

The  record  demonstrates  that  none  of  the  vacant  R-5  parcels  in
Arlington  Heights  could  be  used  for  subsidized  housing.  Each  of
these  properties  had  been  zoned  multi-family  for  a  particular  com-
mercial  project  (Tr.  812).  Lincoln  Green,  or  any  other  subsidized
proposal,  could  not,  therefore,  be  developed  on  any  of  them  with-
out  new  and  different  petitions  being  submitted  to  the  Village  for
approval.
The  planned  high-rent  projects  have  such  names  as
“Frenchmen’s  Cove,”  “Three  Lakes,”  and  “Randhaven”  (Ds.Ex.
120,  App.  II  120).  These  sites  could  not  be  used  for  more  modest
priced  housing  (Tr.  830).  Even  more  important,  few  of  the  remain-
ing  parcels  were  available  for  sale,  and  those  that  were  commanded
prices  so  high  that  they  made  a  Section  236  development  impossible
(Tr.  813,  824-25,  830,  969,  972).  For  example,  Mr.  Opelka,  petition-
ers’  expert  witness  on  Arlington  Heights  land  values,  testified  that  he
did  not  know  of any  pieces  of  vacant  R-5  land  of  more  than  10 acres
(Tr.  944-45)  and  that  the  one  R-5  parcel  whose  value  he  did  know
was  priced  at  $50,000/acre  (Tr.  973),  two  and a half  times  the  cost
of  the  Viatoriari  land  to  MHDC.  No  other  R-5  land  was  available
for  Lincoln  Green  at  anywhere  near  the  minimum  cost  set  by  the
Viatorians  in  their  effort  to  have  low  and  moderate  income  housing
built  on  their  land  (Tr.  972-73).

ghettos  with  ‘‘truly  integrated  and  balanced  living  pat-
terns.’’  Trafficanti  v.  Metropolitan  Infe  Insurance  Co.,
409  U.S.  205,  211  (1972).

Judicial  interpretations  and  administrative  interpreta-
tions  of  the  fair  housing  laws  establish  private  efforts
that  would  substantially  reduce  suburban  housing  segre-
gation  cannot  be  blocked  absent  a  compelling  public  inter-
est.  In  addition,  The  Fair  Housing  Law  should  be  con-
strued  like  other  civil  rights  laws  to  eliminate  arbitrary
and  unjustifiable  barriers  to  equal  housing  opportunity
such  as  those  erected  by  Arlington  Heights  here.

=
yer

ITI.

The  lower  courts  correctly  treated  all  respondents  as
proper  parties  with  adequate  standing  to  challenge  peti-
tioners’  discriminatory  action.  Since  Lincoln  Green  was
a  specific  proposal  for  an  integrated  housing  development
in  Arlington  Heights  to  be  occupied  by  the  individual
respondents  and  their  class,  Warth  v.  Seldin,  422  US.
490,  45  L.Ed.  2d  343  (1975)  does  not  undercut  standing
here  and  indeed  indicates  that  the  facts  of  this  case  clear-
ly establish  that  respondents  have  standing  because  they
have  an  adequate  stake  in  the  outcome  of  the  litigation.

therefore  violated  the  Equal  Protection  clause  of  the
Fourteenth  Amendment,  since  no  compelling  public  inter-
est  necessitated  that  decision.*

SUMMARY  OF  ARGUMENT

i

Lincoln  Green  is  carefully  planned  as  a  private,  high-
quality  townhouse  development  that  is  specifically  de-
signed  to  help  alleviate  housing  segregation  in  the  sub-
urban  Chicago  area,  and  is  compatible  with  neighboring
single-family  homes  and  legitimate  community  interests.
Petitioners’  refusal  to  permit  construction  of  Lincoln
Green  has  a  racially  segregative  effect  and  perpetuates
residential  segregation  in  and  around  Arlington  Heights.
Since  Arlington  Heights’  rejection  of  Lincoln  Green  was
racially  discriminatory  and  not  justified  by  any  compel-
ling  public  interest,  it  therefore  violates  the  Equal  Pro-
tection  clause  of  the  Fourteenth  Amendment.

II.

Petitioners’  refusal  to  permit  construction  of  Lincoln
Green  also  violates  the  1968  Fair  Housing  Act  and  other
Federal  Civil  Rights  Acts.  In  enacting  the  1968  Fair  Hous-
ing  Act,  (42  U.S.C.  §§3601  et  seq.),  Congress  prohibited
all  acts,  public  as  well  as  private,  that  make  housing  un-
available  because  of  race  in  order  to  replace  the  nation’s

*  Fairchild,  Ch.  J.,  dissented  on  a  question  of  fact  but  agreed
that,  as  a  matter  of  law,  if  respondents  “establish  that  the  Village’s
enforcement  of  its  zoning  scheme  makes  construction  of  housing  for
low  and  moderate-income  individuals  not  reasonably  possible,  the
refusal  perpetuates  racial  segregation  and,  absent  compelling  gov-
ernmental  justification,  the  respondents  would  be  entitled  to  the
relief  they  seek.”

(517  F.2d  409,  415).

24

ARGUMENT

a

A.

THE  COURT  OF  APPEALS  CORRECTLY  HELD  THAT
PETITIONERS’  REFUSAL  TO  PERMIT  THE  CON-
STRUCTION  OF  LINCOLN  GREEN  VIOLATES  THE
AMENDMENT.

A.  The  record  shows  that  petitioners’  refusal  to  permit
the  construction  of  Lincoln  Green  was  racially  moti-
vated.

The  members  of  the  Plan  Commission  and  the  Trustees
were  aware  that  by  blocking  Lincoln  Green  they  would
perpetuate  segregation  in  Arlington  Heights  by  prevent-
ing the  construction  of any  low  and  moderate  income  hous-
ing  in  Arlington  Heights,  thereby  making  housing  un-
available  for  blacks  and  other  minorities  (517  F.2d  409,
418).  Nevertheless,  petitioners’  brief  is  laced  with  pro-
testations  that  illicit  racial  ‘‘motive’’,  ‘‘intent’’,  or  ‘‘racial
purpose’’  has  not  been  proved,  (e.g.,  Pet.  Br.  pp.  6,  7,
8, 15,  17,  22,  27).*

The  record  shows  that  members  of  the  Arlington
Heights  community  responded  to  news  of  the  proposed
MHDC  development  with  hostile,  over-flow  crowds  at  the

*  Petitioners’  argument  is  particularly  objectionable  because  evi-
dence  with  respect  to  their  state  of  mind  and  motivation  in  block-
ing  the  MHDC  development  was  excluded  at  their  insistence  (Tr.
Despite  this  evidentiary  limitation,  the  record  contains
170-71).
substantial  evidence  establishing  petitioners’  racial  motivation.
For
example,  Village  President  Walsh  admitted  that  the  Trustees’  re-
jection  of  Lincoln  Green  was  in  response  to  community  opposition,
in  which  race  played  a  clear  part  (Ps.  Ex.  42,  p.  6).

25

hearings  before  the  Plan  Commission  and  the  Board  of
Trustees  and  with  angry,  often  racially-explicit  letters
and  petitions  in  opposition  to  Lincoln  Green  (supra,  pp.  17,
18).*

As  the  Tenth  Circuit  stated  in  Dailey,  supra,  at  1039,
no  Official  statement  of  intent  to  discriminate  is  necessary
to  prove  a  violation  of  the  equal  protection  clause.

If  proof  of  a  civil  rights  violation  depends  on  an  open
statement  by  an  official  of  an  intent  to  discriminate,
the  Fourteenth  Amendment  offers  little  solace  to  those
seeking  its  protection.
In  our  opinion  it  is  enough
for  the  complaining  parties  to  show  that  the  local
officials  are  effectuating  the  discriminatory  designs
of  private  individuals.  Dailey,  supra,  at  1039.

Here,  petitioners  admitted  that  they  blocked  Lincoln
Green  ‘‘in  response  to  community  opposition,’’  (Ps.  Ex.
42,  p.  6),  a  result  they  knew  would  maintain  residential
segregation  in  this  area.**

*  Lower  courts  have  consistently  recognized  the  probative  value
of  evidence  of  white  hostility  in  establishing  that  municipal  action
excluding  low  and  moderate  income  housing  was  racially  motivated.
Kennety  Park  Homes  v.  City  of Lackawanna,  436  F.  2d  108  (2nd
Cir.  1970);  United  States  v.  City  of  Black  Jack,  508  F.  2d  1179
(8th  Cir.  1974);  Banks  v.  Perk,  473  F.  2d  910  (6th  Cir.  1973) ;
United  Farmworkers  v.  City  of Delray  Beach,  493  F.  2d  797  (Sth
Cir.  1974).  While  discrimination  may  be  measured  solely  on  the
basis  of  effect,  the  evidence  of  purpose  may  be  taken  into  account.
“This  is  not  to  say  that  proof  of  motive,  purpose  or  intent  may  not
reinforce  a  finding  of  racial  discrimination  or  serve  as  a  basis  of
such  finding.”  Hawkins  v.  Town  of  Shaw,  461  F.  2d  1171,  1175
(197).

**  The  rule  is  that  a  man  is  held  to  intend  the  foreseeable  conse-
quences  of  his  conduct,  Radio  Officers  v.  Labor  Board,  347  U.S.
17,  45  (1953).  This  presumption  applies  to  public  officials  as  well.
Rabinowitz  v.  United  States,  366  F.  2d  34,  56  (5th  Cir.  1966).

26

27

B.  Petitioners’  refusal  to  permit  the  construction  of  Lin-
coln  Green  unlawfully  perpetuates  residential  segrega-
tion.

Although  there  is  substantial  evidence  of  petitioners’
discriminatory  purpose  in  their  rejection  of  Lincoln  Green
here,  the  primary  reason  their  action  violates  the  Four-
teenth  Amendment  is,  as  the  Seventh  Circuit  recognized,
that  it  has  the  effect  of  discriminating  against  minorities.
Burton  v.  Wilmington  Parking  Authority,  365  U.S.  715
(1961).  Wright  v.  City  of Emporia,  407  U.S.  451  (1972).
When,  as  here,  the  effect  of  a  municipality’s  action  is  to
reinforce  and  prolong  the  severely  segregated  housing
patterns  in  its  area,  that  action  is  subject  to  ‘‘strict  seru-
tiny’’  and  cannot  stand  absent  a  compelling  justification.*
The  result  of  petitioners’  action  is  effectively  to  exclude
minorities  from  and  perpetuate  segregation  in  Arlington
Heights,  a  result  this  Court  can  not  condone.  See  Bucha-
nan  v.  Warley,  245  U.S.  60  (1917).  Since  the  measure  of
state  action  is the  result  it achieves,  it follows  indisputably
that  municipal  action  that  results  in  such  perpetuation  of
segregation  is  illegal.  When  confronted  with  a  situation
in  which  petitioners  must  act,  they  must  at  least  act  so  as

*  Petitioners’  emphasis  on  Lindsey  v.  Normet,  405  U.S.  56
(1972),  to  argue  that  housing  is  not  a  “fundamental  interest”  and
therefore  the  “strict  scrutiny”  test  does  not  apply  here  is  misplaced.
“Strict  scrutiny’  is  required  here  simply  because  petitioners’  action
is  racially  discriminatory.  Lindsey  was  a  landlord-tenant  case  that
in  no  way  involved  race  and  is  therefore  irrelevant  to  the  equal
housing  opportunity  values  raised  by  this  case,  values  which  Con-
gress  has  determined  involve  the  highest  national  priority.
See
Trafficante  v.  Metropolitan  Life  Insurance  Co.,  409  U.S.  205  (1972).

not  to  interfere  with  private  efforts  to  accomplish  substan-
tial  integration  and  so  as  not  to  perpetuate  segregation.*

The  Court  of  Appeals  began  its  analysis  of  the  racially
segregative  effect
of  Arlington  Heights’  rejection  of
Lincoln  Green  by  noting  that  a  greater  percentage  of
blacks  than  whites  would  be  injured  by the  municipality’s
action.**
Contrary  to  petitioners’  repeated  assertions,
however,  the  court  did  not  hold  that  this  racial  disparity
alone  amounted  to  a  violation  of  the  Fourteenth  Amend-
ment.  Indeed,  the  Seventh  Cireuit  especially  recognized
that  action  such  as  that  in  James  y.  Valtierra,  402  U.S.
137  (1971)  which  discriminates  on  an  economic  basis  can
only  be  exempt  from  ‘‘strict  scrutiny’’  if  that  action  is
challenged  solely  on  the  ground  that  proportionately  more
blacks  are  poor.***

As  the  Court  of  Appeals  pointed  out,  however,  the  seg-
regative  effect  of  petitioners’  refusal  to  permit  construc-

*  A  municipality’s  refusal  to  approve  zoning  in  order  to  block
an  integrated  housing  development  is  state  action  for  equal  protec-
E.g.,  Dailey  v.  City  of  Lawton,  425  F.2d  1037
tion  purposes.
(10th  Cir.  1970).

“**  Blacks  would  be  disproportionately  affected  insofar  as  blacks
make  up  40%  of  the  potentially  eligible  population  for  low  and
moderate  income  housing  in  the  SMSA  while  they  are  only  18%  of
the  population  of  the  SMSA.  (Tr.  592-93).  The  suspect  class
affected  by the  action  of  Arlington  Heights  is composed  of  individu-
Blacks  and  other  minorities
als  with  low  and  moderate  incomes.
do  make  up  a  greater  percentage  of  such  class  than  they  do  of  the
population  generally  in  the  Chicago  metropolitan  area.
(517  F.2d
409).

***  James  v.  Valtierra,  402  U.S.  137  (1971)  is  not  applicable
here.
First,  it  did  not  involve  racial  discrimination,  402  U.S.  at
141,  and  second,  it  did  not  involve  governmental  action  but  rather
dealt  with  referendum  procedures  and  rights  not  in  issue  in  this  case.

28

29

tion  of  Lincoln  Green  cannot  be  judged  alone  by  the  fact
that  its  impact  will  fall  more  heavily  on  blacks  but  must
also  be  viewed  in  the  context  of  the  over-all  patterns  of
residential  segregation  in  the  Arlington  Heights  area.
See:  Unitea  States  v.  City  of Black  Jack,  508  F.2d  1179,
and  Kennedy  Park  Homes  v.  City  of  Lackawanna,  318
F.Supp.  669.

This  context  includes  the  facts  that:

historically  the  Chicago  metropolitan  area  has  been
segregated  in  terms  of  housing  ...  (and)  that  there
exists  in  Chicago  and  its  environs  a  high  degree  of
racial  residential  segregation.  As  the  court  said  in
Clark  v.  Universal  Builders,  Inc.,  501  F.2d  324,  334-35
(7th  Cir.  1974),  ‘‘Nor  do  we  think  it beyond  the  stric-
tures  of  judicial  notice  to  observe  that  there  exists  in
Chicago  and  its  environs  a  high  degree  of  racial  resi-
dential  segregation.’’  More  specifically,  the  popula-
tion  of  Arlington  Heights  in  1970  was  64,884,  but  only
twenty-seven  residents  were  black.!  The  four-town-
ship  northwest  Cook  County  area,  of  which  Arling-
ton  Heights  is  a  part,  had  a  population  increase  from
1960  to  1970  of  219,000  people,  but  only  170  of  these
were  black.*  Indeed,  the  percentage  of  blacks  in  this
area  actually  decreased  over  this  ten-year  span  while
the  percentage  of  the  population  of the  entire  Chicago
metropolitan  area  that  was  black  increased  from  four-
teen  percent  to  eighteen  percent."

*  According  to  statistics  of  plaintiffs’  expert,  demographer  and

dentially  segregated  community  in  the  Chicago  metropolitan  area
among  municipalities  with  more  than  fifty  thousand  residents.

of  jobs  in  the  four-township  Arlington  Heights  area  rose  from

As  the  Court  of  Appeals  observed,  the  Lincoln  Green
proposal  represents  a  unique  opportunity  to  help  reverse
this  trend  toward  increasingly  segregated  housing  pat-
terns,  both  in  the  entire  Chicago  area  and  more  dramati-
cally  in  the  composition  of  Arlington  Heights.  In  addi-
tion,  if  ‘‘Lincoln  Green  were  built,  it,  unlike  the  rest  of
the  Village,  would  be  an  integrated  community,’’  and  ‘‘it
might  well  result  in  increasing  Arlington  Heights’  mi-
(517
nority  population  by  over  one  thousand  percent’’
F.2d  at  417).  Furthermore,  petitioners’  rejection  of  Lin-
coln  Green  means  that  no  integrated,  subsidized  housing
will  be  built  in  Arlington  Heights  (517  F.2d  at  417),  and
petitioners  have,  therefore,  rejected  ‘‘the  only  present
hope’”’  that  even  a  modest  private  effort  could  be  made
toward  eliminating  the  pervasive  problem  of  segregated
housing  in  the  area.  7d.  Understood  in  this  context,  peti-
tioners’  action  not  only  disproportionately  injures  blacks,
but  it  exploits  and  perpetuates  segregated  housing  pat-
terns  in a  way  that  directly  violates  the  national  housing
policy  of  ‘‘replacing  the  ghettos  by  truly  integrated  and
balanced  living  patterns.’’  Trafficante,  supra.

Petitioners’  zoning  powers  are  not  so  broad  as  to  justify
actions  that  perpetuate  segregation.
Neither  Euclid  v.
Ambler  Realty  Co.,  272  U.S.  365  (1926)  nor  Village  of

Blacks,  however,  have  not  been  able  to  take

In  1970  only  137  of  the
full  advantage  of  these  job  opportunities.
12,000  people  who  worked  in  Arlington  Heights  were  black.  Part
of  the  explanation  for  this  is  that  many  black  workers  have  been
unable  to  find  housing  they  can  afford  in  Arlington  Heights.  A
study  issued  by  the  Cook  County  Office  of  Economic  Opportunity
indicated  that  based  on  its  survey  almost  all  the  black  workers  in
Arlington  Heights  resided  in  Chicago.  Moreover,  the  report  stated
that  one  of  the  main  problems  Arlington  Heights’  employers  faced
in  hiring  minorities  was  the  lack  of  adequate  housing  within  a
reasonable  distance  of  their  plants.

30

31

Belle  Terre  v.  Boraas,  416  U.S.  1  (1974)  sanctions  such
unbridled  municipal  discretion  to  use  these  powers  to  re-
ject  private  desegregation  efforts,  absent  compelling  pub-
lic  justifications.  In  Euclid,  the  Court  sustained  a  zoning
ordinance  that  prevented  industrial  use  of  certain  land
but  recognized  ‘‘the  possibility  of  cases  where  the  general
public  interest  would  so  far  outweigh  the  interest  of  the
municipality  that  the  municipality  would  not  be  allowed
to  stand  in  the  way.’’  272  U.S.  at  390.  Belle  Terre  was
concerned  with  a  zoning  provision  designed  to  protect
natural  families  from  unrelated  groups,  not  white  homes
from  integrated  townhouses  nor  ‘‘land  use  controls  ..  .
confining  minorities  and  the  poor  to  the  ghetto’’  (Mar-
shall,  J.,  dissenting,  39  L.  Ed.  2d  at  807).  Neither  Euclid
nor  Belle  Terre  involved  racial  discrimination  at  all,  and
as  the  Eighth  Circuit  held  in  invalidating  an  exclusionary
zoning  action  similar  to  the  one  at  issue  here,  ‘‘The  dis-
cretion  of  local  zoning  officials,  recently  recognized  in
Village  of Belle  Terre  v.  Boraas,  416  U.S.  1  (1974)  must
be  curtailed  where  ‘‘the  clear  result  of  such  discretion  is
the  segregation  of  low  income  Blacks  from  all  White
Neighborhoods.’’  U.S.  v.  Black  Jack,  508  F.2d  1184  (8th
Cir.  1974).

All  of  the  Courts  of  Appeals  that  have  dealt  with  the
problem  of  exclusionary  zoning  and  residential  segrega-
tion  have  recognized  that  compelling  public  interests  are
necessary  to  justify  governmental  actions  which  interfere
with  private  efforts  such  as  MHDC’s  to  promote  desegre-
gation.

Dailey  v.  City  of Lawton,  425  F.2d  1037  (10th  Cir.  1970)
is  similar  in  many  ways  to  the  instant  case.  There,  a  de-
veloper  unsuccessfully  sought  rezoning  to  a  multi-family
classification  in  order  to  build  low  and  moderate  income
housing.  In  striking  down  the  municipality’s  action,  the

court  found  that  the  city’s  denial  of  the  request  was
prompted  by  a  desire  to  keep  a  large  concentration  of
blacks  from  moving  out  of  the  black  ghetto  of  Lawton  into
the  white  area  in  which  the  property  was  located.

In  Kennedy  Park  Homes  Ass’n,  vy.  City  of Lackawanna,
318  F.  Supp.  669  (W.D.N.Y.  1970),  aff'd,  486  F.  2d  108
(2nd  Cir.  1970),  cert.  denied,  401  U.S.  1010  (1971),  the
Catholic  Archdiocese  of  Buffalo  committed  itself  to  sell
land  in a  white  section  of a  Buffalo  suburb  for  the  develop-
ment  of  a  low  income  housing  project.  The  development
was  prevented  when  the  Mayor  refused  to  sign  a  sanitary
sewer  form  needed  for  construction.  The  District  Court
found  that  ‘‘[t]he  history  of  Lackawanna  is  that  of  a
racially  separate  community.’’  (318  F.  Supp.  at  694).  The
city  had  refused  to  permit  private  parties  to  break  down
the  racial  separation  of  the  community.  The  court  held
that  this  refusal  denied  plaintiff’s  ‘‘equal  housing  op-
portunity’’  (id.  at  694)  in violation  of  the  equal  protection
clause  and  the  1968  Fair  Housing  Act."

Crow  v.  Brown,  332  F.  Supp.  382  (N.D.  Ga.  1974),  aff’d,
457  F.2d  788  (Sth  Cir.  1972)  is  even  more  explicit  on  the
duty  of  municipalities  not  to  prevent  private  efforts  aimed
at  desegregation.
There,  officials  of  suburban  Fulton
County  (of  which  Atlanta  is  the  core)  refused  to  issue’
building  permits  for  two  private  developers  of  public
housing.  The  Court  held:

Given  the  decisions  previously  cited  [including  Lack-
awanna  and  Dailey]  it is  abundantly  clear  that,  in  the
absence  of supervening  necessity  any  county  action  or
inaction  intended  to  perpetuate  or  which  mm  effect  does
perpetuate  residential  segregation  in  the  county  with
attendant  school  segregation  and  dislocations  of jobs
and  housing  cannot  stand.  Nor  can  county  action  or

*  See  infra  for  discussion  of  the  Fair  Housing  Act.

32

33

inaction  which  would  thwart  their  correction  be  per-
mitted  to  continue.

The  county  defendants  testified  that  they  are  aware
of  these  conditions,  yet  they  have  come  forward  with
no  justification,  compelling  or  otherwise,  for  their
passivity,  their  refusal  to  cooperate,  indeed  their
active  opposition  to  plans  designed  to  alleviate  them.
332  F.  Supp.  at  392  (emphasis  supplied)

The  Fifth  Circuit  reaffirmed  this  principle  recently  in
United  Farm  Workers  of  Florida  Housing  Project,  Inc.
v.  City  of Delray  Beach,  Florida,  493  F.  2d  799  (5th  Cir.
1974),  where  it  ruled  that  a  municipality  cannot  refuse  to
provide  water  and  sewer  services  to  a  private  low  and
moderate  income  development:

...  [ijn  the  realm  of  federally  assisted  housing,  local
authorities  have  an  obligation  to  further  the  national
housing  policy  of  balanced  public  housing  and  to  re-
frain  from  frustrating  efforts  to  carry  out  that  policy.
(citations  omitted)

As  discussed  earlier,  Arlington  Heights  is  a  segregated
community  (supra  at  p.  16)  with  a  highly  exclusion-
ary  housing  stock.  It is a  part  of  a  segregated  region  that
is  becoming  even  more  segregated.  Lincoln  Green  would
be  the  only  integrated  housing  in  the  Village  and  would
substantially

action  is  inconsistent  with  their  duty  of  non-interference

C.  No  compelling  public  justification  exists  that  can  ex-

cuse  petitioners’  rejection  of  Lincoln  Green.

The  Court  of Appeals  correctly  found  that  there  was  no
compelling  interest  served  by  Arlington  Heights’  refusal
to  permit  construction  of  Lincoln  Green.

As  we  have  already  indicated,  the  Village  has  not
even  been  consistent  in  applying  its  zoning  plan  when
considering  requests  for  zoning  changes  to  R-5.  More-
over,  even  if  a  municipality’s  desire  to  limit  multi-
family  dwellings  to  instances  which  they  might  serve
as  a  traditional  buffer  zone  could  ever  be  a  compelling
interest,  it is certainly  not  in  this  case  (517  F.  2d  409,
415).

The  court  recognized  that  the  low-rise  structures  pro-
posed  were  compatible  with  surrounding  housing  and  the
expressed  planning  policy  would  at  best  have  only  minimal
application.

As  noted  by two  dissenting  members  of  the  Arlington
Heights  Plan  Commission,  in  terins  of  density,  archi-
tecture  and  most  other  characteristics,  such  town-
houses  are  more  similar  to  R-3  dwellings  than  R-d
buildings.  The  planning  rationale  behind  the  buffer
zone  policy  has  only  minima!  applicability  to  this  type
of  low-rise,  open-space  development.  It  is  not  a  suffi-
ciently  compelling  interest  to  justify  the  racially  dis-
criminatory  effect  (517  F.2d  409,  415).

34

35

As  the  court  found,  petitioners’  inconsistent  application
of its claimed  ‘‘buffer  policy’’  could  not  justify  the  racially
discriminatory  effects  present  here  (517  F.2d  409,  415).

The  Court  next  dealt  with  the  supposed  diminution  in
surrounding  property  values  that  petitioners  claimed
would  result  from  Lincoln  Green  and  found  such  diminu-
tion  does  not  constitute  a  compelling  interest:

Nor  do  we  find  that  the  decision  can  be  supported  by
the  fact  that  neighboring  property  values  might  be
diminished  by as  much  as  five  to  ten  percent  as  assert-
ed  by respondents’  witness.  It  is questionable  whether
this  purely  monetary  consideration  could  be  a  com-
pelling  interest.  In  this  instance  it is not.

*

*

*
Not  only  have  there  been  other  variances  in  Arlington
Heights,  but  the  neighboring  residents  certainly  could
not  expect  that  the  zoning  plan  would  always  be
adhered  to  even  when  a  racially  discriminatory  effect
would  result  (517  F.2d  409,  415).

Petitioners’  argument  that  an  affirmative  duty  has  been
imposed  on  Arlington  Heights  is  not  well  founded.  (Pet.
Br.  25).  What  is  sought  here  is  the  right  to  build  Lincoln
Green  and  the  order  of the  Court  of Appeals  merely  allows
MHDC  to  construct  the  proposed  integrated  housing.  No
affirmative  duty  or  obligation  is being  placed  on  Arlington
Heights  but  rather  a  duty  of  non-interference.

Petitioners  also  erroneously  suggest  that  rulings  in  cer-
tain  state  court  cases  (Pet  Br.  30-34)  set  forth  criteria
which,  when  applied  to  the  facts  here,  can  be  claimed  as
support  for  the  segregative  decision.  However,  when  the
criteria  are  applied  the  results  do  not  establish  a  com-

*  The  evidence  shows  that  the  plan  referred  to  was  passed  in
1959  and  that  most  of  the  homes  were  built  prior  thereto  (Tr.  812).

pelling  or  substantial  reason  for  allowing  a  decision  which
results  in a  segregative  effect.*

Petitioners  have  not  offered  any  legitimate  justification
which  courts  confronting  the  issue  of  exclusionary  zoning
have  recognized  as  satisfying  the  strict  scrutiny  test.
Rather,  they  have  simply  offered  a  preservation  of  local
property  values  defense  and  reliance  on  zoning  ‘‘integrity”’
(517  F.2d  421).  That  defense  is  not  enough.

As  anticipated  by  the  Court’s  observation  in  Euclid,
in  recent  years  less  tolerance  has  been  shown  for  mu-
nicipal  action  which  serves  only  the  parochial  demand  of
those  in  the  immediate  vicinity  to  the  detriment  of  the

*  The  criteria  set  forth  by  petitioners  as  that  used  by  many  state
courts  to  test  zoning  validity  do  not  apply  here  where  Fourteenth
Amendment  and  Fair  Housing  Act  claims  are  involved.  Even  if
applied,  they  would  allow  the  decision  of  the  Seventh  Circuit  to
stand:
1.  The  existing  uses  and  zoning  of  nearby  property:  the
zoning  of  surrounding  properties  is  not  persuasive  against  the  pro-
posal  since  the  planned  townhouses  are  much  like  the  single  family.
2.  The  extent  to  which  property  values  are  diminished  by  the  par-
ticular  zoning  restrictions:
the  testimony  on  diminution  is  not
enough  to  support  a  reversal.  3.  The  extent  to  which  the  destruc-
tion  of  property  values  of  the  plaintiffs  promotes  the  health,  safety,
morals  and  welfare  of  the  public:  the  project  is  highly  related  to
public  welfare  so  the  refusal  did  not  promote  health,  safety,  morals
or  welfare.  4.  The  relative  gain  to  the  public  as  compared  to  the
hardship  imposed  upon  the  individual  property  owner:
the  gain
to  the  public  is  high  while  hardship  to  individuals  is  insignificant.
5.  The  suitability  of  the  subject  property  for  the  zoned  purposes:
the  respondents  showed  the  multiple-family  use  is  the  highest  and
6.  The  length  of  time  the  property  has  remained  vacant
best  use.
as  zoned,  considered  in  the  context  of  land  developments  in  the
area:  the  Clerics  of  St.  Viator  own  the  entire  parcel  which  makes
it  unique.  No  evidence  was  solicited  with  respect  to  their  reasons
for  not  acting  on  the  property  previously.

36

37

entire  region.  Courts  have  recognized  the  necessity  for
considering  the  rights  of  non-residents  as  well  as  resi-
dents  in  assessing  the  reasonableness  of  suburban  zoning
actions.

As  the  Pennsylvania  Supreme  Court  noted  in  Appeal  of

Kit-Mar  Builders,  Inc.,  268  A.  2d  765,  769  (Ba.  1970)  :

We  fully  realize  that  the  overall  solution  to  these
problems  lies  with  greater  regional  planning;  but
until
the  time  comes  that  we  have  such  a  sys-
tem,  we  must  confront  the  situation  as  it  is.  The
power  currently  resides  in  the  hands  of  each  local
governing  unit,  and  we  will  not  tolerate  their  abusing
that  power....

Illinois  courts  have  explicitly  recognized  housing  needs  as
an  important  consideration  in  judging  the  reasonableness
of  a  zoning  decision  which  excludes  certain  types  of  hous-
ing.

In  Lakeland  Bluff,  Inc.  v.  County  of  Will,  242  N.E.
2d  765  (Ill.  App.  1969),  a  county’s  refusal  to  rezone  for  a
mobile  home  park  was  overturned.  The  court  found  that
the  plaintiff  had  shown  a  need  for  low-cost  housing  in  the
area,  that  mobile  homes  would  help  satisfy  that  need,  and
that  this  ‘‘was  an  element  which  should  be  considered  in
determining  the  reasonableness  of  the  restrictive  zoning
ordinance.’’  252  N.E.  2d  at  770.  See  also  Glassey  v.  County
of  Tazewell,  297  N.E.  2d  235  (Ill.  App.  1973).  The  evi-
dence  here  clearly  establishes  that  a  desperate  area-wide
need  for  low  and  moderate  income  housing  exists.

Petitioners  ignore  this  need  in  contesting  the  reasonable-
ness  of  the  decision  of  the  Court  of  Appeals.  Instead  they
rely  on  a  mechanistic  formula  for  determining  reason-
ableness,  but  even  following  that  approach,  the  denial
of  zoning  is  improper  for  several  reasons.  First,  Lincoln

Green  is  consistent  with  the  existing  uses  and  zoning  of
nearby  property.  As  the  record  demonstrates,  the  pro-
posed  development  is  complementary  to  the  surrounding
housing,  and  only  the  Village’s  absence  of  an  appropriate
zoning  classification  scheme  forced  respondents  to  seek
a  zoning  classification  which  would  allow  the  individual
townhouse  type  construction  (517  F.  2d  410).

Second,  the  District  Court  found  that  ‘‘Plaintiffs  did
prove  that  the  ‘highest  and  best  use’  of  this  property  from
an  economic  standpoint  is  for  multi-family  dwellings.’’
(373  F.Supp.  209).*

Third,  and  most  important,  the  public  interest  considera-

tions  favor  development  of  Lincoln  Green.

As  stated  by the  court  in  Stalzer  v.  Village  of Matteson,
303  N.E.  2d  489  (Ill.  App.  1973),  the  use  of  property  can-
not  be  restricted  or  limited  merely  because  neighboring
property  owners  so  desire,  or  because  they  think  it might
protect  the  value  of  their  residences.
In  upholding  the
plaintiffs’  right  to  develop  his  parcel  for  a  planned  unit
multi-family  development  there,  the  court  stated:

No  gain  of any  kind  to  the  general  public  appears  from
the  R-1  zoning.  It  would  appear  that  the  public  in-
terest  would  be  better  served  by creation  of  an  apart-
ment  project  which  would  make  more  than  200  apart-
ments  available  for  family  use  at  reasonable  rentals.
(emphasis  added)  303  N.E.  2d  at  495.

The  public  interest  will  be  served  by Lincoln  Green,  which
will  begin  to  satisfy  the  desperate  need  for  housing  for

*Any  diminution  in  neighboring  home  values  attributable  to  the
proposed  development  would  be  merely a reflection  of  the  prejudice
of  the  local  homeowners,  as  the  District  Court  acknowledged  when
it noted  that  the  hostility  of  these  residents  may  have  been  a factor.
373  F.  Supp.  209.

38

39

low  and  moderate  income  persons  outside  the  inner-city
in a  booming  area  rich  in jobs.*

No  compelling  justification  exists  for  the  refusal  to  per-
mit  construction  of  Lincoln  Green  and  therefore  such  re-
fusal  is  a  violation  of  the  Equal  Protection  Clause  of  the
Fourteenth  Amendment.

II.

PETITIONERS’  REFUSAL  TO  PERMIT  DEVELOP-
MENT  OF  LINCOLN  GREEN  VIOLATES  THE  FAIR
HOUSING  ACT  AND  CIVIL  RIGHTS  ACTS.

A.  Introduction

The  Court  of Appeals  concluded  that  Arlington  Heights’
rejection  of  the  Lincoln  Green  proposal  violated  the  Equal
Protection  Clause  since  it  had  racially  discriminatory  ef-
fects  which  could  not  be  adequately  justified  by petitioners.
The  Court  below  did  not  go  on  to  consider  the  other  bases
on  which  petitioners’  action  was  also  challenged.  Its  con-
clusion,  however,  that  the  rejection  of  Lincoln  Green  would
have  the  effect  of  perpetuating  residential  segregation  in

*In  support  of  the  decision  below,  the  District  Court  referred  to
the  “integrity  of  the  Village’s  zoning  plan.”  The  evidence  shows,
however,  that  the  Arlington  Heights  comprehensive  plan  was  de-
veloped  in  1959  (Tr.  812)  when  its  population  was  about  one-third
its  present  size  and  at  a  time  when  planning  for  residential  uses
(Tr.  812).  As  the  evidence  showed,
was  limited  to  single-family.
the  only  multi-family  residences  built  in  Arlington  Heights  are  the
product  of  rezonings  such  as  that  requested  by  the  respondents.
Even  with  the  advent  of  substantial  industry  in  the  area,  no  provi-
sion  whatsoever  was  made  to  update  the  Village’s  comprehensive
plan  to  accommodate  the  needs  of  the  low  and  moderate  income
persons  working  there.

Arlington  Heights  is  also  sufficient  to  establish  a  violation
of  the  1968  Fair  Housing  Act,  whose  purpose  is  to  ‘‘re-
place  the  ghettos  by  truly  integrate]  and  balanced  living
patterns’’.  Trafficante  409  U.S.  205,  211.

Specifically,  petitioner’s  conduct  violates  $§804  (a)  and
817  of the  Fair  Housing  Act  (Title  VIII  of  the  Civil  Rights
Act  of  1968,  82  Stat.  81,  42  U.S.C.  §$3601  et  seq.)  and  the
Civil  Rights  Act  of  1866  (42  U.S.C.  $1982)  by  making
housing  for  minority  persons  unavailahle*  and  by interfer-
ring  with  the  provision  of  housing  opportunities  to  such
persons.**  E.g.,  United  States  v.  City  of Black  Jack,  Mis-

1042  (1975).  Apart  from  whatever  affirmative  responsi-
bilities  petitioners  may  have,  based  on  their  exploitation
of  the  patterns  of  severe  and  growing  residential  segrega-
tion  in  Arlington  Heights,  the  municipality  has  at  least
the  duty  under  the  Fair  Housing  Act  to  use  its  zoning
powers  so  as  not  to  interfere  with  private  efforts  which

.  a  dwelling  to  any  person  because  of  race.”

(a)  (42  U.S.C.  §3604  (a))  makes  it  unlawful  to  “make  unavailable

B.  Arlington  Heights’  conduct  had  the  effect  of perpetuat-
ing  segregation,  thereby  placing  a  burden  on  Arling-
ton  Heights  to  show  a  compelling  non-racial  justifica-
tion  for  the  refusal.

In  recent  decades,  Arlington  Heights  has  become a large,
99.9%  white  city  through  zoning  and  other  municipal  de-
cisions  permitting  the  development  of  thousands  of  high
rent  housing  units  that  have  not  been  made  available  to
Clark,  supra.  As  the  Court  of  Appeals  con-
minorities.
cluded,  petitioners  have  not  merely  ignored  the  segregated
housing  patterns  in  their  area;  they  have  exploited  them.
Here,  they  have,  without  compelling  justification,  obstruct-
ed  the  only  development  which  would  have  provided  mi-
norities  with  low-cost,  affirmative  marketed  housing  units,
the  only  development  that  could  have  appreciably  reduced
segregation  within  the  Village.  It  was  precisely  this  type
of  municipal  zoning  interference  with  private  efforts  to
develop  and  market  low  and  moderate  income  housing  for
minorities  in  places  where  those  opportunities  had  been
unavailable  before  that  the  Eighth  Circuit  held  violated  the
Fair  Housing  Act  in  United  States  v.  City  of Black  Jack,
Missouri,  supra.

The  justifications  advanced  by Arlington  Heights  for  its
refusal  to  approve  Lincoln  Green  are  not  persuasive.  Since

*  Contrary  to  the  suggestion  often  made  by  petitioners,  respon-
dents  never  claimed  that  they  had  a  “right  to  housing”  or  a  right
They  are  not  asking  Arlington
to  live  anywhere  in  particular.
Heights  to  build  housing  for  them.  (Compare  Shapiro  v.  Thompson,
394  U.S.  618  (1969)  and  Memorial  Hospital  vy.  Maricopa  County,
94  S.Ct.  1076  (1974),  which  did  require  that  the  state  or  county
spend  money  on  the  injured  class,  with  the  record  here  which  estab-
lishes  that  Arlington  Heights  will  benefit  financially  if Lincoln  Green
is  built.)

the  development  would  significantly  reduce  segregation  by
increasing  the  number  of  blacks  in  the  Village  ten  times
over,  it  comes  within  the  protection  of  the  Fair  Housing
Act  and  petitioners’  efforts  to  obstruct  it  should  be  en-
joined.
As  the  Eighth  Circuit  observed  in  Black  Jack,
the  Fair  Housing  Act  requires  removal  of  the  artificial,
arbitrary,  and  unnecessary  barriers  to  housing  opportu-
nities  when  those  barriers  operate  invidiously  to  discrimi-
nate  on  the  basis  of  race.  ‘‘The  discretion  of  local  zoning
officials,  recently  recognized  in  Village  of  Belle  Terre  v.
Boraas,  416  U.S.  1  (1974),  must  be  curtailed  where  the
clear  result  of  such  discretion  is  the  segregation  of  low
income  Blacks  from  all  White  neighborhoods
[citing
eases  |.’’  508  F.2d  at  1184.

Petitioners  misconstrue  their  responsibilities  under  the
Fair  Housing  Act  and  the  decision  below  when  they  argue
that  the  logic  of  this  decision  would  dictate  that  there  can
no  longer  be  any  single-family  zoning  district  in  any  mu-
nicipality  if  a  developer  seeks  to  build  housing  which  he
can  show  might  be  occupied  by  a  greater  percentage  of  a
minority  group  than  presently  existed  in  that  municipal-
(Pet.  Br.  at  16)  On  the  contrary,  all  that  is  to  be
ity.
determined  here  is  that  the  single-family  zoning  classifica-
tion  of  a  long  vacant  parcel,  such  as  the  one  here,  cannot
be  used  to  frustrate  a  unique  development  which  the  de-
veloper  proves,  because  of  the  nature  of  the  housing  and
of  his  marketing  program,  would  in  fact  be  significantly
more  integrated  than  the  community  and  thereby  would
appreciably  decrease  segregation  in  the  community  when
the  municipality  has  no  compelling  reasons  to  prevent  the
development  from  going  forward.

Petitioners  also  misconstrue  the  responsibilities  imposed
by the  Fair  Housing  Act  and  the  decision  below  when  they
argue  that  the  Seventh  Circuit  determined  ‘‘that  Arlington
Heights  has  an  affirmative  duty  to  ignore  its  own  Zoning

OE

42

43

Ordinance  in  order  to  take  affirmative  action  to  resolve
the  alleged  segregation  pattern  of  housing  in  the  Chicago
metropolitan  area.’’  (Pet.  Br.  at  25)  The  law  of  this  case
imposes.no  affirmative  duty  on  Arlington  Heights.  It  seeks
to  prevent  municipal  interference  with  MHDC’s  private
creation  and  distribution  of  desegregated  housing  oppor-
tunities  for  minorities,  not  to  impose  upon  Arlington
Heights  an  affirmative  duty  to  create  housing  opportunities
or  incur  any  cost  in  the  creation  of  such  opportunities  by
the  private  sector.

The  Second  Circuit  has  explicitly  recognized  this  dis-
tinction.  Thus,  while  refusing  to  impose  upon  a  county  ‘‘an
affirmative  duty  to  construct  housing,’’  Acevedo  v.  Nassau
County,  500  F.  2d  1078,  1081  (2d  Cir.  1974)  or  to  order  a
city  affirmatively  to  take  steps  to  insure  that  a  subsidized
housing  development  would  be  built,  Citizens  Committee
for  Faraday  Wood  v.  Lindsay,  507  F.  2d  1065  (2nd  Cir.
1974),  cert.  denied,  421  U.S.  948  (1975),  it  reaffirmed  that
.  the  governmental
Courts  should  continue  to  enjoin  ‘‘.  .
obstruction  of  private  projects  beneficial
to  minority
groups  or  to  integration’’  (dcevedo,  500  F.  2d  at  1081).  In
language  particularly  relevant  to  the  case  at  bar,  the  Sec-
ond  Circuit  concluded  that:

...  the  City  of  Lackawanna  was  clearly  segregated
.  Against  this  background  we  held  that  the  city
could  not,  absent  a  compelling  interest,  thwart  the
efforts  of  a  private  organization  to  build  housing  for
low-income  families  in  the  white  area  of  the  city  by,
among  other  things,  refusing  to  accede  to  a  reasonable
rezoning  request.  Other  courts  have  reached  similar
results.  Faraday  Wood,  507  F.  2d  at  1069  (Citations
omitted).

No  affirmative  duty  is sought  to  be  imposed  upon  Arling-
ton  Heights;  the  injunction  sought  is  prohibitory,  not
mandatory.  Arlington  Heights  will  not  be  asked  to  incur

any  costs  of  the  development.*  All  that  is  sought  is  an  in-
junction  restraining  the  respondents  from  interfering
with  the  development  of  Lincoln  Green.

C.  The  Acts  here  complained  of  violate  Title  VIII,  the
Fair  Housing  Act,  which  is as  broad  as  Congress  could
have  made  it,  and  other  civil  rights  laws  as  well.

Congress  has  declared  that  the  purpose  of  the  Fair
Housing  Act  is  ‘‘to  provide,  within  constitutional  limita-
tions,  for  fair  housing  throughout  the  United  States’’  (42
U.S.C.  §3601),  and  this  Court  has  made  clear  that  the  Act
should  be  given  a  ‘‘generous  construction’”’  to  effectuate
its  purpose  of  replacing  ‘‘the  ghettos  by  truly  integrated
and  balanced  living  patterns.’’  Trafficante  v.  Metropolitan
Life  Insurance  Co.,  409  U.S.  205,  211  (1972).**  HUD  has
administratively  established  criteria  to  provide  for  integra-
tion.***

*As  the  Court  of  Appeals  noted,  the  development  would  have  a
positive  tax  impact  upon  the  Village  and  the  school  district  (see
supra  at  9);  they  will  both  receive  more  in  tax  dollars  from  the
development  than  they  will  expend  in  servicing  Lincoln  Green  and
its  residents.  Compare  Valtierra,  402  U.S.  137,  143  and  Palmer,
403  U.S.  217,  228-30,  where  the  majority  and  two  concurring  jus-
tices,  respectively,  expressed  reluctance  in  granting  relief  because
the  municipality  would  be  forced  to  incur  perpetual  tax  loss.

**  Clearly,  Congress  saw  the  1968  Fair  Housing  Act  as  a  vehicle
to  eliminate  the  barriers  that  prevent  movement  by blacks  into  white
areas  and  to  remedy  segregated  housing  patterns  and  the  problems
they  cause—segregated  schools,  the  unavailability  of  suburban  em-
ployment  opportunities  to  minorities,  and  the  alienation  of  white
and  black  Americans  due  to  “lack  of  experience  in  actually  living
114  Cong.  Rec.  2274-76.
next”  to  each  other.
See  also  the  further  discussion  by  Senator  Mondale  at  114  Cong.

Rec.  at  2279-80,  9559,  and  9590.

***HUD’s  Project  Selection  Criteria  applicable  to  FHA  §236
Housing  24  C.F.R.  part  200,  P.H.E.O.H.  Rptr.  Para.  4311  include
requirements  that  need  for  subsidized  housing  be  considered  in  site
selection  along  with  providing  minority  opportunities  outside  exist-
ing areas  of minority  concentration.

Dn

44

45

In  construing  the  Civil  Rights  Act  of  1866  to  prohibit

housing  discrimination,  this  Court  declared  that

...  When  racial  discrimination  herds  men  into  ghettos
and  makes  their  ability  to  buy  property  turn  on  the
color  of  their  skin,  then  it  too  is  a  relic  of  slavery.
Jones  v.  Mayer  Co.,  392  U.S.  409,  442,  443  (1968).

The  next  year,  this  Court  reaffirmed  that  a  ‘‘narrow
construction  of  the  language  of  $1982  would  be  quite  in-
consistent  with  the  broad  and  sweeping  nature  of  the  pro-
tection  meant  to  be  afforded’’  by  it.  Sullivan  v.  Little
Hunting  Park,  396  U.S.  229,  237  (1969).*

Lower  courts  have  consistently  reaffirmed  that  the  pur-
pose  of  the  Fair  Housing  Act  is  to  foster  residential  in-
tegration.  As  the  Second  Circuit  has  observed,  the  goal
is  ‘‘open,  integrated  residential  housing  patterns  and
to  prevent  the  increase  of  segregation,  in  ghettos,  of  racial
groups  whose  lack  of  opportunities  the  Act  was  designed
to  combat.’’  Otero  v.  New  York  City  Housing  Authority,
484  F.2d  1122,  1134  (2nd  Cir.  1973);  see  also  Crow  v.
Brown,  332  F.  Supp.  382,  391  (N.D.  Ga.  1971),  aff’d,  457
F.  2d  788  (5th  Cir.  1972);  Barrick  Realty  v.  City  of Gary,
491  F.2d  161,  164  (7th  Cir.  1974).

*  §1982  is designed  to  eliminate  housing  practices,  whether  overtly
discriminatory  or  not,  which  have  the  unjustified  effect  of  perpetu-
ating  residential  segregation.
In  Clark  v.  Universal  Builders,  501
F.2d  324,  331  (7th  Cir.  1974),  cert.  denied,  419  U.S.  1070  (1974),
sellers  who  exploited  the  dual  housing  market  by  selling  to  blacks
at  inflated  prices  were  held  to  have  violated  §1982  in  part  because
their  actions  “prolong  and  perpetuate  a  system  of  racial  residential
segregation.  .
.  .”  (citation  omitted).  The  court  went  on  to  rule
that  discrimination  against  black  citizens  could  not  be  perpetuated,
even  if  it  was  not  of  the  actor’s  own  making  and  even  if  the  acts
having  the  discriminatory  effect  were  ‘‘well-intentioned,  good-faith
efforts...  .”  501  F.2d  at  332.

Specifically,  the  lower  courts  have  not  shirked  from
carrying  out  this  clear  Congressional  intent  by  holding
that  municipal  exclusion  of low  and  moderate  income  hous-
ing  violates  the  1968  Act,  where,  as  here,  that  municipal
action  has  the  effect  of  perpetuating  residential  segrega-
tion  and  is  not  justified  by  compelling  state  interests.
United  States  v.  City  of Black  Jack,  supra,  Kennedy  Park
Homes  v.  City  of  Lackawanna,  318  F.  Supp.  (W.D.N.Y.
1970),  aff’d,  436  F.  2d  108  (2nd  Cir.  1970),  cert.  denied,
401  U.S.  1010  (1971)  ; see  also  Sisters  of Providence  v.  City
of Evanston,  335  F.  Supp.  396  (N.D.  Ill.  1971);  cf.  United
Farm  Workers  of  Florida  Housing  Project,  Inc.  v.  City
of Delray  Beach,  493  F.2d  799  (5th  Cir.  1974).

In  Black  Jack,  for  instance,  the  Eighth  Circuit  struck
down  a  zoning  ordinance  that  prevented  construction  of  a
subsidized,  integrated  development  such  as  Lincoln  Green
in  an  all  white  area,  despite  a  finding  below  that  the  mu-
nicipality  had  not  been  shown  to  have  acted  with  racially
discriminatory  motives.  After  reviewing  the  broad  scope
and  remedial  purposes  of  the  Fair  Housing  Act,  the  Court
of  Appeals  held  that  the  respondent  in  such  a  case

‘‘need  prove  no  more  than  that  the  conduct  of  the
defendant  actually  or  predictably  results  in  racial  dis-
crimination;  in  other  words,  that  it  has  a  discrimina-
tory  effect  ...  The  respondent  need  make  no  showing
whatsoever  that  the  action  resulting  in  racial  discrimi-
nation  in  housing  was  racially  motivated.  Effect,  not
motivation,  is  the  touchstone,  in  part  because  clever
men  may  easily  conceal  their  motivations,  but  more
importantly,  because

...  [whatever  our  law  was  once,  ...  we  now  firmly
recognize  that  the  arbitrary  quality  of  thoughtlessness
can  be  as  disastrous  and  unfair  to  private  rights  and
the  public  interest  as  the  perversity  of  willful  scheme.

46

47

United  States  v.  City  of  Black  Jack,  Missouri,  508  F.2d
at  1184-1185  (citations  and  footnotes  omitted.)

The  Eighth  Circuit  then  went  on  to  reverse  the  lower
court’s  determination  that  the  challenged  zoning  ordinance
had  no  discriminatory  effect.  The  trial  judge  in  Black
Jack  had  focused,  as  petitioners  attempt  to  do  here,  only
on  the  statistics  with  respect  to  eligible  residents  that
showed  that  the  obstruction  of  the  development  would
have  a  slight  disproportionate  impact  on  minorities.  The
Court  of  Appeals  there,  as  the  Seventh  Circuit  did  here,
noted  that  such  a  myopic  view  failed  to  take  into  account
either  the  ‘‘ultimate  effect’’  or  the  ‘‘historical  context,’’
which  specifically  included  assessment  of  the  municipali-
ty’s  action  in  light  of  the  overall  segregated  housing  pat-
terns  in  the  greater  St.  Louis  area.  508  F.2d  at  1186.  Al-
though  Black  Jack  had  not  caused  these  segregation  pat-
terns  to  develop  (as  petitioners  foreswear  responsibility
here),

‘‘Black  Jack’s  action  is but  one  more  factor  confining
blacks  to  low-income  housing  in  the  center  city,  con-
firming  the  inexorable  process  whereby  the  St.  Louis
metropolitan  area  becomes  one  that  has  the  racial
shape  of  a  donut,  with  the  Negroes  in  the  hole  and
with  mostly  whites  occupying  the  ring...  .’’  id.

Since  the  proposed  development  ‘‘was  particularly  de-
signed  to  contribute  to  the  prevention  of  this  prospect  so
antithetical  to  the  Fair  Housing  Act’’  (id.),  since  there  was
ample  proof  that  many  blacks  would  live  in  the  develop-
ment  and  since  the  ‘‘exclusion  of the  townhouses  would  con-
tribute  to  the  perpetuation  of  segregation  in  a  community
which  was  99  percent  white’’  (id.),  the  Eighth  Circuit  de-

termined  that  the  zoning  decision  violated  $804(a)  and
§817  of  the  Fair  Housing  Act.*

D.  This  Court’s  interpretation  of  similar  legislation  with
similar  provisions  shows  that  the  complained-of  action
violates  the  Fair  Housing  Act.

This  Court  has  yet  to  review  an  exclusionary  zoning
case  under  the  Fair  Housing  Act.  Its  determination  to
construe  similar  remedial  legislation  in  an  expansive  man-
ner  to  effectuate  the  Congressional  commitment  to  equal
opportunity,  however,  strongly  suggests  that  the  Attor-
ney  General  and  the  Eighth  Circuit  are  correct  in  inter-
preting  §804(a)  and  $817  to  prohibit  petitioners’  obstruc-
tion  of  Lincoln  Green.  In  Griggs  v.  Duke  Power  Co.,  401
U.S.  424  (19....),  for  example,  a  unanimous  court  invali-

*In  both  Black  Jack  and  Lackawanna,  supra,  the  Attorney  Gen-
eral,  who  has  enforcement  responsibilities  under  the  Act  in  certain
circumstances  (see  42  U.S.C.  §3613),  successfully  argued  that  §§ 804
(a)  and  817  should  be  construed  in  precisely  the  fashion  suggested
by  respondents  here.

.  [t]he  contention  that  this  is  not  an  “interference”  case

interfered  with  and  prevented  the  implementation  of  the  plan

interference  case  is  established.  By  the  same  token,  the  city
made  dwellings  unavailable  to  Negroes  within  the  meaning  of
42  U.S.C.  3604(a)  for,  were  it  not  for  the  city’s  action,  the
land  would  have  been  obtained  and  in  all  probability  the  homes
would  have  been  constructed.  What  needs  to  be  proved—and,
in  our  view,  has  been  proved—is  that  the  actions  were  dis-
criminatory.

dated  a  job  test  that  excluded  a  higher  proportion  of

Title  VII  was  not  to  be  limited  to  racially-explicit  classifi-

‘‘The  Act  prescribes  not  only  overt  dis-
crimination  but  also  practices  that  are  fair  in  form,  but

and  devices  which  have  fostered  racially  stratified  job  en-
