.

The  operative  language  of  the  Fair  Housing  Act  and
the  Equal  Employment  Opportunity  Act  are  virtually
identical  in  describing  the  discriminatory  practices  that
they  prohibit.  §804(a)  makes  unlawful  housing  practices
performed  ‘‘because  of  race.’’
Title  VII  prohibits  em-
ployment  practices  performed  ‘‘because  of  an  individual’s
(42  U.S.C.  §2000c-2(a)  (1)  and  (2)).  This  Court
race.’?

in  Griggs  ruled  that  Title  VII  prohibits  the  use  of  employ-
ment  tests  that  disproportionately  exclude  blacks  from
job  opportunities,  since  they  discriminated  against  blacks
‘‘because  of  ...  race.’?  The  same  language  in  Title  VIII
should  be  similarly  construed  to  prohibit  housing  prac-
tices  that  exclude  minorities  from  desegrated  housing  op-
portunities  and  that  create  the  ‘‘built-in  headwinds’’  and

.

ployment  field  are  no  longer  permissible.*

In  summary,  then,  the  language  and  legislative  history
of  the  Fair  Housing  Act,  its  interpretation  by  the  courts
and  the  Attorney  General,  its  administrative  and  judicial
interpretation  and  judicial  construction  of  similar  civil
rights  legislation  mandate  a  conclusion  that  $§ 804(a)  and
§817  invalidate  petitioners’  decision  to  prevent  construc-
tion  of  Lincoln  Green  since  the  decision  denies  minorities
desegregated  housing  opportunities  in  an  all-white  area
without  compelling  public  justifications.

Ill.

ALL  OF  THE  RESPONDENTS  HAVE  STANDING  BE-
CAUSE  THEY  HAVE  A  SUFFICIENT  STAKE  IN  THE
OUTCOME  OF  THE  LITIGATION.

This  Court  held  in  Trafficanti,  the  words  of  the  Act
showed  ‘‘a  congressional  intention  to  define  standing  as

*  Just  as  Title  VII  was  designed  to  “achieve  equality  of  employ-
ment  opportunities,  Griggs,  401  U.S.  424,  429,  so  Title  VIII  was
designed  to  insure  equality  of  housing  opportunities.  Indeed,  under
the  facts  here  and  in  similar  exclusionary  zoning  cases  such  as
Black  Jack  that  dramatically  demonstrate  how  access  to  growing
numbers  of  jobs  may  turn  largely  upon  access  to  desegregated  sub-
urban  housing,  it  would  defeat  the  purposes  of  Title  VII  if the  Fair
Housing  Act  were  held  inapplicable  here,  since  not  only  would  mi-
norities’  opportunities  to  live  in  Arlington  Heights  be  prevented,
but  their  chances  of  working  there  would  be  limited  as  well.

50

ol

broadly  as  is permitted  by Article  IIT  of the  Constitution.’’
409  U.S.  at  209.  Petitioner  did  not  present  the  question  of
standing  to  the  Court  of  Appeals  for  review,  and,  accord-
ingly,  the  Court  of  Appeals  did  not  consider  the  issue.
This  Court  generally  does  not  consider  issues  neither
raised  nor  considered  by  the  Courts  of  Appeals.  See  e.g.,
Adickes  v.  S.  H.  Kress  and  Co.,  398  U.S.  144,  147,  n.  2
(1970)  and  cases  cited.  In  any  event,  all  of the  respondents
have  standing;  numerous  exclusionary  zoning  cases  estab-
lish  that  both  the  corporate  and  individual  respondents  here
have  a  sufficient  stake  in  the  outcome  of  this  litigation  to
make  them  proper  parties.  F.g.,  Park  View  Heignts  Corp.
v.  City  of Black  Jack,  467  F.  2d  1205,  1212-1214  (8th  Cir.
1972)  ; Kennedy  Park  Homes  Ass’n.  v.  City  of Lackawanna,
436  F.  2d  108,  111-112  (2d  Cir.  1970),  cert.  denied.

A.  The  individual  respondents  have  standing.

The  individual  respondents  are  minority  citizens  of  the
United  States  unable  to  find  housing  they  could  afford  in
Arlington  Heights  (see,  e.g.,  Tr.  220-2).
They  would  be
eligible  for  Lincoln  Green.  (see,  e.g.  Tr.  43-44:  Ps.  Ex.  24
(Ex.  B);  and  Tr.  207-208).  Under  Warth  v.  Seldin,  45  L.
Ed.  2d  343  (1975)  they  have  standing  to  sue  since  ‘‘absent
the  (petitioners’)  restrictive  zoning  practices,  there  is  a
substantial  probability  that  they  would  be  able  to...
lease  in  (Arlington  Heights)  and  that,  if  the  court  affords
the  relief  requested,  the  asserted  inability  of  (respondents)

*  Petitioners  assert,  without  record  reference,  that  “the  trial  court
found  (that)  Ransom  and  Guthrie  are  not  even  proper  parties  to
this  action.”  (Brief  46).  The  trial  court  made  no  such  finding,  but
rather  adjudicated  their  substantive  rights  and  thereby  treated  the
two  as  proper  parties.

would  be  removed.’’  Warth,  45  L.  Ed.  2d  343,  358.  Unlike
the  Warth  plaintiffs  whose  inability  to  live  in  Penfield
stemmed  not  from  an  unavailability  of low  and  moderate  in-
come  housing,  which  they  could  not  afford*  (except  for
Reyes,  whose  income  was  too  high;  see  Warth  at  359,  n.16),
but  from  the  ‘‘economics  of the  area  housing  market’’  which
prevented  the  construction  of  housing  they  could  afford,
the  inability  of  the  individual  respondents  in  the  instant
case  to  live  in  Arlington  Heights  stems  directly  from  the
fact  that  the  petitioners  have  interfered  with  the  private
housing  market  and  prevented  the  construction  of  Lincoln
Green.

B.  MHDC  has  standing.

Respondent  MHDC  also  has  standing.  It  can  assert  its
own  rights,  since  it is being  directly  harmed  by petitioners’
conduct.  Its  corporate  purpose  is  to  build  low  and  mod-
erate  income  housing,  and  the  petitioners  are  directly  frus-
trating  that  purpose.  It  has  also  expended  over  $38,000
on  the  proposal.

It  also  has  standing  to  raise  the  rights  of  the  potential
tenants,  since  they  are  being  harmed  by  petitioners’  con-
duct.
See  Park  View  Heights,  467  F.  2d  1208,  1213-14:
Lackwanna,  436  F.  2d  108,  cert.  dented,  401  U.S.  1010;
Dailey,  425  F.  2d  10387,  ............  ; and  Sisters  of Providence,
335  F.  Supp.  396.  This  Court  has  held  in  a  number  of
housing  cases  that  the  provider  of  housing  has  standing
to  raise  the  interests  of  the  black  customer  of  the  housing
when  the  provision  is  being  interfered  with.  See  Sullivan,

*  There  was  no  indication  that  they  “had  the  resources  necessary
to  acquire  the  housing  available  in  the  projects”  which  may  have
been  built,  Warth  at  359,  n.  16,  since  there  was  “not  the  slightest
suggestion  that  they  would  be  adequate,  and  of  sufficiently  low  cost,
to  meet  the  petitioners’  needs,”  Warth  at  359,  n.  15.

Respondents,

ELUTERIA  D.  MALDONADO,

ON  WRIT  OF  CERTIORARI  TO  THE  UNITED  STATES  COURT
OF  APPEALS  FOR  THE  SEVENTH  CIRCUIT.

PETITIONERS’  SUPPLEMENTAL  BRIEF.

Introduction.

Since  the  filing  of  the  reply  brief  for  petitioners,  certain  sub-
sequent  decisions  of  this  Court  have  been  rendered  which  give
further  support  to  the  petitioners’  position  in  this  cause.  There-
fore,  pursuant  to  Rule  41  of  the  Supreme  Court  Rules,  peti-
tioners  submit  herewith  their  supplemental  brief  presenting  late
authorities.

%

THE  FAILURE  TO  GRANT  THE  REZONING  REQUEST  OF
THE  RESPONDENTS  DID  NOT  VIOLATE  THE  CIVIL
RIGHTS  ACTS,  THE  FAIR  HOUSING  LAW,  OR  THE
FOURTEENTH  AMENDMENT.

The  decision  of the  Court  of Appeals  in this  case  reversed  the
trial  court,  but  in doing  so  generally  sustained  the  findings  of the
trial  judge.  The  District  Court  refused  to  find  intentional  racial
discrimination  on  the  part  of the  petitioners.  The  District  Court
had  found  that  the  claim  of  the  denial  of  equal  protection  had
not  been  proven,  and  the  Court  of  Appeals  declined  to  hold
that  that  finding  was  clearly  erroneous.  517  F.  2d  409,  412.
The  Court  of  Appeals  also  held  that  the  District  Court’s  find-
ing that  the  petitioners  were  concerned  with  the  integrity  of  the
Village’s  zoning  plan  was  not  erroneous.  517  F.  2d  409,  412.
The  Court  of Appeals  further  recognized  that  merely  because  a
racial  minority  constituted  a  higher  percentage  of the  lower  and
moderate  income  category,  this  disparity  did  not  necessarily  mean
that  the  zoning  change  refusal  had  the  type  of  racially  dis-
criminatory  effect  that  required  the  invocation  of the  compelling
interest  test.  The  Court  of Appeals  conceded  that  the Village  had
not  created  the  segregated  housing  pattern  which  that  Court  said
existed  in the  entire  Chicago  area.  517  F.  2d  409,  414.

The  trial  court  concluded  that  the  weight  of  the  evidence
proved  that  the  Village  was  motivated  by a legitimate  desire  to
protect  property  values  and  the  integrity  of the  Village’s  zoning
plan.  The  Court  of  Appeals  did  not  challenge  these  findings,
but  held  that  the  diminution  of neighboring  property  values  was
a  purely  private  monetary  consideration,  and  in  this  instance
was  not  a  compelling  interest.  While  recognizing  that  there  was
no  discriminatory  administration  with  respect  to  the  Arlington
Heights  Zoning  Ordinance,  the  Court  of  Appeals  based  its  de-
cision  on  a  purported  violation  of the  Fourteenth  Amendment.

3

In  the  recent  case  of  Hills  v.  Gautreaux,  ...........  a

eaten

47  L.  Ed.  2d  792  (April  20,  1976),  the  Court  recognized  that
any  effort  to  remedy  admitted  segregation  in  public  housing  by
remedial  court  orders  in  a  metropolitan-wide  area  must  be  sub-
ject to the  police  power  of municipalities  in which  public  housing
was  to  be located.  The  Court  pointed  out  that  the  use  of the  pro-
gram  to  expand  low  income  housing  opportunities  outside  areas
of  minority  concentration  would  not  have  a  coercive  effect  on
suburban  municipalities.  At 47  L. Ed.  2d  808,  this  Court  said:

“For  under  the  program,  the  local  governmental  units  re-
tain  the  right  to  comment  on  specific  assistance  proposals,
to  reject  certain  proposals  that  are  inconsistent  with  their
approved  housing  assistance  plans,  and  to  require  that  zon-
ing  and  other  land  use  restrictions  be  adhered  to  by
builders.”

The  Court  went  on  to  say:

“The  remedial  decree  would  neither  force  suburban  govern-
ments  to  submit  public  housing  proposals  to  HUD  nor  dis-
place  the  rights  and  powers  accorded  local  government
entities  under  federal  or  state  housing  statutes  or  existing
land  use  laws.”

Thus,  it  is  clear  that  any  court  ordered  efforts  to  cure  the
so-called  segregated  housing  market  found  to exist  in the  Chicago
metropolitan  area  must  be  subject  to  the  zoning  and  land  use
restrictions  of the  communities  where  low  and  moderate  income
housing  is to  be  placed.  Where,  as  here,  the  Zoning  Ordinance  of
the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights  is  admittedly  a  reasonable  ex-
ercise  of  the  legislative  discretion  vested  in  the  corporate  au-
thorities  of  the  Village,  which  was  held  invalid  only  because  the
Court  of  Appeals  attempted  to  apply  a  compelling  interest  test
in  light  of  the  metropolitan  area  situation,  the  doctrine  of
Hills  v.  Gautreaux  requires  that  such a legitimate  land  use  and
zoning  restriction  should  be  sustained.

Also  in  point  was  this  Court’s  recent  decision  in  the  case  of
Washington  v.  Davis,  ...........  oe  We  es  ,  48  L.  Ed.  2d  597

4

5

(June  7,  1976).  In  that  case  this  Court  repeated  its  previous
holdings  to  the  effect  that  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  did  not
require  striking  down  public  acts  in  the  absence  of  proof  of
discriminatory  racial  purpose  merely  because  there  was  a  sub-
stantial  disproportionate  racial  impact.  In  that  case  the  Court
was  concerned  with  a  qualifying  examination  administered  to
prospective  police  officers.  It  was  claimed  that  there  had  been
a  violation  of  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  because  a  dispropor-
tionate  number  of Negroes  were  excluded.  At  48  L.  Ed.  2d  610,
this  Court  said:

“Both  before  and  after  Palmer  v.  Thompson,  however,
various  Courts  of  Appeals  have  held  in  several  contexts,
including  public  employment,  that  the  substantially  dis-
proportionate  racial  impact  of  a  statute  or  official  practice
standing  alone  and  without  regard  to  discriminatory  pur-
pose,  suffices  to  prove  racial  discrimination  violating  the
Equal  Protection  Clause  absent  some  justification  going
substantially  beyond  what  would  be  necessary  to  validate
most  other  legislative  classifications.’*  The  cases  impressively
demonstrate  that  there  is  another  side  to  the  issue;  but,
with  all due  respect,  to  the  extent  that  those  cases  rested  on
or  expressed  the  view  that  proof  of  discriminatory  racial
purpose  is unnecessary  in  making  out  an  equal  protection
violation,  we  are  in  disagreement”.

In  footnote  12  at  page  610,  the  Court  listed  a  number  of  lower
court  opinions  which  expressed  the  view  that  proof  of  dis-
criminatory  racial  purpose  was  unnecessary.  Among  those  cases
specifically  noted  and  which  were  specifically  disapproved  was
the  Court  of  Appeals’  decision  in  the  instant  case.

We  respectfully  suggest  that  the  recent  decisions  in  Hills  v.
Gautreaux  and  Washington  v.  Davis  are  totally  consistent  with
the  previous  decisions  of  this  Court  cited  in  petitioners’  original
and  reply briefs.  The  unquestionable  conclusion  to be drawn  from
these  decisions  is  that,  in  the  absence  of  proof  of  purposeful
racial  discrimination,  the  mere  fact  a  greater  percentage  of
blacks  might  occupy  Lincoln  Green  than  other  housing  in

Arlington  Heights  was  insufficient  to  invalidate  the  Zoning
Ordinance  of the  Village  as  it affected  the  subject  property.  Both
the  trial  court  and  the  Court  of Appeals  in  this  case  recognized
that  the Zoning  Ordinance  of the Village  was  based  upon  proper
planning  considerations  and  that  the  Village’s  efforts  were  under-
taken  to preserve  the  integrity  of that  ordinance.  No racial  motive
nor  discriminatory  practice  was  proven—none  existed.

As  this  Court  has  now  made  specific  in  Hills,  the  placing  of
low  or moderate  income  housing  in suburban  communities  is sub-
ject  to  existing  valid  zoning  ordinances  and  other  land  use
restrictions.  Tlie  mere  fact  that  low  or  moderate  income  housing
is involved  does  not  warrant  the  repudiation  of  otherwise  valid
police power  ordinances.  The  fact  that  a disproportionate  number
of  minority  persons  may  be  excluded  does  not  make  out  an
equal  protection  violation  absent  proof  of  discriminatory  racial
purpose.

Washington  v.  Davis  also  further  lays to rest  respondents’  argu-

ment  made  from  analogy  to  Title  VII  cases.

I.

THE  RESPONDENTS  FAILED  TO  OVERCOME  THE  PRE-
SUMPTION  OF  VALIDITY  ATTACHING  TO  THE  ZONING
ORDINANCE  OF  THE  VILLAGE
OF  ARLINGTON
HEIGHTS.

In  our  original  brief  we  argued  that  the  law  was  well  estab-
lished  that  a  Zoning  Ordinance  is  presumptively  valid  and  the
party  assailing  its  validity  must  show  by  clear  and  convincing
evidence  that  it is invalid.  If there  is any  room  for  a  reasonable
difference  of  opinion,  the  Court  will  not  substitute  its  judgment
for  that  of  the  legislative  body.

In  the  recent  decision  of Eastlake  v.  Forest  City  Enterprises,
ia ,  49  L.  Ed.  2d  132  (June  21,  1976),
OR.  iss.  i
this  Court  was  faced  with  an  attack  upon  a  charter  provision  of

6

7

the  City  of  Eastlake  which  required  a  referendum  to  approve  a
zoning  change.  This  Court  again  recognized  the  limits  of  con-
stitutional  protections  as  applied  to  property  owners  seeking  to
set  aside  a  zoning  restriction.  In  the  Eastlake  case,  as  in  the
instant  case,  there  was  no  contention  that  the  existing  zoning
classification  rendered  the  respondents’  property  valueless  or
diminished  its  value  below  the  value  when  the  respondents  ac-
quired  it.  In  the  instant  case,  the  subject  property  was  to  be
purchased  subject  to  rezoning  for  $300,000.  The  respondents’
own  witness  conceded  that  the  subject  property  was  worth  at
least  $375,000  under  its  existing  single-family  classification  (R.
471).  In  a  footnote  at  49  L.  Ed.  2d  138,  this  Court  in  the
Eastlake  case  recognized  that  mere  interference  with  a  property
owner's  desires  was  insufficient  to  invalidate  a  zoning  ordinance.
The  Court  said:

“8.  [5b]  By its nature,  zoning  “interferences”  significantly
with  owners’  uses  of  property.  It  is  hornbook  law  that
“Cmjere  diminution  of  market  value  or  interference  with
the  property  owner’s  personal  plans  and  desires  relative  to
his  property  is insufficient  to  invalidate  a  zoning  ordinance
or  to  entitle  him  to  a  variance  or  rezoning.”  8 McQuillan,
Municipal  Corporations  § 25.44,  at  111.  There  is,  of
course,  no  contention  in  this  case  that  the  existing  zoning
classification  renders  respondent’s  property  valueless  or
otherwise  diminishes  its  value  below  the  value  when  re-
spondent  acquired  it.”

At  page  139  this  Court  commented  on  its  landmark  decision
in Euclid  v.  Ambler  Realty  Co.,  272  U.  S.  365,  71  L.  Ed.  303,
47  S. Ct.  114  (1926).  In that  case  the  Court  held  that  a property
owner  could  challenge  a  zoning  restriction  if  the  measure  was
clearly  arbitrary  and  unreasonable,  having  no  substantial  relation
to  the  public  health,  safety,  morals  or  general  welfare.  But
this  Court  went  on  to  say  in  Eastlake  at  page  139,  in  language
particularly  appropriate  to  the  case  at  bar:

“But  no  challenge  of  the  sort  contemplated  in Euclid  v.
Ambler  is before  us.  The  Ohio  Supreme  Court  did  not  hold,

and  respondent  does  not  argue,  that  the  present  zoning
classification  under  Eastlake’s  comprehensive  ordinance
violates  the  principles  established  in  Euclid  v.  Ambler.  If
respondent  considers  the  referendum  result  itself  to  be  un-
reasonable,  the  zoning  restriction  is  open  to  challenge  in
state  court,  where  the  scope  of the  state  remedy  available  to
respondent  would  be  determined  as  a  matter  of  state  law,
as  well  as  under  Fourteenth  Amendment  standards.  That
being  so,  nothing  more  is required  by the  Constitution.”

We  suggest  that  the  evidence  in  the  trial  court,  as  recognized
by the  Court  of Appeals,  was  insufficient  to overcome  the  presum-
tion  of  validity  attaching  to  the  Zoning  Ordinance.  It  was  only
by  the  erroneous  use  of  the  compelling  interest  test  that  the
Court  of Appeals  was  able  to  transform  this  garden  variety  zon-
ing  case  into  an  equal  protection  cause.  This  Court's  recent
recognition  in  the  Eastlake  case  that  a  mere  interference  with
the  owner’s  plans  to  develop  his  property  is  insufficient  to  in-
validate  a  zoning  ordinance  clearly  is  applicable  to  the  case  at
bar.  The  respondents  failed  to  overcome  the  presumption  of
validity  attaching  to  the  Zoning  Ordinance  as  it related  to  the
subject  property.  The  mere  fact  that  a nondiscriminatory  Zoning
Ordinance,  administered  in  a  nondiscriminatory  manner,  might
result  in  the  exclusion  of some  low  income  people  from  an  area
zoned  for  single-family  purposes  does  not  elevate  a  conventional
zoning  controversy  to  the  level  of  an  attack  on  basic  constitu-
tional  rights.

CONCLUSION.

We  respectfully  direct  this  Court’s  attention  to  its  recent  de-
cisions  in the Hills,  Washington  and  Eastlake  cases  as  further  sup-
port  for  the  arguments  originally  advanced  by  the  petitioners
in  this  case.

Respectfully  submitted,

JACK  M.  SIEGEL,

39  South  LaSalle  Street,
Chicago,  Illinois  60603,
312-263-2968,

Attorney  for  Petitioners.

RESPONDENTS’  REPLY  TO
PETITIONERS’  SUPPLEMENTAL  BRIEF

Introduction

In  its  supplemental  brief,  Arlington  Heights  all  but
calls  for  repeal  of  the  Supremacy  Clause  in  exclusionary
zoning  matters  based  on  its  ‘‘analysis’’  of  three  cases  re-

2

-

3

cently  decided  by  this  Court  (Hills  v.  Gautreaua,  ........
We  eicenee ,  47  L.Ed.2d  792  (1976);  Washington  v.  Davis,
scales  US.  .........  48  L.Ed.2d  597  (1976);  and  Eastlake  v.
Forest  City  Enterprises,  Inc.,  ........  Lif  aureee ,  49  L.Ed.2d
132  (1976)  ). While  important  differences  of  opinion  charac-
terized  the  Court’s  rulings  in  each  of  these  cases,  the  re-
sults  reached  are  not  challenged  here.  What  respondents
do  vigorously  dispute,  however,  is  the  suggestion  that  any
of  these  decisions  sanctions  the  efforts  of  a  virtually  all-
white  community  to  perpetuate  the  massive  residential
segregation  of  its  growing  area  by  blocking  construction
of  190  attractively  designed  townhouses  of  two  stories  or
less  by  a  private,  non-profit  developer  that  ‘‘might  well
result  in  increasing  Arlington  Heights’  minority  popula-
tion  by over  one  thousand  percent.’’  517  F.2d  409,  414.

I,

WASHINGTON  v.  DAVIS:  EQUAL  PROTECTION

Washington  v.  Davis,  supra,  involved  an  equal  protec-
tion  challenge  to  a  qualifying  examination  for  prospective
police  officers  based  solely  on  the  test’s  discriminatory  im-
pact  in  screening  out  black  applicants.  No  claim  of  ‘‘in-
tentional  discrimination  or  purposeful  discriminatory  ac-
tions’?  was  made.  48  L.Ed.2d  597,  604.  In  sustaining  the
validity  of  the  test,  the  Court  rejected  the  proposition  that
its  disproportionate  racial  impact  ‘‘standing  alone  and
without  regard  to  discriminatory  purpose’’  would  trigger
the  rule  that  official  acts  are  subject  to  the  strictest  judicial
scrutiny  under  the  Equal  Protection  Clause  when  racial
classifications  are  involved.  Jd.,  at  609-610.

In the  instant  case  the  Court  of Appeals  held  that  ‘‘  racial
disparity  alone  as  it  relates  to  the  housing  project  under

consideration  does  not  amount  to  racial  discrimination.”
517  F.2d  409,  416.  Thus  the  Washington  injunction  against
an  equal  protection  claim  solely  on  the  disproportionate
impact  of  the  challenged  action  is  not  violated.

The  Court’s  opinion  in  Washington  recognized  that  the
necessary  discriminatory  purpose  need  not  be  express  nor
appear  on  the  face  of  the  statute  and  that  a  law’s  dispro-
portionate  impact  is  certainly  a  relevant  factor,  if  not  the
relevani  factor,  in  proving  Constitution-based  claims  of
racial  discrimination.  Jd.,  at  608.  Indeed,  since  an  official
who  intends  to  discriminate  would  rarely  admit  his  real
motive,  the  required  invidious  purpose  generally  would,  of
necessity,  have  to  be  ‘‘inferred  from  the  totality  of  the
relevant  facts,  including  the  fact,  if it is  true,  that  the  law
bears  more  heavily  on  one  race  than  another.’’  Jd.,  at  608-
609.

Nevertheless,  the  disproportionate  impact  of  the  police
test  in  Washington  did  not  ‘‘warrant  the  conclusion  that
it  was  a  purposeful  device  to  discriminate  against  Ne-
groes,’’  the  Court  held,  because  any  inference  that  the
Metropolitan  Police  Department  purposefully  discriminat-
ed  on  the  basis  of  race  was  negated  by the  Department’s
evidence  of  its  affirmative  efforts  to  recruit  black  officers,
of  the  changing  racial  composition  of  the  recruit  classes
and  of the  force  in general  (44%  of which  were  black),  and
of  the  proven  relationship  of  the  test  to  the  police  train-
ing  program.  /d.,  at  611.

The  Court  of  Appeals’  decision  here  is  consistent  with
both  the  reasoning  and  the  result  of  Washington  v.  Davis.*

*  Petitioners  misread  the  Court’s  opinion  in  Washington  when
they  contend  that  inclusion  of  the  decision  below  in  its  footnote  12
The
means  that  that  decision  has  been  “specifically  disapproved.”

The  court  began  its  analysis  of  petitioners’  exclusion  of
Lincoln  Green  with  the  fact  that  their  action  would  have  a
disproportionate  impact  on  minority  persons,  but  it  spe-
cifically  held  that  this  fact  alone  did  not  establish  an  equal
protection  violation.  517  F.2d  409,  413.  As  the  Washington
opinion  was  to  point  out,  the  importance  of  this  dispropor-
tionate  impact  in  establishing  a  Constitution-based  claim
of  racial  discrimination  could  only  be  assessed  against
‘‘the  totality  of the  relevant  facts’’  (48  L.Ed.2d  597,  608).
The  Court  of  Appeals  went  on  to  make  such  assessment
by  reviewing  the  context  in  which  the  decision  to  block
the  MHDC  development  had  been  made.

Among  the  totality  of  relevant  facts  that  the  record
established  were:  (1)  Arlington  Heights  had  used  its  zon-
ing  and  other  powers  to  expand  from  a  small  village  to
one  with  a  1970  population  of  nearly  65,000  people,  99.9%
of  whom  were  white,  making  Arlington  Heights  the  most
residentially  segregated  large  municipality  in  the  greater

and  continue  to  exploit  racial  segregation  and  since  that
action  was  not  adequately  explained  on  nonracial  grounds
(see  Washington  v.  Davis,  48  L.Ed.2d  597,  608-609),  the
Court  of  Appeals  properly  held  that  it  involved  the  kind
of  invidious  racial  discrimination  condemned  by the  Equal
Protection  Clause.**

*  All  of  the  professional  evidence  presented  to  petitioners  during

In  contrast,  petitioners  never  received  or  even

At  trial,  petitioners
came  up  with  two  zoning  claims  to  rationalize  their  decision:  (1)  the

fears  that  the  value  of  their  homes  might  be  diminished  by  their
proximity  to  an  integrated,  subsidized  development.
The  Court  of
Appeals’  analysis  and  rejection  of  these  insubstantial  defenses  was
correct,  regardless  of  what  standard  of  judicial  scrutiny  might  be
appropriate.

**As  Mr.  Justice  Harlan  wrote  in  commenting  on  the  Equal  Pro-
tection  Clause  in  Adickes  vy.  S.  H.  Kress  &  Co.,  398  U.S.  144,  150-
152  (1970):  “Few  principles  of  law  are  more  firmly  stitched  into
our  constitutional  fabric  than  the  proposition  that  a  State  must
not  discriminate  against  a  person  because  of  his  race  or  the  race  of
his  companions,  or  in  any  way  act  to  compel  or  encourage  racial
segregation.”

Even  though  this  court’s  opinion  in  Washington  makes
clear  that  direct  proof  of  official  racial  motivation  is  not
required,  respondents  have  nevertheless  always  contended
that  petitioners’  decision  was  indeed  racially  motivated
(see  373  F.  Supp.  208,  210;  compare  Washington  v.  Davis,
48  L.  Ed.2d  597,  604),  and  the  record  amply  supports  this
contention  (see  Respondents’  Brief  16-19;  24-25).  There
is, indeed,  no  small  irony  in  the  claim  in  petitioners’  sup-
plemental  brief  that  ‘‘[nJo  racial  motive  was  proven’’
(p. 5), because  it was  petitioners,  themselves,  who  success-
fully  urged  the  district  court  [on  the  strength  of Palmer  v.
Thompson,  403  U.S.  217  (1971)  and  U.S.  v.  O’Brien,  391
U.S.  367  (1968)]  to  prohibit  any  inquiry  into  their  state
of  mind  or  motivation  when  they  rejected  Lincoln  Green.*

Nevertheless,  the  circumstantial  evidence  of  petitioners’
illicit  motivation  is  substantial.  For  example,  petitioners’
review  of  Lincoln  Green  was  clearly  not  directed  toward
the  merits  of  the  proposal  because  all  of  the  professional
evidence  that  was  produced  favored  it,  all  of  the  sugges-
tions  of  the  Arlington  Heights’  staff  and  Plan  Commission
were  incorporated  into  the  Lincoln  Green  plans,  and  peti-
tioners  never  received  nor  even  asked  for  the  professional
opinion  of their  own  zoning  and  planning  expert  regarding
the  proposal  (App.  I 68;  Tr.  114-115).  What  petitioners
did  respond  to  was  the  overwhelming,  hostile,  unprecedent-
ed  reaction  of the  community  against  Lincoln  Green,  which
often  involved  racially  explicit  statements  and  which,  the

Village  President  stated,  was  ‘‘a  mandate  to  reject  this

(See  Respondents’  Brief  17-19;  Ps.  Ex.  42,
p.  6).*

The  contrast  with  the  petitioners’  behavior  in  Washing-
ton  v.  Davis  is  striking.  While  44%  of  the  new  police  offi-
cers  recruited  by  the  Washington  petitioners  in  recent
years  were  black  (the  same  percentages  as  20  to  29  year
old  blacks  in  the  recruiting  area),  the  spectacular  growth
of  Arlington  Heights  has  been  limited  to  whites,  even
though  the  number  and  percentage  of  blacks  in  the  overall
Chicago  area  have  risen  substantially  during  the  period
1960  to  1970  (Tr.  554-555).  While  the  Washington  petition-
ers  “systematically  and  affirmatively  sought  to  enroll  black
officers’’  (48  L.Ed.2d  597,  605),  Arlington  Heights  has  re-
fused  to  accept  any  responsibility  for  dealing  with  its
totally  segregated  housing  patterns  and  indeed  has  even
blocked  all  private  efforts,  such  as  MHDC’s,  to  alleviate
this  problem.  And  while  the  Washington  defendants  estab-

*  Neither  of  the  courts  below  dealt  directly  with  this  evidence.
The  District  Court,  having  ruled  during  discovery  and  at  trial  that
direct  inquiry  into  petitioners’  motivation  was  impermissible  under
Palmer  v.  Thompson,  403  U.S.  217  (1971),  noted  that  “motives  are
The
irrelevant  if  the  effect  is  illegal.”  373  F.  Supp.  208,  210.
Court  of Appeals  adopted  a  similar  view,  assessing  the  discriminatory
aspects  of  petitioners’  actions  “[r]jegardless  of  the  Village  Board’s
motivation.”  517  F.  2d  409,  413.
Of  course,  an  official’s  racial
motivation  may  not  be  equivalent  to  the  “racially  discriminatory
purpose”  reflected  in  his  official
act,  as  that  phrase  is  used  in
The  lower  courts’  language,  therefore,  does
Washington  v.  Davis.
not  necessarily  conflict  with  Washington.
Washington’s  discrimina-
tory  purpose  rationale  was  not  discussed  per  se  in  their  opinions.
Nevertheless,  the  Court  of  Appeals’  requirement  that  more  than
disproportionate  impact  be  shown,  its  assessment  of  that  impact  in
terms  of  the  totality  of  relevant  facts,  and  its  review  of  petitioners’
claimed  justifications,  taken  together,  indicate  that  its  approach  and
result  are  consistent  with  Washington.

“

lished  that  performance  on  their  Test  21  was  related  to
performance  in  their  training  program,  neither  of  Arling-
ton  Heights’  belatedly  conceived  zoning  rationales  for  the
rejection  of  Lincoln  Green  can  be  said  to  advance  any
legitimate  municipal  purpose.
Petitioners  utterly  disre-
gard  the  established  record  of their  own  discriminatory  be-
havior  when  they  ask  this  court  to  extend  Washington  v.
Davis  to  sanction  their  rejection  of  Lincoln  Green,  an  ex-
tention  that  would  render  the  constitutional  guarantee
against  official  racial  segregation  all  but  useless  except  in
the  most  blatant,  unsophisticated  cases  of  discrimination.

Whether  or  not  the  Court  of  Appeals  might  properly
have  buttressed  its  determination  that  petitioners  violated
the  Equal  Protection  Clause  with  the  evidence  of  their
illicit  motivation,  its  opinion  clearly  reflects  the  belief  that
their  discrimination  was  purposeful.  Its  analysis  of  the
history  and  context  of  Arlington  Heights’  rejection  of
Lincoln  Green  and  of the  claimed  justifications  for  that  de-
cision  convinced  the  court  below  that  petitioners’  action
would  perpetuate  the  massive  residential  segregation  that
Arlington  Heights’  land  use  policies  had  fostered.  The
opinion’s  comment  that  ‘‘[w]Je  have  no  doubt  that  if
Lincoln  Green  were  built,  it, unlike  the  rest  of the  Village,
would  be  an  integrated  community’’  (517  F.2d  409,  414)
reflects  the  conviction  that  the  exclusion  of  blacks  from
Arlington  Heights  has  not  been  (as it could  not  have  been)
the  result  of  racially  neutral  decisions.  And  the  court’s
conclusion  that  Arlington  Heights  has  been  ‘‘exploiting,’’
not  merely  ignoring,  its  segregated  housing  patterns  (id.)
is  a  determination  of  purposeful  discrimination.

The  record  in  Washington  v.  Davis  did  not  establish
purposeful  racial  discrimination.  It  does  here.  Without
suggesting  that  the  Court  of  Appeals  anticipated  the

10

11

Washington  opinion,  respondents  do  submit  that  its  de-
cision  is  based  on  a  proper  analysis  under  Washington
of  all  of  the  relevant  facts  and  that  it  is  correct,  because
it  applies  the  Equal  Protection  Clause  to  prevent  official
conduct  that  reflects  a  racially  discriminatory  purpose  (see
Washington  v.  Davis,  48  L.Ed.2d  597,  607).

II.

WASHINGTON  v.  DAVIS:  TITLE  VIII

Another  important  element  of  the  Washington  opinion
is its  distinction  between  what  may  be  required  to  establish
an  equal  protection  violation  on  the  one  hand  and  what
may  be  required  under  a  Congressional  act  prohibiting
discrimination  on  the  other.  48  L.Ed.2d  597,  611.  While
purposeful  racial  discrimination  is  necessary  to  sustain  a
Constitution-based  claim,  Congress  had  provided  that  dis-
criminatory  purpose  need  not  be  proved  in  a  proper  case
to  establish  a  violation  of  Title  VII.  Jd.  When  employ-
ment  practices  that  disproportionately  disqualify  large
numbers  of  blacks  are  challenged  under  Title  VII,  the
Washington  opinion  noted,  ‘‘it  is  an  insufficient  response
to  demonstrate  some  rational  basis  for  the  challenged
.  However  this  process  proceeds,  it  involves
practices.  .
a  more  probing  judicial  review  of,  and  less  deference  to,
the  seemingly  reasonable  acts  of administrators  and  execu-
tives  than  is  appropriate  under  the  Constitution.  ..  .’’
Id.,  at  611-612.

.

Thus  Washington  indicates  that  the  appropriate  stan-
dard  of judicial  review  in a case  alleging  official  racial  dis-
crimination  may  well  be  more  probing  if the  claim  is based
on  a  specific  Congressional  prohibition  of  discrimination
than  if it is based  solely  on  the  Constitution.  Since  the  day
their  complaint  was  filed,  respondents  have  consistently

contended  that  Arlington  Heights’  rejection  of  Lincoln
Green  violated  the  federal  fair  housing  laws  [Title  VIII
of  the  Civil  Rights  Act  of  1968,  (The  Fair  Housing  Act,
42  U.S.C.  $§ 3601  et  seg.)  and  the  Civil  Rights  Act  of  1866
(42  U.S.C.  § 1982) | as  well  as  the  Equal  Protection  Clause.
Although  the  Court  of  Appeals  did  not  explicitly  consider
the  statutory  challenge,  its  analysis  of  the  overall  racial
impact  of  and  the  insubstantial  rationalizations  for  peti-
tioners’  rejection  of  Lincoln  Green  and  its  conclusion  that
Arlington  Heights’  action  caused  an  unjustifiable  perpetua-
tion  of  residential  segregation  are  more  than  sufficient  to
establish  a  violation  of  Title  VIII  (see  Respondents’  Brief
38-49).*

Congress  has  determined  in  Title  VIII  that  ‘‘[ijt  is
the  policy  of  the  United  States  to  provide,  within  consti-
tutional  limitations,  for  fair  housing  throughout  the  Unit-
ed  States”  (42  U.S.C.  § 3601).  To  that  end,  it  has  pro-
scribed,  in  the  broadest  language  possible,  all  practices—
public  or  private—that  make  housing  unavailable  to  any
person  because  of  race  or  that  interfere  with  the  provision
of  such  nondiscriminatory  housing  (42  U.S.C.  §  3604(a)
and  § 3617)  in  an  effort  to  achieve  the  vital  national  policy
of  replacing  ‘‘the  ghettos  by truly  integrated  and  balanced
living  patterns.’’  Trafficante  v.  Metropolitan  Life  Insur-
ance  Co., 409  U.S.  205,  211  (1972).  Since  Arlington  Heights’

*  Indeed,  in  determining  that  petitioners’  action  involved  illegal
segregation,  the  court  below  relied  on  a  number  of  decisions  that
were  based  on  the  federal  fair  housing  laws  (507  F.2d  at  413-415).
See,  e.g.,  United  States  v.  City  of  Black  Jack,  Missouri,  508  F.2d
1179  (8th  Cir.  1974),  cert.  denied,  422  U.S.  1042  (1975),  where  a
zoning  ordinance  that  prevented  construction  of  a  subsidized,  in-
tegrated  development  such  as  Lincoln  Green  in  an  all  white  area
was  held  to  violate  Title  VIII  because  of  its  racially  discriminatory
effects.

12

13

refusal  to  permit  construction  of  Lincoln  Green  excludes
large  numbers  of  blacks  from  a  highly  segregated  white
area  rich  in  jobs  and  :  nce  the  claimed  justifications  for
that  refusal  are  insub  tantial  at  best,  petitioners  have
violated  Title  VIII  reg..rdless  of  whether  the  court  below
found  the  kind  of  invidious  purpose  necessary  to  an  equal
protection  challenge.  Cf.  Washington  v.  Davis,  48  L.Ed.2d
597,  611.

Il.
HILLS  v.  GAUTREAUX

It  is  hard  to  imagine  how  petitioners  could  take  any
solace  from  this  Court’s  decision  in  Hills  v.  Gautreaux,
which  held  that  HUD’s  violation  of  the  Fifth  Amendment
and  the  1964  Civil  Rights  Act  by  helping  to  create  Chi-
cago’s  racially  segregated  public  housing  program  per-
mitted  a  federal  court  to  fashion  a  remedial  order  extend-
ing  beyond  Chicago’s  borders.  The  facts  here  show  a  use
of  local  governmental  powers  in  violation  of  Federal  con-
stitutional  and  statutory  rights  to  prevent  construction  of
an  integrated  development.
The  portions  of  the  Hills
decision  quoted  by  the  petitioners  in  their  supplemental
brief  speak  merely  of  those  municipalities  which  have  not
yet  so  misused  their  powers  and  such  quotes  are  irrelevant
here.

It was  understood  in Hills  that  the  entire  Chicago  metro-
politan  area  ‘‘is  a  single  relevant  locality  for  low  rent
(47  L.Ed.2d  792,  800)  and  that  a
housing  purposes’’
‘‘city-only’’  remedy  would  not  succeed  in  alleviating  the
effects  of  HUD’s  past  segregative  practices,  because  it
would  not  result  in  housing  being  developed  in  desegre-
gated  neighborhoods.  Id.,  at  800,  803.  Nevertheless,  HUD
raised  two  objections  to  metropolitan  area  relief  on  the
basis  of  Milliken  v.  Bradley,  418  U.S.  717  (1974),  both  of
which  were  rejected  by the  Court.

First  of  all,  as  Justice  Stewart’s  opinion  pointed  out
‘‘{t}he  critical  distinction  between  HUD  and  the  suburban
school  districts  in  Milliken  is  that  HUD  has  been  found
to  have  violated  the  Constitution.’’  47  L.Ed.2d  792,  803.
That  is  an  important  factor  here  as  well.
Arlington
Heights,  like  HUD,  has  violated  constitutional  and  statu-
tory  prohibitions  against  racial  discrimination.
Unlike
HUD,  however,  Arlington  Heights’  violation  creates  no
special  problems  with  respect  to  the  appropriate  remedial
order.  No  affirmative  duty  of  any  kind  is  sought  to  be  im-
posed  on  Arlington  Heights  as  a  result  of  its  segregative
action.  All  that  respondents  have  ever  asked  is  that  peti-
tioners  stop  their  illegal  interference  with  MHDC’s  pri-
vate  efforts  to  begin  to  ease  the  problems  created  by  the
overwhelmingly  segregated  housing  patterns  that  have
been  developed  in  Arlington  Heights.  A  simple  injunc-
tion,  requiring  neither  planning  nor  expenditures  by  peti-
tioners,  will  suffice.

The  Hills  opinion  then  went  on  to  demonstrate  that  a
remedial  order  against  HUD  affecting  its  conduct  beyond
Chicago’s  boundaries  would  not  impermissibly  interfere
with  suburban  authorities  that  had  not  been  implicated  in
any  unlawful  conduct.  Jd.,  at  805.  This  was  so,  the  Court
noted,  because  local  governments  have  a  role  to  play  in
many  federal  housing  assistance  programs,  including  the
important  § 8 Lower-Income  Housing  Assistance  program
of  the  1974  Housing  and  Community  Development  Act  (42
U.S.C.  §§  1487  et  seq.),  and  HUD’s  efforts  to  expand  low-
income  housing  opportunities  outside  areas  of  minority
concentration  would  thus  not  necessarily  have  a  coercive
effect  on  suburban  municipalities.  Jd.,  at  805-808.

Incredibly,  petitioners  read  this  part  of the  Hills  opinion
as  establishing  a  kind  of  ‘‘reverse-Supremacy  Clause,”’

14

15

under  which  local  zoning  restrictions  can  be  exercised  at
the  discretion  of  municipal  officials  to  frustrate  Congres-
sional  acts  designed  to  provide  for  integrated  and  balanced
living  patterns  (Title  VIII)  and  to  promote  greater  choice
of  housing  opportunities  for  assisted  persons  outside  of
areas  of  minority  concentration  (Housing  and  Community
Development  Act  of  1974;  see  47  L.Ed.2d  792,  806-807).
Nothing  could  be  further  from  the  intent  of  Hills.  While
indicating  a  concern  for  the  legitimate  interests  of  suburbs
not  found  to  have  violated  any  law  (a protected  status  that
Arlington  Heights  cannot  claim  here),  the  Hills  opinion
pointed  out  that  the  approval  of  even  these  municipalities
is  not  required  as  a  condition  of  § 8’s  applicability  to  a
locality  (¢d.,  at  807)  and  that  while  municipal  interests  are
to be  considered  by HUD,  they  cannot  block  Congress’  goal
in  § 8 of  providing  for  expanded  low-income  housing  op-
portunities  distributed  throughout  a  metropolitan  housing
market.  Jd.,  at  807-808.

Petitioners  not  only  misconstrue  the  Hills  opinion,  their
pos'tic.  would  have  the  ultimate  effect  of  nullifying  Hills.
.  sngton  Heights,  which  has  used  its  zoning  and  other
If  .
lanu
se  powers  to  grow  into  the  most  residentially  segre-
gatea  large  municipality  in  the  Chicago  metropolitan  area,
ean  block  all  private  efforts  to  provide  subsidized,  inte-
grated  housing  in  the  Village  on  the  basis  of  the  insub-
stantial  excuses  offered  here,  then  every  Chicago  suburb
would  be  justified  in  totally  excluding  such  housing,  and
neither  HUD  nor  the  District  Court  on  remand  from  Hills
could  even  consider  a  metropolitan  area  remedy.  Nor,  for
that  matter,  could  HUD  use  the  § 8  program  to  achieve
the  Congressional  purpose  of  expanding  low-income  hous-
ing  opportunities  outside  areas  of  minority  concentration
in  any  metropolitan  housing  market  in  the  country.
In
order  for  the  national  housing  policy  of balanced,  dispersed

federally  assisted  housing  to  succeed,  local  authorities  such
as  petitioners  must  at  least  have  the  obligation  to  refrain
from  frustrating  private  efforts  to  carry  out  that  policy.
United  Farm  Workers  of Florida  Housing  Project,  Ine.  v.
City  of Delray  Beach,  Florida,  493  F.2d  799,  811  (5th  Cir.
1974).

IV.

EASTLAKE  v.  FOREST  CITY  ENTERPRISES,  INC.

In  Eastlake,  the  Court  rejected  a  commercial  developer’s
challenge  on  procedural  due  process  grounds  to  a  local
charter  provision  that  required  a  referendum  to  approve
a  zoning  change.
Since  Arlington  Heights  does  not  use  a
referendum  in  establishing  its  zoning  classifications  and
since  it is the  substance  of what  petitioners  have  done,  not
the  procedures  which  they  used,  that  is  attacked  here  as
violative  of  respondents’  equal  protection  and  fair  hous-
ing rights,  Kastlake  is not  in point.

Indeed,  the  Court  in  Eastlake  specifically  distinguished
that  case  from  a  challenge  to  the  merits  of  the  particular
zoning  Classification  involved  (49  L.Ed.2d  132,  139),  which
in a  commercial  case  traditionally  presents  the  due  process
issue  in terms  of whether  the  zoning  restriction  bears  a sub-
stantial  relation  to  the  public  health,  safety,  or  general
welfare.
L.g.,  Euclid  v.  Ambler  Realty  Co.,  272  U.S.  365
(1926);  Nectow  v.  Cambridge,  277  U.S.  183  (1928).  Arling-
ton  Heights’  exclusion  of  Lincoln  Green,  of  course,  raises
more  important  issues  than  the  mere  diminution  in  the
market  value  of  MHDC’s  property  although,  as  a  matter
of  fact,  respondents  have  consistently  maintained  that
Arlington  Heights’  action  cannot  be  justified  even  under
traditional  zoning  principles  that  are  appropriate  for  com-

16

17

mercial  cases  where  racial  segregation  is not  involved  (see
517  F.2d  at 412  and  415;  Respondents’  Brief  3, 6-12, 33-38).*

Wholly  apart  from  racial  considerations,  Arlington
Heights’  prevention  of  the  construction  of  Lincoln  Green
on  the  proposed  site  would  in  no  way  advance  the  public
health,  safety,  or  general  welfare.
Except  for  the  high
school  and  novitiate,  the  80  acre  Viatorian  property  has
long  been  vacant,  and  most  of  it  will  remain  open  space
after  Lincoln  Green  is built.  The  townhouses  are  carefully
planned  to  harmonize  with  the  desirable  features  of  the
landscape  and  the  neighburing  single  family  homes  (517
F.2d  at  415;  Ps.  Ex.  5,  11,  1).  The  MHDC  development
would  make  a  net  contribution  to  the  finances  of Arlington
Heights  and  its  schools  and  would  have  only  a  minor  im-
pact  on  traffic  in  the  area  (see  Respondents’  Brief  9).
There  is  an  overwhelming  need  for  the  kind  of  housing
that  Lincoln  Green  would  provide,  a need  much  greater  than
that  for  any  commercial  housing  that  could  be  proposed
for  the  site  (id.  10-12;  517  F.2d  at  411).

The  other  side  of the  coin  is that  petitioners’  two  claimed
justifications  for  rejecting  the  MHDC  proposal  are  not
rationally  related  to  any  legitimate  municipal  interest.
First  they  argue  that  it is  their  policy  to  permit  multiple
family  developments  only  as  ‘‘buffers’’  between  single
Apart  from
family  and  commercial  or  institutional  uses.

*  Petitioners’  repeated  claim  that  their  overall  “Zoning  Ordinance”
(whose  importance,  if not  legality,  they  would  magnify  by  the  con-
stant  use  of  capital  letters)  is  “admittedly”  reasonable  is,  at  this
No  one  has  ever
late  stage  of  the  litigation,  particularly  galling.
admitted  any  such  thing.
As  a  matter  of  fact,  the  record  demon-
trates  that  Arlington  Heights’  residential  zoning  scheme  classifies
vacant  land,  like  the  Viatorian  property,  as  single-family  merely  as
a  holding  device  that  is  changed  regularly  for  private  projects,  but
is  available  to  frustrate  developers  like  MHDC  who  are  not  ac-
ceptable  to  Arlington  Heights  (see,  e.g.,  Ps.  Exs.  12,  33,  52).

the  fact  that  petitioners  have  not  consistently  applied  this
policy  and  that  it  has  limited  applicability  to  the  town-
houses  proposed  by  MHDC  (517  F.2d  at  415),  Lincoln
Green  would  in fact  comply  with  this  policy,  since  it will  be
located  between  the  single  family  homes  to  the  south  and
east  and  the  novitiate  and  high  school  on  the  Viatorian
property  to  the  north  and  west.
Nor  does  the  alleged
diminution  in  neighboring  property  values  justify  peti-
tioners’  action.
To  the  extent  that  this  claim  is  based
on  the  neighbors’  ‘‘reliance’’  on  the  1959  comprehensive
plan,  it is without  substance  both  because  their  homes  were
built  before  the  plan  was  developed  and  because  Arlington
Heights  has  permitted  commercial  multiple  family  develop-
ments  next  to  every  high  school  except  St.  Viator’s  and
has  made  dozens  of amendments  to  its  zoning  plan  for  con-
struction  of  such  developments  adjacent  to  single  family
homes  (see  Ps.  Ex.  12,  33,  52).  And  to  the  extent  that
this  claim  reflects  the  neighbors’  displeasure  in living  near
an  integrated,  subsidized  development,  it  is  entitled  to
no  weight  whatsoever.

In  sum,  construction  of  Lincoln  Green  would  advance
the  general  welfare,  while  Arlington  Heights’  rejection  of
the  development  is not  justified  by any  legitimate  govern-
mental  interest  under  the  Euclid  analysis.

Conclusion

Under  these  recent  decisions  of  this  Court,  as  well  as
under  prior  law,  the  decision  of  the  Court  of  Appeals  is
correct,  and  it should  be  affirmed.

Respectfully  submitted,

F.  Wituis  Caruso
Rosert  G.  ScHEMM
CaroL  M.  PETERSEN
Attorneys  for Respondents
and  Intervening  Respondents

IN THE
Supreme  Court  of the Gnited  States
OCTOBER  TERM,  1975.

No. 75-616

METROPOLITAN  HOUSING  DEVELOPMENT
CORPORATION,  ET  AL.,

vs.

Respondents,

THE  VILLAGE  OF  ARLINGTON  HEIGHTS,  ET  At.,

Petitioners,

NORTHWEST  OPPORTUNITY  CENTER  Anp
ELUTERIA  D.  MALDONADO,

Intervening  Respondents.

ON  WRIT  OF  CERTIORARI  TO  THE  UNITED  STATES  COURT
OF  APPEALS  FOR  THE  SEVENTH  CIRCUIT.

REPLY  BRIEF  FOR  PETITIONERS.

The  respondents  herein  have  offered  a purported  statement  of
the  case  which  is not  only  inaccurate  and  misleading,  but  which
in  effect  seeks  to  argue  the  merits.  Although  it  is  difficult  to
separate  the  argument  from  the  facts  as  set  forth  in  the
respondents’  brief,  it  is  necessary  to  correct  some  of  the  more
glaring  errors.

The  respondents  accuse  the  petitioners  of a  series  of misstate-
ments  and  exaggerations,  without  bothering  to  indicate  a single

2

3

example.  They  assert  that  the  Village’s  zoning  has  no  integrity
and  has  not  been  consistently  applied.  In  citing  the  Court  of
Appeals’  opinion,  they  even  misstate  the  opinion  which  is  the
subject  matter  of  this  proceeding.  The  Court  of  Appeals  at
page  412  points  out  “that  the  evidence  does  not  necessitate  a
finding  that  Arlington  Heights  administered  this  policy  in  a
discriminatory  manner.”  At  page  415  the  Court  of Appeals  does
not  question  the  integrity  of  the  zoning,  but  simply  incorrectly
applied  the  “compelling  interest”  test  and  determined  that  the
zoning  ordinance  failed  to  meet  this  test.  The  trial  court  specif-
ically  found  that  the  defendants  were  motivated  with  respect
to  the  property  in  question  by  a  legitimate  desire  to  protect
property  values  and  the  integrity  of  the  Village’s  zoning  plan.
373  F.  Supp.  208,  211.  This  finding  was  not  disturbed  by  the
Court  of  Appeals’  opinion.

The  Village  is being  asked  to  “plan  for”  multiple-family  devel-
opment  on  the  subject  property  by changing  the  long-established
single-family  classification  which  is  totally  consistent  with
surrounding  land  uses  and  zoning.  There  is  absolutely  no
evidence  in  the  record  of  any  exploitation  of  local  segregated
housing  patterns  by  the  petitioners.  Instead,  the  evidence  indi-
cates  clearly  that  the  Village  zoning  ordinance  has  permitted
the  development  and  planning  of  thousands  of  multiple-family
dwellings,  as  well  as  single-family  dwellings  in appropriate  places
throughout  the  Village.  The  Village’s  housing  pattern,  which  has
been  established  by  the  market,  still  encompasses  almost  200
poverty  families  (Rec.  481)  and  the  respondents’  own  evidence
indicates  that,  based  on  1970  census  data,  more  than  3,000
blacks  could  afford  to  live  in  the  Village  and  473  black  house-
holds  could  rent  housing  in the  Village  based  on  economic  data
revealed  by  the  census.  These  figures  did  not  even  take  into
account  possible  movements  from  existing  single-family  homes
outside  the  Village  to  apartments  in  the  Village,  or  movements
from  apartments  outside  the  Village  to  single-family  homes  in
the  Village.  The  respondents’  own  evidence  also  showed  that

2,500  blacks  who  would  be  eligible  for  Lincoln  Green  housing
would  also  be  eligible  for  other  housing  in  the  Village.  (Tr.
595)  The  evidence  also  indicates  that  this  selfsame  Village
Board  approved  a  report  that  recommended  the  development
of  150  to  250  units  of  moderate-income  housing  and  50  units
of low-income  housing  in the  Village.  (App.  I, 68)

The  respondents  also  do  not  mention  that  the  Answers  to
Interrogatories  filed  by  the  petitioners  in  this  case  also  reveal
that  the  Village  has  entered  into  a  cooperation  agreement  with
the  Housing  Authority  of  Cook  County  to  provide  low-income
housing  for  the  elderly,  and  that  in  fact  such  housing  has  been
approved  and  is about  to  commence  construction  in the  Village.
(Answers  to  Interrogatories,  No.  36)  The  trial  court  refused
to  admit  into  evidence  the  Village’s  Fair  Housing  Ordinance
adopted  in  January  of  1968  on  grounds  of  relevancy,  but  the
response  to  interrogatories  also  indicates  the  adoption  of  such
ordinance.  (Answers  to  Interrogatories,  No.  40)

In  fact,  the  minutes  of  the  Plan  Commission  received  over
objection  of  the  Village  indicate  that  the  motion  made  with
respect  to  the  application  of  the  respondents  was  that  it  “be
denied,  as  it is a request  for  spot  zoning.  While  the  need  for  low
and  moderate  income  housing  may  exist  in Arlington  Heights  or
its  environs,  the  Plan  Commission  would  be  derelict  in  recom-
mending  it at  the  proposed  location.”  (Plf.  Ex.  17)

These  unquestioned  facts,  well  known  to  the  respondents,
hardly  justify  the  intemperate  characterizations  directed  against
the  Village.  In  fact  there  is no  evidence  in  the  record  that  the
action  of the  municipality  in any  way  has  racially  discriminatory
effects.  The  most  that  the  respondents  have  been  able  to  demon-
strate  is  that,  based  on  census  figures  blacks  represent  40%
of  the  eligible  applicants  for  housing  which  was  proposed  to  be
built.  The  respondents  were  unable  to  produce  a  single  black
citizen  whose  income  fell  within  the  range  or  who  could  be
proven  to  be  an  occupant  of  the  housing  once  built.  There  was

4

5

no  evidence  in  the  record  with  respect  to  the  two  blacks  who
originally  were  plaintiffs,  but  who  actually  lack  standing,  which
indicated  either  that  they  were  eligible  for  the  Lincoln  Green
project  or  that  they  would  in fact  reside  in  the  project.

Even  if  it  be  assumed  that  a  racially  discriminatory  effect
would  warrant  setting  aside  the  otherwise  valid  and  long-
Standing  single-family  zoning  on  the  subject  property,  which
contention  we  strenuously  deny,  it is clear  that  the  respondents
have  fallen  far  short  of  showing  any  racially  discriminatory
effect  of the  action  complained  of here.  It comes  with  little  grace
to  argue  on  the  one  hand  that  blacks  would  represent  40%  of
the  eligible  applicants  for  190  units  and  therefore  the  Village’s
action  was  discriminatory,  while  it  is  the  respondents’  own
evidence  that  some  2,500  of the  blacks  who  would  be eligible  for
Lincoln  Green  housing  are  also  eligible  for  other  existing
housing  in  the  Village—such  housing  actually  built  under  the
existing  zoning  ordinance.

There  is  no  question  that  the  zoning  ordinance  has  been
amended  many  times  to  permit  multiple-family  developments.
There  is no  evidence  that  all  of these  or  any  of them  were  “high
rent  apartments.”  The  evidence  is clear,  as  recognized  by both
the  trial  court  and  the  Court  of  Appeals,  that  the  Village  has
generally  adhered  to  its  apartment  policy  with  respect  to  zoning
changes.  If the  Village  had  not  made  zoning  changes  in order  to
accommodate  multiple-family  construction  where  such  construc-
tion  met  proper  planning  criteria,  undoubtedly  these  respondents
would  be  complaining  that  the  Village  was  practicing  exclu-
sionary  zoning  by  refusing  to  allow  development  of  multiple-
family  dwellings  within  its  corporate  limits.  Cf.,  United  States
Vv.  City  of Blackjack,  508  F.  2d  1179  (8th  Cir.  1974).

Totally  ignored  by the  respondents  is the  fact  that  the  Village
on  at  least  39  occasions  rejected  applications  for  multiple-family
zoning  which,  like  the  application  in  the  instant  case,  did  not
meet  planning  criteria  for  such  rezoning.  The  most  that  can  be
said  for  the  respondents’  position  is that  if the  Village  had  been

willing  to  ignore  all  basic  zoning  criteria  and  spot  zone  the
subject  property,  at the  expense  of the  neighbors  and  the destruc-
tion  of the  Village’s  plan,  possibly  190  low  and  moderate  income
families  could  occupy  the  site.  There  is  no  evidence  that  any
of these  families  would  be  black,  nor  is there  any  evidence  that
any  of these  families  would  be white.  The  Village’s  decision  was
in  fact  “colorblind”  and  the  unwarranted  and  totally  unsup-
ported  assertions  of the  respondents  of racial  bias  simply  do  not
find  any  support  in the  record  or  in fact.

Background  of MHDC.

The  respondent  MHDC  in  its  brief  indicates  that  it  has
developed  scores  of  apartments  and  houses  made  available  to
minorities  under  federal  subsidy  programs.  This  statement  hardly
squares  with  the  evidence  in  the  record.  Mr.  Kane,  Director  of
Real  Estate,  testified  that  they  had  constructed  37  single-family
homes  in South  Elgin  and  had  rented  three  or  four  others.  (Tr.
56)  The  only  application  ever  made  for  rezoning  in  the  north-
west  suburban  community  was  the  subject  property.  (Tr.  74)

The  respondents  do  not  inform  this  Court  that  the  only
multiple-family  development  which  MHDC  had  ever  constructed
was  the  212-unit  development  known  as  Rand  Grove  Village
located  in Palatine  Township  and  mentioned  in the  respondents’
brief.  What  the  respondents  do  not  tell  this  Court  is that  Rand
Grove  is three  quarters  of  a  mile  from  the  corporate  limits  of
Arlington  Heights.  It  is  located  on  property  properly  zoned
and  was  developed  without  objection  or  interference  of  any
kind  on  the  part  of  the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights  or  any  of
the  other  surrounding  communities.

This  hardly  qualifies  MHDC  as  a  substantial  force  in  the
housing  market  or  the  northwest  suburban  community  area  of
which  Arlington.  Heights  is  a  part,  as  a  provider  of  low  and
moderate  income  housing.

While  the  respondents  indicate  the  benefits  of  the  236
program  u.ud  discuss  the  income  limitations,  they  do not  tell  the

6

7

Court  that  at  the  time  of  trial,  some  two  years  after  their
application  was  denied  at  the  local  level,  the  respondents  had
never  applied  for  a 236  commitment.  (Tr.  273)  The  trial  court
found  in  fact  that  federal  funding  for  the  project  was  not
available.  373  F.  Supp.  208,  211.

In  describing  the  proposed  plan  for  Lincoln  Green  having  a
density  of  13  units  to  the  acre,  the  respondents  do  not  mention
the  fact  that  this  density  is more  than  three  times  the  R-3  density
which  applies  to  all  the  surrounding  single-family  area.  To  assert
that  a  multiple-family  development  of  13  units  to  the  acre  is
similar  to  neighboring  houses  in  all  material,  physical  respects
is blatantly  misleading.

Totally  ignored  is the  testimony  of Rolf  C. Campbell,  a zoning
and  planning  consultant,  who  testified  for  the  Village  that  the
proposed  development  would  be  adverse  to  adjacent  property
owners  and  would  adversely  affect  the  livability  of  the  single-
family  residences.
(Tr.  876)  Campbell’s  opinion  of  adverse
effect  was  based  upon  the  plan  that  the  respondents  had  sub-
mitted  to  the  Village,  which  was  in  evidence.  His  opinion  with
respect  to  the  effect  on  the  surrounding  areas  had  nothing  to
do  with  the  character  of  the  persons  who  would  be  residing  in
the  development.  (Tr.  882)  Campbell  testified  that  ground
coverage  for  single-family  homes  is  almost  always  a  higher
percentage  than  ground  coverage  in  multiple  developments.
(Tr.  884)  He  testified  as  to  his  work  as  a  consultant  on  several
amendments  to  the  Comprehensive  Plan  first  adopted  in  1959.
His  work  took  place  in  1961,  1962  through  1966,  1967  and
1968.  He  testified  that  the  decision  of  the  Village  in  rejecting
the Lincoln  Green  proposal  was  consistent  with  the  recommenda-
tions  of  the  Comprehensive  Plan.  (Tr.  888)

Also  ignored  was  the  testimony  of the  Village  Planner,  Joseph
Kesler,  that  the  actual  density  of the  land  utilized  for  residential
purposes  in  the  proposed  plan  was  15  units  to  the  acre.  (Tr.
783)  Kesler  was  of  the  opinion  that  the  proposed  development
would  have  a detrimental  effect  on  the  surrounding  area  from  a

planning  standpoint.  (Tr.  788)  He  testified  that  at  the  time  of
the  application,  traffic  conditions  along  Euclid  Avenue  were
hazardous  and  the  number  of  trips  per  day  to  be  generated  by
the  project  would  be higher  than  if the  land  was  used  for  single-
family  purposes.  (Tr.  793)  In Kesler’s  opinion,  the  highest  and
best  use  of the  subject  property  was  for  single-family  homes  in
view  of the  character  of the  surrounding  area.  (Tr.  805)

Also  ignored  in  the  respondents’  description  of  the  Lincoln
Green  plan  and  its  impact  on  the  neighborhood  was  the  testi-
mony  of F.  Gregory  Opelka,  a professional  real  estate  appraiser
and  Vice-President  of Fairfield  Savings  & Loan  Association.  He
testified  that  the  subject  property  adjoins  the  rear  yards  of
single-family  homes  that  run  from  Euclid  to  Oakton.  These
homes  renge  in  value  from  $50,000  to  $70,000.  The  majority
were  built  in  the  last  10  years;  some  are  newer.  (Tr.  914)
Across  from  the  high  school  on  Oakton,  the  homes  were  built
in the last few years  and  some  new  homes  are  under  construction.
(Tr.  915)  West  of  the  subject  property  are  homes,  many  of
which  were  developed  in the  1940’s  and  1950's.  To the  south  are
single-family  homes  in  the  $30,000  to  $40,000  range,  built  in
the  late  1940’s  and  1950’s.  On  Euclid  Avenue  across  the  street
from  the subject property  are  homes,  some  of which  were  built  in
the  last  5 to  10 years.  (Tr.  916)

Opelka  testified  that  the  proposed  development  will  diminish
the  value  of  the  properties  in  the  immediate  area  and  in  other
parts  of the  Village.  (Tr.  931)  This  range  in negative  effect  on
the  single-family  homes  would  be  between  5%  and  10%  of
their  value,  with  the  most  severe  effect  on  the  properties  adjoin-
ing the  subject  property  on  the  east.  (Tr.  931)  He  testified  that
this  was  a completely  developed  area,  except  for  the  high  school
property,  and  that  people  who  buy  in  a  completely  R-3  area
have  a  right  to  rely  on  the  land  use  and  zoning.  (Tr.  932)
People  have  a  right  to  rely  on  the  plans  of  municipalities  for
this  type  of  protection.  Opelka  testified  that  his  opinion  would
be  the  same  if the  property  were  developed  as  proposed  with

totally  private  financing,  and  no  matter  what  price  range  or
rental  range  the  R-5  development  would  have.  (Tr.  935)

Mr.  Drummond’s  alleged  “expertise”  can  best  be  measured
by the  fact  that  he  had  had  no  experience  with  a  236  project
that  had  been  actually  built  and  had  done  no  previous  planning
studies  in  Arlington  Heights.  He  conceded  that  the  whole  area
in which  the  subject  property  lies  has  been  developed  in  accord-
ance  with  the  official  plan  of  1959,  and  that  the  proximity  of
Euclid  Avenue  does  not  inhibit  the  development  of single-famil
homes  on  the  subject  property.  (Tr.  423)  Drummond  also
testified  that  practically  all  the  way  from  the  eastern  boundary
of Arlington  Heights  to  Northwest  Highway  on  the  west,  Euclid
Avenue  is  developed  with  single-family  homes  and  this  is  the  .
predominant  land  use  on  both  sides  of  Euclid.  (Tr.  424)  He
saw  nothing  different  about  the  subject  property  from  the  land
which  had  been  developed  for  single-family  use  along  Euclid
Avenue.

Drummond  also  testified  that  he  had  no  knowledge  of  what
was  economically  feasible  in Arlington  Heights  in  1971,  and  had
had  no  experience  with  single-family  home  development  in  a
builtup  community  such  as  Arlington  Heights.  (Tr.  433)  He
testified  that  R-5  Districts  are  used  as  buffers  in  Arlington
Heights  between  single-family  areas  and  manufacturing  and
commercial  uses.  (Tr.  439)  In  his  opinion  it was  not  unusual
to  plan  along  those  lines.  (Tr.  441)

Drummond  conceded  that  there  would  be  more  automobiles
in the  morning  rush  hour  from  the  proposed  project  than  if the
land  were  developed  for  single-family  purposes.  When  he  char-
acterized  the  proposed  development  as  comparable  to  single
family,  he meant  only that  a developer  who  was  formerly  serving
that  portion  of the  single-family  market  was  now  offering  town-
houses  or  condominiums.  (Tr.  451)  He  concluded  that  the  site
was  just  as  desirable  for  single-family  use  as  for  the  proposed
development.  (Tr.  456)

9

Thus,  the  characterization  in the  respondents’  brief  of Drum-
mond’s  testimony  is  highly  inaccurate  since  he  conceded  that
single-family  homes  were  equally  well  suited  for  the  subject
property,  and  his  definition  of  compatibility  had  nothing  to  do
with  the  use  of the  land,  but  rather  the  character  of the  builders
‘in  the  market.

Reference  is  made  to  a  purported  tax  impact  study.  This
study  supposedly  shows  that  the  development  would  make  a
net  contribution  to  the  Village  and  to  the  Arlington  Heights
schools  in terms  of tax  revenue.  The  law  of course  is clear  that
municipalities  are  not  required  to  formulate  zoning  require-
ments  based  upon  discouraging  families  with  children  or  with
reference  to  the  revenues  to  be  derived  by  the  school  district.
Cosmopolitan  National  Bank  v.  Mount  Prospect,  22  Ill.  2d  463,
468-69  (1961).  Moreover,  the  tax  study  was  produced  based  on
figures  prepared  by a John  Ducey  who  did not  testify  in the  case.

The  validity  of  the  tax  impact  study  may  be  gauged  by the
fact  that  Ferrara,  who  had  supposedly  modified  Ducey’s  figures
because  he didn’t  agree  with  the  number  of children  that  Ducey
thought  would  be produced,  testified  that  he had  not  studied  the
assessed  valuation  of the  property  involved  in the  study  and  did
not  know  the  assessed  valuation  of  the  project  if  it  should  be
completed.

In  this  instance,  as  in  several  others,  throughout  their
brief,  the  respondents,  like  the  Court  of  Appeals,  made  use  of
evidence  that  had  been  objected  to  and  had  been  introduced  for
a limited  purpose  only. The  first  40 plaintiffs’  exhibits  were  docu-
ments  which  had  been  prepared  by  MHDC  and  were  in  the
possession  of  the  Village  Trustees  at  the  time  they  made  their
decision  at  the  local  level.  They  were  introduced  over  objection
of  the  Village  and  admitted  solely  for  the  purpose  of  showing
that  the  documents  were  in  the  hands  of  the  Trustees  and  not
for  the  truth  of  the  matters  purportedly  contained  within  them.
(Tr.  158,  194)

10

11

In  most  instances  the  respondents  made  no  attempt  during
the course  of the  trial  to  support  the  exhibits  by the  testimony  of
those  persons  who  prepared  them.  Thus,  the  Village  was  denied
the  right  to  cross-examine  or  to  inquire  into  the  validity  of  the
exhibits.  Nevertheless,  the  respondents  now  seek  to  argue  the
substance  of  these  exhibits  as  if  the  matters  contained  had  in
fact  been  proven  during  the  course  of  the  trial.

For  example,  reliance  is placed  upon a traffic  report  prepared
by  Barton  Aschman  Associates  and  identified  as  Plaintiffs’
Exhibit  13.  No  one  from  Barton  Aschman  appeared  to  testify
in support  of that  report,  and  the  reference  to  the  transcript  con-
tained  in  the  respondents’  brief  simply  indicates  that  the  study
had  been  commissioned  by  MHDC.  Yet,  the  respondents  now
argue  the  substance  of  the  consultant’s  report.

This  is  the  pattern  exhibited  throughout  the  respondents’
brief.  They,  for  example,  argue  extensively  from  a  study  issued
by  the  Cook  County  Office  of  Economic  Opportunity.  This
exhibit,  again  admitted  over  the  Village’s  objection,  was  not
verified  by  anyone  who  made  the  study,  and  the  Court  per-
mitted  its  admission  only  for  the  purpose  of showing  that  it was
before  the  Village  Board  and  not  for  the  truth  of the  assertions.
Yet,  the Court  of Appeals  apparently  relied  upon  this  document.

MHDC  contends  that  it had  consulted  with  the  staff  of  the
Village  for  preliminary  review  of  the  proposed  development.
Such  consultation  of  course  goes  on  for  every  application  for
zoning  change  and  in no  way  is controlling  on  any  of the  issues
in this  case.  What  is of great  significance  is the  testimony  of the
Village  Planner,  Mr.  Kesler,  that  he,  like  Hanson  the  Village
Manager,  offered  his  assistance  to  MHDC  after  the  hearing  in
finding  other  sites,  but  MHDC  never  used  his  services.  (Tr.  773)

Reference  is  made  to  the  self-serving  exhibits  designated  as
Plaintiffs’  Exhibits  20,  21,  22,  27  and  35.  These  exhibits  were
not  substantive  in  any  manner  and  simply  admitted  for  the
purpose  of showing  that  they  were  before  the  Board  of Trustees
at  the  time  of action.

The  claim  that  the  questions  raised  by the  Plan  Commission
were  promptly  resolved  by  MHDC  simply  is  contrary  to  the
fact  that  MHDC  insisted  upon  what  the  Plan  Commission  found
to  be  spot  zoning,  and  then  refused  to  accept  the  Village
Planner’s  offer  of  assistance  in  seeking  alternative  sites.

The Need for Low and Moderate  Income  Housing.

In  arguing  a  need  for  low  and  moderate  income  housing,  the
respondents  rely  on  a  Village  report  adopted  after  the  hearings
in question,  which  recognized  the  need  for  some  units  of  mod-
erate  and  low  income  housing.  In  their  brief  the  respondents
argue  that  their  decision  was  made  upon a study  by the  Institute
of  Urban  Life,  which  was  admitted  in  evidence  as  Plaintiffs’
Exhibit  18.  Here  again,  its  admission  was  over  objection  of  the
Village  and  it was  admitted  for  the  limited  purpose  of showing
that  it was  one  document  which  had  been  presented  by MHDC
at  the  hearing  and  subsequently  submitted  to  the  Board  of
Trustees.  No  effort  was  made  to  introduce  any  testimony  by the
Institute  of  Urban  Life  to  support  the  findings  of  that  study,
which  of  course  encompasses  areas  well  beyond  the  Village  of
Arlington  Heights.  The  Court  of  Appeals  apparently  again
relied  upon  this  study,  failing  to  note  the  limited  purpose  for
which  it had  been  admitted  in evidence  and  the failure  to support
the  study  by qualifying  testimony.

Again,  Ferrara  testified  to  a market  study  that  he  had  made.
This  study  had  consisted  of an  inquiry  of 900  people;  173  were
eligible  for  236  housing,  and  25  of  those  were  residents  of
Arlington  Heights.  Ferrara  did  not  know  whether  low  and  mod-
erate  income  housing  was  available  in  the  towns  where  the
other  eligibles  lived.  (Tr.  293)  He  had  not  made  a  survey  to
determine  whether  the  eligibles  desired  to  live  in  the  kind  of
housing  proposed  by MHDC.

Thus,  the  evidence  as  to  need  for  low  and  moderate  income
housing  in  the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights  is  sketchy  at  best
and  has,  in  any  event,  no  relationship  to  racial  composition.

12

13

The  fact  that  the  Village  has  rezoned  60  parcels  of  land
for  multiple-family  development  indicates  the  desire  of  the
Village  to produce  a multiplicity  of housing  types  and  to  accom-
modate  different  lifestyles  within  the  community.  The  fact  that
some  of these  projects  are  adjacent  on  one  side  to  single-family
homes  indicates  merely  that  multiple-family  developments  are
used  as  buffers  under  appropriate  conditions.  No  reference  is
made  in  the  respondents’  brief  to  the  numerous  rejections  by
the  Village  of  multiple-family  developments  of  an  unsubsidized
nature  when  the  location  was  inappropriate.

The  only  other  236  project  ever  submitted  in  Arlington
Heights  was  actually  withdrawn  before  it was  ever  presented  to
the  Village  Board.  (Tr.  853)  There  is  no  evidence  that  the
petitioners  have  approved  rezoning  for  90%  of  the  apartment
developments  proposed  to  be  built  in the  Village.  The  evidence
with  respect  to  the  rental  range  for  apartments  indicates  that
apartments  are  available  as  low  as  $160.00  a month.  (Tr.  777)

Contrary  to  the  statement  in  the  respondents’  brief,  there  is
no  evidence  that  there  are  not  units  available  that  persons  of
moderate  income  can  afford.  The  respondents’  references  to  the
transcript  simply  do  not  support  the  assertions  made  in  their
brief.

in the Arlington Heights Area.
Residential  Segregation

As  previously  indicated,  the  testimony  of  Pierre  deVise  at
best  shows  that  3,244  blacks  could  afford  housing  in  Arlington
Heights  based  on  the  1970  census  figures  with  respect  to  income
and  housing.  If a  substantial  number  of blacks  do  not  reside  in
Arlington  Heights,  therefore,  it  is  not  the  result  of  the  zoning
pattern  or  even  the  economics  of  the  market  place,  but  rather
for  reasons  known  only  to  black  home  seekers  and  to  other
factors  clearly  not  related  to  the  Village’s  zoning  function.  373
F.  Supp.  208,  210.

The  statements  made  with  respect  to  respondents,  Guthrie
and  Ransom,  who  were  employed  at  the  Honeywell  Plant

simply  are  not  related  to  the  issues  in  this  case.  As  we  shall
indicate  below,  there  is no  evidence  that  either  would  qualify  for
the  MHDC  project.  One  is  a homeowner  in Chicago;  the  other
occupies  a  home  in Evanston.  373  F.  Supp.  208,  210.

It  is not  true  that  no  black  workers  have  been  able  to  find
housing  they  can  afford  in  Arlington  Heights.  The  record  no-
where  so  indicates  and  the  testimony  of Mr.  Ransom  at  page  322
was  properly  stricken.  Guthrie  conceded  in  fact  that  he  had
never  actually  sought  out  any  housing  from  any  realtor  in
Arlington  Heights.  (Tr.  221)

Again,  most  of the  respondents’  argument  under  this  point  in
their  Statement  of  Facts  is  based  upon  exhibits  prepared  by
the  Office  of  Economic  Opportunity  and  the  Urban  League,
and  which  were  admitted  solely  for  the  purpose  of showing  that
they  were  before  the  Village  Board  at  the  time  of  decision  and
not  for  the  truth  of  the  facts  therein  stated.  No  representative
of the  OEO  or  the  Urban  League  was  present  to  testify  or  verify
in any  respect  the  substance  of these  exhibits.  Yet,  these  totally
unverified  exhibits  became  the  basis  for  the  Court  of  Appeals’
conclusions.

The Effect  on  Minorities  of the Rejection  of the
MHDC  Development.

Respondents  argue  that  the  refusal  to  permit  construction  of
Lincoln  Green  will  be to  virtually  maintain  the  all-white  housing
patterns  of  Arlington  Heights  and  deprive  non-white  citizens
of housing  and  employment  opportunities.  There  is of course  no
evidence  in  the  record  to  support  this  conclusion.  The  citations
to  the  transcript  simply  do  not  bear  out  this  conclusion.  The
testimony  of  Mr.  deVise,  upon  which  much  of the  respondents’
argument  is based,  is simply  that  “blacks  make  up  about  40%  of
the  potentially  eligible  population  for  moderate  income  housing
in  the  SMSA.  ..  .”  (Tr.  592)

14

15

There  is  actually  no  evidence  to  show  any  relationship  be-
tween  the  rejection  of  the  MHDC  development  and  the  racial
character  of  the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights.  The  Court  of
Appeals’  conclusion  that,  if Lincoln  Green  were  built  it  would
be  an  integrated  community,  is  not  based  upon  any  evidence.
Since  there  could  have  been  more  than  3,000  blacks  residing
in Arlington  Heights  based  upon  the  1970  census  figures  without
the  MHDC  development,  it  is  difficult  to  conclude  that  the
building  of  the  MHDC  project  would  in  any  way  change  the
racial  composition  of  the  community.  Moreover,  this  argument
ignores  the  basic  fact  that  what  is  at  issue  here  is  not  racial
discrimination  in  any  manner,  but  simply  a  refusal  to  alter  a
