valid  and  existing  zoning  pattern  so  that  people  of  low  income
can  occupy  property  they  would  otherwise  not  be  able  to  afford.

There  is no  need  for  any  signal  that  the  Village  of Arlington
Heights  is no  longer  closed  to  blacks.  The  Village  has  never  been
closed  to  blacks,  and  there  is no  present  or  future  intent  to  ever
attempt  to  close  the  Village  to  blacks  or  anyone  else.

There  is  no  demonstrable  effect  on  minorities  wt  ich  arises
from  the  rejection  of the  MHDC  development.  As  we  have  indi-
cated  in  our  original  brief,  the  mere  fact  that  a  valid  police
power  regulation  might  have  a  greater  impact  upon  poor  people
or  a  minority  group  does  not  invalidate  that  regulation.  The
highly  emotional  and  totally  unsupported  allegations  which
appear  in  the  Statement  of  the  Case  presented  by  the  re-
spondents  cannot  fill  the  absolute  void  of evidence  which  occurs
in  this  case  as  far  as  the  respondents  are  concerned.

The  Village Hearings and  the Community  Response.

The  respondents  in thier  brief  attempt  to  create  out  of  whole
cloth  a fabric  of racial  bias  in the  history  of the hearings  before
the  Plan  Commission  and  the  Village  Board.  The  evidence
clearly  does  not  support  any  such  characterization.  There  is  no
evidence  that  the  Commission  or  Trustees  were  “besieged  with

advice  on  the  social  issue.”  In  any  event,  the  trial  court  specif-
ically  found  that  the  decision  was  not  based  on  any  racial
motivation,  and  the  Court  of  Appeals  refused  to  disturb  this
finding.  As  a  matter  of  fact,  neither  opinion  indicates  that  the
Court  took  seriously  this  totally  unsupported  claim  of racial  bias
in the hearings  below.

The  fact,  if true,  that  certain  of the  objectors  might  have  been
motivated  by racial  factors  of  course  does  not  either  imply  that
such  considerations  influenced  the  Village  officials,  or  in any  way
affect  the  validity  of  the  proceedings  before  the  Village  Board.
Ranjel  v.  City  of  Lansing,  417  F.  2d  321  (6th  Cir.  1969).

The  fact  that  the  respondents  were  able  to  come  up  with  two
or  three  letters  to  a  local  newspaper  from  a  town  of  almost
70,000  of course  does  not  demonstrate  any  racial  bias  upon  the
part  of  the  Plan  Commission  or  the  Village  Board.  While
certain  of  the  homeowners  filed  petitions  in  opposition  to  the
project,  the  respondents  concede  that  there  were  also  staunch
supporters,  including  church  groups  and  the  League  of  Women
Voters,  and  that,  in  fact,  petitions  in  support  of  the  proposal
were  received  and  considered  as  well  as  those  of  the  objectors.

The  so-called  emotional  racial  reactions  on  the  part  of  over-
flowing  crowds  simply  are  figments  of the  respondents’  imagina-
tion.  The  references  to  the  transcript  simply  indicate  that  the
hearings  were  well  attended  and  that  there  were  staunch  par-
tisans  on  both  sides.  Significantly,  Joseph  Kesler,  the  Village
Planner  who  attended  the  meetings  of  the  Plan  Commission,
testified  that  there  were  similar  meetings  on  other  projects
attended  by just  as  many  people,  and  that  these  were  usually
meetings  or  hearings  which  involved  multiple-family  projects.
(Tr.  112-113)

The  ability  to  cite  three  letters  to  the  editor  and  one  Jetter
to  the  Plan  Commission  and  Village  Board  hardly  demonstrates
the  kind  of  bedlam  and  racial  antagonism  that  the  respondents
would  like  this  Court  to  believe  took  place.  As  a  matter  of fact,

16

17

the  testimony  of  Mrs.  Harms,  the  only  Trustee  actually  called
by  the  respondents,  was  that  she  had  received  a  total  of  one
letter  in  opposition  to  the  project.  (Tr.  168)

To  equate  this  evidence,  if  it  can  be  dignified  by  calling  it
such,  with  the  situation  in  the  Dailey  case  simply  indicates  that
the  respondents  have  lost  touch  with  reality.  The  opinion  in
Dailey  v.  City  of  Lawton,  425  F.  2d  1037  (10th  Cir.  1970)
was  based  upon  specific  findings  by the  District  Court  that  the
actions  of  the  planning  commission  and  city  council  were  a
direct  result  of the  bias  and  prejudice  on  the  part  of the  owners
of other  property  in the  North  Addition,  which  was  carried  over
to  members  of those  bodies.  There  were  further  findings  that  the
motivation  for  the  denial  of  the  zoning  change  was  to  keep
a large  concentration  of Negroes  and  other  minority  groups  from
living  in the  North  Addition.  The  evidence  in Dailey  noted  that
the  racial  situation  was  discussed  in  connection  with  the  circu-
lation  of  the  petitions,  and  that  the  sponsors  had  received
anonymous  phone  calls  opposed  to  the  project  on a racial  basis.
A  member  of  the  planning  commission  testified  that  the  oppo-
sition  was  based  on  racial  bias.  425  F.  2d  1037,  1039.

No  such  evidence  is present  in the  record  in this  case  and  no
such  findings  were  made  by the  trial  court.  In fact,  the  trial  court
here  specifically  found  that  the  action  of the  Village  Board  was
not  racially  motivated.

An  examination  of  Kennedy  Park  Homes  Asso.  v.  City  of
Lackawanna,  318  F.  Supp.  669,  and  United  States  v.  City  of
Blackjack,  508  F.  2d  1179,  also  shows  no  relationship  between
the  factual  situation  there  and  what  transpired  in  Arlington
Heights.

The  objections  of the  property  owners  presumably  were  based
on  the  opposition  to  multiple-family  zoning  usually  expressed
by single-family  homeowners  when a project  with  a  density  of
three  times  the  existing  density  is proposed  to  be  inserted  in  the
midst  of  a  prime  and  well-established  single-family  area.  It  is
indeed  ironic  that  in  the  body  of  their  brief,  the  respondents

argue  that  what  this  Court  should  consider  is  the  effect  of  the
decision  and  not  the  motivations  of those  who  made  the  decision.
Yet,  in  their  Statement  of  the  Case,  the  respondents  would
attempt  to  convince  this  Court  that,  contrary  to  the  facts  and  the
evidence,  the  Village  Board  was  motivated  by racial  bias.

The Proceedings  Below.

As  indicated  in  the  respondents’  brief,  much  of  their  case
on  the  trial  was  based  upon  their  own  exhibits  which  were
admitted  for  the  sole  purpose  of  showing  that  they  were  before
the  Village  Trustees  when  the  development  was  rejected.  In  the
Court  of  Appeals  and  in  this  brief,  the  limited  purpose  for
which  those  exhibits  were  admitted  is being  stretched  to  indicate
that  somehow  or
other  the  substance  of  those  exhibits  were
proven  at  the  trial.

The  respondents’  brief  of course  fails  to  indicate  in  any  man-
ner  the  nature  of the  evidence  adduced  by the  Village  during  the
course  of the  trial.  That  evidence  included  the  expert  testimony
of  the  Village  Planner,  Mr.  Kesler,  the  zoning  and  planning
consultant,  Mr.  Campbell,  the  real  estate  appraiser,  Mr.  Opelka,
Trustee  Alice  Harms,  and  Planning  Engineer,  John  Best.  All  is
ignored  in  the  respondents’  brief.

The  respondents  also  fail  to  note  the  numerous  examples  of
rejection  of  other  improperly  located  multiple-family  develop-
ments.  Nowhere  is there  any  evidence  of any  “tremendous”  need
for  low  and  moderate  income  housing  in Arlington  Heights,  nor
do  the  respondents  remind  this  Court  that  there  are  only  a  half
dozen  communities  in the  Chicago  metropolitan  area  with  more
than  50,000  residents.

Contrary  to  the  allegations  contained  in  the  respondents’
brief,  the  record  does  not  demonstrate  that  there  were  no
other  suitable  sites  for  low  and  moderate  income  housing  in  the
Arlington  Heights  area.  In  fact.  MHDC’s  only  other  multiple-
family  development,  Rand  Grove  Village,  is  located  three-

18

19

nine  undeveloped  tracts  of  land  in  excess  of  fifteen  acres
zoned  R-5  (multi-family  housing).  The  record  does  not
contain  a  sufficient  showing  by  plaintiffs  that  it  was  not
reasonably  possible  to  construct  the  proposed  project  on
one  of  these  sites.”  517  F.  2d  409,  416.

In  fact,  as  indicated,  Plaintiffs’  Exhibit  56  indicates  that
these  nine  sites  were  rejected  without  any  extensive  investigation.
Thus,  the  contention  of  the  respondents  that  there  were  no
economically  feasible  and  suitable  alternate  sites  simply  is
not  supported  by the record.  In the  course  of cross-examination,
Kane  indicated  that  two  of  the  sites  were  rejected  because  they
were  a  mile  away  from  shopping.  (Tr.  62)  It was  his  position
that  all  the  developments  had  to  be  within  one  mile  of shopping
facilities  (Tr.  63),  although,  of  course,  their  existing  project,
Rand  Grove,  did  not  meet  that  requirement.  Kane  also  testified
that  he  personally  had  contacted  only  one  broker  or  owner  of
alternative  parcels.  (Tr.  69)

fourths  of  a  mile  away  from  the  corporate  limits  of  the  Village
of  Arlington  Heights.

Mr.  Kane  admitted  that  the  subject  property  could  be  de-
veloped  for  single-family  homes  under  the  present  zoning  under
Section  235  federal  funding  if  the  land  could  be  bought  for
$200,000  instead  of  $300,000,  and  that  the  cost  of  the  land
was  the  only  impediment  to  single-family  development.  (Tr.
54)  Kane  further  testified  that  two  sites  in  Arlington  Heights
were  rejected  because  they  were  a  mile  or  a  mile  and  a  quarter
away  from  shopping  facilities.  He  rejected  other  sites  because  of
the  purported  distance  from  schools  and  shopping  facilities.  As
a  matter  of  fact,  Plaintiff's  Exhibit  56  shows  the  nine  possible
sites  were  rejected  out  of  hand  because  they  were  thought  too
remote  or  too  far  from  convenience  shopping  centers  or  too
closely  surrounded  by single-family  homes.

Despite  Mr.  Kane’s  testimony  that  he  rejected  these  sites
because  of the  distance  from  schools  and  shopping,  the  fact  of the
matter  is  that  the  Rand  Grove  facility  is  two  miles  away  from
the  nearest  shopping  facility  and  two  miles  away  from  the  nearest
school.

One  of  the  five  possible  sites  was  rejected  because  no  reply
had  been  received  from  a  telephone  call  and  letter;  several  of
the  remaining  four  were  rejected  because  of price,  although  there
is no  evidence  of anv  direct  attempt  to negotiate  the  acquisition  of
any  other  site.  Significantly,  the  Village  Manager  had  initially
met  with  Mr.  Kane  ic  suggest  sites  available  in  the  community,
and  Mr.  Kesler’s  willingness  to  meet  after  the  hearings  to  suggest
other  sites  was  totally  ignored.

The  lack  of  evidence  that  the  respondents  really  did  not
seek  alternate  sites  in  an  area  which  would  be  more  appropriate
for  multiple-family  zoning  is reflected  in Chief  Judge  Fairchild’s
dissenting  opinion,  where  he  says:

“A  review  of  the  record  reveals  that,  at  the  time  of  the
Village’s  decision  to  deny  the  variance,  there  were  at  least

20

ARGUMENT.

I.

THE  COURT  OF  APPEALS  ERRED  IN  HOLDING  THAT  THE
PETITIONERS’  REFUSAL  TO  PERMIT  THE  CONSTRUC-
TION  OF  LINCOLN  GREEN  VIOLATED  THE  FOUR-
TEENTH  AMENDMENT.

A.  The  Record  Fails  to  Show  That  the  Petitioners’  Refusal  to
Permit  the  Construction  of  Lincoln  Green  Was  Racially
Motivated.

The  trial  court  specifically  found  that  the  plaintiffs  failed  to
carry  their  burden  of  proving  discrimination  by  defendants
against  racial  minorities.  373  F.  Supp.  208,  210.  The  Court
of Appeals  refused  to  set  aside  this  finding  of fact.  It specifically
found  that  the  evidence  did  not  support  a  finding  that  Arlington
Heights  administered  its  zoning  policy  in  a  discriminatory  man-
ner.  517  F.  2d  409,  412.  Yet,  the  respondents  in  their  brief
somewhat  hysterically  maintain  their  unsupported  allegation
that  the  petitioners’  refusal  to  rezone  a  15-acre  tract,  located
in the  midst  of  a  single-family  area,  to  permit  multiple  dwellings
at  three  times  the  single-family  density,  was  racially  motivated.

This  Court  has  repeatedly  held  that,  even  in  a  civil  rights
case,  the  motivations  of  municipal  officials  are  not  subject  to
judicial  inquiry.  Palmer  v.  Thompson,  403  U.  S.  217  (1971),
20  L.  ed.  2d  438;  United  States  v.  O’Brien,  391  U.  S.  367,
383  (1968),  20  L.  ed.  2d  672,  683;  Detroit  United  Railway
v.  Detroit,  255  U.  S.  171  (1920),  65  L.  ed.  2d  570;  Hing  v.
Crowley,  113  U.  S.  708  (1884),  28  L.  ed.  1145,  1147.

Despite  their  repeated  assertions,  the  respondents  fail  to
point  out  anything  in the  record  which  is susceptible  of showing
racial  motivations  on  the  part  of  the  Village  Plan  Commission

21

or  the  Village  Trustees.  The  fact  that  the  public  hearings  were
well  attended  and  frequently  emotional  does  not  prove  racial
motivation  on  the  part  of  Village  officials.  As  Mr.  Kesler
pointed  out,  zoning  hearings  involving  proposed  multiple-family
rezoning  petitions  frequently  have  produced  larger  crowds  and
extreme  emotions.  The  fact  that  the  respondents  are  reduced
to  talking  about  two  or  three  letters  which  appeared  in  the
newspaper  falls  far  short  of  proving  any  racial  bias  or  motiva-
tion  on  the  part  of  Village  officials.

The  fact  that  the  Village  President  noted  that  there  was  com-
munity  opposition  to  the  proposed  rezoning  certainly  is  no  in-
dication  of  racial  bias  on  the  part  of  President  Walsh  or  anyone
else.  As  a  matter  of  fact,  Walsh  himself  proposed  assistance  in
seeking  other  sites,  and  the  Village  Planner,  as  well  as  the
Village  Manager,  made  themselves  available  for  such  purposes.

The  citation  of  the  case  of  Dailey  v.  City  of  Lawton,  425
F,  2d  1037,  simply  is of  no  relevance.  In  the  Dailey  case  the
area  for  which  rezoning  was  sought  was  totally  surrounded  by
property  zoned  for  high-density,  multiple-family  purposes.  Yet,
similar  rezoning  was  denied  on  the  subject  property.
In  the
Dailey  case,  the  District  Court  had  specifically  made  findings
of  racial  motivation.  The  Court  had  found  that  there  was  no
evidence  in  support  of  contentions  that  the  rezoning  was  denied
for  any  other  reason,  and  the  director  of the  plan  commission  in
Dailey  had  testified  that  there  was  no  reason  why  the  property
there  should  not  have  been  rezoned  to  the  R-4  high-density
multiple  sought  for  low  income  housing.

In  the  instant  case,  of  course,  both  the  trial  court  and  the
Court  of Appeals  recognized  that  the  Village  was  administering
its  Zoning  Ordinance  in  a  non-discriminatory  manner,  and  all
the  Court  of  Appeals  held  was  that,  maintaining  the  integrity
of  the  zoning  plan  and  protecting  neighboring  property  values,
both  of which  objectives  were  concededly  achieved  by the  Zoning
Ordinance,  simply  did  not  meet  the  “compelling  interest”  test.
It simply  is contrary  to  the  facts  to  allege  that  the  petitioners

22

23

admitted  they  blocked  Lincoln  Green  in response  to  community
opposition,  a result  they knew  would  maintain  residential  segrega-
tion  in  the  area.  The  purpose  was  maintaining  a  single-family
zoning  Classification  which  was  completely  consistent  with  the
well-established  single-family  community.  It had  nothing  what-
soever  to  do  with  racial  considerations.

The  other  cases  cited  in  the  footnote  to  respondents’  brief,
including  Kennedy  Park  Homes,  Black  Jack,  Banks,  United
Farmworkers,  and  Hawkins,  have  no  application  to  the  case  at
bar,  particularly  in  light  of  the  faci  that  there  was  no  finding
by either  the  trial  court  or  the  Court  of  Appeals  of  any  racial
motivation  in  the  instant  case.

B.  The  Petitioners’  Refusal  to  Permit  the  Construction  of
Lincoln  Green  Does  Not  Unlawfully  Perpetuate  Residential
Segregation.

The  holding  of  the  Seventh  Circuit  was  that  the  rejection  of
the  Lincoln  Green  proposal  has  racially  discriminatory  effects.
517  F.  2d  409,  415.  That  finding,  we  respectfully  suggest,  is
totally  unsupported  by  the  record  in  this  case.  The  Court  of
Appeals  ignored  the  finding  of  the  trial  court  that  the  failure
to  rezone  affects  low  earners  “but  this  affects  the  entire  group,
not  merely  blacks  or  Mexican-Americans.
The  Fourteenth
Amendment  and  the  Civil  Rights  Act  prohibit  discrimination
against  blacks  and  certain  other  minorities  but  does  not  afford
rights  to  poor  people  as  such.”  373  F.  Supp.  208,  210.

Moreover,  an  analysis  of  the  record  clearly  indicates  that
the refusal  to rezone  Lincoln  Green  does  not  have  a demonstrable
effect  of  discriminating  against  minorities.

The  Court  of  Appeals  accepted  two  premises  offered  by  the
respondents  which  simply  are  not  justified  by  the  record.  The
first  was  a  conclusion  reflected  in  the  footnote  at  page  414  of
the  opinion  that  Arlington  Heights  was  the  most  residentially
segregated  community  in  the  Chicago  metropolitan  area  among

municipalities  with  more  than  50,000  residents.  deVise  never
said  that.  What  deVise  did  say  was  that,  based  purely  on  a
highly  theoretical  distribution  of  black  households  throughout
the  metropolitan  area,  5%  of the  population  of Arlington  Heights
would  be  black,  but  that  was  based  solely  on  the  economics
of  the  housing  market.  (Tr.  625)  deVise  never  suggested  that
the  failure  of  more  than  5%  of  the  black  population  to  reside
in  Arlington  Heights  was  due  to  anything  but  the  economics  of
the  situation.  He  described  Arlington  Heights  as  a  dormitory
community.  (Tr.  631)  It  had  the  most  expensive  housing  of
all  the  communities  having  more  than  50,000  population.  Even
so,  deVise  conceded  that,  based  upon  the  economics  of  the
housing  market,  at  least  3,244  blacks  could  afford  to  live  in
Arlington  Heights,  of  which  378  black  households  would  be
able  to  afford  single-family  housing  in  Arlington  Heights,  and
473  black  households  would  be able  to  rént  housing  in Arlington
Heights.  (Tr.  588)  deVise  never  suggested  that  the  action  of
Village  officials  through  zoning  or  anything  else  was  keeping
blacks  from  residing  in  Arlington  Heights.  deVise’s  testimony
to  the  effect  that  he  believed  the  reasons  blacks  didn’t  reside  in
suburban  communities  was  their  fear  of  lack  of  acceptance,
was  stricken  from  the  record.  (Tr.  637,  638)  The  most  that
can  be  said  is that  the  economics  of  the  housing  market  restrict
the  number  of poor  people  who  could  afford  to  live  in Arlington
Heights.

It  is  of  course  not  true,  as  stated  by  the  Court  of  Appeals,
that  a  greater  percentage  of  blacks  than  whites  are  affected  by
Even  if  we  accept  the  premise  that
the  Village’s  decision.
blacks  would  comprise  40%  of  the  eligible  prospective  tenants
for  Lincoln  Green,  that  would  mean  that  60%  non-blacks  would
also  comprise  the  eligible  prospective  tenants  and  the  impact
would  be  greater  on  non-blacks  than  on  blacks.

But  the  assertion  that  40%  of  the  eligible  prospective  tenants
would  be  black  simply  is not  supported  by the  record.  All  deVise
said  was  that  blacks  make  up  about  40%  of  the  potentially

24

25

eligible  population  for  moderate  income  housing  in  the  SMA.
(Tr.  592)  The  SMA  consists  of six  counties.  There  was  abso-
lutely  no  evidence  in  the  record  to  indicate  that  a  single  black
or  Spanish-speaking  person  would  in fact  seek  to  live  in Lincoln
Green.  Here  again,  as  the  trial  court  noted,  “there  are  many  rea-
sons  why  members  of  minority  groups  would  not  necessarily
move  into  the  defendant  village,  besides  alleged  discriminatory
violations.”  373  F.  Supp.  208,  210.

Respondents’  reliance  upon  Burton  v.  Wilmington  Parking
Authority,  365  U.  S.  715  (1961),  and  Wright  v.  City  of
Emporia,  407  U.  S.  451  (1972)  is  unavailing.  Neither  case
cited  supports  the  proposition  that  the  refusal  to  rezone  is  un-
lawful.  The  Burton  case  involved  the  specific  refusal  to  serve
a Negro  in a restaurant  located  in a  public  building.  The  Wright
case  involved  a  dual  school  system  which  had  the  effect  of
perpetuating  racial  discrimination  in  violation  of  this  Court's
decision  in  the  Brown  case.

There  is no  showing  that  the  Village’s  action  has  anything  to
do  with  reinforcing  or  prolonging  segregated  housing  patterns
in  the  Village.  The  respondents’  assertion  that  the  Village’s  ac-
tion  is  subject  to  “strict  scrutiny”  and  requires  a  “compelling
justification”  is  not  in  accord  with  the  numerous  decisions  of
this  Court  and  other  courts  of  appeal  throughout  the  country.
Village  of  Belle  Terre  v.  Boraas,  416  U.  S.  1  (1974),  39  L.
ed.  2d  797;  Lindsey  v.  Normet,  405  U.  S.  56  (1972).  31  L.  ed.
2d  36:  San  Antonio  Independent  School  District  v.  Rodriguez,
411  U.S.  1  (1973),  36  L.  ed.  2d  16;  Lawrence  v.  Oakes,  361
F.  Supp.  432  (D.  Vt.  1973);  Skillken  &  Co.  v.  City  of Toledo,
Nos.  74-2116  and  74-2320  (6th  Cir.  December  10,  1975);
Citizens  Committee  for Faraday  Wood.  v.  Lindsay,  362  F.  Supp.
651  (S.  D.N.  Y.  1973),  affd  507  F.  2d  1065  (2nd  Cir.  1974).
The  result  of  the  Village’s  action  in  the  present  case  of
course  is not  to  exclude  minorities  from  or  perpetuate  segrega-
tion  in  Arlington  Heights.  As  indicated  in  the  Skillken  case,  a

rational  basis  test  rather  than  a  compelling  interest  test  is to  be
applied  in  testing  of  zoning  ordinances.

The  Court  of  Appeals’  analysis  that  a  greater  percentage  of
blacks  than  whites  would  be injured  by the  municipality’s  actions
simply  is contrary  to  the  facts.  However,  James  v.  Valtierra,  402
U.  S.  137  (1971),  stands  for  the  proposition  that  the  fact  that
a  legal  requirement  may  have  a  greater  impact  upon  the  poor
or  other  minority  does  not  render  that  requirement  invalid  under
the  Fourteenth  Amendment.  The  Seventh  Circuit  reluctantly
admitted  that  James  stands  for  the  proposition  that  racial  dis-
parity  alone,  as  it relates  to  the  housing  project  under  considera-
tion,  would  not  amount  to  racial  discrimination.

Reliance  on  the  Dailey  case,  as  indicated,  is  simply  un-
warranted  because  of  the  difference  in  the  facts  shown  by  the
record.  There  is  no  segregative  effect  which  results  from  the
petitioners’  refusal  to  permit  construction  of  Lincoln  Green.  As
the  trial  court  properly  recognized,  the  only  effect  of  that  re-
fusal  is  to  deny  the  right  of  poor  people  to  live  wherever  they
want  to  live,  notwithstanding  a  reasonable  zoning  classification.

The  respondents  in  their  brief  cite  the  footnotes  in  the  Court
of  Appeals’  opinion  setting  forth  certain  statistical  data.  The
fact  of  course  that  the  Chicago  metropolitan  area  has  seen  a
shift  in  population  and a shift  of  job  locations  does  not  mean
that  the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights  violated  the  Fourteenth
Amendment  when  it refused  an  irrational  zoning  request.  There
of course  is no  evidence  that  the  reason  only  137  of  the  12,000
people  who  worked  in  Arlington  Heights  were  black  was  due
to  the  zoning  pattern  of  the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights.  In
fact,  as  indicated  above,  a  substantial  number  of  blacks  can
afford  to  live  in  Arlington  Heights  and  their  reasons  for  fail-
ing to  do  so  have  nothing  to  do  with  the  zoning  pattern  of  the
Village.

Citation  of  an  OEO  study  showing  that  almost  all  the  black
workers  in Arlington  Heights  reside  in Chicago  also  has  nothing
to  do  with  the  issues  in  this  case.  As  a  matter  of fact,  there  are

26

27

more  residents  of  Arlington  Heights  working  in  Chicago  than
there  are  residents  of  Arlington  Heights  working  in  Arlington
Heights.  The  choice  of job  locations  is obviously  dependent  on
many  factors  and  certainly  cannot  be  used  to  impute  to  the
Village  of Arlington  Heights  racial  motivations  in  preserving  a
single-family  zoning  classification  in an  area  which  is and  always
has  been  exclusively  single  family.

Nowhere  in their  brief  do  the  respondents  choose  to  refer  to
Milliken  v.  Bradley,  418  U.  S.  717  (1974),  where  this  Court
in  a  school  segregation  case  held  that  racial  imbalance  in  itself
is  not  to  be  equated  with  a  constitutional  violation  calling
for  remedy.  This  Court  indicated  that  the  fact  that  Detroit
schools  were  segregated  did  not  require  an  intra-district  plan
outside  the  corporate  limits,  absent  any  showing  that  the  subur-
ban  school  district  had  in  fact  discriminated.

Here,  it was  conceded  by both  the  trial  court  and  the  Court
of  Appeals  that  the  Village  had  not  discriminated  in  its  zon-
ing  pattern.  The  Lincoln  Green  proposal  does  not  present  a
unique  opportunity  to  reverse  a  supposed  trend  toward  the  seg-
regated  housing  pattern  in  the  Chicago  area  or  in  Arlington
Heights.  There  is no  evidence  that  any  blacks  would  occupy  the
Lincoln  Green  project,  nor  is there  any  evidence  that  the  Zoning
Ordinance  of  the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights  had  anything  to
do  with  segregation,  either  in  the  Chicago  area  or  in  the
Village  of Arlington  Heights.  It is not  true  that  the  rejection  of
Lincoln  Green  means  that  there  will  be  no  integrated  or  sub-
sidized  housing  built  in  Arlington  Heights.

The  test  as  to  whether  a  zoning  ordinance  perpetuates  dis-
crimination  is  whether  it  has  been  applied  in  a  racially  dis-
criminatory  manner  and  completely  out  of  context  with  the
surrounding  land  uses.  Thus,  as  in  Dailey  v.  City  of  Lawton,
425  F.  2d  1037,  it  may  well  be  argued  that,  when  an  R-4
multiple-family  zoning  classification  is denied  to  a  tract  of  land
which  is  totally  surrounded  by an  R-4  classification  simply  be-
cause  there  would  be  blacks  residing  on  the  rezoned  site,  racial

discrimination  exists,  but  that  argument  cannot  stand  when  the
zoning  is applied  in a  nondiscriminatory  manner  which  is totally
consistent  with  sound  zoning  and  planning  principles.  Con-
trary  to  the  contention  in the  respondents’  brief,  the  Dailey  case
is in no  way  similar  to  the  insiant  case.

The  citation  of  United  States  v.  Black  Jack,  508  F.  2d  1179,
is likewise  totally  inappropriate.  In Black  Jack,  the  village  passed
an  ordinance  totally  banning  multiple-family  dwellings  after  a
reservation  of  federal  funds  had  been  made  for  a  low  income
multiple  housing  project.  In  that  case  the  evidence  adduced  in
support  of  the  ordinance  was  inadequate  with  respect  to  traffic
and  burden  on  the  schools.  There  was  evidence  showing  no
depreciation  on  nearby  properties,  which  was  not  controverted.
As  a  matter  of  fact,  the  ordinance  in  that  case  would  have
resulted  in  less  than  one  per  cent  of  the  land  in  the  community
being  zoned  for  apartments,  despite  a  demonstrated  need  for
apartments.  Black  Jack  in  fact  stands  for  the  proposition  that
the  plaintiff  has  to  establish  a  prima  facie  case  by demonstrat-
ing  a  racially  discriminatory  effect.  508  F.  2d  1179,  1185.
There  was  of  course  no  racially  discriminatory  effect  demon-
strated  in  the  instant  case.

The  case  of  Kennedy  Park  Homes  Association  v.  City  of
Lackawanna,  318  F.  Supp.  669,  also  cited  by the  respondents,
again  is  not  in  point.  In  that  case  property  was  rezoned  to
prevent  a  specific  housing  development  and  a  moratorium  was
declared  on  new  subdivisions,  after  the  intent  of the  Archdiocese
to  sell  land  for  a  low  income  housing  project  was  made  known.
After  the  rezoning  was  set  aside,  the development  was  prevented
by  the  mayor’s  refusal  to  sign  a  permit  for  sewer  extension,
despite  the  fact  that  the  sewer  conditions  relied  upon  to  sup-
port  the  refusal  of the  permit  had  existed  for many  years  and  had
not  been  the  basis  for  any  other  refusal.  The  factual  situation  in
Kennedy  included  the  fact  that  the  mayor  and  the  president
of the  city  council  had  signed  a  petition  to  the  Archdiocese  op-
posing  the  sale  of the  land.  The  City  of Lackawanna  historically

28

29

had  demonstrated  a  segregated  pattern  with  the  Negroes  being
confined  almost  entirely  to  one  ward.  What  is significant  for  the
instant  case  is  the  holding  found  on  page  694  of  the  Kennedy
Park  Homes  case  to  the  effect  that  the  Fair  Housing  Act  of  1968
does  not  include  discrimination  based  on  poverty.

Crow  Vv.  Brown,  332  F.  Supp.  382,  also  relied  upon  by the
respondents,  does  not  support  the  respondents’  position.  In  that
case  there  was  a  refusal  to  issue  building  permits  for  public  low
rent  housing  on  land  properly  zoned.  The  record  in  that  case
showed  that  the  denial  of  the  permits  was  purely  for  racial
reasons.

The  case  of  United  Farmworkers  v.  City  of  Delray,  493  F.
2d  799,  is  not  a  zoning  case.  In  that  instance  there  was  a
request  to  tie  into  the  city’s  existing  water  and  sewer  system
which  was  800  feet  away  from  the  farmworkers  housing  project
site.  The  city  had  consistently  offered  sewer  service  to  other
property  outside  the  corporate  limits.  Under  those  circum-
stances  the  Court  held  that  since  the  city  had  provided  services
outside  its  geographic  limits,  it  must  do  so  in a racially  non-
discriminatory  manner.  The  evidence  was  clear  that  the  minority
citizens’  requests  were  refused,  but  white  citizens’  requests  were
granted.  The  city’s  master  plan  had  been  changed  for  whites,
but  not  for  farmworkers.  As  a  matter  of  fact,  at  page  808  of
the  opinion  the  Court  pointed  out  that  the  farmworkers’  appeal
was  not  based  primarily  on  a  claim  of a denial  of a  fundamental
right to decent  housing,  citing  Lindsey  v.  Normet,  405  U.  S.  56,
or  upon a claim  of  discriminatory  treatment  engendered  by  a
suspect  wealth  classification,  citing  San  Antonio  Independent
School  District  v.  Rodriguez,  411  U.  S.  1.  Clearly,  in  United
Farmworkers,  there  was  a  denial  of  equal  protection  because
the  minority  group  there  was  denied  privileges  regularly  ex-
tended  to  white  citizens.  No  such  distinction  is  shown  in  the
case  at  bar.

C.

It  Was  Not  Necessary  to  Show  Compelling  Public  Justi-
fication
to  Support  Petitioners’  Rejection  of  Lincoln
Green.

The  respondents  rely  upon  the  Court  of  Appeals’  determina-
tion  that  the  refusal  to  amend  the  Zoning  Ordinance  could  only
be justified  by a compelling  state  interest.  As  we  indicated  in our
original  brief,  the  compelling  interest  test  should  not  be  applied
to  the  case  at  bar.

The  respondents  make  no  attempt  to  answer  the  substantial
line  of  cases  in  which  this  Court  and  the  various  courts  of
appeal  have  held  in  zoning  cases  and  similar  actions  that,  be-
cause  there  was  no  fundamental  corstitutional  right  at  stake,
the  compelling  interest  test  was  not  appropriate.

There  is no  attempt  made  to  distinguish  the  clear  holding  of
this  Court  in  Village  of  Belle  Terre  v.  Boraas,  416  U.  S.  1
(1974),  that  a  zoning  ordinance  involved  no  fundamentai  right
guaranteed  by  the  Constitution.  Also  ignored  are  the  holdings
of this  Court  in  Lindsey  v.  Normet,  405  U.  S.  56  (1972),  and
San  Antonio  Independent  School  District  v.  Rodriguez,  411
U.  S.  1  (1973),  which  clearly  held  that  in  the  case  of  housing
there  is no  need  of justifying  a  law  by showing  compelling  state
interest.

Totally  ignored  is the case  of Skillken  & Co.  v.  City of Toledo,
Nos.  74-2116  and  74-2320,  decided  by  the  Court  of  Appeals
for  the  Sixth  Circuit  on  December  10,  1975.  There  in  a  zoning
case  the  Court  of  Appeals  specifically  held  that  the  District
Court  erred  in  applying  the  compelling  interest  test  rather  than
a  rational  basis  test  in  determining  the  validity  of  the  Toledo
zoning  ordinance.  Also  totally  ignored  is  Citizens  Committee
for Faraday  Wood  v.  Lindsay,  362  F.  Supp.  651,  affd  507  F.
2d  1065  (2nd  Cir.  1974),  where  the  Court  held  that  low  in-
come  status  is  not  recognized  as  a  suspect  classification.

No  mention  is  made  in  the  respondents’  brief  of  the  recent
of  Sonoma

in  Construction  Industry  Association

decision

30

31

County  v.  City  of Petaluma,  No.  74-2100,  decided  by the  Court
of  Appeals  for  the  Ninth  Circuit,  wherein  the  Court  rejected
the  concept  that  an  allegedly  exclusionary  zoning  ordinance
was  a  violation  of the  Fourteenth  Amendment.  The  Court  there
insisted  that  the  test  of  a  zoning  ordinance  was  whether  it bore
any  rational  relationship  to  a  legitimate  state  interest.  It  re-
fused  to  apply  the  compelling  interest  test.  This  Court  denied  a
petition  for  certiorari  in  the  Petaluma  case  on  February  23,
1976,  44  L.  W.  3473.

In  the  case  of  Ybarra  v.  City  of Town  of Los  Altos,  503  F.
2d  250,  the  Court  of  Appeals  for  the  Ninth  Circuit  specifically
refused  to  apply  the  compelling  interest  test  in  a  case  challeng-
ing a  large-lot  zoning  ordinance  on  the  grounds  that  it excluded
Mexican  Americans.  The  Court  there  held  that  discrimination
against  the  poor  does  not  become  discrimination  against  a
minority  simply  because  there  is a statistical  correlation  between
poverty  and  ethnic  background.  503  F.  2d  250,  253.

Thus,  the  Court  of Appeals’  application  of the  compelling  in-

terest  test  simply  is contrary  to  the  settled  law  on  the  subject.

Respondents’  convoluted  argument  that  an  affirmative  duty
has  not  been  imposed  upon  Arlington  Heights  simply  flies  in  the
face  of  the  language  of  the  Court  of  Appeals’  opinion  which
mandates  the  Village  to  take  the  affirmative  action  of  changing
the  Zoning  Ordinance  because  of  the  alleged  possibility  that
blacks  may  reside  in  the  housing  which  could  then  be  built.
At  page  414  of the  opinion,  the  Court  said:  “Since  no  direct  ac-
tion  attributable  to  Arlington  Heights  created  the  segregated
housing  pattern,  the  municipality  argues  that  it has  no  affirmative
duty  to  alleviate  the  problem.  We  do not  agree.”  That  language
clearly  imposes  an  affirmative  duty,  contrary  to  the  decision  of
this  Court  in  Milliken  v.  Bradley,  418  U.  S.  717  (1974),  and
the  numerous  other  cases  cited  in  this  and  our  original  brief.

The  footnote  in  the  respondents’  brief  to  the  effect  that  the
Village  Plan  was  adopted  in  1959  and  most  of  the  homes  were
built  prior  thereto  is not  supported  by the  record.  Mr.  Opelka

testified  that  the  subject  property  adjoins  the  rear  yards  of single-
family  homes,  the  majority  of  which  were  built  in  the  last  10
years.  Across  from  the  high  school  of  Oakton,  the  homes  were
built  in  the  last  few  years,  and  some  new  homes  are  under
construction.  On  Euclid  Avenue,  across  the  street  from  the
subject  property,  are  homes  which  were  built  in  the  last  5  to
10  years.  (Tr.  914,  915,  916)  Thus,  a  substantial  number  oi
single-family  homes  were  built  after  the  adoption  of  the  1959
Plan  and  in  reliance  upon  that  Plan.

The  petitioners’  legitimate  justification  for  refusing  to  change
the  zoning  is the  protection  of  surrounding  property  values  and
the  preservation  of the  integrity  of the  Zoning  Ordinance.  These
objectives  are  of  course  the  proper  objectives  of  any  zoning
ordinance.  The  respondents  fail  to  cite  a  single  case  relating
to  so-called  exclusionary  zoning  wherein  the  refusal  to  rezone
has  been  held  invalid  under  circumstances  similar  to  the  case
at  bar.

No  attempt  is  made  to  even  mention,  let  alone  distinguish,
Ybarra,  Skillken,  or  Confederation  de  La Raza  Unida  v.  City  of
Morgan  Hill,  324  F.  Supp.  895  (N.  D.  Calif.  1971),  all  zoning
cases  cited  in our  original  brief  and  all of which  refused  to  apply
the  strict  scrutiny  test.

We  have  already  demonstrated  in  our  original  brief  that,
applying  the  criteria  set  forth  by  the  courts  of  the  State  of
Illinois  in  determining  the  validity  of  a  zoning  ordinance,  the
refusal  to  rezone  the  subject  property  in  the  midst  of  a  single-
family  area  to  increase  the  density  threefold  was  a  reasonable
and  legitimate  exercise  of the  police  power.  Again,  we  state  that
the  possibility  that  housing  will  be  occupied  by low  or  moderate
income  persons  of whatever  race  does  not  justify  the  application
of  different  standards  when,  admittedly,  the  zoning  ordinance
has  not  been  administered  in a  discriminatory  manner  and  is,  in
fact,  a  totally  reasonable  exercise  of  the  police  power.

The  respondents’  assertion  that  the  courts  have  recognized
the  necessity  for  considering  the  rights  of  non-residents  as  well

32

33

as  residents  in  assessing  the  reasonableness  of  suburban  zoning
actions  simply  ignores  the  holding  of  this  Court  in  Warth  v.
Seldin,  422  U.  S.  490  (1975).  See  also,  Cornelius  v.  Parma,
374  F.  Supp.  730  (N.  D.  Ohio  1974),  aff'd  521  F.  2d  1401,
cert.  denied  3/8/76,  44  L.  W.  3501.

The  case  of  Lakeland  Bluff,  Inc.  v.  County  of  Will,  114  Ml.
App.  2d 267  (1969),  252. N. E. 2d 765,  applied  the  conventional
tests  for  determining  the  validity  of  a  zoning  ordinance  which
were  set  forth  in  our  original  brief.  The  Court  at  page  279
specifically  indicated:  “We  do  not  predicate  our  conclusion
upon  the  need  for  lower  cost  housing.”  Lakeland  is  simply  a
garden  variety  zoning  case  in  which  the  need  in  the  community
for  mobile  homes  was  considered.  Glassey  v.  County  of  Taze-
well,  11  Ill.  App.  3d  1087  (1973),  297  N.  E.  2d  235,  was
another  mobile  home  park  case  in  which  the  Court  considered
as  one  of eight  factors  the  community  need  for  the  proposed  use.

The  statement  in the  respondents’  brief  that  the  evidence  here
clearly  establishes  “a desperate  areawide  need  for low  and  moder-
ate  income  housing”  is  neither  supported  by  the  record,  nor
does  it justify  putting  high-density  multiple  housing  in  the  midst
of a single-family  area.  The  Courts  of Illinois,  while  occasionally
considering  need  for  a  specific  use  as  one  possible  factor  in
determining  the  validity  of  a  zoning  restriction,  have  refused  to
Locker  v.  City  of
give  this  factor  any  controlling  weight.
McHenry,  89  Ill.  App.  2d  457,  643  (1967).

The  proposed  development  clearly  is  not  complimentary  to
the  surrounding  housing,  but,  as  the  trial  court  found,  would
have  a  detrimental  effect  upon  the  value  of  surrounding  prop-
erties.  This  diminution  of  neighboring  home  values  is  not  a
reflection  of  the  prejudice  of  the  local  homeowners,  but  in
fact  the  workings  of  the  housing  market  wherein  the  intrusion
of a high-density  development  in the  midst  of a single-family  area
inevitably  lowers  property  values  of  the  existing  homes.

The  fact  that  multiple  family  might  represent  the  “highest
and  best  use”  from  a  purely  economic  standpoint  is neither  con-

clusive  with  respect  to  the  validity  of  a  zoning  ordinance,  nor
does  it support  a finding  of a violation  of the  Fourteenth  Amend-
ment  because  of  racial  discrimination.  As  the  Court  stated  in
Seith  v.  City  of  Wheaton,  89  Ill.  App.  2d  446  (1967)  at  451
“Although
in  passing  on  a  restriction  for  single-family  use:
the  highest  and  best  use  of the  property  may  be for  either  duplex
and  less  dense  multiple  use  or  the  fact  that  the  property  would
be  worth  substantially  more  to  the  owner  if the  restriction  were
removed  is not  in our  opinion  sufficient  to overcome  the  presump-
tion  of the  validity  of the  Wheaton  zoning  ordinance  ..

.”

There  are  no  public  interest  considerations  favoring  the  de-
velopment  of  Lincoln  Green,  however,  the  respondents  choose
to  define  the  concept  of  “public  interest.”  In  Stalzer  v.  Village
of Matteson,  14  Ill.  App.  3d  891  (1973),  303  N.  E.  2d  489,
the  subject  property  had  been  vacant  for  70  years  and  adjoined
a  tract  zoned  for  industrial  use  to  the  west  and  to  the  north.
No  expert  witness  for  either  side  testified  that  the  R-1  zoning
on  the  property  represented  its  highest  and  best  use.  The  Court
found  that  the  existing  zoning  and  uses  of  nearby  property  were
incompatible  with  the  R-1  zoning  and  the  light  industrial  uses  to
the  immediate  north  of the  subject  property  made  such  classifica-
tion  incongruous.  The  case  was  finally  decided  on  the  basis
that  the  zoning  imposed  a substantial  reduction  in  the  value  of
the  property  owned  by  the  plaintiffs.  In  the  instant  case,  the
owners  are  not  even  plaintiffs,  and  the  property  is  admittedly
worth  $75,000  more  for  the  permitted  single-family  use  than
the  owners  will  secure  under  the  contract  of sale.

The  respondents  refer  to  the  District  Court’s  finding  that  the
refusal  to  rezone  was  based  upon  “the  integrity  of  the  Village’s
zoning  plan.”  All  the  evidence  supports  the  conclusion  of  the
District  Court,  and  that  finding  was  not  disturbed  by the  Court
of  Appeals.  Apparently  the  respondents  complain  because  the
development  subsequent  to  1959  was  in fact  in conformance  with
the  1959  Plan.  The  rezonings  to  multiple-family  use  were  under-
taken  by the  Village  for  the  purpose  of  providing  a  multiplicity

NE  CCC

ee

34

35

of housing  types  in the  community  at  appropriate  locations.  De-
spite  the  rhetoric  of the  respondents,  there  is no  evidence  of any
need  to  accommodate  low  and  moderate  income  persons  work-
ing in the  Village  of Arlington  Heights.  The  record  is totally  de-
void  of  any  specific  evidence  as  to  the  number  of  persons  work-
ing  in  the  Village  who  would  meet  the  criteria  for  low  and
moderate  income  housing,  and  would  choose  to reside  in Lincoln
Green.

It  was  unnecessary  to  show  any  compelling  justification  to
support  the  refusal  to  permit  construction  of  Lincoln  Green  in
the  location  sought  by the  respondents.  The  refusal  to  rezone,
when  such  rezoning  would  be  detriment»!  to  surrounding  prop-
erty  values,  violate  the  Village’s  established  zoning  plan,  and
result  in a  totally  incompatible  development  in a  well-established
single-family  area,  is  not  a  violation  of  the  equal  protection
clause  or  the  Fourteenth  Amendment.

II.

THE  PETITIONERS’  REFUSAL  TO  PERMIT  DEVELOPMENT
OF  LINCOLN  GREEN  DOES  NOT  VIOLATE  THE  FAIR
HOUSING  ACT  OR  THE  CIVIL  RIGHTS  ACT.

A.

Introduction.

The  trial  court  in  this  case  specifically  found  that  no  specific
section  of  the  Fair  Housing  Act  is referred  to  in  the  pleadings,
and  none  seem  applicable  to  the  facts  of this  case.  373  F.  Supp.
208,  209.  In  referring  to  the  Civil  Rights  Act  and  the  Fair
Housing  Act,  the  trial  court  found  that  these  statutes  do  not
reach  zoning  per  se.  373  F.  Supp.  208,  212.  The  Court  of
Appeals  made  its  decision  solely  on  the  basis  of  the  Fourteenth
Amendment,  without  reference  to  either  the  Civil  Rights  Act
or  the  Fair  Housing  Act.

The  respondents  still  do  not  favor  us  with  any  specific  viola-
tion  of  any  specific  provision  of  either  statute.  Section  804(a)

of  the  Fair  Housing  Act  makes  it  unlawful  “to  refuse  to  sell
or  rent  after  the  making  of  a  bona  fide  offer,  or  to  refuse  to
negotiate  for  the  sale  or  rental  of, or  otherwise  make  unavailable
or  deny,  a dwelling  to  any  person  because  of race,  color,  religion,
Or  national  origin.”  Clearly,  there  is no  evidence  in  this  record
of any  violation  of  any  of the  petitioners  of this  section.  Section
817  of the Fair  Housing  Act  makes  it unlawful  to  interfere,  coerce
or  intimidate  any  person  in  the  exercise  of  their  rights  granted
under  Sections  803,  804,  805  and  806  of  the  Statute.  No  evi-
dence  exists  in  this  record  to  support  any  charge  of  such  in-
terference  or  coercion.

The  case  of  United  States  v.  City  of Black  Jack,  508  F.  2d
1179,  is  completely  distinguishable  on  its  facts.  As  indicated
above,  the  city  there  had  passed  an  amendment  to  its  ordinance
after  HUD  had  issued  a feasibility  letter  for  federal  funding  of
a  236  project.  That  amendment  would  have  prchibited  the
construction  of  any  multiple-family  dwellings  and  made  present
ones  nonconforming  uses.  The  evidence  in  that  case  was  clear
that  many  blacks  would  live  in  the  development  proposed  and
that  the  broad  exclusionary  ordinance  would  deny  persons  hous-
ing  on  the  basis  of  their  race.  No  such  situation  pertains  in
the  case  at  bar.

The  Village  is  not  using  its  zoning  power  to  interfere  with
private  efforts  which  would  appreciably  reduce  segregation  in
Instead,  it  is
its  jurisdiction,  as  alleged  by  the  respondents.
refusing  to  amend  its  perfectly  proper  Zoning  Ordinance  merely
because  the  proposed  project  conceivably  could  house  some
blacks,  as  well  as  whites.  No violation  of either  the  Fair  Housing
Act  or  the  Civil  Rights  Act  has  been  demonstrated.

In the  case  of  Trafficante  v.  Metropolitan  Life  Insurance  Co.,
409  U.  S.  205  (1972),  this  Court  held  that  any  person  who
claims  to  have  been  injured  by a  discriminatory  housing  practice
had  standing  to  sue  under  the  Civil  Rights  Act.  The  case  in-
volved  persons  in  a  housing  unit  who  claimed  to  have  been  in-
jured  by discrimination  against  non-whites  in  the  rental  of  the

36

37

apartments  of  the  complex  in  which  they  resided.  That  decision
clearly  has  absolutely  no  bearing  on  the  factual  situation  in  the

naee
ase  atl  Val.

at  thea

B.

Arlington  Heights’  Conduct  Did  Not  Have  the  Effect  of
Perpetuating  Segregation  and  It  Had  No  Burden  to  Show
a  Compelling  Nonracial  Justification  for  the  Refusal.

There  is  nothing  in  the  record  to  support  the  respondents’
claim  that  Arlington  Heights  has  become  a  99%  white  city
through  zoning  and  other  municipal  decisions.
In  fact,  the
zoning  decisions  of  the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights  have  pro-
duced  some  6,000  apartments  and  12,000  homes  available  to
all  persons  without  regard  to  race.  Undoubtedly,  the  housing
market  in  the  past  decade  has  prevented  many  people  lacking
sufficient  income  from  buying  or  renting  homes  in  Arlington
Heights,  but  this  fact  does  not  mean  that  the  Village  of Arlington
Heights  has
to  abandon  its  Zoning  Ordinance  under  the
Fourteenth  Amendment,  the  Civil  Rights  Act,  or  the  Fair  Hous-
ing  Act.  There  is  no  evidence  that  the  Village  has  exploited
anyone.

As  we  have  indicated  above,  the  compelling  interest  test  is
not  applicable  to  this  zoning  controversy.  We  have  also  dis-
tinguished  the  Black  Jack  case  at  length.  There  is no  evidence
that  Lincoln  Green  would  reduce  segregation,  or  in  fact  that
a  single  black  person  would  necessarily  reside  in  the  Lincoln
Green  project.  It was  clear  in  the  Black  Jack  case  that  black
citizens  were  being  effectively  barred  by  the  passage  of  an
ordinance  prohibiting  any  multiple-family  development.

The  Zoning  Ordinance  is not  being  used  to  frustrate  a  unique
development  which  would  in fact  be significantly  more  integrated
than  the  community.  The  Zoning  Ordinance  is  being  used  in
conformance  with  the requirements  of the  police  power  and  being
applied  in  an  admittedly  nondiscriminatory  manner.  It  is  clear
from  the  language  of the  Court  of Appeals’  opinion  that,  in fact,

it is  seeking  to  impose  on  the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights  the
“affirmative  duty  to  alleviate  the  problem  (of segregated  housing
in  the  Chicago  area).”

In  citing  Acevedo  v.  Nassau  County,  500  F.  2d  1078  (2d
Cir.  1974),  1081,  the  respondents  fail  to  note  that  Acevedo  held
that  there  was  no  duty  under  the  Fair  Housing  Act  on  any
government  body  to  construct  or  to  “plan  for,  approve  and  pro-
mote  any  housing.”  500  F.  2d  1078,  1082.  The  citation  from
Acevedo  applies  to  “refusing  to  accede  to  a  reasonable  rezoning
request.”
Here,  neither  the  District  Court  nor  the  Court  of
Appeals  determined  that  the  refusal  was  an  unreasonable  one
and,  in fact,  the  refusal  was  totally  consistent  with  sound  zoning
principles  and  admittedly  the  result  of  a  nondiscriminatory  ad-
ministration  of  the  Zoning  Ordinance.

An  affirmative  duty  is  being  sought  to  be  imposed  upon
Arlington  Heights—that  is  the  duty  to  change  its  ordinance,
regardless  of  the  soundness  of  the  ordinance  from  a  zoning  and
planning  standpoint,  to  accommodate  a  hypothetical  minority
occupancy  at  the  expense  of  the  surrounding  property  owners
and  the  integrity  of  the  zoning  plan.

C.  The  Acts  Complained  of Do  Not  Violate  Title  VIII  of  the
Fair Housing  Act.

Again,  the  respondents  have  failed  to  point  out  any  specific
acts  which  violate  Title  VIII].  The  Trafficante  case  turned  on
standing  to  sue  under  the  Civil  Rights  Act.  It  of  course  had
nothing  to  do  with  the  Fair  Housing  Act.  HUD’s  administrative
criteria  to  provide  for  integration  is  not  at  issue  in  this  case.
We  concur  in  a  broad  interpretation  of  the  Civil  Rights  Act
of  1966,  but  there  is no  evidence,  intent,  or  accomplishment  of
racial  discrimination  by the  actions  of  the  Village  in  this  case.
The  case  of  Clark  v.  Universal  Builders,  Inc.,  501  F.  2d
324  (7th  Cir.  1974),  is  clearly  not  in  point.  The  decision
involved  private  action  in  creating  a  dual  housing  market  and

38

39

turned  on  the  question  of  the  propriety  of  granting  a  directed
verdict  for  the  defendants  at  the  close  of  plaintiff's  case.  Clark
merely  held  that  the  allegations  made  a  prima  facie  case  for
a  violation  of the  Civil  Rights  Act.  It has  no  relationship  to  the
questions  raised  in this  action.

We  have  previously  distinguished  the  Black  Jack,  Kennedy
Park  Homes,  and  Delray  Beach  cases.  Sisters  of Providence  v.
City  of Evanston,  335  F.  Supp.  396  (N.  D.  Ill.  1971),  merely
held  that  a  complaint  stated  a  cause  of  action.  The  allegations
in  that  complaint  were  substantially  different  than  those  in  the
case  at  bar.  The  Black  Jack  case,  even  if good  law,  is  clearly
distinguishable  because  of the proof in that  case  that  blacks  would
in  fact  reside  in  an  already  approved  federally  aided  project,
and  that  the  city  had  taken  the  affirmative  action  of  barring  all
multiple-family  development  as  soon  as  federal  funding  of  the
project  became a reality.

The  Village  of  Arlington  Heights  has  maintained  single-
family  zoning  on  the  subject  property  since  its  first  Zoning
Ordinance  long  before  the  advent  of Lincoln  Green.  The  entire
area  has  developed  in  reliance  upon  that  single-family  zoning,
which  is completely  in keeping  with  sound  planning  and  zoning
principles.  The  possibility  that  some  blacks  may  reside  in  the
Lincoln  Green  project  is  not  a  sufficient  basis  for  requiring
Arlington  Heights  to abandon  this  undoubtedly  reasonable  zoning
restriction.

Reliance  on  the  Lackawanna  case  is  particularly  ironic  in
view  of  the  specific  holding  at  318  F.  Supp.  669,  694,  that
“the  nature  of the discrimination  prescribed  under  the  Fair  Hous-
ing Act  is limited  in that  it does  not  include  discrimination  based
on  poverty.”

D.  The  Court’s  Interpretation  of Similar  Legislation  Does  Not
Show  the  Complained  of Action  Violates  the  Fair  Housing
Act.

The  respondents  attempt  a  bootstraps  approach  by  relying
upon  this  Court’s  decision  in  Griggs  v.  Duke  Power  Co.,  401
U.  S.  424  (1971).  That  case  decided  under  the  Equal  Employ-
ment  Opportunity  Act  of course  stands  for  the  proposition  that
employment  testing  must  be  job  related.  The  respondents  do
not  cite  the  language  of  this  Court  in  Griggs  at  431,  where
the  Court  said:  “Congress  did  not  intend  by Title  VII,  however,
to  guarantee  a  job  to  every  person  regardless  of  qualifications.
In  short  the  Act  does  not  command  that  any  person  be  hired
simply  because  he was  formerly  the  subject  of discrimination,  or
because  he  is  a member  of a minority  group.”  What  Griggs  says
is that  arbitrary  and  unrelated  job tests  may  not  be  used  to  dis-
criminate  against  a  racial  minority.  It clearly  does  not  require
that  all  reasonable  legislative  acts  must  be  set  aside  merely  be-
cause  minority  group  members  might  benefit.

The  McDonnell  case  is  likewise  not  in  point  and  simply
required  the  remandment  for  trial  of  a  complaint  alleging  that
the  reason  for  refusing  to  reemploy  a  Negro  violated  Title  VII.
The  Court  there  noted  that,  if the  reason  assigned  for  refusing
to  reemploy  was  not  pretextual  or  discriminatory  in  its  applica-
tion,  the  employer’s  refusal  to  rehire  must  stand.  The  case
clearly  is not  applicable  to  the  facts  at  bar.

The  respondents’  argument  must  of  course  fall  because  the
action  of the  Village  in refusing  to  alter  its existing,  valid  zoning
restrictions  was  not  predicated  on  any  racially  discriminatory
pretext,  nor,  as  was  conceded  by both  the  trial  and  the  Court
of  Appeals,  was
dis-
criminatory  in  character.

the  Village’s  zoning  administration

Griggs  and  the  cases  following  it  do  not  impose  upon  the
Village  any  requirement  that  it zone  for  the  purpose  of providing
a  racial  quota  in  the  housing  pattern  of  the  Village.  The  most

40

recent  employment  case  dealing  with  this  question  in  the  Court
of  Appeals  for  the  Second  Circuit  was  determined  on  the  basis
of  the  Civil  Rights  Acts  of  1866  and  1871.
In  the  case  of
Kirkland  v.  Dept.  of  Correctional  Services  (U.  S.  D.  C.,
S.  D.  N.  Y.,  August  6,  1975),  11  FEP  Cases  36,  the  Court
specifically  rejected  the  concept  of  racial  quotas  in  the  hiring
of  officers  in  the  New  York  State  Department  of  Correctional
Services.  The  Court  there  noted:

“The  replacement  of  individual  rights  and  opportunities  by
a  system  of  statistical  classifications  based  on  race  is  re-
pugnant  to  the  basic  concepts  of  a  democratic  society.

“The  most  ardent  supporters  of  quotas  as  a  weapon  in
the  fight  against  discrimination  have  recognized  their  un-
democratic  inequities  and  conceded  that  their  use  should
be limited.  Commentators  merely  echo  the judiciary  in their
disapproval  of  the  ‘discrimination  inherent  in  a  quota
system.’  ”

In  Kirkland  the  Court  noted  with  approval  the  dissenting
opinion  of  Mr.  Justice  Douglas  in  DeFunis  v.  Odegaard,  416
U.  S.  312  (1974),  40  L.  ed.  2d  164,  where  at  page  336,  Mr.
Justice  Douglas  said:

“There  is  no  constitutional  right  for  any  race  to  be  pre-
ferred.  The  years  of  slavery  did  more  than  retard  the
progress  of  blacks.  Even  a  greater  wrong  was  done  the
whites  by  creating  arrogance  instead  of  humility  and  by
encouraging  the  growth  of  the  fiction  of  a  superior  race.
There  is  no  superior  person  by  constitutional  standards.”

However  appropriate  the  language  of the  Equal  Employment
Opportunity  Act  may  be  with  respect  to  meeting  problems  in
that  area,  neither  that  statute  nor  cases  decided  under  it  have
any  relevance  to  a  situation  where  a  municipality  in  a  non-
discriminatory  manner  merely  seeks  to  preserve  the  integrity
of  its  zoning  pattern  and  protect  surrounding  property  values
from  the  incursion  of  a  high  density  use.

41

Ill.

THE  RESPONDENTS  HEREIN  HAVE  NO  STANDING  TO  SUE.

As  pointed  out  in  our  original  brief,  the  question  of  stand-
ing was  originally  raised  by the  petitioners  in  a  pretrial  motion
and  the  question  of  standing  was  carefully  considered  by  the
trial  court.  Thus,  the  question  is  properly  before  this  Court.
We  respectfully  suggested  that  the  case  of  Warth  v.  Seldin,  422
U. S. 490,  45  L.  ed.  2d  343,  is conclusive.

The  individual  respondents  clearly  do  not  have  standing.  The
two  individuals  who  actually  testified  in  the  case,  Guthrie  and
Ransom,  were  black  citizens  living  outside  of Arlington  Heights.
Guthrie  drives  his  car  from  the  south  side  of  Chicago  where
he  owns  his  two-flat  home.  He  purchased  his  home  in  1973
for  $21,000  and  receives  $160  a  month  rent  from  the  other
unit.  His  wife  is  employed,  but  he  did  not  know  her  salary.
He  had  not  gone  to  any  real  estate  brokers  in Arlington  Heights
or  asked  to  be  shown  any  housing.  There  was  no  testimony  as
to  the  total  family  income.  It  is  clear  that  he  never  personally
sought  housing  in  Arlington  Heights.

Mr.  Ransom  lives  in  a  single-family  home  in  Evanston  with
his  son  and  his  mother.  His  mother  is  also  employed  and,  here
again,  there  is  no  showing  that  he  was  eligible  by  income  to
reside  in  Lincoln  Green.  He  testified  he  had  never  sought  out
any  housing  in  Arlington  Heights.

The  allegations  of  the  complaint  in  fact  fail  to  set  forth  the
kind  of  facts  which  this  Court  found  was  necessary  in  Warth
to  give  any  standing  to  the  individual  plaintiffs.  There  were  no
allegations  that  either  Ranson  or  Guthrie  either  desired  or
would  occupy  the  Lincoln  Green  housing  if  constructed.  Mrs.
Maldonado  of  course  resides  in  Arlington  Heights  and  there-
fore  clearly  cannot  be  held  to  have  been  zoned  out  of  the
Village.

42

43

The  Village  of  Arlington  Heights,  contrary  to  the  statement
in  the  respondents’  brief,  of  course  has  not  interferred  with  the
private  housing  market  to  prevent  the  construction  of  Lincoln
Green.  It  is  in  fact  the  workings  of  the  housing  market  which
have  set  the  price  level  and  determined  the  occupancy  of  hous-
ing accommodations  in  Arlington  Heights.

The  respondents  in  their  brief  make  no  effort  to  justify  the
standing  of  the  Northwest  Opportunity  Center  or  Mrs.  Mal-
donado.  Clearly,  neither  have  any  standing.  MHDC  however
alleges  that  it  has  standing.  In  this  regard  it  should  be  noted
that  MHDC  is in  fact  not  injured  in  any  manner  by the  refusal
of  the  Village  to  rezone  at  its  request.  It  has  a  contract  to  pur-
chase  based  upon  the  ability  to  secure  zoning  and  financing
under  Section  236.  The  failure  to  secure  either  results  in  the
cancellation  of  the  contract.  Its  99  year  lease  for  $1.00  a  year
is  also  conditioned  on  zoning  and  the  ability  to  secure  236
financing.  Having  failed  to  secure  either,  MHDC  is  not  ob-
ligated  under  either  its  contract  to  purchase  or  its  longterm
lease.  Moreover,  as  the  trial  court  noted,  MHDC  as a corpora-
tion  cannot  be  the  victim  of  racial  discrimination.  It  has  no
racial  identity.  It  suffers  no  damage  in  its  corporate  capacity.
Also  in  point  was  the  decision  of  the  District  Court  for  the
Northern  District  of  Ohio  in  Cornelius  v.  City  of Parma,  374
F,  Supp.  730  (N.  D.  Ohio  1974),  521  F.  2d  1401.  In  that
case  the  Court  held  that  low  income  black  residents  of  the
inner  city  had  no  right  to  challenge  the  conduct  of  an  all-white
suburb  in  refusing  a  building  permit  for  a  236  development
which  would  have  had  black  residents.  On  March  8,  1976,  this
Court  denied  certiorari  noting  at  44  L.  W.  3501:

“Appeal  from  dismissal  of complaint  filed  by lower  income
black  residents  of  inner  city  challenging  conduct  of  all-
white  suburb  excluding  subsidized  lower  income  housing
is,  in  accordance  with  Supreme  Court  order  remanding
case  for  further  consideration  in  light  of  Warth  v.  Seldin,
422  U.S.  490,  43  L.W.  4906  (1975),  remanded  to  dis-
trict  court  with  instructions  to  dismiss  complaint.”

The  respondents’  reliance  on  Trafficante  v.  Metropolitan  Life
Insurance  Co.,  409  U.  S.  205  (1972),  is  simply  misplaced.
Trafficante  was  distinguished  in  Warth  because,  as  here,  none
of  the  non-minority  plaintiffs  made  any  allegations  that  they
had  suffered  any justiciable,  cognizable  injury.  In Trafficante,  the
plaintiffs  were  residents  of  housing  facilities  covered  by  the
statute.  They  were  held  to  have  standing  to  be  free  from  the
adverse  consequences  to  them  of  racially  discriminatory  prac-
tices  in  the  facility  directed  at  and  immediately  harmful  to
others.  No  party  in  this  action  alleged  any  such  circumstances,
nor  was  there  any  proof of any damage.

We  respectfully  suggest  that  not  only  has  there  been  no  viola-
tion  of  the  Fourteenth  Amendment,  the  Civil  Rights  Act  or
the  Fair  Housing  Act  by the  conduct  of  the  petitioners  herein,
but  that  these  respondents  have  no  standing  to  challenge  the
acts  of  the  Village  of Arlington  Heights.

IV.

THE  BRIEFS  OF  THE  AMICI  FAIL  TO  JUSTIFY  THE
DECISION  OF  THE  COURT  OF  APPEALS.

Two  amicus  curiae  briefs  have  been  filed  in this  action,  one  by
the  American  Society  of  Planning  Officials,  and  the  other  by a
number  of  organizations  led  by the  League  of  Women  Voters.
Both  briefs  indicate  not  only  a  lack  of  knowledge  of  the  record
in  this  case,  but  a  lack  of  understanding  of  what  the  trial
court  and  the  Court  of Appeals  actually  held.  Both  go  well  out-
side  the  record  and  reflect  a  particular  social  policy  rather  than
any  attempt  at  legal  analysis.  Most  of  all,  they  also  ascribe  to
the  Village  racial  motivations  and  discriminatory  practices  which
not  even  the  Court  of Appeals  was  willing  to  find.

The  League  of  Women  Voters’  brief  states  that  Arlington
Heights  has  made  no  effort  to  alleviate  the  pattern  of segregated
housing  in  the  metropolitan  area.  This  statement  comes  with
ill  grace  from  the  local  League  of  Women  Voters  which  has

at

45

actual  knowledge  of facts  which  are  contrary  to  the  allegations
of this  kind.  We  direct  the  Court’s  attention  to  facts  which  are
well  known  to  the  League  of  Women  Voters  in  Arlington
Heights.  The  Village  has  taken  the  following  official  actions
before  and  after  the  particular  zoning  controversy  which  is the
subject  matter  of this  cause:

1.

It  adopted  a  Fair  Housing  Ordinance  in  1968,
becoming  one  of  the  first  communities  in  the  northwest
suburban  area  to  do  so.

2.

It  received  approval  for  119  units  of  low  income
housing  for  the  elderly,  presently  administered  through  the
Cook  County  Housing  Authority,  with  construction  ex-
pected  to  begin  within  the  calendar  year.  The  Cooperation
Agreement  with  the  Cook  County  Housing  Authority  was
approved  by  the  Village  on  April  20,  1970,  before  any
action  was  taken  on  the  Lincoln  Green  project.

3.  The  Village  has  empowered  the  Village  Housing
Commission  to  encourage  private  developers  to  include
a  percentage  of low  income  units  within  proposed  projects,

4.  The  Village  officials  adopted  a  housing  assistance
plan  which  seeks  provision  of  125  subsidized  units  (first
year  goal)  and  375  units  (three  year  goal)  as  part  of  the
Village’s  application  for  entitlement  funds  under  the  Hous-
ing and  Community  Development  Act  of  1974.

These  facts  are  public  records  and  are  clearly  available  for
verification  by  any  person  sincerely  interested  in  knowing  the
facts.  Moreover,  there  has  been  a  continuing  movement  of
black,  oriental  and  spanish-speaking  families  and  individuals
into  the  Arlington  Heights  community  during  recent  years.

The  Lincoln  Green  controversy  was  a  zoning  controversy,
pure  and  simple.  The  briefs  of  the  amici  indicate  clearly  that
they  are  not  interested  in  the  facts  or  law,  but  are  simply
concerned  with  unjustly  tarring  this  Village.

Thus,  the  statement  in  the  League  of  Women  Voters’  brief
that  Arlington  Heights  has  made  no  effort  to  alleviate  the

eS

segregated  housing  pattern  in  the  Chicago  metropolitan  area
and  in  the  Village  simply  is not  true.  The  brief  ignores  the  fact
that  both  the  trial  and  the  Court  of  Appeals  specifically  found
that  the  Zoning  Ordinance  has  not  been  administered  in  a
discriminatory  manner.  The  holding  of  Belle  Terre  is that  there
is  no  fundamental  constitutional  right  to  housing  and  that  a
zoning  ordinance  is  not  measured’  by  the  compelling  interest
test.

The  League  further  cites  Milliken  v.  Bradley,  418  U.  S.  717,
but  ignores  the  holding  of  that  case  which  clearly  indicates  that
racial  imbalance  in  itself  is  not  to  be  equated  with  a  constitu-
tional  violation  calling  for  a  remedy,  and  that,  absent  a  show-
ing  of  discrimination  by  Arlington  Heights  in  the  administra-
tion  of  an  ordinance  clearly  valid  on  its  face,  the  Village
does  not  have  an  affirmative  duty  to  ignore  its Zoning  Ordinance
because  the  Chicago  area  is  allegedly  segregated.

There  is  absolutely  no  evidence  that  the  Zoning  Ordinance
of  Arlington  Heights  has  created  or  perpetuates  a  segregated
community.  The  Village  has  not  engaged  in  exclusionary  zon-
ing  practices,  nor  was  the  refusal  to  alter  a  rational  and  valid
zoning  plan  in  this  case  anything  but  a  reasonable  exercise  of
the  police  power.  The  League’s  allegations  of  an  attempt  to
attract  industry  but  keep  out  workers  flies  in the  face  of deVise’s
testimony  that  Arlington  Heights  is  a  dormitory  suburb  and
has  less  industry  than  any  of  the  surrounding  communities.

We  have  demonstrated  above  that  there  has  been  no  violation
of  the  Fair  Housing  Act  of  1968  and  the  Civil  Rights  Acts.
The  League  fails  to  cite  any  authority  for  requiring  the  Village
of  Arlington  Heights  to  disavow  its  Zoning  Ordinance  simply
because  it  is  possible  that  some  blacks  might  occupy  Lincoln
Green.  We  deny  that  the  actions  or  inactions  of  the  Village
have  contributed  to  the  existence  or  perpetuation  of  segregated
housing.  The  League  and  its  fellow  amici  fail  to  cite  any  facts
or  law  to  support  this  unwarranted  conclusion,

46

47

The  housing  pattern  in  Arlington  Heights  does  not  warrant
the  remedy  sought  by  the  respondents  in  this  case.  No  consti-
tutional  rights  have  been  impinged  upon  and  no  violations  of
any  federal  statute  or  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  have  been
The  League  speaks  of  “restrictive  practices  of

Arlington  Heights,”  but  it  fails  to  cite  any  specific  restrictive
practice  because  none  exists.  Again,  though  the  League  cites
Milliken,  it fails  to  set  forth  facts  showing  wherein  the  Village
or  Arlington  Heights  participated  in or  caused  any  wrong.

The  brief  of  the  American  Society  of  Planning  Officials,
which  was  filed  without  authority  of  its  Board  of  Directors,
betrays  a  lack  of knowledge  of  the  facts  and  the  record,  as  well
as  the  law  in  this  case.  Most  of  the  cases  cited  in  the  ASPO
brief  have  been  distinguished  in  the  body  of  this  brief.  The
case  of Sisters  of Providence  of St.  Mary  of the  Woods  v.  Evans-
ton,  335  F. Supp.  396  (N.  D.  Ill.  1971)  involved  the  sufficiency
of  the  allegations  of  a  complaint  to  withstand  a  motion  to
dismiss.  We  are  not  involved  in  a  case  of  refusing  to  rezone  for
a  black  project  under  the  same  circumstances  the  Village  would
have  granted  a  variance  to  an  all-white  project.  The  trial  court
and  the  Court  of  Appeals  held  that  the  Zoning  Ordinance  of
Arlinctou  Heights  had  been  administered  in a  nondiscriminatory

aa  seeey  Vv

manner.

The  amicus  fails  to  recognize  that  the  failure  to  grant  the
rezoning  here  has  no  effect  on  a  racial  minority.  At  best,  a  low
income  group  generally,  clearly  not  a  suspect  class,  may  have
been  deprived  of the  right  to  live  in  a  high  density  development
in the  midst  of a single-family  district.

ASPO  improperly  cites  the  holding  of  this  Court  in  Palmer
v.  Thompson,  403  U.  S.  217  (1971)  to  the  effect  that  the
mere  fact  that  a  governmental  act  may  have  a  greater  impact
upon  the  poor  or  a  minority  group  does  not  thereby  invalidate
it.  There  is of course  no  evidence  that  the  Lincoln  Green  project
would  have  been  occupied  by  a  large  percentage  of  minority

families.  In  fact,  the  majority  of  those  eligible  for  the  Lincoln
Green  housing  were  admittedly  non-minority  families.

ASPO  like  the  respondents  asserts  that  a  compelling  interest
test  should  be  applied  to  this  zoning  controversy,  notwith-
standing  the  fact  that,  as  we  have  demonstrated  above  and  in
our  original  brief,  the  compelling  interest  test  simply  is  not
applicable  to  questions  of this  nature.

We  are  not  involved  here,  as  in  Lackawanna,  with  refusing
to  accede  to  a  reasonable  rezoning  request.  What  is  at  stake  is
a  totally  unreasonable  request  which  would  violate  the  well-
established  and  sound  zoning  regulations  upon  which  the  com-
munity  has  relied.  There  is absolutely  nothing  in  the  record  to
indicate  that  Arlington  Heights  is  “clearly  a  segregated  com-
munity”  because  of  the  zoning  pattern.  There  is  no  showing
that  the  respondents’  development  would  alleviate  the  pur-
ported  segregated  housing  situation.  The  denial  of  rezoning
clearly  is  not  to  be  subjected  to  the  compelling  governmental
interest  test  and  as  we  have  indicated  above,  the  zoning  action
here  is clearly  not  in  violation  of  the  Civil  Rights  Acts  or  the
Fair  Housing  Act.  Like  the  respondents,  ASPO  has  failed  to
set  forth  any  specific  provision  of  the  statute  which  it contends
has  been  violated  nor  any  specific  act  which  falls  within  the  pro-
hibitions  of  the  statute.  The  action  of  the  Village  does  not
have  the  effect  of  discriminating  on  the  basis  of  race  and  it is
not  subject  to  strict  scrutiny  nor  the  compelling  governmental
interest  criteria.

Unlike  Black  Jack,  the  respondents  totally  failed  to  establish
a  prima  facie  case  by  demonstrating  a  racially  discriminatory
effect.  In fact,  the  trial  court  specifically  found  to  the  contrary.
Unlike  Black  Jack,  the  evidence  in  this  record  clearly  showed
a  devaluation  of adjacent  property  values  and  a  violation  of  the
integrity  of the  Zoning  Ordinance.

ASPO  repeatedly  asserts  that  the  compelling  interest  test
is  to  be  applied  and  insists  that  the  interests  put  forth  by  the

48

49

This  constant  reversion  to  the
Village  were  not  sufficient.
compelling  interest  test  simply  reflects  the  failure  on  the  part
of the  amicus  to  understand  what  this  Court  has  repeatedly  said,
i.e.,  that  there  is  no  fundamental  constitutional  right  of  access
to  any  particular  kind  of  housing,  and  therefore  the  compelling
interest  test  does  not  apply.

This  case  of  course  does  not  involve  a  “holding  zone.”  It
involves  a  single-family  district  which  is  part  of  the  well-estab-
lished  area  in  which  it  finds  itself.  The  single-family  classi-
fication  is a  realistic  one,  totally  in keeping  with  the  established
land  uses  in  the  area.  Although  ASPO  believes  that  the  buffer
plan  had  not  been  applied  consistently,  the  trial  court  after
hearing  the  evidence  held  otherwise.  The  nature  of  the  project
here  is  high  density,  ranging  between  12  and  15  units  to  the
acre,  more  than  three  times  the  established  density  in  the  area.
ASPO  cites  two  dissenting  members  of  the  Plan  Commission
and  ignores  the  majority  of  the  Plan  Commission  and  the  six
members  of  the  Village  Board  who  in  fact  found  that  this  high
density  multiple-family  development  was  spot  zoning  and
totally  inconsistent  with  the  surrounding  area.

ASPO’s  conclusion  that  this  proposal  would  not  so  seriously
disrupt  the  character  of  the  area  as  to  destroy  the  integrity  of
the  comprehensive  zoning  plan  simply  indicates  an  ignorance
not  only  of  the  specific  facts  of  this  case  as  revealed  by  the
evidence,  but  betrays  an  abysmal  ignorance  of  basic  city
planning  principles.

ASPO  is quick  to  say  that  the  neighboring  property  owners
are  not  entitled  to  the  protection  of  the  Zoning  Ordinance  and
should  be  prepared  to  accept  a  five  to  ten  per  cent  decrease  in
their  values  simply  because,  in  ASPO’s  judgment,  the  preserva-
tion  of  the  property  rights  is  not  a  compelling  interest.  The
design  and  layout  of the  project  does  not  insure  that  an  adverse
In  fact,  the  evidence  shows  just
impact  will  be  minimized.
the  contrary.  The  evidence  in this  record  is clear  that  the  racial
character  of  future  inhabitants  of  Lincoln  Green  had  nothing

to  do  with  the  detrimental  effect  that  a  multiple-family  develop-
ment  would  have  on  the  surrounding  area.  We  are  not  talking
about  a  “premium”  as  justifying  racial  discrimination  or  per-
petuation  of  segregated  housing.  Instead,  we  are  talking  about
the  entire  purpose  of  zoning  and  planning—the  purpose  of
insuring  compatible  development  from  a  land  use  standpoint
and  the  preservation  of property  values.

Contrary  to  the  assertion  of  ASPO,  this  case  is  not  con-
cerned  with  racial  discrimination.  We  are  concerned  with  a
development  proposal  that  is in  clear  contradiction  to  an  estab-
lished  and  consistently  followed  policy  of a  community’s  compre-
hensive  plan.  What  purports  to  be  a  brief  filed  on  behalf  of
professional  planners,  on  analysis  turns  out  to  be  nothing  but
an  ill-conceived  polemic  tract,  by a  misguided  and  totally  unin-
formed  group  who  know  not  whereof  they  speak.

CONCLUSION.

Based  on  the  foregoing  and  on  the  matters  suggested  in  our
original  brief,  we  respectfully  suggest  that  the  Court  of  Appeals
for  the  Seventh  Circuit  erred  in  its  decision  and  judgment.  We
respectfully  pray  that  the  judgment  of  the  Court  of  Appeals  be
reversed  and  the  decision  of  the  trial  court  affirmed.

Respectfully  submitted,

JACK  M.  SIEGEL,

39  South  LaSalle  Street,
Chicago,  Illinois  60603,
312-263-2968,

Attorney  for Petitioners.

MOTION  OF  THE  LEAGUES  OF  WOMEN  VOTERS  OF  THE
UNITED  STATES,  ILLINOIS,  COOK  COUNTY,  AND  ARLING.
