AMICUS  CURIAE,

A2

A3

NOW  COME  the  Leagues  of Women  Voters  of  the  Unit-
ed  States,  Illinois,  Cook  County  and  Arlington  Heights-
Mt.  Prospect  Area,  the  American  Jewish  Committee,  the
Arlington  Heights  Clergy  Fellowship,  and  the  Minority
Industrial  Relations  Council,  Inc.,  by their  undersigned  at-
torneys,  and  respectfully  apply  for  leave  to  file  a  brief  as
amicus  curiae  in the  above-captioned  case  pursuant  to  Rule
42(3),  and  in  support  of  their  application  state:

1.  Amici  pursue  and  support  programs  aimed  at  insur-
ing  equal  opportunity  for  all  Americans.  They  are  deeply
concerned  with  the  rapid  growth  of residential  segregation,
particularly  in  suburban  areas,  and  the  resulting  denial
of  employment  and  other  opportunities  in  these  areas  to
minorities,  including  blacks  and  Spanish-speaking  persons.

2.  The  League  of  Women  Voters  of  the  United  States
(‘‘League’’),  incorporated  under  the  laws  of  the  District
of  Columbia  in  1920  and  with  a  current  membership  of
that  time  promoted
approximately  140,000,  has  since
political  responsibility  through  informed  and  active  par-
ticipation  of  citizens  in  government,  ‘The  League’s  studies
on  the  impact  of  inequality  of  opportunity  in  employment,
education  and  housing  in  the  United  States  establish  that
in  the  field  of  housing,  exclusionary  zoning  and  other  re-
strictive  practices  against  low-  and  moderate-income  citi-
zens  have  their  greatest  impact  on  black  and  Spanish-speak-
ing minorities.  The  League  has  concluded  that  such  residen-
tial  segregation  is one  of  the  primary  causes  of  lack  of  op-
portunity  in  the  critical  areas  of  employment  and  educa-
tion.  It is committed  to a  policy  aimed  at  eradicating  exclu-
sionary  zoning  and  other  restrictive  practices  which  serve
to  deny  citizens  of  low  and  moderate  income  equal  access
to  the  suburban  housing  and  employment  market.

3.  The  Leagues  of  Women  Voters  of  Illinois,  Cook
County  and  Arlington  Heights-Mt.  Prospect  Area  are,
respectively,  an  affiliate  of  the  League  of  Women  Voters  of
the  United  States  (with  approximately  9,000  members  in
81  local  chapters  throughout
an  interleague
leagues  serving  43
organization  consisting  of  30  local
municipalities,  ¢nd  the  local  league  directly  involved  in  the
Arlington  Heights  area.

Illinois),

The  Illinois  League  has  adopted  a  position  advocating
revision  of  zoning  laws,  when  necessary,  to  accommodate
the  development  of  low-  and  moderate-income  housing.
The  Cook  County  League  is  concerned  with  the  marked
trend  toward  a  poor  black  inner  city  ringed  by white,  mid-
dle  class  suburbs.
The  Arlington  Heights-Mt.  Prospect
League,  recognizing  the  great  extent  to  which  suburban
zoning  practices  and  segregated  housing  in  both  the  City
of  Chicago  and  Arlington  Heights  have  resulted  in  the
denial  to  racial  minorities  of  equal  access  to  expanding
employment  opportunities  in  the  Arlington  Heights  area,
has  been  a  moving  force  behind  and  continues  to  be  an
active  supporter  of  the  housing  project  which  is  the  subject
of  this  appeal.

4.  The  American  Jewish  Committee  (‘‘AJC’’),  a  nation-
al  organization  of  approximately  40,000  members,  founded
in  1906  for  the  purpose  of  protecting  the  civil  and  religious
rights  of  Jews,  believes  that  helping  to  preserve  the  se-
curity  and  the  constitutional  rights  of  all  Americans,  irre-
spective  of  race,  creed,  national  origin,  social  or  economic
background  will  best  protect  the  security  and  the  consti-
tutional  rights  of  American  Jews.  For  this  reason,  the
AJC  has  for  many  years  vigorously  supported  the  exten-
sion  of  equal  housing  and  employment  opportunities  to  all
individuals.  The  AJC  views  restrictive  zoning  practices

A4

A5

as  preventing  low  and  moderate  income  persons,  in general,
and  minority  residents  of  the  inner  cities,  in  particular,
from  living  in  those  suburban  areas  in  and  around  which
today’s  expanding  job  opportunities
increasingly
found.

are

5.  The  Arlington  Heights  Clergy  Fellowship  is an  inter-
denominational  association  of ministers  with  congregations
in Arlington  Heights.  Its  members  believe  that  restrictive
zoning  practices,  such  as  are  involved  in  this  case,  ought
to  be  prohibited  because  they  perpetuate  the  discrimina-
tory  denial  to  minorities  and  other  low-  and  moderate-in-
come  persons  who  work,  or  may  desire  to  work,  in  or
near  Arlington  Heights,  of  the  opportunity  to  live  there.

6.

‘The  Minority  Industrial  Relations  Council,  ine.
(‘‘MiIRC’’)  is  a  consortium  of  twenty-nine  suburban  com-
panies  (some  located  in  Arlington  Heights)  that  Lave  or-

suburbs.

minorities

in  Chicago’s

ganized  to  improve  economuc  opportunities  tor  biacks  and
Il'ederal  law
Other
requires  the  member  companies  otf  MIRU  to  recruit  and
hire  blacks  and  other  minority  group  members.  because
of  the  lack  of  low-  and  moderate-income  housing  in  Ar-
lington  Heights  and  other  suburbs,  these  companies  ex-
perience  great  difficulty  in  meeting  their  affirmative  action
hiring  goals.  MIRC  supports  efforts  to  remove  the  barriers
which  prevent  poor  and  minority  people  from  living  in
Arlington  Heights  and  other  suburban  commuuities.

7.  Amice  believe  that  an  amicus  brief  on  their  behalf
will  help  to  demonstrate  the  relationship  between  the  issues
in  this  case  and  the  nationwide  problem  of  metropolitan
area  housing  segregation.  Because  of  studies  conducted
during  the  past  decade,  amici  are  familiar  with  the
causal  relationship  between  suburban  exclusionary  zoning
The
practices  and  metropolitan  area  residential  patterns.

result  is that  poor  black  and  other  racial  minority  members
are  locked  into  inner  city  ghettos  while  both  employers  and
whites  pass  readily  through  open  doors  into  suburbia.  As  a
result  of  their  combined  national  and  local  viewpoints,  the
amici  can  discuss  for  the  Court  the  extent  to  which  the  ac-
tion  involved  herein  is  typical  of  the  perpetuation  by  mu-
nicipalities  of  racial  segregation  in  metropolitan  areas
through  the  exercise  of  local  zoning  powers.

8.  The  undersigned  attorneys  requested  the  written
consent  of  the  parties  in  this  litigation  to  file  a  brief  as
amicus  curiae,  and  such  consent  was  refused  by counsel  for
the  Petitioner,  the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights.

9.  Forty  printed  copies  of  the  proposed  brief  of  the
amici  are  being  lodged  with  the  Clerk  contemporaneously
with  this  motion.

Respectfully  submitted,

Rosert  8.  Jacoss

On  Writ  Of  Certiorari  To  The  United  States  Court
Of  Appeals  lor  The  Seventh  Circuit.

BRIEF  OF  THE  LEAGUES  OF  WOMEN  VOTERS  OF  THE
UNITED  STATES,  ILLINOIS,  COOK  COUNTY,  AND  ARLING-
TON  HEIGHTS-MT.  PROSPECT  AREA,  THE  AMERICAN
JEWISH  COMMITTEE,  THE  ARLINGTON  HEIGHTS  CLER.-
GY  FELLOWSHIP  AND  THE  MINORITY  INDUSTRIAL  RE-
LATIONS  COUNCIL,  INC.  AS  AMICUS  CURIAE.

’

3

practices  further  exacerbate  the  national  school  integra-
tion  crisis,  by maintaining  residential  separation  of  races.

The  action  of  the  Village  of Arlington  Heights,  affecting
the  interstate  metropolitan  Chicago  area,  also  violates  na-
tional  policy  against  racially  restrictive  practices  in  hous-
ing,  expressed  by  Congress  in  the  Civil  Rights  Act  of  1866
and  the  Fair  Housing  Act  of  1968.  Specifically,  the  applica-
tion  of  the  Arlington  Heights  zoning  ordinance  prevents,
in an  interstate  metropolis,  utilization  of a  federal  program
for  subsidized  housing  designed  to  imnplement  this  national
policy.
The  Constitution  and  the  congressional  policy
should  not  be  thwarted  by  efforts  of  suburban  communi-
ties  which  are  an  integral  part  of  a  metropolitan  area  to
erect  and  maintain  white  picket  fences  around  themselves,
thereby  violating  the  constitutional  rights  of  other  citizens.

MAY  IT  PLEASE  THE  COURT:

2

PRELIMINARY  STATEMENT

The  Leagues  of Women  Voters  of  the  United  States,  Lb-
nois,  Cook  County  and  Arlington  Heights-Mt.  Prospect
Area,  The  American  Jewish  Committee,  The  Arlington
Heights  Clergy  Fellowship  and  The  Minority  Industrial
Relations  Council,  Inc.  respectfully  submit  this  brief  ami-
cus  curiae.  Their  interest  is  set  forth  in  their  motion  for
leave  to  file  this  brief,  which  is  incorporated  herein  by
reference.

Lhe  refusal  of  the  Villiage  of Arlington  Heights  to  grant
the  request  of  the  Metropolitan  Housing  Development  Cor-
poration  tor  rezoning  was  an  unconstitutional  application
of  the  Village’s  zoning  ordinance  wlien  viewed  against  the
historical  pattern  of  residential  segregation  in  Arlington
Heights  aud  in  the  metropolitan  Chicago  area  and  the  fail-
ure  of  the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights  to  take  any  action
to  ameliorate  this  condition.

Arlington  Heights’  zoning  ordinance,  as  administered,
perpetuates  the  practice  of  preventing  racial  minorities
from  residing  in Arlington  Heights,  and  by so  doing  it fur-
ther  tightens  the  vise  around  inner-city  minorities.
It
operates  to  exclude  these  low-  and  moderate-income  minor-
ity persons  from  housing,  employment  and  other  opportuni-
ties  on  the  same  basis  as  white  suburban  residents  and
thereby  deprives  minority  residents  of  their  right  to  equal
protection  of  the  law.

Arlington  Heights’  action  is  also  typical  of  the  critical
national  problem  of  metropolitan  area-wide  racial  housing
segregation  created  and  perpetuated,  in  large  part,  by
These
restrictive  practices  of  suburban  municipalities.

ARGUMENT

The  Petitioner’s  Zoning  Ordinance  Was  Applied  to  Perpet-
uate  Local  and  Area-Wide  Segregated  Housing,  Depriving
Citizens  of  Equal  Protection  of  the  Law.

The  metropolitan  Chicago  area  consists  of  some  seven
million  peovle,  extending  beyond  artificial  state,  county  and
municipal  boundary  lines,  from  southern
Wisconsin,
through  northeastern  Illinois,  into  northern  Indiana.  The
heart  of the  area  is the  inner  city  of  Chicago,  but  the  rings
of  communities  surrounding  it  are  equally  a  part  of  one
large  urban  entity.  What  in the  past  may  have  been  a  num-
ber  of  independent  communities,  economically  self-suffi-
cient  and  physically  separated  by green  belts,  has  become
today  one  megapolis,  one  large  community,  melded _ to-
gether,  extending  mile  after  mile,  with  no  visible  separa-
tion  of  technically  independent  governmental  units.  How-
ever,  in Chicago  and  elsewhere  across  the  nation,  the  urhan
areas  have  become  marred  by the  blight  of  racial  segrega-
tion.

lt  is  indisputable  that  the  metropolitan  Chicago  area  as
a  whole  is  racially  segregated.  Lower  federal  courts  have
taken  judicial  notice  of  such  segregation.
Gautreaux  v.
Chicago  Housing  Authority,  503  F.2d  930,  937  (7th  Cir.
1974),  cert.  granied  sub.  nom.  Hills  v.  Gautreaux,  421  U.S.
962  (1975)  ; Clark  v.  Universal  Builders,  Inc.,  501  F.2d  324,
334-35  (7th  Cir.  1974),  cert.  denied,  419  U.S.  1070  (1974).
Within  the  area,  the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights  is  one
of  the  most  segregated  suburban  communities.  Of  its  64,-
000  residents  in  1970,  only  twenty-seven  were  biack.  Be-
tween  1960  and  1970,  the  black  population  of  the  four-
township  area  of  which  Arlington  Heights  is  a  part  in-

5

creased  by  170  persons,  while  the  area’s  white  population
had  an  explosive  increase  of  219,000  people.

Nor  is  this  a  static  picture  of  racial  segregation,
limited  to  Arlington  Heights;  rather,  the  accelerating  seg-
regation  of  Arlington  Heights  and  similar  satellite  com-
munities  of  Chicago  is  producing  increased  segregation  in
the  core  city,  Chicago,  itself.
‘The  percentage  of  city
inhabitants  who  are  members  of  racial  minorities  is steadi-
ly increasing,  while  such  minorities’  proportionate  repre-
sentation  in  suburban  communities  is, with  few  exceptions,
decreasing.  (Tr.  533-34).

Chicago,  along  with  other  great  urban  centers,  has  fallen
victim  to  an  inexorable  process  whereby  it has  taken  ‘‘the
racial  shape  of  a  donut  with  the  Negroes  in  the  hole  and
mostly  Whites  occupying  the  ring’’,  quoted  in,  United
States  v.  City  of Black  Jack,  Missouri,  508  F.2d  1179,  1186
(8th  Cir.  1974),  cert.  denied,  422  U.S.  1042  (1975).

This  residential  phenomenon  is  accoimpanied  by  a  cor-
responding  ghettoization  of  employment  opportunities,  of
which  segregated  housing  is  both  cause  and  effect.  Thus
white  residents  fleeing  from  cities  to  suburbs  are  joined
by  the  actual  and  prospective  employers  of  the  racial  mi-
This  case  offers  dramatic  evidence
norities  left  behind.*
of  such  shift  in  employment  opportunities:  between  1960
and  1970,  the  City  of  Chicago  lost  230,000  jobs,  while  the
number  of jobs  in the  Arlington  Heights  area  doubled  from
100,000  to  200,000  (Tr.  574).  This  loss  of  jobs,  combined
with  dramatically  high  unemployment  rates  for  inner-city
minority  residents,  creates  a  dangerous  mixture  which
leads  to  violence:  motivation  created  by the  despair  of pov-

*  Orshansky,  The  Poor  in  City  and  Suburbs,  1964,  29  Soctat  SE-
curity  BuLt.,  Dec.  1966,  at  29;  NATIONAL  COMMITTEE  AGAINST
DISCRIMINATION  IN  Housinc,  THE  IMpact  oF  HousING  ON  JoB
OpporTUNITIES  24-26  (1968).

6

erty,  and  rationalization  created  by  loss  of  constitutional
rights.  Along  with  housing  and  employment  ghettos,  this
pattern  produces  accelerated  segregation  in  the  schools.

in  this  context  of  a  pattern  of  segregated  housing  in
the  metropolitan  Chicago  area  and  in  Arlington  Heights,
and  where  Arlington  Heights  has  made  no  eiiort  to  al-
leviate  the  situation,  the  use  by  Arlington  Heights  of  its
zoning  ordinance  to  prevent  the  construction  of  subsidized,
integrated  housing  such  as  Lincoln  Green,  discriminated
unlawiully  against  racial  imunorities.
‘l'hus,  the  pres-
ent  case  is  not  governed  by  Vilage  of  Belle  Jerre
v.  Boraas,  416  U.S.  1  (1974).  Belle  Terre  did  not  present
an  issue  of  zoning  restrictions  adopted  or  administered
with  the  obvious  and  foreseeable  effect  of  excluding  blacks
and  other  minorities  from  white  suburbs  and  confining  them
to  a  black  city.
Nothing  in  Belle  Lerre  demonstrated
a  pattern  of discrimination  in  which  minorities  are  exclud-
ed  from  a  suburb  with  the  result  that  they  are  contined  to
the  major  city  of  which  the  suburbs  are  satellites  and  are
excluded  from  employment  opportunities  in  the  suburb
itself.

The  Court  found  in  Milliken  v.  Bradley,  418  U.S.  717
(1974),  that  governmental  action  having  the  ‘‘natural,
probable,  foreseeable  and  actual  effect’’  of  creating  and
perpetuating  school  discrimination  within  Detroit  con-
stituted  a  sufficient  predicate  for  violation  of  the  Kqual
Protection  Clause.  418  U.S.  at  725-26.
So  here,  the
obvious  and  foreseeable  effect  of  the  Arlington  Heights
zoning  ordinance  has  been  to  create  and  perpetuate
a  segregated  community  in which  black  and  other  minority
persons  have  been  unable  to  live  and  work,  and  where  they
have  been  denied  the  opportunity  of  attending  local,  inte-
grated  public  schools.  This  is a violation  of the  Equal  Pro-
tection  Clause  of  the  Fourteenth  Amendment.  It  is  also,
unfortunately,  not  unique  to  Arlington  Heights.

7

II.

The  Problem  of  Racial  Residential  Segregation  Pervades
Metropolitan  Areas.

Our  nation  has  been  constitutionally  committed  to  equali-
ty of  opportunity  for  two  centuries  and  to  elimination  of
racial  discrimination  for  more  than  one  century.
These
goals,  promised  by the  Thirteenth  and  Fourteenth  Amend-
ments,  have  been  largely  illusory.  ‘hey  will  remain  an  illu-
sion  unless  positive  steps  are  taken  to  eradicate  those  con-
ditions  caused  by  governmental  action  or  inaction  which
prevent  the  full  admission  of  racial  minorities  into  our
historically  white-dominated  society.  Among  these  objec-
tionable  conditions  are  both  racially  segregated  housing
and  accompanying  racially  segregated  employment  condi-
tion  throughout  the  country.  McDonnell  Douglas  Corp.  v.
Green,  411  U.S.  792,  800  (1973);  United  States  v.  City  of
Black  Jack,  Missouri,  supra  at  1186.  Both  of  these  condi-
tions  are  present  in  this  case.

Despite  the  existence  of legislation  generaily  condemning
housing  discrimination,  our  nation’s  urban  centers  have
become  more  and  more  heavily  populated  by  black  and
other  minority  residents,  while  they  are  surrounded  by  a
See  United  States  v.  City
growing  ring  of  white  suburbs.
of Black  Jack,  Missouri,  supra.

Low  income  minorities  are  forced  to  remain  in  inner  city
ghettos  because  man-made  barriers  block  their  entrance
into  white  suburban  neighborhoods.  LKixclusionary  zoning
practices  utilized  by  suburban  municipalities  are  among
and  impregnable  of  such _ bar-
the  most  formidable
riers.
Lacka-
wanna,  436  F.2d  108  (2d  Cir.  1970),  cert.  dented,  401  U.S.
1010  (1971);  Dailey  v.  City  of Lawton,  Oklahoma,  425  F.2d
1037  (10th  Cir.  1970)  ; Orshansky,  supra  at 22, 26, 30.  Their
clear  effect  and  their  purpose,  except  in  rare  situations,  is
to  erect  a  barrier  against  racial  minorities,  by  raising

See  Kennedy  Park  Homes  Assn.

v.

8

9

artificially  the  cost  of  suburban  housing  to  levels  beyond
the  reach  of  the  low-income  inner-city  dwellers,  a  proven
disproportionate  percentage  of  whom  are  members  of
racial  minorities.*  Developing  municipalities  eagerly  seek
industries  and  then  just  as  eagerly  seek  to  exclude  racial
minorities  from  employment  in  those  industries.  They  do
this  by rendering  it impossible  for  lower  paid  employees  of
these  industries  to  live  in  the  community  where  such  em-
ployees  work.**  This  result  is constitutionally  proscribed:
it  is  state  action  which  discriminates  in  housing  and  em-
ployment  opportunities  on  the  basis  of  race.

III.

Petitioner’s  Denial  of  the  Rezoning  Request  Contravenes
National  Housing  Policy,  the  Fair  Housing  Act  of  1968
and  Civil  Rights  Acts.

Pursuant  to  its  mandate  under  the  Thirteenth  and  Four-
teenth  Amendments  to  the  Constitution,  Congress  has  en-
acted  legislation  aimed  at  prohibiting  both  subtle  and  ob-
vious  forms  of  discrimination  in  employment  and  housing.
Wiliams  v.  The  Matihews  Company,  499  F.2d  819,  826
(8th  Cir.
(1974),

These  remedial  laws  impose  upon  governmental  bodies
whose  actions  or  inactions  have  contributed  to  the  existence
and  perpetuation  of  segregated  housing,  the  obligation  not
to  obstruct  efforts  to  create  integrated  housing  opportuni-
ties  in  metropolitan  areas.
The  Village  of  Arlington
Heights  has  failed  to  meet  its  obligation.  .

IV.

‘=

The  Pattern  of  Segregated  Housing  in  Arlington  Heights
Warrants  the  Remedy  Sought  by Respondents.

Two  fundaniental  conditions  have  come  together  in  this
case:  the  massive  interstate  urban  area  involved  (metro-
politan  Chicago)  and  the  pervasive  nature  of  the  housing
discrimination  existing  in  mefropolitan  Chicago,  in  which
the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights  has  participated.  As  a
result,  the  supremacy  of  the  traditionally  local  function  of
zoning,  which  can  be  justified  when  it  does  not  interfere
rights  of  citizens,  must  give  way
with  constitutional
when  its  exercise  does  impair  constitutional  rights.
See
Village  of  Belle  Terre  v.  Boraas,  supra;  So.  Burlington
Cty.  N.A.A.C.P.  v.  Tp.  of  Mt.  Laurel,  67  N.J.  151,  336
A.2d  713  (1975).

This  Court  recognized  the  primacy  of  constitutionally
guaranteed  rights  over  local  action  which  interferes  with
such  rights  in  Milliken  v.  Bradley,  supra,  the  school  segre-
gation  case  involving  the  City  of  Detroit.  The  teaching  of
Milliken  is  that  ‘‘no  state  law  is  above  the  Constitution.

School  district  lines  and  the  present  laws  with  respect  to
local  control,  are  not  sacrosanct  and  if  they  conflict  with
the  Fourteenth  Amendment  federal  courts  have  a  duty  to
prescribe  appropriate  remedies.’’  418  U.S.  at  744.  ‘*The
controlling  principle  consistently  expounded  in  our  hold-
ings  is  that  the  scope  of  the  remedy  is  determined  by  the
nature  and  extent  of  the  constitutional  violation.’’
418
U.S.  at  744.

While  this  Court  found  no  inter-district  segregation  in
Milliken,  it anticipated  one  of  the  significant  aspects  of  the
present  case:  the  effect  of  the  present  zoning  law  is  un-
constitutional  not  only  in  its  impact  in  Arlington  Heights
itself,  but  also  in  its  impact  on  the  urban  area  of  Chicago
of which  Arlington  Heights  is a  part.  The  restrictive  prac-
tices  of  Arlington  Heights  and  similar  suburbs  consign
Chicago  to  becoming  progressively  a  ghetto  area  for  black
and  minorities.  The  whites  flee  to  the  suburbs  and  build
an  effective  wall  which  serves  to  exclude  minorities  from
their  own  community  and  to  confine  them  to  the  City  of
Chicago.  Government  action  by  Arlington  Heights  has
deprived  citizens  of  their  constitutional  rights.

In  Milliken  this  Court  stated:

‘*Before  the  boundaries  of  separate  and  autonomous
school  districts  may  be  set  aside  by  consolidating  the
separate  units  for  remedial  purposes  or  by  imposing
a  cross-district  remedy,  it  must  first  be  shown  that
there  has  been  a  constitutional  violation  within  one
district  that  produces  a  significant  segregative  effect
in  another  district.’’?  418  U.S.  at  744-45.

It  follows  that  a  remedy  must  be  fashioned  to  eliminate
inter-district  wrongs  where  the  district  to  be  affected  by
In  this
the  remedy  participated  in  or  caused  the  wrong.

12

case,  however,  the  remedy  sought  is not  an  ‘‘inter-district”’
remedy,  although  such  a  remedy  would  be  appropriate  if
required;  the  remedy  sought  here  is  applicable  only  to  one
of  the  participants  in  the  area-wide  wrong  of  segregated
housing.

The  Court  of Appeals  was  correct  in holding  that  Arling-
ton  Heights’  rejection  of  the  Lincoln  Green  proposal  had  a
racially  discriminatory  effect  and,  being  without  compelling
justification,  violated  plaintiffs’  right  to  equal  protection  of
the  law  guaranteed  by  the  Fourteenth  Amendment.  To
sanction  the  challenged  action  would  be  to  provide  every
suburban  municipality  an  opportunity  to  escape  from  its
statutory  and  constitutional  obligation  to  refrain  from  con-
duct  that  interferes  with  private  efforts  to  construct  inte-
grated  low-  and  moderate-income  housing  and  would  nulli-
fy  federal  programs  designed  to  implement  this  national
policy.

The  effect  of  such  action  by  suburban  municipalities
is  to  perpetuate  residential  segregation  and  the  con-
tinued  denial  to  racial  minorities  of  their  right  to  equal
access  to  housing  and  employment  opportunities  guaran-
teed  by  the  Equal  Protection  Clause.  The  sought-for  an-
swer  to  the  problem  of  integrated  public  schooling  which
has  repeatedly  been  before  this  Court,  and  has  been  the
source  of  deep  national  controversy  and  division,  may  ul-
timately  be  found  by attacking  its  root  cause:  segregated
patterns  of  residential  housing.

13

CONCLUSION

For  the  foregoing  reasons,  amici  respectfully  request

that  the  decision  of the  Court  of  Appeals  be  affirmed.

Respectfully  submitted,

the  parties.  That,  in  fact,  the  respondents  herein  have  hereto-
fore  filed  a  brief  in  excess  of  50  pages  which  comprehensively
raises  and  briefs  all  the  questions  involved  in  this  cause.

or  fact  which  are  relevant  to  the  disposition  of  the  case  which
have  not  been  raised  in  the  briefs  heretofore  filed  with  this
Court.

4.

That  none  of  the  applicants  herein  sought  to  intervene
or  participate  in  the  trial  below  in  the  District  Court  for  the  .
Northern  District  of  Illinois  or  in  the  proceedings  before  the
Court  of  Appeals  for  the  Seventh  Circuit.  That  the  general
allegations  of  the  motion  are  insufficient  to  show  the  interest
of the  applicants  or  the  necessity  of their  filing  a brief  as  amicus
curiae  in  order  to  fully  advise  and  inform  the  Court  as  to  the
issues  in  this  cause.

5.

That  the  petitioners  herein  have  heretofore  withheld
consent  for  the  filing  of  said  brief  for  the  reason  that  the
necessity  of  answering  the  brief  as  amicus  curiae  would  impose
an  unreasonable  and  unnecessary  burden  upon  these  petitioners,
without  any  additional  aid  to  this  Court  in  determining  the
issues  before  it.

MOTION  OF THE
AMERICAN  SOCIETY  OF  PLANNING  OFFICIALS
FOR  LEAVE  TO
FILE  A BRIEF  AS AMICUS  CURIAE

COMES  NOW  THE  AMERICAN  SOCIETY  OF  PLAN-
NING  OFFICIALS  by  its  undersigned  attorney,  and
respectfully  moves  for  leave  to  file  the  attached  brief
as  Amicus  Curiae  in  the  above  encaptioned  case
pursuant  to  Rule  42(3)  of  the  Rules  of  the  Supreme
Court  of the  United  States.  In support  of this  applica-
tion,  it  states:

1.  The  consent  of the  attorneys  for  the  respondents
has  been  obtained.  The  consent  of  the  attorney  for
the  petitioners  was  requested  but  refused.

2.  The  American  Society  of  Planning  Officials
(ASPO)
is  a  national  membership  association
serving  governmental  planning  agencies  and  local
municipal  officials.  As  such,  it  has  studied  the
problems  of  planning  and  land  use  decision  making
for  over  four  decades.  ASPO  provides  technical
assistance  and  does  research  on  urban  planning  and
local  growth  management  systems.  A  very  signifi-
cant  part  of  this  area  of  concern  and  expertise
pertains  to  the  development  of  adequate  housing
opportunities  for  all  citizens  in  this  country.  ASPO
works  to  ensure  that  the  comprehensive  planning
process  encompasses  rather  than  frustrates  this
concern  with  adequate  housing  opportunities.

position  to  proffer  a  balanced  perspective  of  the
issues  presented  by  this  case.  The  brief  of  Amicus
will  assist  the  court  in  fully  understanding  the
context  of  the  governmental  decision  making  pro-
cess  in this  case,  and  the  rights  of the  various  parties
involved.

Respectfully  submitted,

Conrad  N.  Bagne,  Attorney  for Amicus
1313  E. 60th  St.
Chicago,  IL 60637
312-947-2071

3.  This  case  presents  a  conflict  between  the  strong
tradition  of  local  land  use  planning  in  this  country
and  efforts  to  foster  development  of  integrated  and
dispersed  low  income  housing.  it  is  the  purpose  of
this  brief  by Amicus  to  show  that  local  comprehen-
sive  planning,  the  rights  of  racial  minorities,  and  the
open  housing  laws  of  this  country  can  function
together.  The  opposition  to  desegrated  housing
opportunities  is often  shrouded  in concerns  for  local
decision  making  and  the  integrity  of the  comprehen-
sive  planning  process.  At  the  same  time,  advocates
of  dispersed
racial
minorities  and  elimination  of  segregated  housing
patterns  may  denigrate  the  importance  of  local
comprehensive  planning.  As  one  of  the  major  urban
planning  spokesman  in  this  country  concerned  with
equal  housing  opportunities,  ASPO  is  in  an  unique

opportunities

housing

for

BRIEF  OF THE  AMERICAN  SOCIETY  OF
PLANNING  OFFICIALS  AS  AMICUS  CURIAE

INTEREST  OF AMICUS  CURIAE

This  Amicus  Curiae  brief  is respectfully  submitted
by  the  American  Society  of  Planning  Officials  by
leave  of court  pursuant  to  Rule  42(3)  of the  Supreme
Court  of  the  United  States.

The  American

Society  of  Planning

Officials
(ASPO)  is a  national,  nonprofit  membership  associ-
ation  devoted  to  the  development  and  effective
application  of the  best  techniques  for  managing  the
development  of  cities,
states  and  the
nation.  ASPO  brings  together  the  professional

regions,

2

3

planner  and  citizen  member  of local  boards  and  com-
missions  from  urban,  suburban  and  rural  areas;  the
the
elected
developer  and  development  regulator;  the  practi-
tioner,  student  and  academician.  Founded  in  1934,
ASPO  presently  has  more  than  13,000  members.

community  § groups;

official

and

the

house

national

clearing

As  a  service  organization  ASPO  facilitates  com-
munication  among  all  groups  in  planning.  It serves
as
for  planning
information  and  identifies  and  analyzes  important
trends
in  the  planning  field.  Through  contract
research  and  membership  service  programs,  ASPO
government
provides
agency,  and  other  interested  persons  with  current
information  on  planning  and  advice  on  emerging
developments  in  the  field.

professional

planner,

the

that

ASPO  believes

a  comprehensive

plan,
formally  prepared  and  adopted,  is  an  essential  tool
of  local  self-governance.  Making  such  a  plan  to
guide  the  development  of  a  community  is  a
peculiarly  local  responsibility  and  prerogative.  The
standards  to  be  set,  the  conflicts  to  be  reconciled,
the  special  character  to  be  sought,  the  values  to  be
preserved,  the  priorities  to be  pursued,  the  sacrifices
to  be  made,  the  goals  that  justify  them—all  of  these
choices  must  be  left  to  the  free  exercise  of  a  com-
munity’s  discretion.  When  that  discretion  denies
equal  protection  of the  laws  to  racial  minorities  there
should  be a burden  upon  acommunity  to  defend  that
discretion.

SUMMARY  OF THE  ARGUMENT

Racial  discrimination  issues  that  are  likely  to
come  before  the  Court  will  not  involve  open  dis-
crimination  that  is unconstitutional  on  its  face.  Such

explicit  acts,  in the  land-use  field,  have  been  defini-
tively  excluded  by  this  court.  Buchanan  v.  Warley,
245  U.S.  60  (1917).

The  more  difficult  issues  that  now  are  brought  to
the court  entail  complex  and  interwoven  motivations.
The  same  act  may  be well-designed  to achieve  legiti-
mate,  necessary  ends  of the  general  welfare  and  yet
have  other  effects  that  are  injurious
to  racial
minorities  and  deny  equal  protection  of  the  laws.

In  local  decisions  involving  land-use,  it  is  the  ad
hoc  determination,  with  a  rationale  drafted  to  fit  the
circumstances  @f  each  case,  that  is  likeliest  to
smuggle  freight  injurious  to  civil  rights  laws  or  to
equal  protection.  Rejections  of  private  efforts  to
alleviate  segregated  housing  situations  have  had
such  injurious  effects.  The  community’s  comprehen-
sive  plan  is  a  vehicle  available  to  prevent  such
smuggling  if  the  plan  is  itself  prepared  with  care,
with  due  participation  of citizens,  set  forth  in explicit
terms,  and  formally  adopted.

In  the  process  of  judicial  review,  it  is  the  pre-
sumption  of  validity  of  an  ordinance  that  is  likeliest
to see  a similar  admixture  of denials  of civil  rights  or
equal  protection  with  rational  and  legitimate  ob-
jectives.  The  compelling  interest  test  provides  the
without
appropriate
improper  substitution  of  the  court’s  judgment  for
local  legislative  determinations.

prevent

means

abuse

to

that

A  community  may,  in  its  comprehensive  plan,
basis  for  its
it  has  a  rational
establish
ordinances  not  in  conflict  with  constitutional  or
statutorily  protected  rights.  The  burden  is  on  a
If,  however,  a
complainant  to  prove  otherwise.
complainant  demonstrates  that  the  clear  effect  of an
ordinance  is  violative  of  constitutionally  or
statutorily  protected  civil  rights,  compelling  interest

4

5

provides  a rigorous  test,  protective  of the  interests  of
both  parties,  as  a  basis  of  review.
A community’s
comprehensive  plan,  properly-drawn,  will  assist  it in
meeting  the  burden  of demonstrating  its  compelling
interests.  Application  of  the  compelling  interest
test  should  by no  means  result  in an  over-riding  of all
local  regulation.

ARGUMENT
l.

THE  FAILURE  OF  THE  VILLAGE  TO  GRANT  THE
REZONING  REQUESTED  BY  THE  RESPONDENT
MUST  BE  EXAMINED  IN  TERMS  OF  ITS  EFFECT
UPON  RACIAL  MINORITIES,  NOT  IN  TERMS  OF  ITS
PURPOSE  OR  INTENT

racially  discriminatory

In  examining  challenges  to  governmental  land  use
regulatory  actions  as
in
violation  of  equal  protection  of  the  laws  under  the
Fourteenth  Amendment,  the  touchstone  of  judicial
inquiry  is  effect,  not  motive.  Most  actions  appear
neutral  on  the  surface  and  involve  a  complex  of
the  minds  of  each
diverse  motivations  within
separate  decision
Legislators  and  local
officials  have  traditionally  been  free  from  juridic
inquests  plumbing  their  individual  thought  pro-
cesses  followed  in reaching  particular  decisions,  and
courts  have  sought  to  avoid  making  such  inquiries.
See  City  of  Fairfield  v.  Superior  Court  of  Salano
County,  14  Cal.3d  768,  537  P.2d  375  (1975).

maker.

This  Court  discussed  the  problems  inherent  in
seeking  out  explicit  racial  motivation  in  its  review  of
a municipality’s  decision  to  discontinue  operation  of
several  municipally  owned  or  leased  swimming
pools  in  face  of  a  desegregation  order  in  Palmer  v.

Thompson,  403  U.S.  217  (1971).  After  reviewing  the
prior  decision  of  United  States  v.  O’Brien,  391  U.S.
367  (1968),  the  Court  observed:

the  hazards

That  opinion  explained  well
of
declaring  a  law  unconstitutional  because  of  the
motivation  of  its  sponsors.  First,  it  is  extremely
difficult  for  the  court  to  ascertain  the  motivation,
or  collection  of  different  motivations,  that  lie
behind  a  legislative  enactment...  .
It  is  difficult
or  impossible  for any  court  to determine  the  “sole”
or  “dominant”  motivation  behind  the  choices  of  a
group  of  legislators.  Furthermore,  there  is  an
element  of  futility  in  a  judicial  attempt  to  invali-
date  a  law  because  of  the  bad  motives  of  its
supporters.  If  the  law  is  struck  down  for  this
reason,  rather  than  because  of  its  facial  content
or  effect,  it would  presumably  be  valid  as  soon  as
the  legislature  or  relevant  governing  body  re-
passed  if  for  different  reasons.
(403  U.S.  at
224-225.)

The  Court  emphasized  that  its  prior  equal  protec-
tion  analysis  focused  upon  actual  effect,  not  motiva-
Griffin  v.  County  School  Board  of  Prince
tion.
Edward  County,  377  U.S.  218  (1964),  and  Gomillion
v.  Lightfoot,  364  U.S.  339  (1960).

Circuit  courts  addressing  racial  discrimination  and
the  question  of  purpose  or  intent  have  also  con-
cluded  that  racial  effect
is  the  more  workable

standard.
challenges  to  a  local  redevelopment  program,  Nor-
walk  CORE  v.  Norwalk  Redevelopment  Agency,  395
F.2d  920  (2nd  Cir.  1968),  the  court  stated:

But  the  fact  that  the  discrimination  is not  inherent
in the  administration  of the  program,  but  is,  in the
words  of  the  District  Court,  “accidental  to  the

plan,”  surely  does  not  excuse  the  planners  from
making  sure  that  there  is  available  relocation
housing  for  all  displaces.  “Equal  protection  of
the  laws”  means  more  than  merely  the  absense  of
governmental  action  designed  to  discriminate;
as  Judge  J.  Skelly  Wright  has  said,  “we  now
firmly  recognize  that  the  arbitrary  quality  of
thoughtlessness  can  be  as  disastrous  and  unfair
to  private  rights  and  the  public  interest  as  the
perversity  of a willful  scheme.”  Hobson  v.  Hansen,
269  F.Supp.  401,  497  (D.D.C.  1967).  (395  F.Supp.
at  931.)

The  complexity  of  the  local  land  use  control  and
planning  process  also  makes  concern  with  only
motivation  or  intent  in an  equal  protection  challenge
difficult.  Many  policies  pursued  by  local  govern-
mental  bodies  are  touted  as  in the  best  interest  of the
local  community  or  argued  to  have  been  pursued  in
good  faith.  However,  “[ijt  is of  no  consolation  to  an
individual  denied  the  equal  protection  of  the  laws
that  it was  done  in good  faith.”  Burton  v.  Wilmington
Parking  Authority,
(1961).
365  U.S.
Municipal  policies  pursued  in the  name  of otherwise
_established  goals,  however,  often  have  an  adverse
effect  upon  racial  minorities.  Local  property  tax
structures  encourage  zoning  for what  are  felt,  though
often  not  supported  in  fact,  to  be  !ow  service-
demanding,  high  tax  ratables.  This  often  results  in
antisocial  consequences  and  misuse  of  the  police
power  for  only  a  privileged  segment  of  society.
Again,  it is necessary  to look  to the effect  of the  local
action  on  racial  minorities,  not  simply  the  motiva-
tion.

715,

725

Comprehensive  planning  is  a  process  of  deter-
mining  the  desired  community  character  and  goals
carried  on  by the  village  in  this  case  and  most  local

governments  in this  country.  As  a  process,  it entails
the assessment  of current  conditions,  resources  and
needs  of  the  community,  and  an  identification  and
choice  of future  goals.  Given  the  disparate  nature  of
communities,  this  is  a  highly  open  ended  process
that  speaks  in  term  of  policies  and  objectives
designed  to  guide  subsequent  decision  making.
Competing  needs  for  scarce  resources,  housing,
employment,  and  social  services  are  addressed
through  this  carefully  thought  out  and  reasoned
endeavor.

Comprehensive  planning  is also  a  highly  localized
process  with  each  community  seeking  to  identify  the
factors  which  it  desires  to  protect  or  perpetuate  to
ensure  that  those  characteristics  which  make  it  a
community  are  achieved  or  preserved.  The  goals  and
purposes  of the  community  are  ensured  or  obtained
through  various  implementing  techniques.
This
implementation  of  the  comprehensive  plan  involves
the  public  influencing  or  control  of  private  activity,
as  well  as  certain  public  investments  designed  to
accomplish  the  plan’s  goals.  On  the  public  fisc  side,
this  may  include  investments  in  capital  improve-
ments  such  as  highways  and  utility  lines  designed  to
encourage  development  of  residential,  commercial
or  industrial  facilities.  As  to  regulatory  imp!ementa-
tion,  direct  control  of  private  activity  through  zoning
restrictions  remains  the  most  common  of  all  imple-
mentation  techniques.

In this  Court’s  opinion  upholding  zoning  as a valid
exercise  of  the  police  power,  the  well  reasoned  and
comprehensive  studies  supporting  that  zoning  were
emphasized.  Village  of  Euclid  v.  Ambler  Realty  Co.,
272  U.S.  365,  394  (1926).  Local  zoning  based  on  a
well  reasoned  and  fair  process  must  still  meet,  inter
alia,  constitutional  requirements  of  due  process

8

9

and  equal  protection.  It is  the  requirement  of  equal
protection  and  the  extent  of justification  required  for
a locai  zoning  decision  that  is at  issue  in  this  case.
While  based  on a fair  and  well  reasoned  process,
zoning  is  a  continually  changing  activity.  A  land-
owner  desiring  to  develop  a  specific  parcel  of
property  must  often  seek  development  permission
individually  tailored  to that  property  and  the  specific
development  proposal.  Permission  to  develop  may
entail  request  for a specific  rezoning  of  the  property
in  question  to  the  proper  use  classification  or
density  limitation,  or  a  special  use  permit  may  be
necessary.  A  project  must  also  often  receive  site
plan  review  approval  as  to  the  design  of the  develop-
ment,  and  ultimately,  a  building  permit  and  utility
hook-up  permission.  All  of  these  Stages  of  per-
mission  entail  negotiation  and  exercise  of discretion
by  the  governmental  body  involved.  They  remain

be

Because  of  this  complex  nature  of  the  local  land
use  decision  making  process,  decisions  claimed  to
have  a  racially  discriminatory  effect  arise  in  many
contexts.  The  decision  challenged  may  involve  an
attempt  to  rezone  a  parcel  of  property  so  as  to  keep
Out  a  project  designed  for  low  income  and  minority
residents,  or  refusal!  of  an  application  for  necessary
zoning  to  allow  development  of  such  a  project.  In
Dailey  v.  City  of  Lawton,  425  F.2d  1037  (10th  Cir.
1970),  for  example,  a  private  nonprofit  housing  de-
veloper  challenged  the  municipal  refusal  to  rezone  a
parcel  of  land  for  multiple-family  dwellings  on  the

ground  of racial  discrimination.  The  court  found  that
the  refusal  to  rezone  was  racially  discriminatory  and
unlawful,  without  a  showing  of  “racial  intent”  on  the
part  of  the  public  officials.  Simiiarly,  in  Sisters  of
Providence  of  St.  Mary  of  the  Woods  v.  City  of
Evanston,  335  F.Supp.  396  (N.D.  Ill.  1971),  the  court
refused  to dismiss  a challenge  to  the  city’s  refusal  to
rezone  a  parcel  of  property  to  a  higher  density.  The
court  observed  that  the  city  could  not  “refuse  to  re-
zone  for  black  projects  where  under  the  same  cir-
Cumstances  it  would  have  granted  a  variance  to  an
all-white  project.”  (335  F.Supp.  at  404.)

housing  pattern.  These  include,

Many  other  devices  of  local  land  use  regulation
may,  as  applied,  effectuate  a  discriminatory  and
segregated
in
addition  to zoning,  refusals  of building  permits,  con-
nections  to  public  water  systems,  and  connections
to  sewer  systems.  In  Kennedy  Park  Homes  Associa-
tion  v.  City  of  Lackawanna,  436  F.2d  108  (2nd  Cir.
1970),  and  Crow  v.  Brown,  457  F.2d  788  (5th  Cir.
1972),  refusals  of  necessary  permits  were  found  to
be  invalid  because  of  racially  discriminatory  effect.
And  again,  more  recently,  in  United  Farm  Workers  of
Florida  Housing  Project,  Inc.  v.  City  of  Del  Ray
Beach,  493  F.2d  799  (5th  Cir.  1974),  refusal  of  water
and  sewer  permits  was  found  to  have a racially  dis-
criminatory  effect,  particularly  in  light  of  evidence
that  other  developments  had  been  extended  services.
The  effect  of  the  city’s  action  was  to  reinforce  the
existing  segregated  housing  pattern  in  the  city  and
to  frustrate  efforts  to  construct  housing  that  would
be  inhabited  by  a  large  proportion  of  low-income
minority  families.  The  circuit  court  held:

Once  the  existence  of  a  racially  discriminatory
effect  is  thus  proven,  the  City  must  bear  the
heavy  burden  of  demonstrating  that  its  refusal

11

and  resulting  discrimination,  were  necessary  to
promote  a  compelling  governmental
interest.
(493  F.2d  at  809.)

The  entire  panoply  of  public  services  provided  by
municipal  governments  has  also  been  challenged  on
the  basis  of  racial  discrimination.  In  Hawkins  v.
Town  of  Shaw,  461  F.2d  1171  (5th  Cir.  en  banc
1972),  the  court  examined  the  challenge  to  the
town’s  allegedly  discriminatory  policies  of  pro-
viding  public  services  in terms  of racial  effect.  The  en
banc  court  reaffirmed  the  decision  of  the  original
panel  of  the  court,  437  F.2d  1286  (5th  Cir.  1971),  to
the  effect  that  a denial  of equal  protection  of the  laws
had  been  established.

A  REZONING  DENIAL  WHICH  SERVES  TO  PER-
PETUATE  A  RACIALLY  SEGREGATED  HOUSING
PATTERN  IN  FACE  OF  PRIVATE  EFFORTS  TO
ALLEVIATE  THAT  PATTERN  HAS A RACIALLY  DIS-
CRIMINATORY  EFFECT  UNDER  EQUAL  PROTEC-
TION  OF  THE  LAWS  OF  THE  FOURTEENTH
AMENDMENT

effect

Adverse  racial

is  established  where  a
housing  project,  which  would  provide  housing  for  a
larger  than  usual  minority  population,  is  rejected  by
a  community  that  is  highly  segregated.  Under  the
Equal  Protection  Clause  of  the  Fourteenth  Amena-
ment  the  burden  then  shifts  to  the  municipality  to
justify  its  actions  by some  compelling  governmental
purpose.  This  Court’s  opinion  in  Palmer  v.  Thomp-
son,  403  U.S.  217  (1971),  does  not  hold  to  the
contrary  as  some  decisions  have  interpreted  it.  In  a

case  similar  to  the  one  at  bar,  the  Sixth  Circuit  cited
Palmer  for  the  proposition  that  a  neutral  policy  with
greater  impact  on  a  minority  group  is  not  invalid.
Skilken  &  Co.  v.  Toledo,  Nos.  74-2116  and  74-2320
(6th  Cir.  filed  December 10, 1975).  Accord  Mahaley  v.
Cuyahoga  Metropolitan  Housing  Authority,  500  F.2d
1087,  1093  (6th  Cir.  1974).  This  is not  what  this  Court
stated  in  Palmer.  It  simply  held  that  there  was  no
evidence  in  that  case  to  show  that  the  city  had
affected  blacks  differently  from  anyone  else  in
closing  down  its  municipal  swimming  pools.  The
Court  reiterated  that  it will  continue  to  look  to  racial
effect  in equal  protection  challenges.  See  Kushner  &
Werner,  Metropolitan  Desegregation  After  Milliken  v.
Bradley:  The  Case  For  Land  Use  Litigation  Strate-
gies,  24  Catholic  Univ.  L.  Rev.  187,  212  (1975).

This  analysis  is  similar  to  that  carried  on  under
other  laws  prohibiting  activities  that  discriminate
against  racial  minorities.  In the  area  of employment,
this  Court  has  invalidated  even  private  practices  that,
while  racially  neutral  on  the  surface,  dispropor-
tionately  affect  minorities.  In  Griggs  v.  Duke  Power
Co.,  401  U.S.  424  (1971)  it  was  stated  that  “good
intent  or  absence  of  discriminatory  intent  does  not
redeem  employment  procedures  or  testing  me-
chanisms  that  operate  as  “built-in  headwinds’  for
minority  groups  and  are  unrelated  to  measuring  job
capability.”
at  432).  See  McDonnell
Douglas  Corp.  v.  Green,  411  U.S.  792  (1973);  Gaston
County  v.  U.S.,  395  U.S.  285  (1969)  (voting  restric-
tions);  U.S.  v.  Real  Estate  Development  Corp.,  347
F.Supp.  766  (N.D.  Miss.  1972)  (public  housing);  and
Keyes  v.  School  District  No.  1,  Denver,  Colorado,
413  U.S.  189,  217  (1973)  (public  schools)  (Powell,  J.,
concurring  in  part,  dissenting  in  part).

This  does  noi  mean  that  the  village  has  an  affirma-
tive  obligation  to  provide  housing  for  anyone,  or  to
solve  the  racial  segregation  problems  of  the  entire
Chicago  metropolitan  region.  The  respondents  seek
only  to  build  a  private  housing  project  without
imposing  any  burdens  upon  the  village.  The  Second
Circuit  has  noted  the  difference  between  imposing
burdens  and  removing  barriers  in  several  cases
involving  subsidized  housing  projects.  That  court

has  refused  to  impose  an  affirmative  duty  upon  a
local  government  to  construct  housing  or  to affirma-
tively  seek  out  subsidized  housing.  Acevedo  v.
Nassau  County,  500  F.2d  1078  (2nd  Cir.  1974),  and
Citizens  Committee  for  Faraday  Wood  v.  Lindsay,
507  F.2d  1065  (2nd  Cir.  1974).  However,  the  court
also  reaffirmed  its  holding  in  Kennedy  Park  Homes
Association  v.  City  of  Lackawanna,  436  F.2d  108
(2nd  Cir.  1970),  that  governmental  barriers  to  private
efforts  to  provide  integrated  housing  in  a  highly
segregated  market  would  be  closely  scrutinized.
Speaking  of  the  Lackawanna  case,  the  Second  Cir-
cuit  held  in  Faraday  Wood:

Against  this  background  [a  clearly  segregated
city]  we  held  that  the  city  could  not,  absent  a
compelling  interest,  thwart  the  efforts  of  a  private
organization  to  build  housing  for  low-income
families  in  the  white  area  of  the  city  by,  among
other  things,  refusing  to  accede  to  a  reasonable
rezoning  request.  (507  F.2d  at  1069.)

This  element  of  imposing  additional  financial
burdens  upon  a community  absent  a duty  to  provide
housing  or  other  services  has  also  been  noted  in  the
opinions  of  this  Court.  James  v.  Valtierra,  402  U.S.
137  (1971)  and  Palmer  v.  Thompson,  403  U.S.  217
those
(1971).
decisions  from  the  case  at  bar.  The  respondents
herein  make  no  such  demand  of  the  village.  In  fact,
there  was  evidence  that  their  development  would
make  a  net  contribution  to  the  public  fisc.

This  factor

The  Village  of Arlington  Heights  is clearly  a segre-
gated  community.  Furthermore,  it lies  within  a  larger
area  that  has  also  been  found  to be segregated.  Clark
v.  Universal  Builders,  501  F.2d  324  (7th  Cir.  1974).
The  circuit  court  in the  case  at  bar  found  that  out  of

14

15

the  village’s  64,884  residents  in  1970,  only  twenty-
seven  were  black.  The  respondents’  development
would  significantly  alleviate  the  segregated  housing
situation.  To  deny  them  the  right  to  go  ahead  with
their  project  in  light  of  the  segregated  character  of
the community  has  discriminatory  racial  effects.  The
denial  of the  rezoning  for this  project  must  be  strictly
scrutinized  and  justified  by some  compelling  govern-
mental  interest.  To  do  less  would  be a violation  of
equal  protection  of  the  laws  as  to  the  respondents
herein.

A  REZONING  DENIAL  WHICH  SERVES  TO
PERPETUATE  A  RACIALLY  SEGREGATED  HOUS-
ING  PATTERN  IN  FACE  OF  PRIVATE  EFFORTS  TO
ALLEVIATE  THAT  PATTERN  HAS A  RACIALLY  DIS-
CRIMINATORY  EFFECT  UNDER  THE  1866  CIVIL
RIGHTS  ACT  AND  THE  1968  FAIR  HOUSING  ACT

Fair and  open  housing  is clearly  the  law  and  policy
of the  United  States.  This  policy  is effectuated,  inter
alia,  by the  1866  Civil  Rights  Act,  42  U.S.C.  § 1982
and  the  1968  Fair  Housing  Act,  42 U.S.C.  §§ 3601  ef.
seq.  As  with  challenges  under  the  Equal  Protection
Clause  of  the  Fourteenth  Amendment,  the  deter-
mination  of violation  of these  acts  is concerned  with
effect  and  not  intent.  The  Fair  Housing  Act  is aimed
at  “all  practices  which  have a racially  discouraging
effect.”  Zuch  v.  Hussey,  366  F.Supp.  553  (E.D.  Mich.
1973).  This  court  has  given  a  broad  interpretation  to
the  Civil  Rights  Acts  also.  See  Jones  v.  Alfred  H.
Meyer  Co.,  392  U.S.  409  (1968)  and  Trafficante  v.
Metropolitan  Life  Insurance  Co.,  409  U.S.  205  (1972).
Denial  of.  development  permission  for  a  project
which  would  alleviate  existing  racial  segregation  has

Gu

a  racially  discriminatory  effect  in violation  of the  fair
housing  laws  and  civil  rights  acts.  Activities  subject
to  review  under  these  laws  must  be  justified  by  a
compelling  governmental  interest  or  they  will  be
invalidated.  United  States  v.  City  of  Black  Jack,  508
F.2d  1179  (8th  Cir.  1974);  Kennedy  Park  Homes
Association  v.  City  of  Lackawanna,  436  F.2d  108
(2nd  Cir.  1970).

IV.

LOCAL  ZONING  ACTION  WHICH  HAS  DISCRIMINA-
TORY  RACIAL  EFFECTS  UNDER  THE  EQUAL  PRO-
TECTION  CLAUSE  OF  THE  FOURTEENTH  AMEND-
MENT,  THE  1866  CIVIL  RIGHTS  ACT,  OR  THE  1968
FAIR  HOUSING  ACT  MUST  BE  JUSTIFIED  BY  SOME
COMPELLING  GOVERNMENTAL  INTEREST

Action  which  has  the  effect  of  discriminating  on
the  basis  of  race  is  subject  to  strict  scrutiny  and
must  be  supported  by  a  compelling  governmental
interest,  or  it  is  invalid  under  the  Equal  Protection
Clause  of the  Fourteenth  Amendment  or  fair  housing
and  civil  rights  laws.  Loving  v.  Virginia,  388  U.S.  1
(1967);  McLaughlin  v.  Florida,  379  U.S.  184  (1964);
Shapiro  v.  Thompson,  394  U.S.  618  (1969).  The
village’s  denial  of  the  rezoning  request,  when  it has
this  effect,  removes  the  presumption  of  validity  of
its  action.  The  burden  is  then  upon  the  village  to
establish  the  compelling  interest  sought  to  be  pro-
tected  by  refusing  that  request.  Dunn  v.  Blumstein,
405  U.S.  330  (1972);  United  States  v.  City  of  Black
Jack,  508  F.2d  1179  (8th  Cir.  1974).

In  United  States  v.  City  of  Black  Jack,  508  F.2d
1179  (8th  Cir.  1974),  the  court  enjoined  enforcement
of  an  ordinance  preventing  construction  of  apart-
ments  because  of  the  ordinance’s  discriminatory

16

17

effects.  The  court  held:

Once  the  plaintiff  has  established  a  prima  facie
case  by  demonstrating  racially  discriminatory
effect,  the  burden  shifts  to  the  governmental
defendant  to  demonstrate  that  its  conduct  was
necessary  to  promote  a  compelling  governmental
interest.  (508  F.2d  at  1185.)

The  court  observed  that  the  compelling  interest  test
would  be properly  applied  whether  the  challenge  was
based  upon  the  Fourteenth  Amendment  or  upon  the
federal  fair  housing  laws.  (508  F.2d  at  1185,  n.  4.)
The  court  continued:

In  determining  whether  any  of  these  [asserted
interests]  rise  to  the  level  of  a compelling  govern-
mental  interest,  we  must  examine:  first,  whether
the  ordinance  in  fact  furthers  the  governmental
interest  asserted;  second,  whether  the  public
interest  served  by the  ordinance  is constitutionally
permissible  and  is  substantial  enough  to  out-
weigh  the  private  detriment  caused  by  it;  and
third,  whether  less  drastic  means  are  available
whereby  the  stated  governmental  interests  may  be
attained.  (508  F.2d  at  1187.)

The  Black  Jack  court  went  on  to  find  that  the  city’s
claimed  .interests  in  traffic  control,  overcrowded
schools,  and  adjacent  property  values  failed  to  meet
even  the  first  element  of the compelling  interest  test.
Concerning  alleged  devaluation  of  adjoining  single-
family  homes,  the  court  indicated  that  a  metro-
politan  area  planning  study  of  apartment  develop-
ments  had  concluded  that  such  developments  did
not  adversely  affect  single-family  property  values.

The  justifications  argued  to  be  compelling  in  the
case  at  bar  were  maintaining  the  integrity  of  the

zoning  plan’s  buffer  policy  and  protecting  neighbor-
ing  property  values.  The  circuit  court  concluded  that
neither  of  these  interests  were  sufficiently
compelling  to  justify  the  racially  discriminatory
effect  of the  village’s  denial  of  the  rezoning  request.

V.

THE  INTERESTS  PUT  FORTH  BY  THE  VILLAGE
HEREIN  WERE  NOT  SUFFICIENTLY  COMPELLING
TO  JUSTIFY  THE  DENIAL  OF  THE  REZONING  TO
THE  RESPONDENTS

The  village  argues  that  the  rezoning  request  was
denied  in order  to  protect  the  integrity  of  the  zoning
plan  and  the  community’s  master  plan.  It argues  that
property  owners  in the  area  had  and  continue  to  rely
upon  the  single-family  nature  of  zoning  in  the  area,
and  the  policy  that  multiple  dwellings  would  only  be
used  as  buffers  to  more  intensive  uses.  Amicus
suggest  that,  however  viewed,  these  reasons  do  not
satisfy  the  compelling  interest  requirement.

Property  owners  in  the  village  have  no  right  to
expect  that  zoning  will  continue  unchanged  forever
as  to  any  particular  parcel  of  property.  This  is
particularly  true  where  the  land  is  previously  un-
developed  as  the  property  involved  here  was.  Low
density  single-family  residential  zoning  is often  used
as  a  holding  zone  until  a  landowner’s  plans  become
more  definite.  Zoning  is  not  a  static  process,  and
change  is a natural  and  expected  part  of the  system.
As one  court  had  occasion  to observe  after  reviewing
a  number  of  zoning  cases:

An  examination  of  the  multitude  of  zoning  cases
that  have  reached  this  court  leads  us  to  the  con-

18

19

clusion  that  the  common  practice  of  zoning
agencies,  after  the  adoption  of  an  original  ordi-
nance,  is  simply  to  wait  until  some  property
owner  finds  an  opportunity  to  acquire  a  financial
advantage  by devoting  his  property  to  a  use  other
than  that  for  which  it  is zoned,  and  then  struggle
with  the  question  of  whether  some  excuse  can  be
found  for  complying  with  his  request  for  a  re-
zoning.  (Fritts  v.  City  of  Ashland,  348  S.W.2d
712,  714  (Ky.  1961).)

The  integrity  of  asystem  which  is based  on  continual
change  and  refinement  does  not  require  denial  of
every  change.  As  the  court  quoted  above  went  on  to
say,  “planning”  not  excuses  is  the  foundation  for
zoning.  The  necessity  of a well  reasoned  and  thought
out  plan  as  the  basis  of  comprehensive  zoning  was
one  of  the  pole  stars  of  this  Court’s  decision  to
uphold  police  power  zoning  in  Village  of  Euclid  v.
Ambler  Realty  Co.,  272  U.S.  365,  394  (1926).

Comprehensive  zoning  is  traditionally  given  a
presumption  of  validity  which  must  be  overcome  in
most  cases  by the challenger.  Petitioners  in the  case
at  bar  point  this  out  and  emphasize  the  refusal  by the
judiciary  to  substitute  its  judgment  for  that  of  a
zoning  body  or  to sit as  Super  zoning  boards.  Though
some  distinction  may  be  made  as  to  piecemeal
changes  in zoning,  Amicus  agrees  that  for  the  most
part  petitioners  have  set  out  a  proper  picture  of  the
usual  zoning  review  situation.  However,  this  is  not
an  usual  zoning  case.  The  element  of  racial  effect  in
the  village’s
rejection  of  respondents’  rezoning
request  removes  this  case  from  that  category.  This
case  must  be subjected  to  strict  scrutiny  by the Court
to ensure  that  the  reasons  behind  the  village’s  action
are  legitimate  and  compelling,  and  determine  if there
are  less  drastic  means  of  achieving  these  ends.

The  village  asserted  that  its  policy  of  using  multi-
family  developments  as  buffers  to  more  intensive
uses  was  part  of the  planning  basis  of  its  rejection  of
this  zoning  application.  However,  there  was  evidence
that  the  buffer  plan  had  not  been  applied  con-
sistently.  While  the  court  found  that  there  had  been
no  purposeful  discrimination  in  the  application  of
the  buffer  policy,  the  questionable  consistency
weakened  the  policy  as  a  compelling  interest  to  be
protected.  If compelling,  why  not  in  every  instance?
Furthermore,  there  was  evidence  showing  that  the
-  nature  of  the  project  proposed  herein  was  not  a
as
high
encompassed  within  the  meaning  of  the  buffer
policy.  The  townhouse  development  proposed  in this
case  was  more  in  character  with  the  single-family
dwellings  in  the  area  than  a  typical  apartment
complex  or  highrise  contemplated  by  the  requested
zoning  district  and  the  buffer  policy.  Two  dissenting
members  of  the  plan  commission  had  pointed  out
that  in terms  of density,  architecture,  and  most  other
characteristics,
the  clusters  of  two-story  town-
houses  were  more  in  keeping  with  the  single-family
zoning  district  than  the  multiple-family  zone.

large  apartment

building

density

or

However  artificial  the  zones  or  districts  may  have
been,  the  respondents’  development  came  only
within  the  definition  of  multiple-family  dwellings  as
permitted  in the  requested  R-5 zone.  In order  to  build
their  project,  the  respondents  had  to  obtain  the
appropriate  classification.  Lines  drawn  to  distin-
guish  types  of  residential  dwelling  must  admittedly
be  put  somewhere.  But  the  distinctions  between  the
residences  contemplated  by  this  project  and  adja-
cent  residences  here  are  not  compelling.

The  buffer  policy  fails  to  meet  the  first  element  of
the  compelling  interest  test,  that  it “in  fact  furthers
the  governmental  interest  asserted.”  The  townhouse

20

21

project  proposed  in  the  case  at  bar  was  not  of  the
typical  apartment  nature  sought  to  be  used  as  a
buffer,  and  had  more  in  common  with  the  adjacent
single-family  dwellings,  This  proposal  would  not  so
seriously  disrupt  the  character  of  the  area  as  to
destroy  the  integrity  of  the  comprehensive  zoning
plan  for  the  area.  With  a  development  proposal  so  ~
similar  to  the  adjacent  single-family  development,
there  were  certainly  other  less  drastic  means  to
ensure  that  any  alleged  adverse  impact  on  the  adja-
cent  single-family  area  might  be  lessened.  Proper
design  assurances,  layout  and  clustering  of  the
townhouses,  and  creative  arrangement  of  open
space  areas  and  access  facilities  have  all  been  useful
in  this  regard.  As  the  development  was  proposed
here,  more  than  sixty  per  cent  of  the  site  would  be
maintained  as  open  space.

The  second  interest  put  forth  by  the  village  as
compelling  was  that  neighboring  property  values
might  be  diminished.  Though  the  circuit  court
questioned  whether  such  considerations  would  ever
be  acompelling  interest,  it held  that  the  facts  in this
case  could  not  support  such  an  interest  even  if
compelling.  While  a  dramatic  or  total  destruction  in
property  values  might  be  a  compelling  interest,  cer-
tainly  only a speculative  five  or  ten  per  cent  decrease
at most,  as  suggested  by the  village’s  own  expert,  is
not.  Furthermore,  the  protection  of  property  values
due  to  reliance  on  the  existing  zoning  is  not
compelling  where  the  property  owners  could  not
expect  the  undeveloped  land  to  stay  the  same
forever,  and  had  no  right  to  see  the  land  used  only  in
a manner  exactly  like  theirs.  The  property  in question
had  been  maintained  as  open  space  by a  private
institution  no  longer  wishing  to  do  so.  Also,  the
nature  of  the  development  proposed  here  is  so
similar  to  the  single-family  dwellings  already  in

existence  that  adverse  impact  is difficult  to  perceive.
The  design  and  layout  of the  project  can  ensure  that
any  adverse  impact  that  might  allegedly  occur  will  be
minimized.

To the  extent  the  fear  of decreased  property  values
is  due  to  antagonism  towards  the  development
because  of its  potential  racially  integrated  character,
this  simply  cannot  create  a  property  value  to  be  pro-
tected.  To  hold  otherwise  would  be to Say  that  first,  a
racially  segregated,  all-white  residential  community
was  more  desirable  and  could  justifiably  command  a
premium  price  for  residency  therein.  And  second,  it
would  mean  that  this  “premium”  was  a  right
justifying  racial  discrimination  and  perpetuation  of
segregated  housing.

The  petitioners’  suggest  in their  brief  that  applica-
tion  of  the  compelling  interest  test  to  local  land  use
decisions  involving  applications  to  build  low-income
multiple-family  dwellings  will  result  in  the  destruc-
tion  of all  single-family  zoning  districts.  (Petitioners’
Brief  at  16.)  This  logic  simply  is  unfounded  and  will
not  result  from  the  Court’s  affirmance  of  the  circuit
court  opinion  in  this  case.  Comprehensive  com-
munity  planning  and  local  land  use  regulations
designed  to  establish  distinct  residential  districts
may  still  be established  and  defended.  The  Sixth  Cir-
cuit  has  spoken  to  the  issue  of  whether  application
of the  compelling  interest  test  would  destroy  all  local
zoning.  Skilken  &  Co.  v.  Toledo,  Nos.  74-2116  and
74-2320  (6th  Cir.  filed  December 10, 1975).  To  the  ex-
tent  this  decision  concludes  that  application  of  the
compelling  interest  means  destruction  of  local
zoning,  Amicus  suggests  it  is  incorrect.  However,
such  a position  is not  entirely  clear  from  the  opinion.
_  The  court  notes  that  the  city could  have  satisfied  the
compelling  interest  test  if necessary.  (Slip opinion  at
20,  n.  4.)  Furthermore,  much  of  the  court’s  concern

22

with  the  future  vitality  of  local  zoning  controls  was
due  to  its  dissatisfaction  with  the  remedy  fashioned
by the  district  court—preparation  of  a  disegregation
plan  by  the  city.  A  remedy  the  court  felt  was  un-
warranted  and  beyond  the  power  of  the  judiciary.

The  case  at  bar  is concerned  with  racial  discrimi-
nation  and  private  efforts  to alleviate  the  problems  of
segregated  housing  patterns.  Communities  are  not
being  asked  to  provide  housing  for anyone  or  to  dis-
card  all  existing  regulations  in  deference  to  low-
income  integrated  housing.  A proposal  to  build  upon
a  flood  plain,  in  an  area  with  unstable  soil  condi-
tions,  or  where  public  facilities  such  as  sewer  and
water  were  not  yet  available  could  be validly  rejected
under  the  compelling  interest  test.  Many  other
conditions  may  also,  in specific  instances,  meet  the
burdens  of this  test.  But  these  concerns  or  interests
must  be  supported  by the  evidence  and  not  used  to
discriminate  against  racial  minorities.
See,
e.g.,  United  Farm  Workers  of  Florida  Housing  Pro-
ject,  Inc.  v.  City  of  Delray  Beach,  493  F.2d  799  (5th
Cir.  1974);  Church  of  God  of  Louisiana  v.  Monroe-
404
Planning
Ouachita
Regional
F.Supp.  175  (W.D.  La.  1975).

Commission,

A development  proposal  that  is  in  clear  contradic-
tion  to  an  established  and  consistently  followed
policy  of  a  community’s  comprehensive  plan  and
land  use  restrictions  may  yet  be  rejected.  Where
policies  are  explicit  and  the  grounds  for  application
and  non-application  are  set  out,  the  compelling
interests  sought  to  be  protected  may  be  upheld.
Communities  that  have  become  highly  segregated
must  ensure  that  their  decisions  which  reject  private
efforts  to  introduce  integrated  housing  develop-
ments  do  not  simply  effectuate  a continuation  of that
segregated  pattern.

23

CONCLUSION

The  use  of zoning  in  this  country  was  established
on  the  legitimate  desire  of communities  to  determine
future  development  for  themselves  as  against  the
right  of  individuals  to  use  private  property  without
restrictions.

This  Court  approved  local  zoning  controls  in
Village  of Euclid  v.  Ambler  Realty  Co.,  272  U.S.  365
(1926).  Two  years  later  the  Court  again  had  occasion
to scrutinize  zoning  powers  and  pull  in the  reins  of a
specific  exercise  of  the  “governmental  power  to
interfere  by  zoning  regulations  with  the  general
rights  of  the  land  owner  by  restricting  the  character
of  his  use.”  Nectow  v.  City  of  Cambridge,  277  U.S.
183,  188  (1928).  This  opinion  indicated  a willingness
to  supervise  exercise  of zoning  powers  as  necessary
to  protect  private  rights.

This  Court  had  the  scope  of  power  of  local  zoning
authorities  before  it again  quite  recently  in  Village  of
Belle  Terre  v.  Boraas,  416  U.S.  1  (1974)  where  it
reaffirmed  the  expansive  power  of  local  zoning
controls.  However,  the  Court  also  evidenced  a
willingness  to  more  closely  scrutinize  zoning  actions
that  infringe  upon  fundamental  rights  or discriminate
against  racial  minorities.  It found  no  evidence  in  the
record  to  indicate  infringement  of  constitutionally
protected  rights  by  that  community’s  definition  of
“family.”  This  case  presents  the  opportunity  for  the
Court  to  examine  modern  zoning  practices  that  in-
fringe  upon  the  rights  of  racial  minorities  seeking
open  housing  opportunties  offered  by  a  private
housing
developer  in  an  otherwise
market.  Just  as  Nectow  complimented  and  clarified
the  Court’s  original  approval  of  zoning,  so  this  case
will  present  a  Clarification  to  Belle  Terre  in  the
to
application

of  the  compelling

segregated

interest

test

24

activities  infringing  on  constitutionally  protected
rights.

For  the  community,

The  invocation  of  the  compelling  interest  test
and  its  application  must  be  done  with  rigor  to  avoid
displacing  local  legislative  judgments  with  judicial
judgments.
its  strongest
evidence  in  meeting  this  burden  of  demonstrating  a
compelling  interest  remains  it  comprehensive  plan,
adopting  policies  for  all  to  read  and  follow.  Just  as
planning  and  forethought  were  important  to  this
Court’s  original  decision  to  uphold  zoning,  so  the
modern  comprehensive  planning  process  retains
validity  in  meeting  additional  burdens  when  consti-
tutionally  protected  rights  are  at  stake.  Courts  need
not  impose  any  uniformity  of standards  or  values  on
communities  as  long  as  constitutionally  or
Statutorily  protected  rights  are  guarded.  Within  this
context,  communities  remain  free  to  lay  out  areas
where  the  “blessings  of  quiet  seclusion,  and  clean
air  make  the  area  a  sanctuary  for  people.”  Village  of
Belle  Terre  v.  Boraas,  416  U.S.  1,  9 (1974).

Amicus  respectfully  submits  that  the  decision  of
the  U.S.  Court  of  Appeals  for  the  Seventh  Circuit  is
correct,  and,  accordingly,  should  be  affirmed.

Respectfully  submitted,

Conrad  N.  Bagne,  Attorney  for Amicus
1313  E. 60th  St.
Chicago,  IL 60637

ON  WRIT  OF  CERTIORARI  TO  THE  UNITED  STATES  COURT
OF  APPEALS  FOR  THE  SEVENTH  CIRCUIT.

OBJECTIONS  TO  MOTION  OF  THE  AMERICAN

AMICUS  CURIAE.

Now  comes  the  Village  of  Arlington  Heights,  et  al.,  peti-
tioners  herein,  by Jack  M.  Siegel,  their  attorney,  and,  pusuant
to  Rule  42(3)  of  the  United  States  Supreme  Court,  object  to
the  motion  of  the  American  Society  of  Planning  Officials  for
leave  to  file  a  brief  as  amicus  curiae.  In  support  of  said  objec-
tions,  petitioners  say  as  follows:

2

3

petitioners  in  an  effort  to  learn  the  point  of  view  of  the  peti-
tioners  herein.

WHEREFORE,  the  petitioners  respectfully  object  to  the  filing
of said  brief  as  amicus  curiae  and  respectfully  request  that  said
motion  be denied.

Respectfully  submitted,

JACK  M.  SIEGEL,

39 South  LaSalle  Street,
Chicago,  Illinois  60603,
312-263-2968,

Attorney  for Petitioners.

1.  That  the  motion  for leave  to file  a  brief  as  amicus  curiae
fails  to  concisely  state  the  nature  of  the  applicant’s  interest  in
the  issues  in  this  case,  but  instead  merely  describes  the  status
of  the  American  Society  of  Planning  Officials  and  contains
general  allegations  not  related  to  the  specific  nature  of  the
case  before  this  Court.  That  the  applicant  seeks  to  “proffer  a
balanced  prospective  of  the  issues  presented  by this  case,”  but
by  the  terms  of  its  motion  is  wholly  biased  and  in  no  way
objective  about  the issues.

2.  That  said  motion  fails  to  set  forth  facts  or  questions
of law  that  have  not  been  adequately  presented  by the  parties,  or
reasons  for  believing  that  any  facts  or  questions  of law  will  not
be  adequately  presented  by  the  parties.  That,  in  fact,  the
respondents  herein  have  filed  a  brief  in  excess  of  50  pages
which,  together  with  the  brief  of  the  petitioners,  fully  sets
forth  all the  facts  and  questions  of law  relative  to  this  cause.

3.  That  the  motion  fails  to  set  forth  any  facts  showing  that
the  membership  of  the  American  Society  of  Planning  Officials
has  in  fact  consented  or  supported  the  filing  of  said  motion
and brief.

4.

That  the  applicant  has  failed  to  participate  in  the  trial
below  in  the  District  Court  for  the  Northern  District  of  Illinois
or  in  the  cause  on  hearing  in  the  Court  of  Appeals  for  the
Seventh  Circuit,  and  has  evidenced  no  interest  in  this  cause
until  its  attempt  to  file  a  brief  as  amicus  curiae.

5.  That  the  petitioners  herein  have  withheld  consent  to  the
filing  of a  brief  by the  applicant  for  the  reason  that  no  necessity
exists  for  the  filing  of  said  brief  since  all  the  questions  of  fact
and  law  are  and  will  be  adequately  presented  to  this  Court
by  the  original  parties  herein,  and  the  additional  burden  of
answering  said  brief  would  be  a  hardship  on  the  petitioners,
without  any  benefit  to  this  Court.  That  the  petitioners  believe
that  the  applicant  has  not  thoroughly  considered  all  the  issues
before  it  determined  to  file  its  brief,  and  never  consulted  the
